Halfway down Rue Saint-Denis, near Rue du Petit-Lion, a weathered house helped historians reconstruct old Paris. Its dilapidated walls revealed timber frames and cracked plaster; a warped triangular roof overhung the street, sheltering the dormer and entrance, while overlapping upper planks lightened the fragile structure.

On a rainy March morning a young man, cloaked like an antiquary, stood opposite studying its peculiarities. He ignored the narrow boarded first-floor windows, noted the third floor’s sash windows clouded with green glass, and glanced at the shop below, where small comic details amused him. A large timber on four pillars bore layers of paint and a whimsical cat with an oversized racket. A faded sign read “Guillaume, Successor to Master Chevrel.”

Dressed in a stylish cloak and white silk stockings, he looked fresh from a wedding or ball; white gloves and dishevelled hair hinted at recent revelry. Market gardeners hurried by; the street was unusually still, his pale face and dazzling cravat drawing attention. Three apprentices at the dormer laughed, sprinkling him with shaving cream he brushed off indifferently. A young girl’s face, recalling a Raphael Virgin, flashed and fled as a shutter slammed. The “Cat and Racket” shutters opened as a manservant hung the cloth sign. Behind iron bars, rows of brown-linen-wrapped packages filled the shop. Though old-fashioned, Monsieur Guillaume was reputed Paris’s best-stocked cloth merchant—honest and profitable—while rivals grumbled about the notary’s heavy discounts.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Guillaume kept a strict routine and lamented changes. An apprentice stopped a prank when Augustine left. Guillaume condemned idleness, insisted on hard work—unlike newer shops; apprentices were from comfortable homes; Madame denied dessert. Their routine included Mass; Joseph earned wages. Guillaume thought him suited to Virginie, though Joseph loved Augustine. Devout Virginie stifled Augustine, eighteen, who read romances. As feelings deepened, Guillaume fretted about his daughters' prospects.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
From that day the old merchant, grieving his eldest daughter’s decline, recalled marrying Mademoiselle Chevrel under circumstances like Joseph Lebas and Virginie’s: marrying his daughter would repay a sacred debt to an orphan benefactor. Joseph Lebas, thirty-three, aware of the fifteen-year gap with Augustine and Monsieur Guillaume’s rule that the elder sister marry first, suffered in silence. Warm-hearted but reserved, he kept his love hidden.

Months earlier a young painter had paused outside the dark Cat and Racket, transfixed by a scene any artist would envy. The cave-like shop opened on a dining room lit by a hanging lamp that recalled Dutch interiors. The family—Augustine among them, with a chubby maid—formed a modest, peaceful tableau. Fresh from Rome and tired of grand art, the painter craved real life. Augustine, pensive and hardly eating, seemed almost angelic in the lamp’s glow. Overwhelmed, he left unable to eat or sleep.

Determined to capture her, he returned to the Cat and Racket, even in disguise, and for eight months lived in love and study, neglecting friends and routine. One morning his friend Girodet broke his seclusion to ask about the Salon submission. The artist unveiled two masterpieces; Girodet, sensing deep feeling, embraced him and declared that love had made the work great. Despite warnings that the public might not understand them, the paintings were shown and caused a sensation. The Interior launched a new genre; the unforgettable portrait won acclaim and offers the artist refused.

These successes contrasted with Augustine’s monastic life in Rue Saint-Denis until Madame Guillaume mentioned the exhibition; with her cousin’s help Augustine obtained permission to see the Louvre. Trembling before the portrait she recognized herself, torn between terror and excitement. The artist, nearby, whispered, “You see how love has inspired me,” leaving her stunned. Fleeing the crowd she tried to leave but was drawn back, wept, and exchanged a quiet nod with him. That brief contact ignited guilt and exhilaration; she feigned a headache to avoid discussing the exhibition, dreading her mother’s planned visit. The next day her parents returned disappointed: the painter had removed the two pictures, and Madame Guillaume had lost her shawl. Yet the paintings’ disappearance had awakened in Augustine a woman’s instinctive sensitivity.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
After the ball, Theodore de Sommervieux, who had won Augustine's heart, hesitated to declare his love because her household constrained them. Augustine sat under her mother's watch in the counting-house between Mademoiselle Virginie and Madame Guillaume. Fearing rivals among the assistants, Theodore bribed a maid to arrange discreet meetings, and they agreed to meet during Mass at Saint-Leu.

At the Cat and Racket the family focused on stock-taking under Monsieur Guillaume, indifferent to Augustine’s longing; after work they relaxed, watching Cendrillon and the apprentices carouse. The next morning Guillaume resumed business, summoned his assistant Joseph Lebas to review the profits, and offered him a partnership. Overwhelmed, Joseph blurted he loved Augustine; Guillaume at first misheard “Virginie.” Corrected, he promised to consider Joseph’s feelings and suggested Joseph accompany Augustine to Mass.

At breakfast Madame scolded Augustine for inattentiveness in church and sent her away; Augustine then confessed her love for Theodore and insisted on his respectability. Monsieur, wary of artists, protested until Madame Roguin arrived with news of Theodore’s honors and fortune and advocated the match. Her report swayed the parents. By evening Guillaume informed Joseph; the family celebrated as Theodore presented a painting of the Cat and Racket. Though Monsieur remained cautious about money, he consented, and the household embraced a joyful future.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
A few months after that memorable Sunday, Saint-Leu’s high altar hosted two contrasting weddings. Augustine and Theodore were radiant while Virginie, simply dressed, followed. Monsieur Guillaume had tried to marry Virginie first, but the priests favored the more elegant bride. Neighbors praised Virginie’s sensible match and envied Augustine for marrying an artist of rank. An old fan-maker warned such extravagance would bring ruin, and Monsieur Guillaume felt vindicated by his cautious settlements. After a splendid ball the Guillaumes returned home and the Lebas family resumed their old residence. The ecstatic artist carried Augustine to their richly adorned apartment.

Their first year was blissful: Theodore lavished affection and Augustine, lost in love, believed she would always charm him. Naïve, she remained largely unaware of the wider world. As pregnancy neared, Theodore felt the pull of work, reconnected with friends, and frequented the Duchesse de Carigliano’s salons, enjoying the prestige of being seen with a handsome, accomplished wife.

Augustine liked society but soon felt out of place among Theodore’s artistic circle. Her admiration for his work was sincere but superficial, and an intellectual disconnect grew. Theodore hoped studio life would engage her, but she could not adapt; her innocence clashed with the freer manners of artists and made her uncomfortable.

Gradually his warmth cooled, and three years in Augustine sank into sorrow as his intimacy with the Duchesse deepened. Attempts to learn poetry and art only underscored her inadequacy. She briefly sought solace at her childhood home, where Virginie and Joseph Lebas ran their drapery with steady, ordinary happiness, but Joseph’s practical consolations helped little. Augustine returned to her husband’s house, burdened and longing, trying every remedy to heal their fractured marriage.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The elderly Guillaumes welcomed their daughter Augustine, whose visit broke years of reminiscence about the Lecocq bankruptcy. Monsieur inspected his shop, the Cat and Racket, and chatted with competitors, while Madame, appointed to the Army’s clothing board, filled their home with décor. Their stagnation contrasted with Augustine’s turbulent life.

Augustine, despite turmoil, spoke of achievements and marital grievances. Madame queried her husband Theodore, a painter; hearing of his late nights and eccentric habits she disapproved. Augustine defended him, turning family incredulity into a debate over genius versus marital duty. When divorce was mentioned the parents were shocked; Monsieur offered support. Overwhelmed and unable to convey her suffering, Augustine recoiled from separation and felt isolated.

Seeking, she visited Duchess de Carigliano—not to force Theodore back but to learn to captivate him. At the Duchess’s house she felt envy; the Duchess advised strength and reserve and gave Augustine a portrait by Theodore to rekindle him. Embodying that image provoked Theodore’s fury; a violent quarrel shattered her hopes. The next morning her mother found Augustine despairing amid the wreckage; she died young. Her death became a poignant reminder of the struggle women face pursuing love and fulfillment beside artistic genius; her grave’s inscription records this sorrow.

############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

The Comte de Fontaine, a loyal royalist from Poitou who had fought for the Bourbons in La Vendée, was ruined when his estate was confiscated. He married Mademoiselle de Kergarouet—noble but penniless—and moved to Paris with his large family seeking relief. After an initially discouraging period he received promotion and royal decorations that revived his hopes, though the later rise of new aristocrats left him feeling his sacrifices were unacknowledged. On the eve of returning to his estate he reentered service during the 1815 upheavals and became a trusted adviser to Louis XVIII, prized for his wit and political sense. The family prospered: his sons obtained important posts and his daughters made advantageous, if generally lesser, matches.

Emilie, the youngest, became the spoiled centre of Parisian society. Beautiful, exacting and capricious, she drew many suitors but remained indifferent, snubbing those she deemed inferior and rejecting matches for trivial faults. As the King’s health declined and political fortunes shifted, the Comte pressed harder to find Emilie a husband to secure the family’s standing, but her disdain through the winter left their future uncertain.

Nature had given Emilie every quality to play Célimène. Tall and slender, she could shift from dignity to coquettish affectation; her long neck and arched brows suggested scorn and impertinence, her fine black hair and melodious voice could charm or silence. Her alabaster complexion stayed composed except for fleeting betrayals of feeling. Many young men she spurned accused her of acting; she replied that she merely inspired desire only to subject it to her whims.

Monsieur de Fontaine realized too late that home adoration had spoiled his daughter. Constant admiration inflated her pride and selfishness, hidden by youthful charm but visible to a father who tried in vain to teach her wisdom. His gentle counsels slid off her like water from marble; her affection often took a condescending, childlike tone—demanding attention then returning to play. Though she sometimes showed fondness for her parents, she often withdrew, jealous when their love was shared with others, including her siblings. She blamed fate for her solitude, failing to see that happiness is inward. Determined to resist her sisters’ marriages, she nevertheless felt pangs of envy at their contentment; her unpredictability led her mother to suspect she might be a little mad.

This pride was common among beautiful daughters of affluent families: feeling misunderstood by mothers, suspecting maternal envy, shedding secret tears, and rebelling against perceived tyranny. They built elaborate ideals of the perfect partner, only to learn by experience that reality rarely matches fantasy.

Emilie’s standards were precise and unyielding. Her husband must be a peer of France and a soldier wearing an Order so they could be respected; he must be slim, for she despised corpulence and believed only the slender could be romantic. Her disdain for failing suitors was plain, and Monsieur de Fontaine feared her eccentricities would invite ridicule and that delay in the marriage market might bring practical consequences.

As winter under Charles X progressed, the Comte redoubled efforts, hosting gatherings to attract suitors despite his own reputation for compromising legislative integrity. After a season of introductions he hoped Emilie would choose, but her whims remained unchanged. One morning he summoned her to discuss her future, arranging his study to look dignified; she greeted him with a light, chilly kiss. He urged the importance of a wise choice for happiness. Emilie interrupted, mocked his suggestions, and reminded him of their prior agreement about suitors. Exasperated, he declared he had fulfilled his duty and that the choice now rested with her, warning that marital happiness depends on mutual respect rather than mere wealth or rank. Touched, Emilie feigned affection, yet her pride stayed intact.

That summer at the country house she asked to attend the Bal de Sceaux, drawn by the crowd’s anonymity. There she noticed a striking young man apart from the throng whose elegance and dignity sparked immediate attraction. Aloof, he watched another dancer, a pale girl named Clara; curious and jealous, Emilie followed them. Days later, learning he was not the noble she’d hoped, she pressed her uncle for an introduction. The promise intensified her interest; her infatuation grew, fed by imagination and youthful ideals, still unaware of the complexities love and marriage would bring.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The next morning, before Emilie woke, her uncle rode to Chevreuse. Recognizing the young man he’d insulted, the Count dropped his hauteur and adopted the civility of an old courtier. “I’m a vice-admiral,” he said, “and I think as little of dueling as of smoking a cigar. I had too much grog and ran you down. Let’s shake hands; I’d rather face a hundred rebuffs from a Longueville than give his family cause for regret.” The young man accepted, and they rode into the woods; the Count, playful, fired a pistol into a birch and urged him to shoot near the mark, which won his approval.

During the ride the Count probed him about gentlemanly codes. “Do you have debts?” he asked. “No.” “What, you pay for everything?” “Punctually; otherwise, we’d lose credit and respect.” The Count teased about mistresses and extravagance, confessed his own recklessness, and promised an introduction at the Pavillon Planat, urging a dinner to meet his nephew, the Comte de Fontaine. The young man—Longueville—found the admiral odd but affable and accepted.

When the house party gathered the next afternoon and a servant announced “Monsieur de Longueville,” everyone rushed to appraise him. Simply and elegantly dressed, Longueville seemed at ease in the Receiver-General’s luxury. His manner and polished education suggested a man of the world; when the admiral mentioned naval architecture he answered aptly, prompting a lady to suspect he had attended the École Polytechnique. He accepted flattery but courteously declined dinner, saying he was “Hippocrates” to his delicate young sister—an excuse that provoked ironic speculation about his profession.

Mademoiselle de Fontaine, who had dressed to attract him, felt slighted by his lack of attention. Emilie, usually charming with newcomers, became unusually simple and natural, prompting family comment and varied interpretations—some thinking she was withholding to dazzle later. Longueville’s visit pleased everyone and intensified curiosity. The Count called him “a cunning rascal.” A sarcastic dinner-table remark and Mademoiselle de Fontaine’s warning—that universal perfection must hide a defect, “the worst fault is to have none”—kept speculation alive.

Despite efforts at indifference, Emilie’s feelings soon betrayed her. Longueville returned, and by his third visit everyone saw she was affected. Accustomed to being the center of attention, she was unsettled by someone who attracted her in turn; she tried to resist but could not dismiss him from her heart. Anxiety grew as his modesty and discretion clashed with her curiosity: he never spoke of himself or his family and deftly evaded hints. If she discussed painting, he answered like a connoisseur; if she played music, he proved a good pianist. Once they sang a duet, a moment of intimate harmony, yet he preserved the mystery of his social status.

Her uncle and the family schemed to learn more, keen to meet Longueville’s sister, Clara. They proposed an informal dance to entertain her. Emilie felt joy at the thought of another person to care for and grew gentler and more indulgent; the change pleased her family, who read it as love transforming her selfishness. She believed Longueville’s visits were chiefly for her, and though they never spoke openly of passion, she sensed his affection. She drew from him small personal details and realized he, too, was studying her and encouraging her improvement.

On the day of the dance Clara and her brother were principal guests. For the first time Emilie took pleasure in another girl’s triumph and lavished Clara with sincere attention. Clara proved deft in conversation, steering away from personal topics while charming Emilie—who felt both envious and reassured. “Mademoiselle,” Clara said, “I’ve heard so much about you from Maximilien that I wanted to know you out of affection for him.” Dismissing worries about birth and rank, Clara put Emilie at ease, and they danced warmly.

Believing him well born and seeing his grace surpass her ideal, Emilie felt satisfaction. As autumn deepened, the couple surrendered to the sweet tide of feeling. Small shared incidents—aimless conversations that masked deeper affections, admiring sunsets, gathering daisies, singing duets—knitted them closer. Each casual talk, musical exchange, and stroll deepened their intimacy and confirmed the mutual affection they had carefully concealed.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

There is a particular twilight that draws reflective artists into their work. At that hour a young painter, finishing a large canvas, falls from his ladder, strikes his head, and loses consciousness. He is awakened by a young woman and her mother who rush to his aid. As he recovers he notices the girl’s beauty—matching an ideal he had imagined—and the older woman’s refined, marquise-like air. The mother tends him with gentle advice; both are reserved and withdraw without pressing for acquaintance.

After they leave, the porter’s wife identifies them as Mademoiselle Leseigneur and her mother. The painter, Hippolyte Schinner, has just moved into the attic studio and begun to gain recognition. Determined to secure a better life for his own mother, who sacrificed much for him, he keeps his private affairs guarded despite modest success. During convalescence Hippolyte replays the encounter, especially the image of the girl, which lingers; he returns to the studio eager to visit, drawn by a kinship born of imagined shared misfortune.

In their modest home he sees poverty but feels only empathy, struck by the contrast between their humble surroundings and the elegance they preserve—worn finery kept meticulously clean. Conversation, after an awkward start, turns to art. Madame Leseigneur shows a decaying portrait of her late naval husband; Hippolyte offers to paint a new one, hoping to deepen their acquaintance, and they accept. Adelaide briefly leaves and returns with candles, and the mood shifts when an older gentleman in anachronistic dress arrives—his noble, libertine air and familiarity with her hint at a layered past. Throughout the evening Hippolyte wrestles with admiration and uncertainty, drawn to her spirit while aware of the fragile boundary between their worlds.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
A remarkable, faded figure stood beside “Louis XIV’s light infantry,” Bonapartist sarcasm for the ragged remnants of the Monarchy: lean, aged, powdered hair dulled, intellect clouded. Rivarol’s quip about Champcenetz seemed apt. The silent old man puzzled the painter Chevalier—no one addressed him. His relation to the gallant man—friend, relative, or companion—was unclear; his presence hinted at a possible link to the gallant’s deceased wife but offered no answers.

One of the least dilapidated men approached Madame de Rouville, kissed her hand, and sat near her; another bowed and took a seat. Adelaide leaned over the old gentleman’s chair in a pose recalling a famous painting, though his familiarity seemed to irritate her. “Are you sulky with me?” he asked, casting a shrewd glance at the painter Schinner as if sizing him up.

Madame de Rouville introduced Hippolyte, the celebrated painter. The old man noticed Hippolyte’s red ribbon and compared it with his own hard-won honors. Hippolyte, captivated by Adelaide’s beauty, was briefly blind to the squalor around them; enthralled, he spoke little while the old gentleman lamented his losing streak at cards. Adelaide offered help; he insisted she stay where he could see her. When the game ended and the gentleman complained of losses, the silent figure only echoed the hour, prompting Hippolyte to take his leave, still under the spell of budding affection.

The next day Hippolyte longed to see Adelaide again. He waited until afternoon before visiting Madame de Rouville and awkwardly asked for the Baron’s portrait. Adelaide, alone in the drawing-room, sent him to fetch it, revealing modesty with a charged glance. Hippolyte took the portrait and promised to return it soon.

Their relationship blossomed over the following days with laughter and confidences. Schinner, eager to learn piquet, often lost, while the unspoken bond between Hippolyte and Adelaide deepened. They became inseparable; Adelaide’s affection grew and Hippolyte’s jealousy flared when the old Count showed familiarity with her. He feared their social life harmed his health and expected more from her. Their love unfolded without grand gestures, founded on mutual trust and subtle exchanges. Within two months Hippolyte visited daily, the old gentleman’s visits faded, and Hippolyte took his place at the card table, sharing equally in the losses.

Still, Hippolyte fretted about Madame de Rouville’s finances. He spent two months on the Baron’s portrait—considering it among his finest—but was puzzled by the Baroness’s silence about payment. One day Adelaide visited his studio and was awestruck by the portrait; moved, Hippolyte declared his desire to marry her. Their moment was interrupted by the old vice-admiral, who praised the work and hinted at financial help.

That evening Hippolyte noticed Madame de Rouville’s unusual excitement during cards. After a night of turmoil he found his purse missing and, in pain, suspected Adelaide and her mother of theft. A friend’s mention of rumors unsettled him further. Torn between love and doubt, he confronted Adelaide; struck by her pallor and sadness, he felt ashamed. During a tender evening of cards, Adelaide discreetly placed a new purse before him to replace the lost one. Overwhelmed, he declared his intention to marry her.

The old vice-admiral soon returned with news of a promotion and offered support to the young couple, praising the portrait and dispelling lingering doubts. The evening closed with renewed unity. Ultimately the tale follows characters navigating love, trust, and misunderstanding, finding solace through faith, reconciliation, and shared bonds.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

In October 1800 Bartolomeo di Piombo, a distraught foreigner in his sixties, stood outside the Tuileries with his wife and daughter, handed his dagger to his wife, and declared he would confront the Bonapartes. A soldier barred him for lacking a written request until Lucien Bonaparte recognized him and brought him to Napoleon. Napoleon dismissed his aides and listened as Bartolomeo sought asylum, admitting he had killed the Portas—the family that burned his vineyard and murdered his son. He confessed to avenging his son by killing all seven Portas, shocking Napoleon. Bitter at their apparent indifference, Bartolomeo reminded them he had protected the Bonapartes’ family.

Unable to condone private vengeance, Napoleon acknowledged the Corsican vendetta tradition, offered lodging in Paris and financial aid, but warned the city would not tolerate private justice. Bartolomeo accepted and pledged loyalty. Lucien found they had arrived with nothing, gave them money and promised help, noting their Corsican property could not sustain them in Paris. Fifteen years later, events would make Bartolomeo’s choices significant.

Servin, a distinguished artist of about forty, was the first to open a studio specifically for young girls who wished to paint. Morally strict and devoted to art, he was married to a general’s daughter who brought no dowry. At first mothers stayed with their daughters; reassured by Servin’s principles they soon left. To avoid social disputes and because proper training required rigor, he limited pupils to young ladies from wealthy families and refused those intent on becoming professional artists. His careful method won him a stellar reputation among the aristocracy: Servin became to feminine art what Herbault was to bonnets and Leroy to gowns; any mother who suspected talent in her daughter recommended him.

The studio—an attic above his apartment reached by a small spiral stair and sealed from below—was arranged with care: a large, light-filled room with big panes shaded by green linen, dark-gray walls hung with caricatures and plaster models, a blend of disciplined disorder and harmony.

On a bright July day the attic hummed with girls at their easels, each absorbed in different styles. Two cliques had formed: daughters of wealthy merchants and aristocrats led by the vain, pretentious Amelie Thirion, and the rest. Among them sat Ginevra Piombo, modestly dressed and anxious. Her talent and beauty provoked the aristocrats’ jealousy; Amelie, intent on ostracizing her, pushed Ginevra’s easel amid whispers. The Bourbons’ second return had sharpened political divides: Ginevra was a devoted Bonapartist and drew disdain from royalist families.

A faint sound behind a partition revealed Servin was sheltering a fugitive, an officer of the Imperial Guard. Ginevra chose proximity to protect him rather than risk exposure. Madame Servin’s arrival—hinting her husband’s absence but seeming aware of the hidden man—heightened the danger. To mask the officer’s breathing, Ginevra quietly hummed a Corsican song while sketching his head with surprising fervor. Servin praised her; the likeness drew the other girls, inflaming Amelie’s envy when her probing failed. The shared awareness of the fugitive forged a tacit bond: Ginevra helped protect the officer and resolved to aid his escape. Outside, political turmoil loomed; inside, rivalries and friendships negotiated themselves around their art.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
When Servin and Ginevra believed themselves alone, Servin admitted a young Imperial officer with his arm in a sling. He recoiled until Servin soothed him and introduced Ginevra as the daughter of the Emperor’s loyal friend, the Baron di Piombo; on hearing her patriotism the soldier relaxed.

Ginevra noticed his wound; he deflected it, but shouts from the street announced a death sentence: “Condemned to death!” At the name “Labedoyere” the officer went pale and collapsed. Tormented that Labedoyere might be executed for their cause while he hid, he bewailed his helplessness and said he would rather die than live dishonorably.

Alarmed, Ginevra offered her savings, saying her wealthy father would not blame her for aiding a soldier. She urged him to wait out the danger and suggested escape and service elsewhere. Her calm compassion moved him; he called her “an angel of goodness,” though his thoughts kept returning to Labedoyere. In silence a bond formed: acquaintance deepened into mutual understanding. When Servin asked if the officer could save Labedoyere, he said he could only avenge him. Ginevra felt stirred by the idea of vengeance and by his devotion to Napoleon.

As the sun set, illuminating his face, Ginevra experienced a near-magical connection, recalling childhood memories and leaving smiling, filled with hope.

The next day the officer—now called Luigi—told of the retreats from Moscow and Waterloo. They spoke Corsican, which deepened their closeness, and Ginevra listened with growing admiration.

Days later, Ginevra’s absence from social gatherings sparked rumors linking her to Luigi. Mademoiselle Thirion withdrew from the class; attendance fell. Laure, a fellow student, said her mother forced her to leave the studio; Ginevra reassured her but felt the loss.

Their bond became unspoken love. Once Luigi signaled Ginevra but withdrew on seeing Laure. Servin dismissed gossip and, when Luigi joked about the missing students, suggested they marry. Their silence acknowledged their love; Servin encouraged the union. Ginevra offered to help Luigi obtain a pardon, confident in her father’s influence. Her parents grew anxious over her late returns; Bartolomeo embraced her when she arrived. In the studio, amid danger and gossip, Ginevra and Luigi’s love—forged in adversity—became their strength and embodied their shared ideals.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“Here she is, my Ginevra… mia Ginevra bella!” cried the old baron, embracing his daughter. Ginevra protested that he hurt her and laughed to soften the moment. The baroness revived; Piombo rubbed his hands with courtly satisfaction and the parents seemed renewed.

At dinner Piombo teased Ginevra about her studio hours and playfully resented her painting. The mood shifted when Ginevra, eyes downcast, confessed she loved a young man. Her parents were stunned: he was no prince but a man without fortune, a comrade of Labedoyere who had been proscribed and hidden by Servin. Piombo, offended, declared she should love only her father; Ginevra answered that love was not a matter of will.

Piombo insisted on his right to expect her fidelity; she reminded him she had never reproached his devotion to Napoleon despite long absences and said she wanted a husband to protect her. Accusations followed—Piombo’s possessive pride versus Ginevra’s plea for the right to choose. She promised she would not break her word: she loved this man truly. The baroness tried to soften things by observing daughters were meant to marry, but Piombo could not accept yielding to what he called an unjust order.

Tension escalated into threats. Piombo raged that if this man loved Ginevra he would kill him; if not, Piombo himself would. Ginevra maintained one cannot compare filial and romantic love; she felt both but admitted the latter might be stronger. He thundered he would not allow the marriage; she calmly replied she would marry him. The baroness entreated peace. Shaken, Piombo asked to think. Ginevra implored him to love her for herself and promised never to abandon him. Their tenderness cooled his anger; he forgave, and they returned to a playful intimacy reminiscent of earlier days.

In the following days Ginevra was more affectionate, as if thanking her parents for a reluctant consent. She sang, saying their nocturnes needed a man’s voice. A week later her mother signaled triumph: she had persuaded Piombo to receive the suitor. That day Ginevra arrived at home on Luigi’s arm.

Luigi, an officer who had reappeared after hiding, had been restored to the officer’s list thanks to Ginevra’s appeals to the Duc de Feltre. Warned of Piombo’s possible disapproval, Luigi was anxious; his pallor deepened Ginevra’s love. Inside, Piombo sat cold and stern. Ginevra presented Luigi as a soldier who had fought beside the Emperor at Mont-Saint-Jean. Piombo inspected him and sarcastically noted he wore no decoration. Luigi said simply he no longer bore the Legion of Honor.

The baroness, trying to bridge the moment, remarked that Luigi resembled someone they knew in Corsica—Nina Porta. Luigi answered that Nina had been his sister and then, when pressed, confirmed he was Luigi Porta.

At this name Piombo reeled. He and the baroness left the room in stunned silence; Ginevra stood pale and speechless. The revelation plunged the household into dread. The baroness had once said the Portas had killed Ginevra’s brother and burned their house; Piombo had then massacred the Portas in revenge. Luigi, bewildered, asked how he had survived. He recounted being bound to a bed before the house was set alight, then taken to Genoa at six and raised by an old man named Colonna, who warned of enemies and advised he assume the name Luigi to hide his identity. How he escaped death remained unclear.

Ginevra, horrified at the vendetta revealed in a single word, urged Luigi to stay for the moment—he was safe in her father’s house—but warned that once he left he would be hunted by Corsicans in Piombo’s service. Luigi, shaken, asked if the vendetta was indeed between them; she smiled sadly and nodded. Their love, she said, must be pure and resolute to endure what lay ahead. He squeezed her hand; they resolved their destiny was sealed. Ginevra escorted him to his modest lodging herself, determined rather than cowed.

Back home the atmosphere turned chill. Piombo fumbled for words but could not speak; he rang for a servant and ordered a fire kindled, his voice trembling. Both father and daughter paid for their pride—her reproachful eyes, his sorrowful ones—each nursing private torment the household could not bridge.

On Ginevra’s birthday, hoping to reconcile them, her mother gathered father and daughter in Piombo’s study. Ginevra read her mother’s intent and smiled sadly. At that moment two notaries arrived with witnesses to fulfill the law regarding Ginevra’s marriage to Luigi Porta. Piombo bristled, furious at what he saw as an assault on paternal authority. He demanded whether French laws could tear a daughter from her father and deprive an old man of his last consolation. The notary tried to speak, but Piombo’s agitation grew; he thrice interrupted with theatrical outrage.

When the notary continued calmly, Piombo suddenly seized a dagger from the wall and rushed toward Ginevra. Witnesses lunged to restrain him, but his fury pushed them aside. Seeing him advance, Ginevra knelt before her father and begged him to be merciful and give his consent. The sight unnerved him; he flung the dagger away and, struck by his own weakness, pushed her aside as if in shame.

Then Piombo pronounced the cruelest judgment: “Go. The wife of Luigi Porta cannot be a Piombo. I have no daughter. My Ginevra Piombo is buried here.” He pressed his hand to his heart and bade her leave his house. The baroness, stunned, could not prevent him; he had renounced Ginevra publicly and irreversibly.

Ginevra fled to Luigi’s humble abode. She confessed the loss of parental blessing and their lack of fortune, but Luigi flung aside material concerns. “Then I am richer than kings!” he exclaimed. He vowed to love her in her parents’ place; she answered they would be happy, though joy mingled with dread at the long ordeal ahead.

Thus remained the hard facts: Ginevra’s candid declaration of love, Piombo’s possessive claim and violent renunciation, Luigi’s return thanks to Ginevra, and the catastrophic discovery that Luigi was a Porta—tied to the vendetta that had cost Ginevra’s brother and destroyed her family’s house. Piombo’s past massacre of the Portas made the union dangerous and impossible in his eyes, and his public repudiation left Ginevra only Luigi as shelter.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The day after Ginevra was expelled from her father’s house she took refuge with Madame Servin until her marriage to Luigi. Madame Servin received her coldly and her husband blamed Ginevra; the woman’s selfishness upset and yet strangely gave her a perverse pride. Ginevra rented a room near Luigi, who devoted himself to her and painted a bright future that lifted her spirits.

One morning trunks from her mother arrived with a loving, sorrowful letter pleading that she reconsider. The letter moved Ginevra and for a moment she thought of returning home. But when Luigi appeared her resolve dissolved; she chose to bury her grief for the sake of their love.

On the wedding day Ginevra was essentially alone; Luigi brought ordinary workmen as witnesses, a stark contrast to the usual gaiety. As they passed joyful processions her beauty silenced the small party. In the mayor’s office the clerk noted her parents’ absence and the mayor learned they had protested. The bureaucratic formality felt oppressive, reducing the ceremony to a legal act.

After signing the registers they left with mixed relief and sadness. An old priest married them in a modest chapel; his remarks about their parents stung, but a passing sergeant’s simple blessing comforted Ginevra.

They moved into a modest apartment Luigi called their true wedding, arranged with artistic supplies. They copied paintings, found clients, and Luigi prospered enough to hire assistants. Though she sometimes regretted the family life she had left, an anniversary portrait she painted for Luigi symbolized their contentment.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
At the beginning of 1819 picture-dealers urged Ginevra to produce originals instead of copies as competition grew. Madame Porta regretted not turning earlier to genre painting; though Ginevra tried portraiture she faced stiff rivalry. With only modest savings, she and Luigi grew anxious about their future.

That winter Luigi labored relentlessly in a crowded, less lucrative writing market. He could no longer afford help and had to work longer for the same pay. Ginevra painted several commendable works, but dealers shunned lesser-known artists and low prices produced few sales. Their household became dire: love and happiness were overshadowed by poverty.

They concealed their fears from each other. One night Luigi slipped out to work late so as not to burden Ginevra; she, finding him gone, took to coloring lithographs and toiled into the night. At dawn they pretended to sleep, each shielding the other from distress. On another night, exhausted and feverish, Luigi saw Ginevra’s lamp across the courtyard; confronting her, he learned she could not idle while he suffered. They shared a tender moment, watched the dawn, and felt tentative hope.

Their struggle deepened when Ginevra gave birth to a son, Bartolomeo. Joy briefly masked their burdens, but soon they sold possessions and jewels. Poverty stripped them of comfort and pride. Months later Ginevra, frail and worn, tended a sickly child while Luigi, feeling helpless, sold her paintings and the remaining furniture for a pittance. She often starved herself to feed him.

One evening she offered Luigi her last piece of bread; realizing her sacrifice, he left to seek aid, selling himself as a military substitute to obtain an advance. He returned with money to find Ginevra asleep with their child; when he woke her, Bartolomeo was cold and lifeless. Ginevra, delirious, professed her love and died in his arms. Her grieving parents were shattered by the messenger’s news—their suffering had claimed another victim, leaving only memories and grief.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############

MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ### MIDDLE OF THE STORY. MIDDLE OF THE STORY. MIDDLE OF THE STORY.

############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Some stories demand a delicate, almost anatomical touch—portraits need a soul; certain truths surface only through subtle harmonies of time, circumstance, or moral inclination.

This brief tale addresses grave, reflective readers who live on tender emotion. If the writer, like a surgeon at a dying friend’s side, reveres the subject, the reader should feel it too. It is meant for those who have known private grief: the luxury of tears, the silent pangs of memory, regrets, and smiles at past joys. Others will set the book aside; those who have never buried a beloved will not grasp these pages. The writer insists the tale is true and worth the reader’s emotional investment.

In contemporary France, speech varies by individual; Parisians describe the same woman in amusingly different ways. A Practical man notes Madame Firmiani’s rue du Bac house, well-furnished salons, an income of a hundred thousand francs, and a husband once receiver-general. A Lounger praises her social standing and excellent tea; an Egotist dismisses salons as trivial. A Student finds her enchanting; a Fop laments faded beauty yet admits lingering charm. A Connoisseur lauds her paintings; a Spiteful woman calls her a dangerous siren. An Attaché recalls former beauty; a Discerning observer notes elite ties despite modest means. A Duke feigns ignorance, a Ninny swears she sang at the Opera; two old ladies cite Cadignan links, a Joke praises the salon’s scoundrels, and rumors tie her to Murat or to ruining a young man by coquetry.

Madame Firmiani thus emerges through many lenses—each account reflecting the observer as much as the observed—and invites reflection on deeper emotional truths.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
In January 1824 opinion about Madame Firmiani varied—widow or wife, rich or poor, beautiful or plain—so no consistent portrait emerged; the social distortion was likened to lithographic blocks producing different images.

That winter Monsieur de Bourbonne, an elderly magnate skilled in estate management, grew worried about his nephew Octave de Camps. After Octave sold an estate without consulting him and a relative claimed Octave had squandered his fortune on Madame Firmiani and been reduced to teaching mathematics, the old man went to Paris to investigate. Disguised as de Rouxellay and using Saint‑Germain contacts, he arranged to meet Madame Firmiani in Octave’s absence. He pitied Octave and wondered how a woman of such luxury could be linked to a man in such straits; yet the salon atmosphere and her presence gradually captivated him.

Madame Firmiani—reported as twenty‑five but nearer twenty‑eight, married at sixteen—combined beauty, wealth, musical talent, intelligence, and wit. Her popularity provoked malicious gossip, revealing social suspicion and a complex character.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The former guardsman lingered after the other guests; Madame Firmiani found him still seated when the clock struck two.

“Madame,” he began as she rose, “I am the uncle of Monsieur Octave de Camps.”

She sat again; Monsieur de Bourbonne could not tell whether her pallor was shame or pleasure. He bluntly relayed rumors that his nephew had ruined himself for her, living in a garret while she enjoyed wealth. She stopped him. “I know of them,” she said. “You are too polite to press and too gallant to question me. It would be absurd to defend myself. Believe me: I scorn money. I married without fortune. Your nephew’s wealth or poverty matters not; I welcome him as a friend. My friends respect one another; I cannot accept those I do not esteem. My guardian angel has kept me from gossip and dishonesty.”

“Madame,” the old man insisted, trembling, “I am almost a father to Octave. Pardon me one thing. I swear as a gentleman your answer shall remain here.” He placed his hand over his heart. “Do you love Octave?”

“To any other man I would answer with a look,” she replied. “To you I ask what you would think of a woman who answered such a question. To confess love is the lover’s reward. To tell another—” She left the sentence unfinished, rose, bowed, and withdrew to her rooms. He departed puzzled.

The next morning Monsieur de Bourbonne found Octave in his garret and scolded him for squandering the advantages of an uncle who meant to leave him an estate. “Have you been gambling? Lost at the Bourse? Say, ‘Uncle, I’m a wretch,’ and I’ll hug you. But if you lie, I’ll return to my youthful ways.” He softened when Octave admitted he had “ruined” himself for Madame Firmiani.

Octave produced a letter Madame Firmiani had given him and asked his uncle to read. It revealed their relation and the moral test she had set. The letter began, in effect, “My beloved—” and explained why she was sad. She said a wife should be gay and confiding, that a woman’s glory is in the man she loves, and yet she had been humiliated by what she learned of Octave’s family. Knowing that his father had unjustly deprived others of property made her feel she could not respect him while he kept such gains. She urged him to consult his conscience: “If you come to me poor, my love shall be redoubled. If not, renounce it. I am your wife, not your mistress. A man who loves a woman sincerely respects the sanctity of her trust too deeply to dishonor himself.” She did not command restitution; she appealed to his honor and begged him to perceive his faults himself.

Octave, moved to tears, said there was more, but smiled away the rest as a lover’s secret. The uncle, surprised by the gravity of the letter and that they considered themselves married, asked whether Octave had taken up teaching and whether he still had the uncle’s capital. Octave explained that the letter had stirred a revolution in him; conscience made every pleasure feel like theft. He rushed to Madame Firmiani, and together they calculated what ought to be paid to the Bourgneufs—the family whom Octave’s father had wronged. Despite limited means, he condemned himself to pay interest and make restitution as far as he could. Madame Firmiani, prudent because of past insecurity, offered her savings and he accepted them.

“You mean that adorable woman actually saves money?” the uncle cried, half mocking, half admiring.

“Do not laugh at her,” Octave replied. He told his uncle that Madame Firmiani’s husband had gone to Greece in 1820 and died three years later; proof of his death and his will had been lost or stolen during the troubles there, so she had lived frugally to protect herself against possible loss of fortune. Octave had been trying to acquire property of his own to provide for her; he took pupils rather than rely on others. But the act of restitution had brought him joys beyond millions: finding the Bourgneufs living in misery—an old agent father, daughters keeping books and house, a sick mother—he felt like Providence in relieving them. Making amends gave him a purity of feeling he had thought he lacked; he called Madame Firmiani “my dear conscience” and hoped to earn an honest fortune by industry.

Touched, the old man said, “Ah, my boy, you have your mother’s soul.” At that moment a carriage sounded outside. Madame Firmiani entered, a little annoyed to find Monsieur de Bourbonne still there smiling. “I came to entreat my husband to accept my fortune,” she said. The Austrian embassy had just sent documents proving Monsieur Firmiani’s death and the will, which his valet had kept safe. Octave could accept it all: he was richer than she by having “treasures here”—she laid her hand on his heart—“to which none but God can add.” She then laid her head on his breast in unrestrained happiness.

Monsieur de Bourbonne concluded with an old man’s reflection: “In my day we made love; in yours, you love. Women are all that is best in humanity; they are not even guilty of their faults, for they come through us.”

############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

The Rue du Tourniquet‑Saint‑Jean, a dark, winding lane by the Hôtel de Ville that ended at the Rue Martroi, was closed by a turnstile until 1823. Less than six feet wide at its broadest, it carried gutter water, stayed perpetually muddy, and received sunlight only briefly in summer; dampness lingered. Residents lit lamps as early as five in June and kept them burning through winter. Approaching the Marais felt like passing through cellars, and the corner house at the Rue du Tourniquet and Rue de la Tixeranderie still bore iron rings from chains once used for public safety.

Built against the damp, the house raised its cellars above ground. Three steps led to a closed arched doorway topped by a keystone carved as a female head. Three high iron‑barred windows lit a small dwelling where two beds draped in green serge stood, while an old woman tended a brazier and prepared a porter’s‑wife stew. The kitchen‑dining room was spare but plainly clean and thrifty.

The old woman’s pale, wrinkled face seemed part of the house; she sat by the fire with mischief mingled with resignation as her daughter, Caroline, worked at an embroidery frame. With slow fingers and antiquated spectacles the mother occasionally glanced out the window; the daughter bent over her work with modest concentration. Their humble home reflected the lives of working‑class Parisian women. Despite poverty, Caroline kept youthful charm—rosy cheeks and bright eyes—and her spirit was not wholly dimmed. The mother speculated about passersby and hoped for a better future for her daughter; Caroline usually kept her gaze down, absorbed in embroidery.

One August day in 1815 the mother noticed a new face among the regular passersby: a tall, thin man in black whose solemn bearing intrigued her. When he passed again, Caroline, roused from indifference, felt an unfamiliar sympathy at the traces of suffering on his face and wondered if he might alter her life.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
For two months the “Black Gentleman” behaved erratically, no longer passing the Rue du Tourniquet at the same hour. Madame Crochard noticed his distracted manner—often lost in thought and seldom acknowledging her or her daughter Caroline. One mid‑September morning Caroline’s lively presence caught his eye; their fleeting glances hinted at a silent understanding.

Weeks later he resumed his twice‑daily walks, and their brief encounters became a guarded routine. Their wordless exchanges showed concern: Caroline sensed when he was troubled, and he knew when she worried about work. Both remained cautious, fearing intimacy might bring trouble.

Annoyed by Caroline’s indecision, Madame Crochard turned her displeasure on the Gentleman, who had once won her mild approval. That winter, after overhearing a conversation about the family’s finances, he was moved and secretly left money for them; Caroline found it the next day, and they shared a silent moment of gratitude.

In early May Caroline proposed a trip to Montmorency, and the Gentleman—more somber than usual—unexpectedly joined. Madame Crochard pretended to sleep, letting Caroline and the Gentleman speak freely. The countryside and easy talk lifted his spirits; sharing family hardships and personal stories deepened their bond. The day brimmed with laughter, and as evening fell Caroline voiced her newfound happiness. Roger—by that name now—playfully held her hand before leaving, sealing an unspoken promise of a brighter future.

Their story continues in Paris. Caroline has spent a month in a second‑floor flat on Rue Taitbout, furnished in an English‑influenced style: grey hangings trimmed with green silk, comfortable chairs, a writing table for love letters, a bronze clock of Love crowning Psyche, and a Gothic‑pattern carpet. As she readies for Roger’s visit, her hairdresser flatters her.

On a chilly September day in 1816, dressed in a green grenadine gown trimmed with chinchilla, she watches from the balcony, heart racing. After fifteen minutes impatience gives way to joy when he arrives; they embrace passionately after three days apart. He must attend a meeting with the Attorney‑General, which disappoints her; she objects to his theatre visits without her and declares her deep affection. Perched on his lap, she says she wishes to take his name—Caroline de Bellefeuille—and use her earnings to help the poor. She plays a sonata as he leaves, watching him drive away, longing for his return.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Five years after settling in her Rue Taitbout home, Mademoiselle Caroline de Bellefeuille sits contentedly with her four‑year‑old son Charles, who plays with a rocking horse. Now twenty‑four, her beauty reflects a happy, fulfilled life. She has embraced motherhood, devoted to raising her children and fostering their goodness. Satisfied with simple pleasures, she is indifferent to society’s expectations and seeks neither luxury nor status.

Rocking her infant daughter Eugénie, Caroline recalls joyful visits to Bellefeuille with Roger. Their love has deepened in the simplicity of shared life, free from social complication, yet she still wonders why Roger insists on secrecy and what their exact arrangement is.

On May 6, 1822—the anniversary of a pivotal day—Caroline prepares for dinner, expecting Roger. He brings a document detailing Eugénie’s marriage portion and insists that Charles, as a boy, must learn self‑sufficiency while Eugénie, as a girl, deserves a fortune. Their evening is warm, ending with a lantern show for Charles. But as night deepens Roger must leave; he promises three days off, and Caroline is left with the familiar ache of separation.

Soon after she hurries to her mother, Madame Crochard, now gravely ill. The widow’s acquaintances, unaware Caroline is her daughter, have summoned a priest for last rites. Arriving amid confusion and opportunism—local women hoping to gain from the widow’s wealth—Caroline tends her mother. Madame Crochard laments their estrangement and reveals that the priest compelled a confession about Caroline’s benefactor, hinting at a more complex web of relationships and secrets.

The narrative then backtracks to November 1805: a young barrister, Monsieur de Granville, precarious after a High Chancellor’s gathering, is urged to return for a proposed marriage to Mademoiselle Angélique Bontems that could secure his future despite the family’s stigma. In Bayeux he finds a household stifled by the widow’s piety; at church he glimpses Angélique, whose beauty rekindles a childhood affection, setting the stage for a reunion that may alter both their lives. The intertwined stories probe love, duty, and social expectation as each navigates desire against familial obligation.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
For about two weeks after the Vicomte de Granville’s engagement to Mademoiselle Bontems he frequented her dull drawing-room and watched Angélique prepare her trousseau. She never argued about religion: when he playfully handled her rosary or mocked practices she calmly returned it to its bag. Firm in Church teaching, she insisted a mother must have a religion and asked how one could judge between disbelievers and God.

Granville, moved by her conviction, sometimes mistook desire for love and was tempted to adopt her beliefs. Angélique reconciled feeling with duty, leaving him unsure of his own emotions. On the day of the marriage contract Madame Bontems exacted a promise from Granville to respect Angélique’s practices and allow her freedom of conscience; Angélique’s gentle look convinced him and the Abbé Fontanon approved. After their wedding they moved to Paris, where Granville became public prosecutor.

Angélique persuaded him to take a ground-floor apartment in the Marais near a church and chapel, arguing it would be better for their future children. Granville, who preferred a more fashionable quarter, yielded. He set up his study and left furnishing to Angélique, who took pleasure in arranging the home. He was dismayed by the cold solemnity she created—a reflection of her austere upbringing. He tried to comfort her that happiness came from love and gentleness, not material things, yet grew frustrated by the lack of warmth.

For a time the couple seemed harmonious; Granville stayed absorbed in work and remained largely oblivious to Angélique’s strict observances—he passed off her Lenten austerities as singular zeal and went to church with her on Sundays without grasping her fervor. He did not fully see it until a disruptive event. In November 1808 the Canon of Bayeux, Angélique’s spiritual director, arrived in Paris alarmed by what he saw as her moral decline and aimed to reclaim her from a lax lifestyle. Angélique adopted stricter practices that sparked misunderstandings about social obligations. Granville was irritated by her frequent excuses to avoid events, especially balls. After a tense evening he confronted her about her demeanor and dress, urging her to fulfill her social role. Arguments escalated: Angélique cited her director’s prohibitions; Granville argued the priest was overreaching. She even wrote to the Pope for guidance.

The Pope’s reply sided with Granville, saying a wife should accompany her husband even at some personal cost. Rather than reconcile them, the decision deepened the divide: Angélique and her director dismissed it. Frustrated, Granville began asserting his preferences more forcibly—ordering meat daily despite her objections—and retreated from social life, weighed down by what he saw as her bigotry.

Their home grew oppressive and joyless; the rigid piety affected everyone, including the servants. Angélique’s austerity dimmed her beauty; duty replaced warmth. Granville felt trapped in a marriage that left little room for affection. Their children suffered: he sent his sons away to school early and planned to marry off his daughters as soon as possible. Increasingly isolated, he sought refuge in work and legal study, while Angélique’s attempts to reconvert him only worsened their estrangement, turning the household into a cold, rigid place rather than a home of companionship.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The Granville household looked calm, but Madame suffered, seeking Mass while Granville lived apart.

She grew bitter after he refused a provincial presidency; her confessor suspected it. Stress and fever ruined her health, worsened by his indifference.

Abbé Fontanon revealed Granville had kept a concubine seven years, sired two children, and ruined their fortune. She meant to confront him, but an argument between Roger and Bellefeuille—who blamed her as Roger spoke of hopes—left Madame fainting. She confronted him; he blamed her piety, admitted the misconduct, refused to mend it; the marriage collapsed, leaving her devastated.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Early one December night in 1833 a white‑haired man whose face showed grief more than age paced the Rue Gaillon and stopped before a modest two‑story house with a dim attic light. A young doctor, Horace Bianchon, came out; recognizing him, he offered his arm and joked about the hour and the slippery pavement. The visitor—Comte de Granville—answered with weary pride that at fifty‑five he was still hale and often walked home from the Courts or the club when not visiting.

Bianchon teased that a man of his station courted robbery by walking alone. Granville shrugged off danger with melancholy, admitting he had been spying on a shadow in the third‑floor window of Bianchon’s house. He took a morbid interest in that garret, likening it to the idle pleasure some take in watching the Palais‑Royal being finished. When Bianchon offered to tell him who lived there, Granville refused: he would not pay to learn whether the shadow was man or woman. Nine years earlier he had ceased to be surprised that old men found pastime in small things; life’s events had taught him to distrust attachment. “I grew old within a few days,” he said. He had renounced ties to living beings, clinging only to “unreasoning animals, or plants, or superficial things”—even the grace of Taglioni moved him more than human feeling. He abhorred life and insisted nothing could interest him.

Bianchon probed: did he have children? Granville answered bitterly, listing them—the eldest daughter, the Comtesse de Vandenesse; another daughter married suitably through that sister; sons respectable in law: a viscount who had been public prosecutor at Limoges and now President of the Court at Orléans, and a younger son a public prosecutor in Paris. Each had their cares; none had devoted themselves to him. If one had tried to fill the void in his breast, that child would have ruined his life by sacrificing itself to him—and why should it? Perhaps they now waited for his money. Bianchon protested; Granville, sarcastic and self‑aware, explained his philanthropy as self‑indulgence: he paid for sensations the way he gambled, seeking illusions that might warm his heart while expecting no gratitude. He would see Bianchon die without blinking and asked that Bianchon feel the same toward him. Life’s events had flowed over his heart like lava over Herculaneum—the town remained, dead.

Unable to leave him so, Bianchon insisted he could treat Granville’s malady and then, reluctantly, described the garret’s occupant. The picture was stark: a woman of about thirty whose love had become fanaticism. She adored a young man of pleasing appearance but every vice—Solvet—a gambler given to wine and women, guilty of acts the law might punish. This woman had abandoned a comfortable life and an adoring husband, the father of her children, for him. She worked day and night; he had stolen from her, even funds saved for the children’s food and clothes. Three days earlier she had sold her hair—the finest Bianchon had ever seen—and when the lover returned she could not hide the gold quickly enough. For a smile or a kiss she gave away the price of a fortnight’s subsistence. The toil had hollowed her cheeks; the children’s cries had broken her heart; she lay ill on her wretched bed that night. When Bianchon had visited, the family had nothing to eat; the children were so weak they could not even cry.

Granville, despite himself, slid his hand toward his waistcoat pocket as if to help; Bianchon begged him instead to pity the woman and help cure her passion. Granville recoiled: how could he pity a misery whose joys he might once have prized with his whole fortune? Did not thousands of dead men, thousands of sick and old men, long to barter everything for a return of youth and feeling? Bianchon murmured “Poor Caroline!” At the name Granville shuddered, gripped the doctor’s arm, and asked hoarsely whether the woman’s name was Caroline Crochard. When Bianchon confirmed it, Granville’s reaction was violent: he accused Bianchon of awakening the worst pain his heart could endure, an emotion he called a gift from hell. He vowed he knew how to pay such debts.

They had reached the Rue de la Chaussée d’Antin when a shriveled night scavenger stood by the curb. Granville asked if the scavenger ever found thousand‑franc notes and, hearing that sometimes he did, handed him such a note with a grotesque injunction: spend it in the wine shop, get drunk, start fights, beat your wife, blacken your friends’ eyes—give work to the watch, the surgeon, the druggist, perhaps to the police, public prosecutor, judge, and prison wardens. “Do not try to do anything else, or the devil will be revenged on you sooner or later,” Granville said. He pointed out the scavenger’s corrupt face to Bianchon and declared he had thus “squared accounts with hell” and found a perverse pleasure in the money. As for Caroline Crochard, he pronounced coldly that she might die of hunger and thirst listening to the cries of her starving children, convinced of her lover’s baseness; he would not give a sou to rescue her. Because Bianchon had helped her, he said, he would see him no more.

Granville left Bianchon stunned and walked to the Rue Saint‑Lazare where a carriage waited. A servant informed him that his son, the attorney‑general, had been to see him an hour earlier and was waiting in his bedroom. Granville dismissed the servant, then confronted his son—in a tone at once imperious and unexpectedly tender—about coming after being ordered not to visit unless summoned.

Eugène, the younger man, spoke with tremor: that afternoon a very young man arrested in the house of a friend he had robbed had appealed to Granville, claiming to be his son. “His name?” Granville asked. “Charles Crochard,” Eugène answered. Granville waved him down: that sufficed. He stood a moment, then spoke as a father. “Charles Crochard spoke the truth. I am glad you came to me tonight, my good Eugène.” He handed his son a bundle of banknotes—“a considerable sum”—and authorized him to use it as he saw fit in the affair. He trusted him implicitly and approved whatever arrangements Eugène might make now or later.

The mood shifted. Granville’s last words of parental counsel and farewell were almost tender. He warned Eugène about the weight of marriage—“that act is the most important society requires of us”—and urged him not to undertake it lightly, to study the character of the woman he would partner with and to consult him. A lack of union between husband and wife, Granville warned—with involuntary shudder—led to terrible misfortunes and social punishment. He promised to write on the subject from Florence. Then, with a quietness that suggested this might be their last parting, he asked his son to kiss him and announced his plan: tomorrow he would beg dismissal from the King and go to Italy. The conversation closed on his wish to preserve his dignity as a father and as a former presiding officer of a supreme court of justice; he would not wish to blush before his son.

Thus the scene shows two faces of Granville: the man in the street hardened into cruelty and misanthropy, throwing a thousand francs to corrupt a scavenger and delighting in the chaos it might cause; and the father in his bedroom, handing money and last counsels to his son, preparing to depart and to write about marriage from Florence. Between those moments stands Bianchon’s account of Caroline Crochard—a portrait of sacrifice, degradation, and the brutal cost of passion—and the Comte’s icy rejection of duty toward her, even as the name awakens a torment proving he is not wholly dead to feeling. The narrative ends with Granville resolved to quit Paris and cut off a friend who had stirred his pity, while secretly acknowledging familial ties he will attempt to leave behind with money and admonition.
