“Ah! madame,” said the doctor, “I have some appalling stories, but each has its proper hour in conversation—” The hostess reminded him it was two in the morning and that Rosina’s story had prepared them. Still, they urged Monsieur Bianchon to tell his tale; he bowed and began.

About a hundred paces from Vendôme, on the bank of the Loir, stands an old brown house with very high roofs, solitary and utterly neglected—no tannery or tavern near it. A garden slopes to the river where once-clipped box shrubs now run wild; willows rooted in the stream form a half-hidden fence. Weeds have taken the bank; fruit trees, uncared for a decade, no longer bear, their suckers forming a thicket; espaliers look like a little copse. The once-graveled paths are overgrown—indeed there is no trace of a path. From the hilltop where the ruins of the Dukes of Vendôme cling, one can see that this spot must once have been the pride of a country gentleman devoted to horticulture. An arbor’s wreck and a partially standing table testify to that bygone care. The garden’s aspect suggests provincial peace—the epitaph of a worthy tradesman. To complete the mournful air, one wall bears a sundial with the motto “Ultimam cogita” (think on your last).

The house itself is dreadfully dilapidated: the roof leaking and warped, shutters always closed, swallows nesting on balconies, doors shut. Grasses green the flagstones of the steps; ironwork is rusty; moon, sun, winter, and snow have eaten into the wood, peeling paint and warping boards. The silence is broken only by birds, cats, polecats, rats, and mice; to the observer an invisible hand has inscribed one single word over it all: Mystery.

From the street you see a large gate with a round-arched top, full of children's holes; the gate, as Bianchon later learned, had been blocked for ten years. Through breaches you view the courtyard: the same ruinous state as the garden side—paving-stones choked with weeds, cracked walls, blackened coping laced with pellitory, disjointed stone steps, a rotten bell-cord, broken gutter-spouts. What fire from heaven, what decree, has struck this dwelling? Has God been mocked, or was France betrayed? Reptiles crawl over it but give no answer. The empty, deserted house is a vast enigma.

It was formerly a small fiefed estate called La Grande Bretèche. During Bianchon’s stay in Vendôme—where he had been left to attend a rich patient—this odd dwelling became one of his keenest pleasures. Better than a ruin, it carried a secret, an unrevealed thought: the slow destruction of a standing house seemed like an avenging hand at work. Many evenings he climbed the wild hedge surrounding the enclosure, braved scratches to enter the ownerless garden, and lingered for hours in its disorder. He would weave romances and indulge in melancholia there, relishing the unwritten poetry of ignorance; had he known the reason for the neglect, the charm would have been gone. To him the place represented various phases of human misfortune: sometimes a graveyard without the dead, sometimes a lepers’ home, sometimes the house of the Atridae—but above all provincial life, contemplative and like an hour-glass. He often wept there; he never laughed.

Occasionally he felt involuntary terrors at the dull hum of a passing wood-pigeon overhead. The damp earth required watching for lizards, vipers, and frogs; a cold would seem to settle on your shoulders like the Commendatore’s hand on Don Giovanni’s neck. One evening a weathercock turned in the wind, its creaking like a cry from the house just as Bianchon was finishing a gloomy drama to account for this enormous air of woe. He returned to his inn in a pensive mood. After supper, the hostess came into his room with a mysterious air to announce, “Monsieur, here is Monsieur Regnault.”

“Who is Monsieur Regnault?” Bianchon asked.

A tall, slim man in black appeared: hat in hand, receding forehead, small pointed head, colorless face like dirty water—a sort of usher. His coat was old, worn at the seams, but he wore a diamond in his shirt frill and gold rings in his ears.

“Monsieur,” Bianchon asked, “whom have I the honor of addressing?”

The man sat by the fire, rubbed his hands and answered, “Dear me, it is very cold. I am Monsieur Regnault.”

Regnault, it turned out, was the notary at Vendôme and, under the will of the late Comtesse de Merret, executor of her estate. He quickly informed Bianchon that the latter’s habit of walking in La Grande Bretèche’s garden constituted a misdemeanor since the estate was enclosed property; he begged the doctor to desist. He insisted he meant no offense and would be glad to allow free access if he were not bound by the testatrix’s instructions: he had never set foot in the house since the will was read. He and his colleagues had done nothing there but verify doors and windows to assess taxes; administering Madame de Merret’s estate had been the most important event of his life. His account was long and voluble, but Bianchon pressed him for the reason La Grande Bretèche had been left to ruin.

Regnault delighted in relating his own history and the tale of Madame de Merret. He had been head-clerk in a Paris office that went bankrupt; unable to buy a practice in Paris in 1816, he came to Vendôme, bought his predecessor’s business, and married into local relations. Three months after being licensed, just before his marriage, he was sent for to the Chateau of Merret by Madame la Comtesse. The Count had gone to Paris and died two months previously after falling into dissipation. On the day he left, Madame la Comtesse had quitted La Grande Bretèche, having dismantled it; some said she had burned the furniture and hangings, perhaps in the Merret meadow. Regnault had been summoned: the Countess was very ill and seemed unlikely to survive the night, having received the Sacrament. The maid who met him at the door was now a servant in the inn.

When Regnault reached the chateau about eleven, he climbed the great staircase, crossed large, lofty, dark rooms—cold and damp—and reached the state bedroom where the Countess lay. Rumors had painted her as a coquette; reality confounded that image. The enormous, old-fashioned room, papered with brown and filled with dust, had only an old Argand lamp for light. The bed was antique, the tester hung with flowered chintz; on a small table lay an Imitation of Christ and the lamp; a deep armchair for the maid and two small chairs formed the whole furnishing; there was no fire. If you had seen that vast room you would have felt transported into a romance; it was icy, funereal.

Madame de Merret lay propped up, waxen and narrow-faced. Her hair, abundant but white as linen, was shown by a lace cap; her large black eyes, dimmed by fever, hardly moved beneath bony brows. Her forehead was clammy; her fleshless hands looked like bones covered with soft skin; veins and muscles showed—she must have once been striking, but disease had consumed her to a phantom. Her lips, pale-violet, scarcely moved when she spoke. Though Regnault’s work had exposed him to many deaths and last wishes, this solitary figure in her vast chateau was the most affecting: no sound, almost no breath, yet an immense presence. When her eyes moved, she tried to raise a hand, then, with difficulty, drew from under her pillow a sealed paper. “I place my will in your hands—Oh! God! Oh!” she uttered, then clutched a crucifix, kissed it, and died. Regnault remembered a joy stamped on her last glance, an expression that made him shudder, and he took away the will: she had appointed him her executor.

The will proved extraordinary. She left most of her property to the Vendôme hospital, with a few legacies. As for La Grande Bretèche, she ordered that, for fifty years from her death, it should remain precisely in the state it was at her death: nobody, whoever he might be, should enter the apartments, no repairs were to be made, and watchmen were even to be paid if necessary to secure this injunction. At the expiration of the term, if the will had been dutifully executed, the house would become the executor’s heirs’ property; otherwise it would revert to the heirs-at-law under terms set in a codicil not to be opened for fifty years. The will had not been contested. Regnault’s account thrilled him and he enjoyed the triumph of such a legal contrivance.

Bianchon, his imagination already bathing the ruin in gothic romance, pressed Regnault for the specifics of the Countess’s eccentric injunctions, but the notary affected comical reticence. He drifted into town gossip—diffuse, contradictory opinions that nearly put the doctor to sleep. Regnault’s monotone and self-satisfaction were too much for curiosity, and the notary took his leave, teasingly suggesting that many would wish to live fifty years longer, and hinting cryptically about age.

After Regnault left, Bianchon, still stirred, sat in his armchair when his landlady, Madame Lepas, entered. A buxom, florid Flemish woman with the keen scrutiny of an innkeeper, she had overheard the notary’s history. Bianchon repeated the story of Madame de Merret, and Madame Lepas leaned close, an instinctive mix of constable, spy, and dealer in her look.

When asked about Monsieur de Merret, she described him as extremely tall, a good gentleman from Picardy, taciturn and hot-tempered but generous in paying his debts, and well-liked by the common folk despite his haughty manner. He and Madame de Merret had once been a handsome couple; she was pretty, sweet, and wealthy—about twenty thousand francs a year. They were a splendid match and their wedding had filled the town. Were they happy? “So-so,” Madame Lepas said—the common folk were not intimate with their life. Madame de Merret was kind and pleasant but must sometimes have endured her husband’s temper; being of noble birth, his ways passed as his place required.

“But there must have been some catastrophe for Monsieur and Madame de Merret to part so violently?” Bianchon pressed.

“I did not say there was any catastrophe,” she replied. Yet she then confided what she knew. During the Emperor’s time, when Spaniards and prisoners of war were sent here, she was required to lodge a young Spaniard sent to Vendôme on parole. Despite the parole, he had to present himself daily to the sub-prefect. He was a Spanish grandee with a fanciful name—Bagos de Férédia or something alike—which she had entered in her books. For a Spaniard he was notably handsome though short—no more than five feet two or three—but well made, with small, fastidious hands; he kept his hands, she said, as carefully as a woman keeps brushes. He had thick black hair, a flame in his eye, a somewhat coppery complexion the hostess admired, and the finest linen she had ever seen—finer than anything even princesses had worn at her inn, despite having housed personages like General Bertrand, the Duc and Duchesse d’Abrantes, Monsieur Descazes, and the King of Spain. The Spaniard ate little but had such polite, amiable ways that he was impossible to dislike; he spoke scarcely at all, answering rarely if spoken to—a manner the hostess guessed might be a national affectation.

He was devout: he read his breviary like a priest and went to mass regularly. Most notably, he always took his station within two yards of Madame de Merret’s chapel. At his first attendance there no one suspected intent; he never raised his eyes above his book, poor young man. In the evenings he walked the hill among the ruins of the old castle—a pastime that must have reminded him of Spain, which, they said, is all hills.

Thus Bianchon closed his tale on the picture of La Grande Bretèche, the Countess’s strange last will that sealed the house for half a century, and the shadow of a Spanish exile whose daily position near the Countess’s chapel and his solitary walks among the ruins suggest a link between foreign passion and domestic catastrophe—an implication Bianchon, Regnault, and Madame Lepas allow the listener to supply, while the dilapidated house stands mute and enigmatic by the Loir.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
One evening shortly after his arrival he stayed out late. I worried when he did not return until midnight, but he had the key and we were used to his whims. He lived in a house we owned on Rue des Casernes. One of our stable-boys thought he had seen the Spanish grandee swimming in the river. When he did come back I warned him about the weeds; he seemed annoyed at being seen.

One morning we found he had not returned at all. In his things I found a note, fifty doubloons—about five thousand francs—and a sealed box of diamonds worth ten thousand francs. The note said if he did not return, the money and diamonds were left to fund masses in gratitude for his escape.

My husband searched and brought back the Spaniard’s clothes, found under a stone on a breakwater near la Grande Bretèche. He had come early, unseen. After reading the letter my husband burned the clothes and, honoring Count Férédia’s wishes, we announced that he had escaped. The sub-prefect sent the constabulary but he was never caught. Lepas thought he had drowned. I suspected otherwise: I believed him implicated in Madame de Merret’s affair. Rosalie had told me the crucifix her mistress cherished and was buried with was ebony and silver, and early in his stay Monsieur Férédia had owned a similar crucifix that I never saw again. “Should I feel remorse about the Spaniard’s fifteen thousand francs?” my hostess asked. “Aren’t they truly mine?”

“Certainly. But have you ever questioned Rosalie?” I asked.

“Oh, I have, sir. But what can be done? That girl is like a wall. She knows something, but it’s impossible to make her talk.”

After that brief, sinister chat I was left with romantic curiosity and a religious dread—like entering a dark church with a faint light far above. La Grande Bretèche, with its overgrown grass, shuttered windows, rusty ironwork, and deserted rooms, loomed in my imagination. I felt compelled to explore the house and uncover the heart of this solemn story that had claimed three lives.

Rosalie became the most intriguing person in Vendôme. Observing her, I sensed some inner thought beneath her blooming health—sorrow or hope; she seemed to keep a secret, like a devout soul or a woman haunted by a past crime. Yet she was simple and clumsy, her vacant smile free of malice; one would deem her innocent seeing the large checked kerchief tucked into her lilac-and-white-striped gown. “No,” I resolved, “I will not leave Vendôme without knowing la Grande Bretèche’s full story. To achieve this, I will make advances toward Rosalie if necessary.”

“Rosalie!” I called one evening.

“Your servant, sir?”

“You’re not married?” She flinched.

“Oh! There’s no shortage of men if I ever fancy being miserable!” she laughed, then composed herself.

“Yes; you’re attractive enough to never lack suitors! But tell me, Rosalie, why did you become an inn-servant after leaving Madame de Merret? Didn’t she leave you a small annuity?”

“Oh yes, sir. But my position here is the best in all Vendôme.”

Her response seemed evasive. Rosalie appeared central to the affair; she seemed bound to its core. It was not a matter for ordinary courtship; this girl contained the last chapter of a romance. From that moment my attention fixed on her. Observing her further, I noticed admirable qualities; she was clean, neat, and undeniably attractive, soon embodying every charm. A fortnight after the notary’s visit, one early morning I said to Rosalie:

“Come, tell me all you know about Madame de Merret.”

“Oh!” she replied, “I will tell you, but keep it a secret.”

“Of course, my child; I will guard your secrets with a thief’s honor.”

“If it’s all the same to you,” she said, “I’d prefer it be with your own.”

She adjusted her head-kerchief and settled in to tell the tale; a certain posture of confidence is necessary for storytelling. If I were to reproduce Rosalie’s diffuse eloquence a whole volume would scarcely suffice; as her account lies between the notary’s gossip and Madame Lepas’s version, I will summarize it concisely.

Madame de Merret’s bedroom at la Grande Bretèche was on the ground floor; a small cupboard in the wall, about four feet deep, served as her wardrobe. Three months before the evening in question Madame de Merret had been seriously ill; her husband had taken to sleeping on the first floor. One night he returned later than usual from the club, delayed by animated discussions and losses at billiards. He had previously relied on Rosalie to tell him if his wife was in bed; that evening he decided to check on her, perhaps to share his misfortunes or seek comfort. He entered thinking he heard a closet door shut; Madame de Merret stood alone by the fireplace, looking anxious.

“You’re very late,” she said huskily.

Monsieur de Merret remained silent as Rosalie entered, which startled him. He began pacing, arms crossed.

“Have you received bad news, or are you unwell?” his wife asked timidly while Rosalie helped her undress. He did not answer.

“You can go, Rosalie,” Madame de Merret said; “I can manage my curl-papers myself.” She wanted to be alone with her husband. Once Rosalie left—or lingered in the passage—Monsieur de Merret confronted his wife, saying coldly, “Madame, there’s someone in your cupboard!” She calmly replied, “No, monsieur.”

That “No” pierced him; he did not believe it, yet his wife appeared more saintly than ever. He moved to open the closet door, but Madame de Merret took his hand and said with strange emotion, “Remember, if you find no one there, everything must end between us.”

Her extraordinary dignity filled him with deep respect and inspired a resolve like a legend: “No, Josephine,” he said, “I won’t open it. Regardless of the outcome, we would be forever parted. I know your soul’s purity; you wouldn’t commit a deadly sin to save your life.” She looked haggard.

“Here is your crucifix,” he continued. “Swear to me before God that there’s no one in that closet; I will believe you—I will never open that door.”

Madame de Merret took the crucifix and swore, “I swear it.”

“Louder,” he insisted; “repeat: ‘I swear before God that there is nobody in that closet.’” She repeated the words without flinching.

“That will do,” said Monsieur de Merret coldly. After a moment, he remarked, observing the ebony-and-silver crucifix, “You have a fine piece of work there, which I’ve never seen before.”

“I found it at Duvivier’s; last year, when that troop of Spanish prisoners passed through Vendôme, he bought it from a Spanish monk.”

“Indeed,” he said, hanging the crucifix back on its nail, and he rang for Rosalie.

He waited for her to arrive. “Go to bed, all of you,” he instructed, beckoning Jean, his coachman and confidential servant, to come close. He whispered, “When they’re all asleep—mind, asleep—you understand?—come down and tell me.”

Monsieur de Merret kept his gaze on his wife while giving orders, returning to her at the fireside to discuss billiards and the club. When Rosalie returned she found them conversing amiably.

Not long before, Monsieur de Merret had had new ceilings installed in all the ground-floor reception rooms. Plaster was scarce in Vendôme; he had ordered a large quantity, knowing he could sell any excess. This circumstance inspired his plan.

“Gorenflot is here, sir,” Rosalie whispered.

“Tell him to come in,” he replied aloud.

Madame de Merret paled at the sight of the mason. “Gorenflot,” said her husband, “go fetch some bricks from the coach-house; bring enough to wall up the cupboard door; you can use the leftover plaster as cement.” Then, pulling Rosalie and the mason close, he whispered: “Listen, Gorenflot, you’ll sleep here tonight; tomorrow morning I’ll provide you a passport to take you abroad to a place I’ll specify. I’ll give you six thousand francs for your journey. You must live in that town for ten years; if you don’t like it, you may settle elsewhere, but it must be in the same country. Go through Paris and wait there until I join you. I’ll give you an agreement for six thousand francs more, to be paid upon your return, provided you fulfill the conditions of the bargain. In return, you must keep silent about what you do tonight. Rosalie, I’ll secure ten thousand francs for you, to be paid on your wedding day—contingent on your marrying Gorenflot; but to marry, you must remain silent. If not, no wedding gift!”

“Rosalie,” Madame de Merret said, “come and brush my hair.”

Her husband quietly paced the room, watching the door, the mason, and his wife, without showing suspicion. Gorenflot made some noise; Madame de Merret seized a moment when he was unloading bricks to say to Rosalie: “My dear child, I’ll give you a thousand francs a year if you tell Gorenflot to leave a crack at the bottom.” Then she added casually, “You’d better help him.”

Both husband and wife remained silent while Gorenflot walled up the door; the silence on Monsieur’s part was deliberate—he did not want to let his wife speak ambiguously. When the wall was half built, the cunning mason broke one of the two panes in the top of the door with his pick. Madame de Merret understood that Rosalie had spoken to him. They all then saw the face of a dark, gloomy-looking man with black hair and fiery eyes.

Before her husband turned around the poor woman nodded to the stranger the signal of “Hope.”

At four in the morning in September the work was completed. The mason was placed in charge of Jean, and Monsieur de Merret slept in his wife’s room.

The next morning he casually mentioned, “Oh, by the way, I must go to the Maire for the passport.” He donned his hat, took a few steps toward the door, paused, and grabbed the crucifix. His wife trembled with joy—she thought he would go to Duvivier’s.

As soon as he left Madame de Merret rang for Rosalie and cried in a terrible voice, “The pick! Bring the pick! We must work. I saw how Gorenflot did it yesterday; we have time to make a gap and build it up again.”

In an instant Rosalie brought a cleaver and began demolishing the wall with an intensity beyond words. She had removed a few bricks when, turning to deliver a stronger blow, she saw Monsieur de Merret behind her. She fainted.

“Lay madame on her bed,” he said coldly.

Foreseeing what would happen in his absence, he had set this trap for his wife; he had merely written to the Maire and summoned Duvivier. The jeweler arrived just as the disorder in the room was being repaired.

“Duvivier,” Monsieur de Merret asked, “didn’t you buy any crucifixes from the Spaniards who passed through town?”

“No, monsieur.”

“Very well; thank you,” he said, casting a fierce glare at his wife. “Jean,” he added to his valet, “you can serve my meals here in Madame de Merret’s room. She is ill, and I won’t leave her until she recovers.”

The cruel man remained in his wife’s room for twenty days. During the early days, when there was some noise in the closet and Josephine wanted to intercede for the dying man, he would cut her off, saying without allowing her to speak, “You swore on the Cross that there was no one there.”

After Bianchon finished this story, all the ladies rose from the table, shaken by the last words.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

During the Restoration the Archbishop of Besançon regularly attended Baronne de Watteville’s drawing‑room because of her devoutness.

Monsieur de Watteville, a gentle, unassuming descendant of the infamous Watteville, lived quietly in La Franche‑Comté. He married Mademoiselle de Rupt—an arranged match made in 1802 and formalized in 1815. After her grandparents died, Madame de Watteville grew more pious and influential in Besançon. Thin and plain, he worked as a turner and collected geological specimens. Many admired his noble soul: he accepted that he would not dominate his wife and devoted himself to work and hobbies, often acting as his own butler. The Rupt residence reflected both families’ standing—grand rooms, elegant furnishings and fine cuisine—Madame’s fortune being considerable while his estate was modest. Their intimacy with the Archbishop attracted several notable abbés.

At a ceremonial dinner in September 1834 Abbé de Grancey announced a favorable lawsuit verdict. The Vicar‑General, calling it unexpected in the political climate, credited a young lawyer, Monsieur Savaron, whose obscure origins intrigued the guests. Amedée de Soulas, present that evening, would later prove significant.

France and England long exchanged cultural influences, especially in language and fashion: what is “English” in Paris often becomes “French” in London. Examples ranged from linking the English anthem “God Save the King” to Lulli, to hoop skirts that, after ridicule, swept Europe; fashions mocked in 1815 later returned. More recently England supplied French with terms like “dandy” and “lion,” the latter popularized by Alfred de Musset and inspiring a vogue for stylish young men that reached the provinces.

In 1834 Besançon had its own “lion,” Amedée‑Sylvain de Soulas—of Spanish descent, ill at ease in the dull military town. He kept a spare apartment, lived modestly yet cultivated an ostentatious appearance, and kept a young servant, Babylas, as his “tiger.” Seeming spendthrift but clever at economizing, his grooming and travel stories were a façade to impress the local aristocracy and win Mademoiselle Rosalie de Watteville, a modest but resolute eighteen‑year‑old whose mother fiercely guarded her honor. His life thus illustrates social ambition, cultural exchange, and provincial constraint as he strives to win Rosalie’s heart.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“Madame,” Monsieur de Soulas began as he waited for his soup to cool, “a gentleman from Paris arrived one morning at the Hotel National and took rooms on the first floor of Mademoiselle Galard’s house on Rue du Perron. He registered at the Mairie as Albert Savaron and left his card with the local officials.”

“The Savaron name is well known,” Mademoiselle de Watteville observed.

“He’s a Frenchman and no man’s son,” Amédée de Soulas replied. “If he claims the Savaron arms he must add a bar‑sinister.” Rosalie noted that a bastard of a Comte de Savarus might still be noble. The Baroness stopped the genealogy discussion, and the Vicar‑General continued.

“In a town like Besançon everything is catalogued,” Monsieur de Soulas said. “Local lawyers accepted Albert quickly. They wondered who had sent him—he left cards with judges instead of calling. He hired Jérôme, the late Monsieur Galard’s servant, who can cook; otherwise no one saw him much.”

“Does he go to mass?” Madame de Chavoncourt asked.

“He attends early service at Saint‑Pierre on Sundays. He rises between one and two, works until eight, breakfasts, works again, strolls in his garden, dines, and goes to bed about six or seven,” Soulas replied, having watched Savaron’s house and talked with Jérôme. Riding and Babylas filled in the rest of the gossip.

“How did you come to hire a stranger for your defense?” the Baroness asked the Vicar‑General.

“The President assigned him to defend a simple peasant accused of forgery. Savaron proved the man innocent, exposed the real culprits, and won their arrest. His defense astonished the Court. A merchant later gave him a difficult case, which he also won. Monsieur Berryer advised we employ Savaron. I felt confident upon meeting him—and was right.”

“Is he extraordinary?” Madame de Chavoncourt asked.

“Certainly,” the Vicar‑General affirmed, and the company pressed for more. The Abbé de Grancey described Savaron’s rooms and appearance: an outer room lined with law books, plainly furnished; a younger man than expected, in a black dressing gown edged with red—colors that struck Madame de Watteville as devilish. The Abbé praised his features: black hair streaked with gray, a broad furrowed forehead, an olive complexion, a square nose, fiery, passionate eyes, a voice both sweet and capable of thunder, average height, and hands like a prelate’s. He had a terrible yet gentle face and seemed to carry a heavy burden.

The Abbé recounted later visits—one in a shabby bedroom, another at a frugal breakfast in his library—confirming the impression of a complex man with something significant behind his aspect. Having studied his head and heard him argue against a prominent Parisian lawyer, the Abbé believed Savaron capable of great things; his portrait enthralled Rosalie.

For Rosalie the Abbé’s words were revelation. She had met ordinary suitors like the Count; now she heard of someone exceptional. She dwelt on the description—“what a fine forehead!”—and realized the window she’d glimpsed from her bed belonged to him. “I must see him!” she resolved. That night she planned to wake between two and three to glimpse his windows; she felt a thrill watching candlelight and imagined his furniture. “While the world sleeps, he is awake—like God!” she thought.

The question of how much to educate girls framed Rosalie’s state: too much enlightenment forces premature maturity; too little yields stunted minds. Her sudden inward stir from the Abbé’s portrait made the ordinary extraordinary and launched a passionate desire to see and know Savaron.

The next morning she saw Albert walking in the garden beside the Hôtel de Rupt. Having seen him, Rosalie resolved to enter his house, uncover his mystery, hear his voice, and glimpse his eyes. She embroidered with the concentration of someone outwardly idle but deeply reflective, and arranged to go to confession at half‑past seven, hoping to encounter him.

She visited her father in his workshop and suggested he build a grotto belvedere in the garden—ostensibly to display his work, really to give her a vantage point into Savaron’s rooms. Her father delighted in the idea; she warned him not to tell her mother. Young girls will contrive much for the sake of a man, even if he remains unaware.

Sunday arrived. Rosalie dressed carefully; her heart quickened as she hurried across the cross‑court. She reached church before Mass, prayed, and watched to see if Abbé Giroud would be in his confessional, positioning herself to see Albert. In her heightened state no man seemed unattractive; Albert Savaron, striking and solemn, made a profound impression. His burdened look, eagle‑like eyes, and deep gaze suggested intense thought and hidden ardor. Rosalie boldly stood in his path to meet his glance, and a rush of warmth overwhelmed her.

After Albert sat, she found a place to observe him during confession. Time in the dark felt fleeting; by the time she emerged, Mass had ended and he had gone. “The Vicar‑General was right,” she thought. “He is unhappy. Why has this eagle come to Besançon? I must know everything! But how?” She returned to embroidery, masking her excitement with an innocent demeanor to deceive her mother. From that Sunday, after their glance, she eagerly supported the belvedere plan.

“Mamma,” she said, “my father has a unique idea for a belvedere in the garden. Do you approve?”

“I approve of everything your father does,” Madame de Watteville replied dryly. Rosalie suggested a cool room in the grotto for coffee; her mother approved and inspected the columns. They chose a site not visible from Monsieur de Soulas’ windows but commanding a view into Savaron’s rooms. A builder was commissioned; the path was to be lined with periwinkles and vines, and the interior rustic, with a mirror and a modest table. Monsieur de Soulas suggested an asphalt floor; Rosalie proposed a rustic chandelier. Rumors spread in Besançon about the Wattevilles’ new garden feature.

“They’re wealthy and can afford such whims,” people said; “A thousand crowns!” Madame de Chavoncourt exclaimed. Young Monsieur de Soulas confirmed the cost and noted a man from Paris would rusticate the interior. No one suspected the true motive: the girl’s design to peer into Albert Savaron’s private study.

Meanwhile, Savaron’s brilliant defense of the Cathedral Chapter soon faded from memory amid lawyers’ envy. He remained secluded, avoiding social gatherings and thus appearing a quiet stranger, but he continued to prove his legal skill, arguing three complex cases at the Commercial Tribunal and gaining four prominent merchants as clients who recognized his talent.

On the day the Watteville family inaugurated the belvedere, Savaron was launching another enterprise: a fortnightly paper, the Eastern Review. Quietly financed by his first ten clients—forty shares of five hundred francs each—this project aimed to promote Besançon as a commercial and intellectual center to rival Strasbourg and Dijon. Savaron’s vision was to challenge regional literary dominance and bring enlightenment to Eastern France, a pitch that appealed to merchants eager to promote trade.

Savaron prudently kept his name out of the spotlight. He let his chief client, Monsieur Boucher, handle finances while he retained editorial control and a share of the profits. To manage the literary side without offending local pride, he enlisted the eager Alfred Boucher to run contributions, subtly guiding him while allowing Alfred to believe he was gaining experience. The Review, priced low, quickly gained five hundred subscribers; two hundred more would begin to generate dividends for shareholders. The editor, Savaron, remained unpaid but in control.

After the third issue, the Review gained recognition among French publications. The third number contained a tale attributed to Savaron, which stirred comment in Madame de Chavoncourt’s drawing room; conservative circles in Besançon dismissed it for liberal views. Rosalie, hearing about the Review, persuaded her father to subscribe.

“Papa,” she said, “there’s a Review published in Besançon; you should subscribe and keep it in your room, as mamma wouldn’t let me read it, but you can lend it to me.”

Monsieur de Watteville, eager to please his affectionate daughter, subscribed and lent her the four issues already published. That night Rosalie devoured the tale—the first story she had ever read. With her limited experience, the impact was profound. She hoped the narrative contained the sentiments and possibly the life of Albert. From the first pages she became convinced it reflected him; others noted that Albert often drew from his own life in such confessions.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
In 1823 two young men left Lucerne to tour the Lake of the Four Cantons. At Gersau, Rodolphe—entranced by a house and a woman at a window—stayed while his friend Leopold continued. They had studied law together; Leopold was bound for a notary’s post in Paris, while Rodolphe—the illegitimate son of a nobleman whose mother had sacrificed everything for his education—was passionate and impulsive, a foil to Leopold’s steadier pragmatism.

Rodolphe lodged with a local couple and learned the woman was Fanny Lovelace, who had brought her elderly father for his health. Of modest means, Fanny supported them by translating and writing; Rodolphe was struck by her beauty, voice, musical talent and quiet self-reliance. Gaining permission to visit the Bergmanns’ gardens, he noticed Gina, a deaf-mute girl whose gestures hinted at a fraught past and the household’s guardedness.

That evening Fanny’s singing enchanted him. A later meeting with Fanny and Gina was interrupted when an unknown assailant wounded Rodolphe; Fanny and Gina aided him to safety, deepening their bond. While convalescing he learned his mother had died, plunging him into depression. Seeking comfort in Fanny, he discovered shared literary and musical tastes and confessed his feelings—only to learn Fanny was married to an older man, Emilio. She admitted the marriage had been under duress, that Emilio was not as old as he seemed, and she encouraged Rodolphe’s ambitions.

Their affection deepened but was strained by Rodolphe’s jealousy, especially when Tito, a youth from Fanny’s past, reappeared. Though they dreamed of freedom, Rodolphe’s restlessness, Fanny’s ties to Emilio and social constraint forced them to weigh love against duty and sacrifice, the landscape reflecting their intense emotions.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
For twenty days Rodolphe stayed with the Bergmanns, observing Francesca with fascinated puzzlement. He perceived youthful imprudence combined with a stubborn reserve; the old man treated her like a father, and her gratitude hinted at an innate nobility that made her more mysterious.

Those days contained secret joys and melancholy, quarrels and harmony. Rodolphe grew captive to Francesca’s constant tenderness and jealous over trifles. One evening she confessed she wished to leave Gersau for its lack of luxuries; when he asked if she prized luxury she answered passionately, linking a love of luxury to an appreciation of art and beauty while insisting she had endured hardship without complaint. Her playful claim that ordinary people aspired to greatness revealed her charm.

She then forbade Rodolphe to accompany them to Geneva, citing town gossip and the need to protect her husband’s reputation. She professed love for Rodolphe but demanded secrecy, kissed him, and left him astonished.

The next day, after the Bergmanns’ lodgers departed, Rodolphe went to Vevay and Geneva. To avoid discomfort he rented a room in Eaux-Vives and sought Italian refugees, hoping to find a bookseller named Lamporani, but learned only that the Colonna and Gandolphini couples had arrived. On his first walk to Villa Diodati he saw Francesca in an elegant carriage with an old lady and felt dizzy. Discovering she was with the Colonnas shattered the intimacy he had imagined: her noble rank made him fear he had been only a whim.

He left his card with Prince Gandolphini and met a false Lamporani who invited him to a concert; curious but discreet, Rodolphe did not press. That night his passion intensified and he felt driven to raise his social standing. At the concert Francesca was radiant; their eyes met, and her beauty convinced him she had remained true despite her rank.

Afterward he was presented to the Colonnas and Francesca, who teasingly called herself “Princess Rodolphini,” reviving Gersau memories. She acknowledged his deep affection; he confessed an ambition to achieve greatness so she could be proud.

Francesca’s life was full of contradiction: born for the cloister, family tragedy forced her marriage to Prince Gandolphini to preserve status, yet she preferred independence, liberal ideas, and sympathized with revolutionary sentiments. Winter in Geneva passed quickly in her intelligent company, but with spring Rodolphe grew restless; her calm clashed with his need for demonstrative feeling. On a terrace he bitterly lamented others’ happiness; hurt, Francesca withdrew. Finding her in tears and kissing her, he was rebuffed—she asserted her dignity, ordered him to leave, and declared they would not meet again until Naples. He, recognizing the strength of her will, complied.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
In Paris Rodolphe discovered a portrait of Princess Gandolphini by Schinner. Though he professed indifference, Francesca persuaded him to commission originals for himself and Emilio and to begin a correspondence.

Driven by love and ambition, Rodolphe then spent three years on a deceitful venture that ruined him when Villele’s ministry fell. After three months at Villa Gandolphini in Naples to recover, he turned to politics; his talents drew notice and a high post seemed imminent, only to be thwarted by the July 1830 upheaval. Only Francesca and God witnessed his brave efforts—a noble heart unlucky in fortune. Sustained by love he endured trials; lovers, pray for him.

When Mademoiselle de Watteville finished, Rosalie flushed and wept. The story—a rare instance of a man narrating his own love—was her first encounter with that voice and convinced her Francesca Colonna was Albert’s beloved. Curiosity hardened into resolve. Doubting her looks and lacking musical talent, Rosalie resolved either to win Albert or to uncover the truth, imagining schemes: slipping into his garden at night, writing to him, or visiting her father’s estate to force a meeting.

From Albert’s window she saw Jerome, his servant, helped over the wall by Mariette, the maid. Mariette’s plainness and relative freedom explained Jerome’s interest and taught Rosalie that servants could bypass her mother’s restrictions. The next morning Rosalie accompanied Mariette to Mass and, confronting her about secret meetings, offered help in exchange for the letters Albert gave Jerome. Relieved by Rosalie’s discretion, Mariette agreed.

Rosalie’s obsession deepened when she read a letter Albert had written to his friend Leopold, revealing his noble lineage, past struggles, ambition, and steadfast devotion to Francesca. Though stung by her inadequacies and by the wrong of invading his privacy, she kept the letters. One evening Mariette brought a letter meant for Francesca; torn, Rosalie read it and, struck by their mutual fidelity, resolved to step back—yearning for the selfless love she had witnessed.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Rosalie spent the winter of 1834–35 in turmoil; by April, at nineteen, she fantasized about triumphing over the Duchesse d’Argaiolo. That private struggle fed a passionate, dark scheme and exemplifies the precarious ambitions of many young women like her.

That winter Albert de Savarus advanced his standing in Besançon while awaiting the Chamber’s dissolution. Backed by a wealthy contractor allied with moderates, he revived the old Arcier aqueduct question. Though the Doubs could supply water, Savarus declared the river water unfit and promoted supposed “Roman” water. The local Review echoed him, and the idea gained cross-party acceptance. The controversy, driven by hidden interests, dismissed opponents as backward while Savarus’s projects dominated debate. After eighteen months he had stirred the stagnant town, gaining influence without broad popularity. He also won lawsuits for local priests; ambition consumed him. His apparent indifference to material gain masked another moral usury: he expected larger rewards. In October 1834 he aided a struggling merchant using Léopold Hannequin’s funds—an act that also made him eligible for election. The Abbé de Grancey, who discerned Savarus’s designs, called him remarkable and suggested he had taken a wrong path.

Meanwhile Rosalie resolved to introduce Savarus to the society of the Hôtel de Rupt, at first content merely to observe him. The Wattevilles’ estate, les Rouxey, yielded ten thousand francs a year but was poorly managed by an inattentive Baron and an old steward, Modinier. The family often retreated there; the late Watteville had improved the site with a dam and lake. His heirs nonetheless pressed a dubious claim over the village of Riceys, whose inhabitants had long-held rights to the lands; the Baron’s death left a legacy of usurpation his descendants continued to assert.

Rosalie’s maneuvers were subtle. In May 1835 she left Besançon with her father for les Rouxey, enchanted by the lake and linking it with love. On a boat trip her father confided legal troubles over Riceys; Rosalie urged him to hire Savarus, stressing the importance of securing the Dent de Vilard for their estate. She argued a lawsuit would add excitement and insisted on bringing Savarus to les Rouxey, clearly aiming to fix her future there and create gardens.

Four days later the Abbé de Grancey visited Savarus to persuade him to represent the Wattevilles. Savarus hesitated, fearing alignment would compromise his neutrality; the Abbé pressed political advantages, notably potential votes. Rosalie, determined to keep Savarus in Besançon and prevent his election, even wrote to the préfet exposing his ambitions, but was ignored. As the election approached, influential men unexpectedly nominated Savarus—he had been unaware. The Abbé warned him of betrayals and urged openness about his past. Savarus’s passionate speech captivated many, yet the préfet worked to undermine him, and the first vote disappointed Savarus. He returned home in despair, thinking of Francesca and an uncertain future. Then the Abbé brought hope: he had secured behind-the-scenes support, promised the Rouxey case would be resolved in time, and suggested a marriage to Sidonie de Chavoncourt might consolidate votes. Savarus, initially resistant, recognized the advantage and prepared to navigate the coming choices of love and politics.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
By nine the next evening Madame la Baronne de Watteville’s rooms filled with Besançon society to debate the surprise nomination of the Rupts’ daughter; her father had been Master of Appeals and secretary to an Elder Branch minister. Madame de Chavoncourt attended with her younger daughter Sidonie; her elder sister stayed away. The Abbé de Grancey steered conversation, probing an elderly statesman about whom the Elder Branch might field. Guests feared Berryer, noted the Duc de Fitz-James’s Toulouse nomination, and worried that backing Monsieur Savarus might push Republicans toward them rather than the Moderates.

By half past nine Albert had not arrived. The Baroness took his lateness as an affront; Madame de Chavoncourt counseled patience. Rosalie, watching hostess and mother, noticed special leniency toward Albert and hinted a possible match with Sidonie. By ten impatience spread and some gentlemen began to play cards. The Abbé, uneasy, went to the window where Rosalie had withdrawn and suddenly cried, “He must be dead!” The Vicar-General, Monsieur de Watteville, and Rosalie went to Albert’s rooms; they were dark and silent. The servant Jérôme said Albert had left in a post-chaise. The Abbé cried ambiguously, “He is ruined! Or he is happy!”—and Rosalie’s face betrayed a joy the Vicar-General pretended not to notice.

Back in the drawing-room Monsieur de Watteville announced Albert’s unexplained departure. By half-past eleven about fifteen guests remained, among them Madame de Chavoncourt and her daughters, the Abbés de Godenars and de Grancey, Monsieur de Vauchelles, Rosalie, Amédée de Soulas, and a retired magistrate who had supported Albert. Grancey positioned himself near the Baroness to watch Rosalie, whose pallor had given way to agitation.

When Madame de Chavoncourt inquired, a servant delivered a letter on a silver tray for the Abbé. He read it; Rosalie went pale and he recognized the handwriting. After a meaningful glance he pocketed the letter and declared, “She is in love with Albert Savarus!” As he moved to leave, Rosalie stopped him: the letter, she confessed passionately, was from Albert, and she begged him to remain silent about Albert’s withdrawal—knowing such an announcement would destroy any hope of her sister’s marriage to him.

On her way to Mass Mademoiselle de Watteville learned from Mariette that Albert had vanished precipitously. An elderly Parisian had visited Monsieur Savaron; Savaron grew pale at the card, met him for over an hour, left with a large package, then hurried to the Prefecture, bought an old carriage with post horses, quit his post, and obtained a passport. His abrupt departure inflamed local prejudice against outsiders. Most soon forgot Albert—except Girardet, the Vicar-General, and Rosalie, who alone knew the truth about the Duc d’Argaiolo’s death.

By April 1836 there was still no news of Albert. The Baroness pressed Rosalie to marry; the Baron promised the family estate, les Rouxey, though his wife fretted over finances. A match with Monsieur de Soulas was arranged; Rosalie refused. Tensions rose during a fraught summer at les Rouxey; after Mariette and Jérôme married the Baron invested in the estate, which only increased the Baroness’s resentment. The Vicar-General tried to mediate.

Alone with him, Rosalie confessed she had sabotaged Albert’s relationship with Francesca, the Duchess d’Argaiolo, by intercepting their letters and causing their separation. Horrified, the Vicar-General urged her to seek forgiveness. Her guilt deepened when a Parisian clerk acting for Albert came to sell his belongings and revealed Albert had renounced the world and entered a monastery. The Vicar-General wrote to the monastery and received Albert’s letter: he forgave Rosalie and wished to remain in solitude.

He urged Rosalie to reconcile with her mother and accept de Soulas; she refused. After his death Madame de Watteville married de Soulas, believing Rosalie’s support. Now of age and financially independent, Rosalie went to Paris to confront the Duchess, publicly admitting at a ball her part in Albert’s downfall. She returned to Besançon having exposed the truth, but at heavy emotional cost—a tale of love, betrayal, and the lasting consequences of ambition.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Mademoiselle de Watteville of Les Rouxey lived alone, rode, hunted, and improved her estate. Madame de Soulas, a young mother of two married to the elderly Monsieur de Soulas (who told Chavoncourt that marrying a bigot had ruined him), visited the Grande Chartreuse annually and recalled a grand‑uncle who forced his way through monastery walls to escape. In 1841 she left Besançon to marry, but after a Loire steamboat boiler explosion disfigured her and cost her right arm and left leg, she returned; in poor health she devoted herself to religion there.

PARIS, May 1842.

ADDENDUM

Characters in the Human Comedy: Vicomtesse de Beauseant (Father Goriot; Deserted Woman); Genovese (Massimilla Doni); Léopold Hannequin (Beatrix; Cousin Betty; Cousin Pons); Jeanrenaud (Commission in Lunacy); Gaston de Nueil (Deserted Woman); Duc Alphonse Rhetore (Bachelor’s Establishment; Distinguished Provincial at Paris; Scenes from a Courtesan’s Life; Letters of Two Brides; Member for Arcis); Savaron de Savarus (Quest of the Absolute); Hippolyte Schinner (Purse; Bachelor’s Establishment; Pierre Grassou; Start in Life; Government Clerks; Modeste Mignon; Imaginary Mistress; Unconscious Humorists); Clarina Tinti (Massimilla Doni).

############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Sweetheart, I too am free! I write first, unless you have already written to Blois.

Do not be alarmed; leaving the convent was simple. The conscience that tormented me gave way to gentler reasons: my aunt feared I would waste away, and my mother relented. I am in Paris thanks to you. If you had seen me when I left you, you would have been proud of your influence on my young heart. At the convent we had been so intimate—our imaginations fused and that closed world became a playground—that when you left I sank into deep melancholy. I was like a turtle-dove widowed of her mate; death seemed sweet, and I nearly succumbed. In Blois, consumed by dread of taking vows and without you, I fell ill.

My aunt could not understand our private life or how the convent’s strict routine ill suited girls of our temperament. She had sacrificed for a brother; to expect the same of us would be too much. For a fortnight I suppressed everything, but letter-writing became my lifeline. I began a journal mirroring yours, and dreamed of Gemenos as you will dream of Paris.

One morning a lady companion and Philippe, my grandmother’s last valet, came for me. My aunt kissed me and spoke of the pride she saw in me, which might lead to heaven or hell. I kissed her hands and promised I would not return unless my heart were broken.

At Beaugency I felt stunned. No one was home to receive me—my mother at the Bois de Boulogne, my father at council, my brother slow to dress. Philippe led me to the suite that had belonged to my beloved grandmother, the Princess de Vauremont, whose fortune was left to me. Entering the rooms where she had died brought grief; though she died in 1817, the place seemed recently occupied. It pained me that so little had been done since: panels empty, marbles broken. Philippe told me the Princess’s furniture had been bequeathed to me and my father awaited a law to restore lost property before renovating—an estimate of three hundred thousand livres had been set.

At first despair gripped me: had my father meant to send me to the convent while sparing no expense on my household? But memories of the Princess softened me; the rooms preserved her spirit. I set Philippe to polishing and rearranging, giving the old furniture a youthful air.

The bedroom and drawing-room, hung in yellow damask and scarlet and filled with family relics—Savonnerie tapestry, a timepiece from Marshal de Saxe, china from Marshal de Richelieu, my grandmother’s portrait—breathed of another century and her coquetries. I remembered how she tended her appearance to the end; traces of nocturnal visitors, disarranged furniture, card-tables, snuff remained. When I was sent to the convent in 1816 she bade me go without display, promising I should remain independent and marry whom I pleased. Six months later she died; her will, entrusted to the Prince de Talleyrand, hinted she disapproved of the vows. I hope to meet the Prince and learn more.

Thus, though no one in flesh met me at first, the Princess’s presence comforted me, and I resolved to keep our pact: to tell each other the smallest domestic details. Send me every minute picture of your life.

October 19.

At half-past five, Rose announced my mother’s return. My mother’s suite corresponds to mine, and we share a secret staircase. I descended anxious about our reunion, wondering if I should feign affection. Her tact surprised me: she was neither distant nor effusive, but greeted me like a friend and kissed my forehead.

She said she preferred my living with family to dying at the convent, and wished to be more sister than mother. I found her charming at thirty-eight—beautiful, with dark-blue eyes, a smooth forehead, a marvelous figure and hands—and I responded kindly. Her manner warmed; she took me in and said we had guests for dinner, advising I not make my first appearance in society before visiting the dressmaker.

My father then arrived and greeted me affectionately, calling me his rebel daughter and praising my looks; he predicted pride in my future. My brother, Duc de Rhetore, arrived leisurely and kissed me; I was conscious of his indifference that I had not been at Blois when expected.

I tidied my rooms and set to write you. Thus ended the return of an eighteen-year-old after nine years at a convent, to a noble household where the old ways linger. Tired, I kept convent hours and slept early.

November 25.

The next morning the rooms were ready; Philippe had put flowers in the vases. Breakfast was troublesome as convent habits left me hungry early. About one, my father knocked and entered to find me writing to you. He gave me a purse containing twelve thousand francs as the annual allowance he intended, and said my grandmother left me five hundred thousand francs in government stock, now yielding about forty thousand francs a year. He had planned to use this to set up my younger brother but now left the decision to me, trusting my good sense. He warned that a Chaulieu daughter must comport herself with pride; any slander might have dire consequences. His frankness pleased and reassured me.

Philippe spent the morning arranging my transformation: dressmaker Victorine, corset-maker, shoemaker, glove-maker—all to fashion me out of convent attire. I was impatient to be made presentable; even a glovemaker was summoned. At lunch, my mother proposed we go to the milliner; I felt dazzled by this independence—like a blind man who had recovered sight. I began to sense how far I had been from society.

My father seemed absent at lunch; he is deeply engaged in state affairs and had refused an appointment as Minister of State, preferring an embassy. At home, family life is fragmentary: my brother and father are often away, my mother devotes mornings to dressing and keeps a busy social calendar of drives, receptions, and theatre evenings. We meet mainly at lunch and dinner; otherwise each pursues his own life. I see now why family affection is so seldom; in Paris people find no time for private intimacy.

Miss Griffith, my governess—recommended by the English ambassador—is a Scotch clergyman’s daughter, thirty-six, well-bred and meant to teach me English and chaperon me. She is cautious but accommodating; I quickly perceive she will be governed by me. She advised against being dazzled by society and against being seduced by what attracts me most—her moral lectures are brief and repetitive.

I have not yet seen the streets; I must first be made presentable. The Italian opera opens soon, and my mother has a box. I read avidly; modern literature often dwells on love—the theme that once obsessed us at the convent. Corinne and Adolphe delighted me. When I asked if I might meet Mme. de Staël, my parents laughed and informed me she was dead.

December.

My Darling—tomorrow I make my debut. I have rehearsed all my follies, tried on stays, shoes, curls, dress, ornaments—performed a private inspection. Griffith alone witnessed my little play. In short, I have left the convent and been reborn as a frivolous society girl. I may be raw in some ways—shoulder lines not yet rounded—but there is strength, health, and a clear, vivid complexion. My features are correct, my hair pale gold with a darker center, my eyes bright and insolent, the mouth expressive. I have grace of gait and voice; my grandmother’s manner and voice are in me. If I wished, I can be innocent, witty, or melancholy enough to command admiration.

My father has provided me with a trousseau—ribbons, shoes, gloves, dressing-case, scent-box, fan, sunshade, prayer-book, cashmere shawl—and promised riding lessons. I can dance; tomorrow evening I am to appear in white muslin, with a Greek garland of white roses, and I intend to play the simpleton to win the other women. My mother underestimates me and sees me as a simpleton; my brother treats me with polite contempt. I suspect Alphonse harbors secret discontent and jealousy toward our father, which none of the household understands.

We are so unlike my parents: my father absorbed in public life and pleasures, my mother devoted to her toilette and society. They both know how to wear their parts; the show of unity in the family conceals indifference. It amazes me how Paris life can consume people from nine until the small hours, draining both energy and money. I am newly curious about this spell of society that keeps people enslaved and separate. For now I have sixteen hours a day to think, while those around me barely have minutes for reflection. There is much to learn and plumb in this world I sought; I am eager and anxious at once.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Yesterday at two o’clock I drove along the Champs-Élysées and through the Bois de Boulogne on a beautiful autumn day that reminded me of the Loire. At last I felt I had experienced Paris; the Place Louis XV struck me as a testament to human artistry.

I was impeccably dressed, pensive, and poised, yet no one smiled. The carriage moved slowly, matching my demeanor. Only one person acknowledged me with satisfaction—a duke, my father—and my mother blew a butterfly kiss. Griffith, ever observant, scanned indiscriminately; a young woman should know where to look.

I was furious. A man inspected my carriage without seeing me—probably a carriage-maker. I had misjudged my appeal; beauty, it seems, is common in Paris. Hats were tipped to the less deserving while my mother drew admiration. I resolved to learn why.

The men generally disappointed: unattractive, mere echoes of former elegance. Their fashion lacked the color and comfort of earlier days; hats perched like truncated columns. It seems easier to incite a revolution than to make a stylish hat, yet the French cling to ridiculous styles. Most faces looked worn, betraying thwarted ambition and bruised vanity; a fine forehead was rare. “And these are the products of Paris!” I said to Miss Griffith. “Most cultivated and pleasant men,” she replied. I was silent; a thirty-six-year-old spinster’s heart practices tolerance.

That evening I went to a ball, staying close to my mother, who received all attention. I was merely a pretext for compliments aimed at her. She found me partners who all praised the heat and beauty of the ball as though I were freezing and blind; each man pretended it was the first time he saw me.

My dazzling dress paled beside the extravagant attire of married women, each surrounded by admirers and watching one another closely. A few shone with genuine beauty—my mother among them. At a ball a girl is merely a dancing machine—insignificant.

The men impressed me no more there than on the avenue: exhausted, bland, uniform. The proud, vigorous men of our ancestors seemed vanished. One man stood out for his beauty yet failed to stir me. He was not noble; no matter his genius, a commoner could not move me. He seemed absorbed in himself, as if intellectuals view women as objects. When they love, they should abandon their writing; otherwise their love is not genuine. I learned he is a professor and author, driven by ambition.

I resolved not to quarrel with society for failing to recognize my merits and instead embraced the simple joy of dancing, which I thoroughly enjoyed. I overheard trivial gossip about people I did not know; perhaps my ignorance spared me. Society is a web of intrigue, and I am perceptive.

Returning home pleasantly tired, I began sharing my experiences with my mother. She interrupted, advising me to confide only in her: “My dear child, it requires as much tact to know when to be silent as when to speak.” Her words revealed feelings that should remain concealed even from a mother. I realized the vast realm of feminine duplicity. Suddenly I felt overwhelming shyness. Must I never again express my joy in dancing? What of deeper emotions?

I went to bed sorrowful, reeling from the clash between my candid nature and society’s harsh rules. Already the hedges of my life are flecked with white wool! Farewell, beloved.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
How deeply your letter moved me, especially as our paths diverge: you to Parisian brightness, I to a quiet retreat.

At the Castle of Maucombe my room is unchanged, now opening onto the Gemenos valley I once saw without understanding. Soon after my arrival my parents took me to dine with M. de l'Estorade, a wealthy, frugal country gentleman. He lost his only son to Bonaparte’s conscription in 1813; the youth evaded the draft, entered the Emperor’s bodyguard, and disappeared after the Battle of Leipzig. The Baron clung to hope and hoarded his income for him. Only later I learned the son had been slowly returning from Russia, Poland, and Germany; his fortunes changed in Berlin where the French Minister helped him home. The Baron, a small Provençal landowner with about ten thousand livres a year, remains obscure in Provence despite these adventures.

The combined sum from the late Mme. de l'Estorade’s estate and the father’s savings is roughly two hundred and fifty thousand livres—excluding property—a considerable fortune here. The Baron has just bought a poorly run estate he plans to plant with ten thousand mulberry trees. Now that his son is found, his one desire is to marry him to a girl of good family.

My parents approved once they learned Renée de Maucombe would be accepted without a dowry and that her prospective inheritance would be acknowledged in the marriage contract. My younger brother Jean signed a document recognizing an advance on his inheritance. This is how the nobles of Provence work under Bonaparte’s Civil Code, which otherwise forces many girls into convents rather than marriage.

That dinner was the first meeting of your sweetheart and the exile. My future father-in-law has no castle, only a simple country house at the entrance of a beautiful valley: unpretentious, with four pebble walls and a red-tiled roof, a typically Provençal garden enclosed by low walls and a touch of neglect. Still, the house did its best for the dinner, serving us on old silver.

The exile is emaciated and pale, marked by suffering. At thirty-seven he looks much older, his once-beautiful hair streaked with white; he is a little deaf, like the Knight of the Sorrowful Countenance. Despite his ravaged appearance, he seems pleased to be marrying the fair Renée de Maucombe.

I have graciously agreed to become Mme. de l'Estorade, bringing the two hundred and fifty thousand livres, on condition that I may transform the grange into a park. I have asked my father for a water grant to help this. In a month I will be Mme. de l'Estorade, having made a good impression.

While you prepare to enjoy Paris’s vibrant life, I descend from our shared dreams into the humble realities of country existence. I am determined to bring joy to this young man, who knew little youth, going straight from his mother’s arms to war and the harshness of Siberia. I will take pleasure in the quiet, improving the little oasis around our home with fine trees and greenery: a hillside park with perhaps a kiosk from which I can glimpse the Mediterranean, orange and lemon trees, and the poetry of the surrounding nature. My father-in-law and the Chevalier share my religious sentiments.

My life will run like a smooth highway, free from the turmoil of another Bonaparte. If I have children they will be mine to nurture and guide; if you remain true to your destiny, your children will have in me a devoted protector. I have bade farewell to the romances and adventures we once imagined: my milestones will be the care of the young Masters de l'Estorade and the mischief they bring. I will embroider their caps and be cherished by a frail man in the Gemenos valley. Perhaps one day I may visit Marseilles; otherwise provincial dangers are minimal. We will interest ourselves in the silkworms whose mulberry leaves we sell and share in Provence’s ordinary ups and downs. M. de l'Estorade has promised to leave household matters to me, and I shall see that he does.

You, dear Louise, will provide the romance in my life. Tell me of your balls, your dresses, and the men you meet; your other self will always be with you, listening and feeling the excitement. If only I could enjoy some Parisian fun while you managed La Crampade—poor M. de l'Estorade thinks he is marrying one woman; will he discover two?

One kiss on each cheek; my lips remain untouched, he has only taken my hand. P.S. I have just opened your third letter. I have about one thousand livres to spend; buy me pretty things we cannot find here or in Marseilles. I will answer your letter later. Goodbye, dear.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The heading shows the head of your house is safe. The massacre of our forebears in the Court of Lions forced us into double identity, but it taught an Arab cunning that likely saved my life.

Ferdinand’s fear made his acting convincing enough for Valdez to believe him. Had I not intervened, the Admiral would have perished. The Liberals still do not grasp kingship. I know this Bourbon well; his repeated assurances of protection only confirm my doubts. A true Spaniard need not repeat promises; excess words often signal deceit.

Valdez left on an English ship. Seeing the cause of my beloved Spain fail in Andalusia, I set my escape in motion. I ordered my steward, sent coral fishers to meet me on a coast, and while Ferdinand sought to seize me, I was already on my barony of Macumer among brigands who defy authority.

The last Hispano-Moorish family of Granada has found refuge in an African desert, even a Saracen horse on an estate inherited from Saracens. The brigands who once feared my authority now shield me from the King’s vendetta, proud to defend the Duc de Soria, a Henarez—the first to visit them since the Moors ruled.

I had hoped to live on the estate’s income, but Quevedo was right: I have many nominal possessions but no real livelihood—vast prairies without houses, virgin forests with no furniture. A million piastres and a resident master for fifty years would be needed to make these lands profitable. This must be addressed.

Watching my noble country reduced to a corpse for monks to exploit moved me to tears; it foreshadows Spain’s grim future. In Marseilles I learned of Riego's fall. It struck me that my life would be prolonged, unnoticed martyrdom. Is existence worth living when one can neither die for country nor live for a woman? To love and to conquer—this was our law. Yet my sabre is broken, the palace in ruins, the spring dried to sand.

Here is my last will and testament.

Don Fernand, you now see why I restrained your enthusiasm and urged loyalty to the rey netto. As your brother and friend I implore you to obey; as your master I command. Approach the King and claim my dignities, property, office, and titles. He may hesitate, but tell him you are loved by Marie Heredia and she can marry none but a Duc de Soria. This will please the King: the Heredia fortune has long been a barrier to my ruin. Your proposal will seem to deprive me of a final resource, and he will gladly hand over my spoils.

You will then marry Marie. Her secret love for you was no secret to me; I have prepared the old Count for your ascension. Marie and I discharged our family duties; everything favors you. You are handsome and possess Marie's heart. I am an ill-favored grandee for whom she harbors an unconfessed aversion; any reluctance due to my misfortunes you will soon overcome.

As Duc de Soria, you will not regret my predecessor nor lose a maravedi. I will keep my mother's diamonds for my independence; the gap they leave can be filled by Marie's jewels. Send them with my nurse, old Urraca—the only servant I shall retain. No one makes my chocolate as she does.

During our short revolution, my wants were reduced to bare necessities, covered by my salary. You will find the income from the last two years with your steward. This sum is mine, but a Duc de Soria cannot marry without expenses, so we will share it. You will not refuse this wedding gift from your brigand brother; I intend it so.

The barony of Macumer, not Spanish territory, remains mine. Thus I still have a country and a name should I wish to re-enter the world.

Thank Heaven, this concludes our business, and the house of Soria is saved!

Just as I resigned myself to being merely Baron de Macumer, the French cannon announced the arrival of the Duc d'Angouleme. You will understand why I must stop here.

October.

When I arrived I had not ten doubloons. A poor leader finds means to live within calamities he cannot avert: for the vanquished Moor, a horse and the desert; for the frustrated Christian, the cloister and a few gold pieces.

My resignation is weariness. I am not ready to abandon all thoughts of life. Ozalga gave letters of introduction, including one to a bookseller who serves our compatriots as Galignani serves the English. He found me eight pupils at three francs a lesson. I teach every other day, earning twelve francs—more than I need. When Urraca arrives I will pass my connection to another Spanish exile.

I lodge in Rue Hillerin-Bertin with a poor widow who takes boarders. My room faces south, overlooking a small garden. It is quiet; I have green trees to gaze on, and I spend one piastre a day. I am amazed at the calm pleasure of this life, reminiscent of Dionysius at Corinth. From sunrise until ten I smoke and enjoy chocolate, contemplating two Spanish plants—a broom rising from a clump of jasmine—gold on white, colors that thrill any scion of the Moors. At ten I give lessons until four, return for dinner, then read and smoke until bed.

I can endure this life of work, meditation, solitude, and modest society for a long time. Be happy, Fernand; my abdication brings no regrets. Five days have passed since I wrote my will; they feel like five centuries. Honor, titles, wealth—as if they never existed.

Now that the conventional barriers of respect are down, I open my heart to you, dear boy. Though outwardly grave, my heart is full of tenderness and devotion—never granted to any object, not even she who was to be my bride since childhood. This is the source of my political enthusiasm. Spain took the place of a mistress and received my homage. Now Spain is lost. Beggared of all, I gaze upon the ruins of my former self and ponder the mystery of my existence.

Why was life given this carcass, and when will it depart? Why did a race famed for chivalry infuse all its virtues into this last offshoot only for the seed not to sprout—no stem, no flower to scatter its Eastern perfumes? What crime did I commit before birth to inspire no love? Was I destined to be stranded, a useless hulk on a barren shore? In my soul I see deserts where my ancestors roamed under a scorching sun that shrivels life. Proud remnant of a fallen race, vain force, love wasted—an old man in the prime of youth—I shall await death's last grace. Under this murky sky no spark will rekindle these ashes. Thus my last words may echo Christ's: "My God, Thou hast forsaken me!" A cry of agony and terror whose depth few mortals have explored.

You can now understand, Fernand, the joy I find in living through you and Marie. I will watch you with the pride of a creator satisfied with his work. Love each other well and continue to do so; any discord between you would pain me more than it would you.

Our mother had a premonition that events would one day fulfill her wishes. Perhaps a mother's longing is a pact with God. She often revealed a heart that rightly belonged to both her sons, yet was given to you alone by an irresistible passion. Her spirit will hover above you as you bow at the altar. What I have done pleases our womenfolk, the dead, and the King; it is God's will. Do not resist, Fernand; obey and remain silent.

P.S. Tell Urraca to call me nothing but M. Henarez. Do not mention me to Marie. You must be the sole living soul to know the secrets of the last Christianized Moor, whose veins carry the blood of a great family that began in the desert and is now about to die out in a solitary exile.

Farewell.

What! To be married so soon? This is unheard of. After a month you become engaged to a stranger about whom you know nothing. He may be deaf—there are many kinds of deafness! He could be sickly, tiresome, insufferable!

Don’t you see, Renée, what they want from you? You’re needed to carry on the glorious stock of the l’Estorades, that is all. You will be buried in the provinces. Are these the promises we made each other? Were I you, I’d rather sail to the Hyères islands in a caique, risk capture by an Algerian corsair, and be sold to the Grand Turk. Then I could be a Sultana and make a stir in the harem while I’m young—yes, and afterwards too!

You are leaving one convent for another. I know you; you are a coward, and you will submit to family life with docility. But I will guide you; you must come to Paris. There we shall drive men wild and hold court like queens. Your husband may join the Chamber in three years. I know how those members work; you will work that machine well; you can live in Paris and become, as my mother calls it, a woman of fashion. Oh! do not think I will leave you in your grange!

Monday.

For a fortnight I have lived the life of society: one evening at the Italiens, another at the Grand Opera, and always a ball afterward. Society is bewitching. The Opera’s music enchants me; while my soul is immersed, I am the focus of admiration and opera glasses. Yet none of the young men have stirred me; not one has evoked the feeling I feel listening to García in Otello. How jealous Rossini must have been to express jealousy so well! What a cry in "Il mio cor si divide!" I speak Greek to you, for you’ve not heard García, but you know how jealous I am!

What a wretched dramatist Shakespeare is! Othello wins battles and leaves Desdemona meekly neglected. Such a sheep deserves slaughter. Let the man I love beware of thinking of anything but loving me!

I prefer the long trials of old chivalry. That lout of a young lord who took offense because his sovereign lady sent him among the lions to fetch her glove was impertinent. Doubtless the lady had some exquisite prize for him, which he lost—the puppy!

But I ramble. My father is likely to represent our master the King in Madrid. I say "our" master, for I shall be part of the embassy. My mother wishes to remain; my father will take me to have a woman with him.

This seems simple, but there are complications. In a fortnight I have probed the household’s secrets. My mother would accompany my father to Madrid if he took M. de Canalis as secretary. But the King appoints secretaries; the Duke cannot offend the King, who hates opposition. The cunning diplomat thinks he cut the knot by leaving the Duchess here. M. de Canalis, the great poet, cultivates my mother’s society and studies diplomacy with her from three to five. Diplomacy must be a fine subject, for he keeps at it as regularly as a gambler at the Stock Exchange.

The Duc de Rhetore, our elder brother, solemn and capricious, will remain in Paris. Miss Griffith has discovered Alphonse is in love with a ballet girl at the Opera. How can one fall in love with legs and pirouettes? Our brother only attends when Tullia dances; he applauds and leaves. Two ballet girls in a family are more destructive than the plague. My second brother is with his regiment; I have not yet seen him. Thus I must act as the Antigone of His Majesty's ambassador. Perhaps I may marry in Spain; perhaps my father imagines a marriage without a dowry. He asked if I’d accompany him and offered me the use of his Spanish master.

"Spain, the country for castles in the air!" I exclaimed. "Perhaps you hope it will mean marriages for me!"

He only gave a knowing look. For days he has teased me at lunch; he watches me, and I dissemble, playing cruelly with him as father and ambassador in petto. He asked my opinion of some young men and girls. I answered with inane remarks about hair, faces, and figures. He seemed disappointed and inwardly blamed himself for asking.
