"Still, father," I added, "don’t suppose I’m saying what I really think: mother made me afraid the other day that I had spoken too frankly about my impressions."

"With your family, you can speak quite freely," said my mother.

"Very well, then," I continued. "The young men I’ve met are too self-centered to excite interest; they’re taken up with themselves. They put on an expression to talk to us, then drop it, apparently satisfied that we don’t use our eyes. The man as he converses is the lover; silent, he is the husband. The girls are so artificial that it’s impossible to know them, except from their dancing; their figures are not a sham. But the alarming thing is society’s brutality. Small incidents when supper is announced give one an idea—comparing small things with great—of what a popular uprising might be. Courtesy is a thin veneer over general selfishness. I imagined society to be very different. Women count for little in it; perhaps this is a survival of Bonapartist ideas."

"Armande is coming on extraordinarily," said my mother.

"Mother, did you think I would never get beyond asking to see Mme. de Staël?"

My father smiled and rose.

Saturday.

I left out one thing. The love we pictured is extremely well hidden; I have seen no trace. True, quick exchanges of glances occur in drawing-rooms, but how colorless it all is! Love as we imagined it—a world of wonders, glorious dreams, charming realities, sorrows that awaken sympathy, smiles bringing sunshine—does not exist. The bewitching words, constant interchange of happiness, misery of absence, joy of presence—where are they? What soil produces these radiant flowers of the soul? Which is wrong? We or the world?

I have seen hundreds of men; not one has moved me. No proof of admiration or devotion on their part, not even a sword drawn in my defense, would have touched me. Love is so rare that one might live a lifetime without meeting the being who can make one happy. The thought chills; if found too late, what then?

For days I have trembled at the thought of women’s destinies and begun to understand why so many wear sad faces beneath social excitement. Marriage is mere chance. Look at yours. A storm of wild thoughts swept me. To be loved every day the same, yet with a difference, to be loved as much after ten years as on the first day—such love demands years. The lover must languish; curiosity must be piqued and satisfied, feelings stirred and reciprocated.

Is there a law for the inner fruits of the heart, as for nature's visible fruits? Can joy be made lasting? In what measure should love mix tears with pleasure? The convent’s monotony seems the only real life; while wealth, splendor, tears, delights, triumph, joy, and the satisfaction of an equal, shared, sanctioned love appear idle visions.

I see no room in this city for the gentle ways of love—precious walks, the full moon on water, the suitor pleading in vain. Rich, young, and beautiful, I have only to love, and love would be my life; yet in three months nothing has appealed to me among the bright, covetous eyes here. No voice has thrilled me, no glance brightened my world.

Music alone has filled my soul; it has even taken the place of our friendship. At night I linger by my window, summoning the future from its mystery. Sometimes, setting out to drive, I dismount and walk the Champs-Élysées imagining the man who will awaken my slumbering soul is near. Instead I meet mountebanks, vendors, jugglers, and secret lovers. I am tempted to stop them, ask, "You who are happy, tell me what is love." But I repress the impulse and return to my carriage, swearing to die an old maid. Love is an incarnation, and how many conditions must be met before it can occur! We are not certain we shall never quarrel with ourselves; how much less certain with another? Only God can solve this.

I begin to think I’ll return to the convent. If I stay in society I may do foolish things, for I cannot reconcile what I see with my sense of delicacy and principle.

Ah! my mother is the happiest, adored by Canalis, her great little man. I sometimes crave to know what goes on between them. Griffith tells me she has passed through such moods; she has longed to lash out at women whose happiness is evident and blackened their characters. According to her, virtue is burying savage instincts in one’s heart. But then what becomes of the heart? It becomes a sink for the worst in us.

It is humiliating that no admirer has yet appeared for me. I am marriageable, but having brothers and a family sensitive about honor may keep men away. If that is why, they are cowards.

The parts of Chimène in the Cid and the Cid himself delight me. What a marvelous play! Farewell.

January.

Our master is a poor refugee, hiding because of his part in the revolution quelled by the Duc d'Angouleme—a triumph that has led to splendid fêtes. Though a Liberal and likely a man of the people, he has piqued my interest; I suspect he was condemned to death. I make him talk to uncover his secret, but he has a truly Castilian taciturnity, proud as if he were Gonsalvo di Cordova, yet angelic in patience and gentleness. His pride is not irritable like Miss Griffith’s; it belongs to his nature, compelling civility through perfect manners and keeping us at a distance with the respect he shows.

My father says much nobility dwells in Señor Henarez, whom he humorously calls Don Henarez. A few days ago I took the liberty of so addressing him. He raised his eyes, usually cast down, and flashed a look that abashed me; he certainly has the most beautiful eyes imaginable. I asked if I had offended, and he replied in grand, rolling Spanish:

"I am here only to teach you Spanish."

I blushed and felt snubbed. I nearly retorted sharply, but remembering what our dear mother used to say, I instead replied:

"It would be kind to tell me if you have any complaints."

A tremor passed through him; the blood rose in his olive cheeks. He answered with some emotion:

"Religion must have taught you, better than I can, to respect the unhappy. Had I been a don in Spain and lost everything in Ferdinand VII.'s triumph, your witticism would be unkind; but if I am only a poor language teacher, is it not heartless satire? Neither is worthy of a young lady of rank."

I took his hand, saying:

"In the name of religion, I beg you to pardon me."

He bowed, opened my Don Quixote, and sat.

This little incident disturbed me more than a harvest of compliments and glances. During the lesson I watched him closely, which I could do safely, as he never looks at me.

From my observations, the tutor, whom we assumed to be forty, is under thirty. My governess admired the beauty of his black hair and pearly teeth. His eyes are velvet and fire, but he is plain and singular. Though Spaniards are often called unclean, this man is meticulously groomed, with hands whiter than his face. He stoops slightly and has an odd, large head. Smallpox scars give his ugliness a piquancy; his prominent forehead and shaggy brows give a harsh, frowning, plaintive look, like a sickly child whose life depends on superhuman care. As my father noted, his features recall a shrunken Cardinal Ximenes. The natural dignity of his manners disconcerts the dear Duke, who dislikes him and never feels at ease before superiority.

Once my father knows enough Spanish we will go to Madrid. When Henarez returned two days after his reprimand, I said to show gratitude:

"I have no doubt you left Spain because of political events. If my father is sent there, as seems likely, we might help you obtain a pardon, if you are under sentence."

"It is impossible for anyone to help me," he replied.

"But," I pressed, "is that because you refuse help or because the situation is impossible?"

"Both," he said, bowing, in a tone that closed the matter.

My father's blood boiled. I was offended by this haughty posture and promptly dropped Señor Henarez.

Yet something admirable lay in his refusal of aid. "He would not accept even our friendship," I thought while conjugating a verb. Suddenly I spoke to him in Spanish. Henarez replied that equality of sentiment is necessary between friends, which did not exist here, making the matter pointless.

"Do you mean equality in feeling, or equality in rank?" I demanded, eager to provoke him.

He raised those awe-inspiring eyes, and mine fell. Dear, this man is a hopeless enigma. He seemed to ask whether my words implied love; the mixture of joy, pride, and agonized doubt in his glance moved me. It was clear that advances, taken lightly in France, might complicate with a Spaniard, and I retreated, feeling foolish.

The lesson ended; he bowed, and his eyes expressed a humble prayer: "Don't trifle with a poor wretch."

This sudden variance from his usual grave dignity made a deep impression. It seems horrible to think and say, but I can’t help believing there are treasures of affection in that man.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“All is over, my dear child,” Mme. de l'Estorade writes. One girl is gone.

I did not consent rashly. My life is planned—certainty suits me; resolve banishes chance. We have property and a house to improve; I will run the household, restore my husband's will to live, and bear children.

Everyday life is not heroic, yet I find the infinite in humble duties; restoring faith to suffering will break monotony. Little suffering remains; much remains to be done. I do not love Louis, but nothing in him offends me.

Ambitions once tormented me, but now serve the family. If not grand, my life will be calm and free from shocks. Nature gives women the choice of love or motherhood; I choose motherhood—my children my gods, this Eldorado. I will live in refinement, keeping charming luxuries; my father-in-law sent Parisian workmen to renovate.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
X. MLLE. DE CHAULIEU TO MME. DE L'ESTORADE
January.

Oh Renée, you have made me miserable. Your beauty, proud features, rich soul and exquisite heart are destined for a dull marriage you will learn to despise; your children will be monsters of habit. You seem resigned—neither hopeful nor afraid—but what if one morning a man awakens you? The thought chills me. At least you will have a friend and become the valley’s guardian angel, learning to cherish its monotony until it becomes part of you. Expect letters echoing the valley’s sameness. I remain untouched by petty society, entirely yours.

Monday.

My Spaniard bewilders me with calm, severe dignity. His solemn silence and reserve provoke curiosity; how strange that a language teacher should fascinate me more than any nobleman. His coldness—wrapped in pride and mystery—both amuses and irritates me.

Yesterday I said something that struck M. Henarez; he did not answer, and the lesson ended with a bow that suggested he might not return—a prospect I secretly prefer, for I dread sentimental catastrophe. I’ve been reading Rousseau and find his moral passion detestable; Richardson’s Clarissa captures English women truer. Love is, to me, a private poem; everything written about it is at once false and true. Since you will become an authority on married love, I feel called to remain unmarried and seek a great passion to enrich our companionship. Tell me every detail of your first days with that strange creature called a husband; I promise to do the same if I ever find love.

Farewell, poor martyred darling.

XI. MME. DE L'ESTORADE TO MLLE. DE CHAULIEU
La Crampade.

Your Spaniard and you make me shudder. Dismiss him. Everything you describe matches a dangerous adventurer who risks all. He cannot be your husband or your lover. I will write more about married life when my heart is less anxious from your letter.

XII. MLLE. DE CHAULIEU TO MME. DE L'ESTORADE
February.

This morning my father visited, unusually serious. After flattering me and admitting he’d misjudged my intelligence, he turned to politics: France remains precarious—the Revolution survives in law and thought; the King is a head without arms, nobles lack authority over elected men, and the family has ceased to exist—individuals and State coffers replacing the old family spirit. He insisted a state cannot stand without paternal authority and warned of calamity if our party fails. Half-joking he asked me to persuade him to sacrifice my inheritance for my younger brother; I asked how I could enjoy life without a fortune. He laughed, then seriously noted my future husband will not take me without a dowry. We parted in good humor.

Your letter made me reflect on the abyss you warned about. I took precautions with Henarez, whose gaze unsettles me. I hesitated to tell my father I wanted no more lessons, yet I’m drawn to Henarez. His voice thrills me; his manners are simple and genuine. After a lesson he almost kissed my hand, then stopped, terrified. I smiled, understanding the courage it takes for a plebeian to love a noblewoman; my smile encouraged him, his eyes were wet, and I felt a sudden, strange happiness. He fascinates me.

Friday.

He is handsome and speaks admirably, revealing intellect and a new world. He told me of a valet who died for a queen’s glance and I was captivated. At a ball M. de Vandenesse hinted Henarez was of noble descent—the Duke de Soria, whose titles were confiscated by King Ferdinand. Suspense followed.

In our next lesson I suspected him; he denied it, claiming to be merely a servant of the King of Spain. His reticence provoked me; I devised a test: I had him translate a letter I wrote describing my ideal lover. He was visibly moved and asked to keep the letter as remembrance. He left asking to speak with my father; from my father I learned Henarez is now the Baron de Macumer, with property in Sardinia.

Two weeks have passed since I saw him; I wonder what he is doing. He has become necessary to me, and I am torn between love and the reality of my situation. Sunday at the Italian Opera I felt his fiery gaze. He has a genius for love.

XIII. MME. DE L'ESTORADE TO MLLE. DE CHAULIEU
LA CRAMPADE, February.

My dear Louise, I waited to write until I could tell you enough for your happiness. A girl cannot fully comprehend a married woman’s situation. I chose to marry Louis de l'Estorade rather than return to the convent. The seriousness of marriage terrified me, but I realized friendship might be the key to a happy union. I accepted his love believing it need not be slavery but could be a gentle reign.

We agreed to separate certain marital rights to preserve my independence. I watched Louis to see whether suffering had softened or embittered him. His love proved passionate, and I judged I could risk much. I proposed a friendship in which we would share life’s burdens; I asked to be left free, promising to attend to his feelings. He rejoiced, and we married soon after.

I am nearly happy now, though I must conceal my complete contentment from him because he is sensitive. For three months we lived much as before; I studied our relation and saw how my presence changed him. I believed I could bring new life to my husband before bearing children. His happiness brightened our home; even my father became more generous. I manage the household with economy—seeking pleasure without excess, avoiding the mad outlay that spoils many.

I harbor ambitious plans for Louis, hoping to see him in politics; he has embraced my designs, a testament to his affection. To finish, here is a line from a letter he wrote expressing his love: he calls me his treasure, and I feel proud of our relationship.

XIV. THE DUC DE SORIA TO THE BARON DE MACUMER
MADRID.

My dear brother, I did not create the title Duc de Soria to betray our name. I cannot bear your exile while we enjoy happiness. Marie and I will delay marrying until you accept the money Urraca offers. We have prayed for your contentment; I believe Heaven will bring you the love you seek. Marie and I will always be grateful to you. Write through the Nuncio. Farewell; take pride in the happiness you have given us.

XV. LOUISE DE CHAULIEU TO MME. DE L'ESTORADE
March.

Ah, my friend, marriage has made a philosopher of you! Your prudent calculations trouble me. Do you think you can convert me to matrimony by cautious arithmetic? We left Blois innocent, armed with meditation, and now your weapons have turned against you. If I did not know your purity, I would suspect vice.

You have made a holocaust of your youth—become a miser before your time. If Louis loves you, he will never realize your actions spring from necessity. Love is the first principle of all virtues; I cannot accept justifying marriage by calculation. You act a man's part; Louis must play the woman!

Your letter plunged me into thought; I see now the convent cannot replace a mother. Please do not turn away from my cries!

Monday.

Last night I looked at the stars and pictured my Spaniard in a tree by my window. I felt both joy and fear. He blew a letter to me through the window, but I burned it unread—it felt improper to read such an offering. At the opera he seemed sad; I learned he had renounced a rich heiress for another man’s sake. I was wretched for burning his letter—what might he have confessed? To be loved in secret, to know a man is devoted—how can I resist?

When I returned home I reopened my window, hoping he might be there. He was, and I received another letter, which I read and treasured.

XVI. THE SAME TO THE SAME
March.

I am dressed in white, with camellias in my hair. I feel an odd desire to delay my decision and make him wait. What a vision—driving my barouche, seeing him, that thrill. I complimented him on his horse; he said it was a gift from his steward. Henarez is no longer merely simple—he grows more distinguished. Do my love affairs amuse you? I am a little off my head and must stop.

XVII. MME. DE L'ESTORADE TO LOUISE DE CHAULIEU
April.

My angel, you have grieved me. You do not realize the force of the word indissoluble in marriage. Each marriage has laws shaped by place and character: a wife of a leader must submit to his guidance; a wife of a weak man must take control. You speak of vice, but passion can be admirable when it springs from the heart. We are answerable to God and ourselves for our happiness.

A wife’s calculation is often necessary to maintain happiness. I have reflected on the duties of wife and mother, and I believe a certain sublime hypocrisy may be required to attain the ideal of a perfect woman. I persist in my principles, convinced virtue adapts to surroundings. If I wished to make Louis unhappy, I would abandon the helm entirely.

I may not have married a man of rank, but I hope to further him toward it. In five years we will see if I was mistaken. You taught me what a woman sacrifices in marriage; regret is not remorse, but I feel the loss of freedom. You say marriage has made you a philosopher; I feel far from it. I have read Bonald, who revealed the strength of the family.

Farewell, my dear friend.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Well, my Renée, you are a wonderful woman, and I now agree we can be virtuous only by cheating. Will that satisfy you? The man who loves us is our property; we may make a fool or a genius of him as we please—though the former is common. You will make yours a genius and keep the secret—two heroic acts, if you will.

If there were no afterlife, you would be nicely sold; this is the martyrdom you choose. You want to make him ambitious and keep him in love! Surely keeping him in love alone is punishment enough.

Tell me, to what extent is calculation a virtue, or is virtue calculation? We won’t quarrel; we have Bonald to refer to. We are, and intend to remain, virtuous; still, I believe you, with all your little knavery, are the better woman.

Yes, I am shockingly deceitful. I love Felipe and conceal it with hypocrisy. I long to see him leap from his tree to my balcony—and if he did, how I would wither him with my scorn! I am frank with you.

What restrains me? Why do I not tell Felipe how supremely happy his pure, overflowing love has made me?

Mme. de Mirbel is painting my portrait, which I intend to give him. Love animates life; every hour, every trifle matters—past, present, and future blur. Is that still so at the heights of happiness? What is happiness—soothing or exciting? I am restless, drawn to him despite reason and propriety. At least I can better understand your feelings.

Felipe’s happiness lies in feeling himself mine; his aloofness and strict obedience irritate me, just as his profound respect did when he was my Spanish master. I want to cry out, “Fool, if you love me as a picture, what will it be when you know the real me?”

Oh, Renée, you burn my letters, don’t you? I will burn yours. If anyone else read these thoughts, I would send Felipe to silence them; perhaps even kill the owners for security.

Monday.

How fathom the human heart? My father should introduce me to M. Bonald; I would ask him. I envy God, who can read the heart’s undercurrents. Does Felipe still worship me? That is the question.

If I ever detect the slightest fall from the respect he used to show me as my instructor, I believe I would have the strength to end it. “Why these fine words, these grand resolutions?” you will say. I will tell you.

My charming father, devoted as an Italian cavaliere servente, had my portrait painted by Mme. de Mirbel. I managed to get a copy for the Duke and sent the original to Felipe with lines implying both reward and challenge: that his single-hearted devotion met a blind confidence, and time would show whether this was to treat a man as more than human. It was given without fully committing. If his reply had contained “Dear Louise!” or even “Louise,” he would be lost!

Tuesday.

No, he is not lost. The constitutional minister is perfect as a lover. His letter—so full I paraphrase—says every moment away from me was filled with ideal images; his eyes closed to the world, fixed on my likeness. My portrait is a talisman; my painted blue eyes have been brought into his life and haunt him. If he delayed writing, it was because he could not tear himself from my presence, which wrung from him secrets he struggled to keep.

Closeted with my likeness, he surrendered to complete happiness for the first time. He speaks of placing me between the Virgin and God in his thoughts, of an agony he will not voice because a single altered glance from me would be torture. He calls me “Queen of my life and soul,” longing only for a fraction of the love he bears.

Doubt wrecks him, like a criminal awaiting judgment. A smile from me alone could calm his dread of displeasing me. From birth no one, not even his mother, smiled on him; the girl meant for him rejected him for his brother. In politics too he has suffered—thwarted, distrusted, regarded almost as an enemy. To be loved had become a dream lost to him until I appeared.

Knowing his graceless exterior and the odds against him, he entered my service because devotion alone could justify his passion. Looking at my portrait alternates hope with fear: hope that, seeing his constancy, I might yield; fear that I will never love him. He promises that if his daring offends, he will return to being my humble servant; but if I consent to try to love him, he begs mercy in breaking it gently to one who has placed his happiness in serving me.

Reading this, he rose before me—pale, humble, but full of a tenderness that was not the ordinary flourish of lovers. It was the breath of happiness.

The weather has been atrocious; impossible to go to the Bois without arousing suspicion. Even my mother, who often goes out regardless of rain, remains at home.

Wednesday evening.

I have just seen him at the Opera; he was another man. He came to our box, introduced by the Sardinian ambassador. Having read in my eyes that his audacity was welcome, he seemed awkward and addressed the Marquise d’Espard as “mademoiselle.” A light brighter than the chandeliers flashed from his eyes. He left with the uncertain air of a man unsure of his next move.

“The Baron de Macumer is in love!” exclaimed Mme. de Maufrigneuse.

“Strange, isn’t it, for a fallen minister?” replied my mother.

Outwardly I kept my composure, regarding Mmes. de Maufrigneuse and d’Espard and my mother as if they spoke a foreign language; inwardly my soul rose in waves of intoxicating joy. There is only one word for what I felt: rapture. Such love as Felipe’s makes him worthy of mine. I am the very breath of his life; my hands guide his thoughts. I have a mad longing to see him clear every obstacle and come boldly for my hand. Then I would know whether this storm would calm at a glance from me.

Ah, my dear, I stopped here, and I am still trembling. As I wrote, I heard a noise outside and rose. From my window I saw him coming along the wall at the risk of his life. I went to the bedroom window and made him a sign; it was enough. He leaped from the wall—ten feet—and then ran along the road, showing me he was not hurt. That he thought of my fear moved me so much that I am still crying; I don’t know why. Poor ungainly man! What was he coming for? What had he to say to me?

I dare not write my thoughts and will go to bed joyful, imagining all we would say if together. Farewell, fair silent one. I have no time to scold you for not writing; it has been over a month since I heard from you! Does this mean you are finally happy? Have you lost the “complete independence” you were so proud of, which nearly played me false tonight?
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
If love is life’s essence, why is it excluded from marriage? Society demands women sacrifice for the family, breeding marital discord. Men, wary of women’s potential power, built defenses; without them, love or aversion might topple theories that subordinate women. Marriage now embodies opposing forces the law must reconcile. Your letter unsettles foundations I once found comforting—the sage of Aveyron’s laws that accept conjugal love stripped of passion, permitting women to yield without desire and reflecting Nature’s emphasis on procreation.

Once a person, I now feel like property. I have swallowed many tears alone, craving a comforting smile. Why are our fates so different? You flourish in lawful love where virtue and pleasure align; I face an uncertain future.

You are loved and adored. Embrace the noble romance we dreamed of. Your refined, spiritual beauty was meant to enchant. Guard your heart and test Felipe’s love—and, above all, your own. Novelty and the illusion of happiness mislead. Remain independent; do not rush into marriage without assurance. In intimate conversation, a gesture or word can reveal hidden depths. You have the courage to explore where others falter.

I watch anxiously from afar; my heart beats with yours. Write about everything; your letters ignite passion in my otherwise mundane life. I swing between despair and hope—perhaps expecting too much. In youth we often hope ideals and reality will align.

Seated beneath a rock in my park, I concluded love in marriage is a fortunate accident, not a rule. The philosopher rightly sees the family as society’s unit, placing women in subservience as history shows. The solution to our dilemma lies in motherhood, which I yearn for to channel my energy and heart.

Louis remains cheerful; his love is active while mine is passive. He enjoys the flowers of my affection without acknowledging the toil behind them. I play the devoted mother, sacrificing for his happiness, and that sacrifice gives me joy. I await the rewards of my efforts, hoping motherhood will fulfill my aspirations and compensate for what I surrender.

---

Dear wedded friend, your letter arrived as I contemplated a bold step. A strange yearning for the unknown revealed the conflict between societal and natural law within me. I want to speak with Felipe alone under the lime trees in our garden. This suits the modern girl I’ve become, my father observes.

There is method in my seeming madness: I wish to reward Felipe for his nights beneath my window while testing his reaction to my boldness. If he respects my folly, he may imagine me as his wife; if not, farewell, Don Felipe.

Meeting him in the garden feels less risky than society’s prying eyes. I longed for your company to share the anticipation of our first clandestine meeting. I sent Felipe a tracing of the garden key with a note warning him his recklessness could tarnish my reputation. Yesterday I took Griffith to the garden, and after a delicious moment of tension, I saw Felipe approach like a shadow.

When he arrived, I introduced him to Griffith, who withdrew a few steps. The night was mild and scented; I felt intoxicated by being almost alone with him. We savored the moment before I urged him to abandon his reckless climbing, fearing scandal. His pallor revealed fear; I reminded him that if he had been caught, suspicion would fall on my family. He apologized, and I reassured him my actions were not as mad as they seemed.

After a silence he expressed joy and reverence, acknowledging my actions as sacred. I told him I was on the path to loving the man who understands me, but that my heart would belong only to someone who truly knows it. He thanked me for my acceptance; I noted he must prove himself worthy. We walked and talked; his tenderness felt divine. His low, melodic voice conveyed his feelings beautifully.

I dismissed him, aware of our meeting’s implications. Griffith remarked on my commitment, but I assured her that in France my intentions were exploratory.

Again I met Felipe; he appeared timid and reverent, which I found appealing. He sensed my power yet remained unaware of my deeper feelings. I am still fond of him, but calm and control have replaced the initial thrill—and I fear I may have lost that excitement.

Love appears in two forms: one that commands and one that obeys. Can both coexist? Will Felipe become my master? These questions unsettle me.

He remains oblivious to my true nature. If I were him, I would see Mlle. de Chaulieu as a calculating coquette. I still care for Felipe, but the calm of control replaces feverish longing—and I wonder if suspense is always more pleasurable than fulfillment. Do we exaggerate feelings by letting imagination run wild? How far can I go? Imagination knows no bounds, but pleasure has limits. How can a woman reconcile these two sides of her existence?
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
If you did not weep over Racine's Berenice and feel it as a profound tragedy, we have no connection and should part. Answer me satisfactorily, or I will forget you; you will be M. le Baron de Macumer to me, or cease to exist.

At Mme. d'Espard's yesterday your self-satisfied air disgusted me and your confidence alarmed me. You have lost the humility of your first letter; instead of the absent-mindedness of a lover you polished epigrams. That is not the behavior of a true believer, who should stand in awe of his divinity.

If you do not see me as the very breath of your life, a being above others, then I must be less than a woman in your eyes. You have bred a spirit of mistrust that drowns my tenderness. Reflecting on our past, I feel justified in my suspicions. Understand, Prime Minister of all the Spains, that I have contemplated the vulnerable state of our sex. My innocence has lit my path, and I have emerged unscathed.

Let me repeat what youth taught me: deceit and broken promises are often punished, but love answers only to its own law—the victim is accuser and judge. The most heartless betrayals remain secret, known only to the two hearts involved. How can the injured reveal them without wounding herself? Love gives its own justice, which the world ignores.

I have resolved never to forgive a serious offense; in love, what is not serious? Yesterday you looked like a man who had already succeeded. If that certainty has stripped you of the charming uncertainty that once characterized you, I blame you. I want you neither timid nor presumptuous; I do not want you to fear losing my affection—that would be an insult—yet I also do not want you to treat your love lightly. Your heart should never be freer than mine. If you cannot grasp the torment a single stab of doubt can inflict, beware the lesson I might teach you.

In a single glance I entrusted my heart to you, and you understood it. The purest feelings of a young girl are yours. My reflections have enriched my mind; yet if a wounded heart seeks counsel from reason, it may ally itself with darker knowledge and daring.

I swear to you, Felipe: if you love me as I believe, and if I sense even the least decline in the fear, obedience, and respect you have shown, or if the pure flame that ignited my heart grows dim, expect no reproaches. I would not burden you with letters of weakness, pride, or rage. But if I became silent, my face would announce that death was near. I would not perish, however, until I had branded you with infamy and sown eternal sorrow in your heart; you would behold the girl you loved dishonored and lost, condemned to suffering.

Do not make me envy the old, happy Louise, the object of your pure worship, whose heart thrived in happiness—Senza brama, sicura ricchezza! I have searched the Inferno for the most terrible punishment, a mental torture to link with divine vengeance.

Yesterday, as I observed you, doubt pierced me like a cold dagger. I mistrusted you, and the pain was unbearable. If my service is too exacting, leave it; I would not retain you. I do not need proof of your cleverness. Keep your wit for me. Do not present a polished façade to others for flattery or praise. Come to me burdened with scorn or hatred, the target of calumny, told that no woman loves you; then you will discover the treasures of Louise's heart.

We are truly rich only when our wealth lies so deep the world could trample it without knowing. If you were merely handsome, I doubt I would have noticed you or found the myriad reasons for my love. We understand little of those reasons, as little as we know why the sun makes flowers bloom. Yet one cause I will reveal: the character and individuality that give meaning to your face are a mystery to all but me. My love transforms you into the most lovable of men, and that is why I wish to keep your mental gifts for myself. To others they should be as meaningless as your eyes and features. Let it be mine alone to ignite your intelligence, as I bring light to your eyes.

I envision you as a proud Spanish grandee, cold and haughty, a monument to be admired from a distance—ruins of barbaric power—rather than a figure of Parisian affability. Have you forgotten my ideal portrait? Your excessive cheerfulness betrayed your love. Had I not restrained you, you would have revealed to the sharpest salons of Paris that your inspiration came from Armande‑Louise‑Marie de Chaulieu.

I believe too much in your greatness to think your love is merely strategic; yet if you did not show childlike simplicity with me, I could only pity you. Despite this fault, you are still deeply admired by LOUISE DE CHAULIEU.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
When God sees our faults, He also sees our repentance. My beloved mistress, you are right; I feared I had displeased you without knowing why. Now that you have explained, I adore you more. Like the God of Israel, your jealousy is holy and precious. Strike me, Louise; the more I am chastised, the more contrite I shall be. If my struggles to overcome timidity were unseen, will Heaven still reward them? It cost me dearly to reveal my former self before love. In Madrid I was thought a good talker; I wished you to see any gifts I had. If that was vanity, I accept punishment.

Your last glance unnerved me more than danger in Cádiz. I searched for the cause of your displeasure; the thought that we lacked sympathy was intolerable. I want only to act by your will, think your thoughts, and share your joys and sorrows. The torment was in the breach of the mutual feelings I believed we shared.

"I have vexed her!" I repeated, distraught. Noble, beautiful Louise, your words have shown me the truth of my feelings. If this is chastisement, how sweet are your rewards!

To be your servant is happiness enough. You have restored life I thought wasted; I no longer draw a useless breath. I have something to offer—suffering for you, devotion forever. I remain the bondman I first offered myself to be. If your honor were stained, my heart would cling to you all the more, seeking to heal and pray for your innocence.

Did I not say my feelings are more than lover's? I will be father, mother, sister, brother—anything you wish or nothing at all. If father and brother sometimes hide behind the lover, they are still there. If you could read my heart when you shine in beauty, you would know how unselfish my pride is when I hear your praises. When you greet me, I feel as if God blesses me; the joy lingers.

Can you be unaware of how you are loved? After seeing you I return to my study; its ornaments pale beside your portrait. I lose myself in thought, link my happiness to verse. Have you never heard the whispers of my prayers for you? By contemplating your face I have known every feature and its relation to your spirit; I have written sonnets in Spanish about your double beauty—perhaps unfit for your eyes, yet they are constant offerings. Not a moment passes without thoughts of you; my whole being is bound, and without you I would ache.

Now, Louise, can you imagine the torture of displeasing you without knowing why? My ideal life seemed cut short; I feared you had ceased to love me. Your letter turned despair into joy. Oh, might I hear such chiding forever!

Once, a child, after falling, hid his hurt and said to his mother, "Forgive me." I am that child and remain so. Here is the key to my character; I humbly place it in your hands. Fear not—no more stumbling. Keep the chain tight that binds me to you; a touch shall convey your lightest wish to your devoted slave, FELIPE.

---

My dear friend, how can you—who married a broken-down invalid to mother him—understand the shifting drama of love, where death can lie in a glance or a light reply?

I reserved one last test for Felipe to see whether his love was as blind and steadfast as a Royalist’s for his King. He walked with me all night without suspicion; the next day his reverence for me was deeper—he loved me with humility. He is a true Spaniard: scaling my wall to kiss my hand from the balcony, risking his life.

But that is nothing; Christians suffer for heaven. I spoke to my father, the would-be ambassador, with a smile and a jest: friends say he is marrying Armande to an ambassador's nephew for wealth and title. The rumor vexed me—my daughter weeps but bows to paternal authority; some say she masks a worldly soul behind tears.

Tonight we go to the gentleman's box at the Opera; M. le Baron de Macumer will join us.

"Macumer needs a spur," my father said, smiling.

"You mistake Clarissa Harlowe for Figaro!" I replied. "When you see me with my right hand ungloved, you will mark your displeasure at this gossip."

"I can rest about your future. You have no more a girl's head than Jeanne d'Arc had a woman's heart. You will be happy, love no one, and allow yourself to be loved."

This was too much; I laughed.

"What is it, little flirt?" he asked. "I tremble for my country's interests..." I added, smiling, "At Madrid!"

"You have no idea how this little nun has learned to mock her father," he told the Duchess.

"Armande makes light of everything," my mother said.

"What do you mean?" I asked.

"You are not even afraid of rheumatism on these damp nights," she said, glancing at me.

"Oh!" I answered, "the mornings are so hot!"

"It's high time she were married," my father said.

"If you wish it," I replied demurely.

Two hours later my mother, the Duchesse de Maufrigneuse, and Mme. d'Espard bloomed in the box. I sat aside, unseen, to observe.

Macumer arrived and leveled his opera glasses at me. In the first interval the Comte Henri de Marsay, always ready with an epigram, came smiling. He greeted my mother and the others, then turned to me: "I do not know whether I shall be the first to congratulate you on an event that will make you the envy of many."

"Ah! a marriage!" I cried. "Is it left for me, a girl fresh from the convent, to say that predicted marriages never come off?"

M. de Marsay whispered to Macumer; I guessed he warned him about me. Macumer met him with noble scorn, saying he was "not in love with any little coquette." His demeanor pleased me; I saw my father ready to act.

Felipe showed no fear or doubt. His faith was in me; society's lies could not ruffle him. Not a muscle of his face moved.

The two young counts left, and I laughed at Macumer: "M. de Marsay has been treating you to an epigram on me."

"He did more," he replied. "It was an epithalamium."

"You speak Greek to me," I said.

My father spoke with Mme. de Maufrigneuse about the scandalous gossip surrounding me; he meant to avoid any suggestion of a marriage. The Duchess and Mme. d'Espard exchanged glances—women scent love like perfume.

Having enjoyed Felipe’s composed response, I signaled him to come to my window by the dangerous route. Later I found him, upright as a statue, watching the light in my room.

"My dear Felipe," I said, "You acquitted yourself well tonight; you behaved as I would if told you were marrying."

"I thought you would hardly tell others before me."

"And what right have you to that privilege?"

"The right of one who is your devoted slave."

"In truth?"

"I am, and shall ever remain so."

"But suppose this marriage was inevitable; suppose I had agreed..." His eyes flashed, measuring danger. A power stronger than passion held him back.

"An Arab cannot take back his word," he said. "I am your slave; my life is not mine to destroy."

I placed my hand on his. "Felipe, my beloved, from this moment I am your wife in thought and will. Go in the morning to ask my father's consent. He wishes to keep my fortune, but if you acknowledge receipt in the contract, his consent will be given. I am no longer Armande de Chaulieu. Leave me; no breath of scandal must touch Louise de Macumer."

He listened, pale and trembling, then cleared the ten feet to the ground in safety. It was agonizing, but he waved goodbye and disappeared.

"I am loved then," I thought, "as never woman was before." I fell asleep content, my destiny fixed.

The next day my father summoned me to his private room; the Duchess and Macumer were present. After civilities I said that if my father and M. Henarez agreed, I had no reason to oppose their wishes. My mother invited the Baron to dinner; we drove in the Bois de Boulogne, and I smiled ironically at M. de Marsay as he passed.

Felipe has had his cards reprinted as follows:
HENAREZ
(Baron de Macumer, formerly Duc de Soria.)

Every morning he brings a splendid bouquet containing a letter with a Spanish sonnet in my honor.

To be concise, I send you only the first and last of these sonnets, translated.

FIRST SONNET (essence)
I have braved bulls and battles and gambled with life. Yet before your sweet eye my courage deserts me; I tremble, shrink, and lose my nerve like a child with ghosts.

SECOND SONNET (essence)
I sought sleep to dream of thee, but jealous wakefulness kept me on the balcony. Heaven itself seemed robbed of color by thy beauty: moon and stars pale beside you; your brow shows snow's shine, your eyes heaven's blue.

What a gracious way to tell a young girl she fills a man’s life! Only in these days have I understood Spanish gallantry.

Ah, dear, what news at La Crampade? I inspect our crops often. How are the mulberry trees and last winter's plantations? While buds open here, have blossoms appeared in the bosom of the wife? Does Louis still sing madrigals? I wonder if your wedding peace is better than the torrent of my love! Has my sweet lady professor taken offense? If so, I would send Felipe post-haste to plead for pardon—or her head. My life here is a splendid success, and I long to know how yours fares. We have added a Spaniard with the complexion of a Havana cigar to our family, and your congratulations still await.

Seriously, my sweet Renée, I worry. I fear you may be eating your heart out in silence, afraid to cloud my sunshine. Write at once, naughty child! Tell me every detail of married life; tell me whether you still hold back, whether your "independence" stands, kneels, or sits comfortably—which would indeed be serious. Can you suppose the incidents of your married life do not interest me? At the Opera, absorbed in the dancers, I think, "It is half-past nine; perhaps she is in bed. What is she doing? Is she happy? Is she alone with her independence, or has it gone the way of other dead and cast-off independences?"

A thousand loves.

---

Saucy girl! Why should I write? What could I say? While your life is varied by social festivities and passions, mine is as monotonous as a convent.

We go to bed at nine and rise with daybreak. Meals are served with maddening punctuality. Nothing ever happens. I have adjusted; perhaps such fixed laws give the world stability.

I have set rules of dress, occupying my mornings. I think it my duty as a wife to look charming; it pleases both Louis and the good old man. After lunch we walk; when newspapers arrive I disappear to manage household affairs or read—often writing to you. I return before dinner; after, we play cards or receive visits. Thus my days pass between a contented old man who has done with passions and the man who owes his happiness to me. Louis' joy warms my heart.

For women, happiness cannot consist solely in satisfying desire. In the evenings, when I can relax, I imagine your life, its riches and passions, and wonder what such a stormy preface can lead to. May I not swallow the book itself? For you, my darling, love's illusions are possible; for me, only homely facts remain. Your love seems a dream!

I find it hard to understand why you insist on romanticizing it. Your ideal man must have more soul than fire, more nobility and self-command than passion. You demand sacrifices to be rewarded by them, testing Felipe to see if desire, hope, and curiosity endure. But behind your stage scenery lies the altar where lasting bonds are forged. The day after your marriage, the charming structure raised by your strategy may give way to the reality that transforms a girl into a woman, a gallant into a husband. Lovers have no exemption; for them as for ordinary folk like Louis and me, the great "Perhaps" of Rabelais lurks beneath wedding rejoicings.

I do not blame your rashness in speaking with Felipe in the garden or spending a night with him; but you play with life, and I fear the tables may turn. I dare not give you every counsel my experience suggests, but from my valley I repeat that the viaticum of married life lies in resignation and self-sacrifice. Despite your tests, I see marriage will mean to you what it meant to me. The greater the passion, the steeper the precipice we have prepared for our fall—that is the only difference.

Oh, how I wish to see the Baron de Macumer and speak with him! Your happiness lies so near my heart.

---

Since Felipe acknowledged my fortune, my mother has grown friendlier, calling me sly-boots and a woman of the world.

"But, dear mama," I said before the contract was signed, "you attribute cunning to what is truly the outcome of the most unselfish love! I assure you I am not the 'woman of the world' you think."

"Come, Armande," she said, drawing me close, "you did not want to return to the convent or die an old maid. Like a noble Chaulieu, you recognized the need to build up your father's family."

I glanced at my father. "I love Felipe de Soria with all my soul. Though this love sprang up without my knowledge, I swear I never yielded until I recognized in him a character worthy of mine."

"But, my dear," she interrupted, "he is as ugly as..."

"As anything you like," I retorted, "but I love his ugliness."

"If you love him, Armande," my father said, "and can govern your love, do not risk your happiness. Happiness in marriage depends largely on the first days—"

"Days only?" my mother interrupted. "You had better leave us to talk."

"You are to be married in three days," the Duchess began, "so I must give you the advice every mother owes her daughter. You are marrying a man you love, and there is no reason for pity. I have only known you a year, but it is long enough to love you. You have gratified maternal pride and shown yourself a sweet daughter. I promise you will always find a staunch friend in me."

"You smile? Alas! it often happens that a mother who has lived well with her daughter as a girl comes to cross purposes with her as women."

"I want your happiness, so listen. The love you feel is that of a young girl—natural to us all, for it is a woman's destiny to cling to a man. Unfortunately, there is but one man for a woman! Sometimes fate’s choice is not ours. If we cannot love the man family chooses, the fault may lie in both. Yet there is no reason the man chosen should not be the one we can love. Barriers between husband and wife often come from lack of perseverance on both sides. Transforming a husband into a lover is as delicate as making a lover into a husband, which you have just proved successful.

"Your happiness is my object. Never forget that the first three months may bring misery if you do not submit to the yoke with the same forbearance, tenderness, and intelligence you showed during courtship. You have indulged in innocent pleasures of clandestine love. If the culmination of your love begins with disappointment, come and tell me. Do not hope for too much from marriage; it may bring more discomfort than joy. The happiness of your life requires as much patient cherishing as the early shoots of love.

"If you lose the lover, you will find in his place the father of your children. This is the secret of social life. Sacrifice everything to the man whose name you bear, whose honor cannot be tarnished without involving you in frightful consequences. Such sacrifice is not only a duty for women of our rank; it is also wise policy. I need say no more.

"I fancy you are jealous, and if you knew how jealous I am! But do not be foolish about it. Public jealousy is like playing politics with your cards on the table; those who let their game be seen learn nothing of their opponents. Whatever happens, we must know how to suffer in silence."

She intended to talk seriously with Macumer the evening before the wedding.

I kissed her hand, moved. In her advice I saw high principles and tenderness; she understood my character. Her words summed life's lessons.

"You've got a nasty crossing before you," she added; most women, in their ignorance or disenchantment, are as wise as the Earl of Westmoreland!" We laughed at the joke about a gentleman who feared crossing the Alps.

You will understand, Renée, that your gloomy philosophy and my mother's lecture revived fears from Blois. As marriage approached, I needed all my strength and affection to face the passage from maidenhood to womanhood. I re-read your letters, feeling a vague sadness.

This anxiety had one advantage: it helped me adopt the proper expression for a bride. Consequently, on the day of signing the contract everyone said I looked charming and right. This morning at the Mairie it was informal, with only witnesses present.

I write this while they prepare my dress for dinner. We shall be married at midnight at the Church of Sainte-Valère, after a gay evening. I confess my fears give me a martyr-like air to which I have no right, but which will be admired—I cannot conceive why. I am delighted to see that poor Felipe is as timid as I; society grates on him, and he feels like a bat in a glass shop.

"Thank Heaven, the day won't last forever!" he whispered innocently.

In his bashfulness he wished no one present.

The Sardinian ambassador presented me with a pearl necklace linked with six splendid diamonds—a gift from my sister-in-law, the Duchess de Soria. Along with it came a sapphire bracelet engraved, "Though unknown, beloved." I could not accept these without consulting Felipe.

"For," I said, "I would not want you to wear ornaments from anyone but me."

He kissed my hand, moved, and replied, "Wear them for the inscription and the sincere feeling."

Here, then, my poor Renée, are the last words of your girl friend. After the midnight Mass we set off for an estate Felipe bought in Nivernais, on the road to Provence. My name is now Louise de Macumer, but I leave Paris in a few hours as Louise de Chaulieu. However I am called, there will always be but one Louise for you.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
I have not written to you, dear, since our marriage nearly eight months ago, and not a line from you! You are inexcusable.

We set off in a post-chaise for the Castle of Chantepleurs, which Macumer has bought in Nivernais on the Loire, sixty leagues from Paris. Our servants, except my maid, were already there; we arrived the next evening after a rapid journey. I slept from Paris to beyond Montargis with my head on Felipe's shoulder. His tenderness moved me; he kissed my forehead twice and looked at me with unchanging passion.

At Briare we breakfasted in the carriage, talking as in our old Blois days and admiring the Loire. By half-past seven we entered the beautiful avenue to Chantepleurs, dined at eight, and retired to a charming Gothic bedroom, made comfortable with modern conveniences. Felipe, often thought ugly, seemed beautiful to me in his kindness. He felt like an old friend, though he later confessed to storms of passion beneath a calm exterior.

I told him there was nothing terrible in marriage; he asked why one should fear it, reminding me he had never been false to his promise. His words and glances mastered me. I thought of duty and the unfair constraint placed on a woman's delicate feelings: what force seeks to crush a woman's sensitivity under obligation? Love should guard its rights and bloom freely under its warmth.

Oh, my noble Renée! I understand you now. A woman who has not used marriage to legalize her heart's choice has only motherhood left to turn to. When earthly ties fail, the soul seeks the divine.

A hard truth: only truly great men know how to love. They balance the sensual and the spiritual; weaker men confuse them. Great souls express instinct with grace, tempering passion with reverence. The depth of feeling follows the caliber of intellect; hence only men of genius possess a woman's delicacy. When mind, heart, and senses unite in rapture, we briefly soar to heaven.

This was the philosophy of my first three months of marriage. Felipe is angelic, another self; I can think aloud with him. His greatness surpasses my understanding, and our bond deepens with possession. I foresee years of marriage will only strengthen his affection and our connection. What joy!

Pleasure invigorates me; its sunshine becomes part of my being. The intervals between ecstasies are like short nights between long summer days. What happy chance has given me such a destiny? My mother's fears proved unfounded; your fears and mine dissolved.

We spent seven and a half months at Chantepleurs like runaway lovers in blissful solitude. One morning, in particular happiness, I reflected on my life and thought of your prosaic marriage. I grasped the meaning of your life. Oh, my sweet, why do we speak different languages? Your marriage of convenience and my love match are worlds apart. You walk the earth, while I soar in the heavens. Love crowned me queen; you reign by duty. My heights are so lofty that a fall would shatter me.

But I will not dwell on that. I hesitate to describe the vibrant joy of love's springtime as we know it.

For ten days we have been in Paris, staying in a lovely house on the Rue du Bac, prepared by the architect who decorated Chantepleurs. I have been listening to Rossini's divine music, which once soothed my restless heart. They say I am more beautiful now, and I take childish pleasure in being called "Madame."

Friday morning.

Renée, my dear, my happiness draws me back to you. I feel I can be more to you than ever. I have studied your married life alongside my own, and you emerge as great, noble, and splendid in your goodness. I declare myself your humble admirer and friend. My marriage has been a joy; I would have perished had it been otherwise. And you live! What feeds your heart? I will never mock you again. Mockery comes from ignorance; we jest at what we do not understand. "Recruits laugh where veterans are grave," as the Comte de Chaulieu said.

I suspect you have not told me everything. You hide wounds; I believe you suffer. From afar I wove romantic theories: perhaps you merely experimented with marriage, and what brought me happiness brought you pain. Your sacrifice seems unrewarded, wrapped in social axioms. If self-denial is noble, what of my joy, sanctified by church and mayor? Is it unnatural?

For the honor of the law and to complete my happiness, I long to see you content. Oh, tell me you see a dawn of love for Louis, who adores you! I return to this midday blaze, fearing it may consume me.

Dear Renée, do you remember how you once said, "I love you so, Louise, that if God appeared to me, I would pray for all your sorrows to be mine"? I take up your prayer, imploring Heaven to grant you a share in my happiness.

I have an idea. I suspect you are nurturing plans for Louis de l'Estorade. Very well; help him get elected deputy in the upcoming election. He will be nearly forty then, and the Chamber won't meet for six months, so he will qualify for a seat. You will come to Paris—wouldn't that be enough? My father and my friends will know and admire you. If your father-in-law consents to a family, we could secure the title of Comte for Louis. That would be something! And we shall be together.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
My dear Louise, your letter left me dizzy with joy and a little envy. I read it at sunset beneath a barren rock overlooking the Mediterranean—“Louise’s seat”—a place of solitude that still reminds me I am not alone.

Your words awakened the child in me: a mingling of warning, delight, pain, promise, and the conviction that I will one day know laughter and love. I now see my purpose as bringing forth life. The certainty of motherhood has turned my sacrifices into a deeper joy than any personal passion. For you, love mirrors Felipe’s ardor; my love will be returned through my children. Your golden harvest may fade, but my delayed motherhood will endure and renew. A smile has dried my tears; while love makes Louis happy, marriage has made me a mother, and in that I find contentment.

I returned to my white grange to sit with these thoughts. For five months the miracle of nature has been unfolding within me, yet I remain emotionally detached. My family is excited; I feel cold and hesitate to confess my true feelings—motherhood still feels abstract. Louis’s surprise shows how little the father’s role seems to matter here. My doctor says childbirth is governed by chance, though children born of passionate love may be luckier; perhaps there are joys for you I will never feel, since your love for Felipe differs from my sense of duty.

These reflections come with nausea and odd cravings—while Louis brings fine oranges, I crave half-rotten ones that ease my sickness. I’ll stay on the grounds to avoid being seen and wonder when the true sense of motherhood will begin; I hope it won’t arrive with suffering. Farewell, my fortunate friend—your love story inspires me. Pray and do good; religion has become more important to me since my marriage. God bless you!
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Madame,— Renée bore a son. We postpone his baptism until your return and hope you'll be godmother to Armand‑Louis. She is well; child resembles her and promises much. A suite awaits; your presence redeems simplicity. The birth reconciled my father to heavy sacrifices: he bought two estates; La Crampade yields 30,000 francs. He seeks King's leave to entail it; your aid securing the title is asked. Your interest heartens me. LOUIS DE L’ESTORADE.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Macumer woke me with your husband’s letter. Yes, we’ll go to Chantepleurs at the end of April. I am delighted to travel, to see you, and to be godmother to your first child — and I insist Macumer be godfather; his look showed how dearly he cherishes me, and sharing the role would be unbearable.

I long to visit La Crampade; I may have a child there too. Can you picture me torn between you and an infant? If anything ever supplanted my place in your heart, I hardly know how I’d bear it—Medea’s rage seems less extreme now.

You have the fruit without the flowers, I the flowers without the fruit. Our lives contrast, yet we have the philosophy to endure. Ten months married is too soon to give up hope.

We live happily, as happy people do. Society now calls the Baronne de Macumer prettier than Louise de Chaulieu—love is a wonderful cosmetic. Driving along the Champs-Élysées this January I reflected on how much has changed since last year.

If I lack a mother’s joys, you can share them with me and I will learn by sympathy. Still, I cannot imagine anything surpassing the rapture of love; often I think it would be better to die at thirty, preserved from later disappointment, than to carry a young heart in an aged body among cold faces. Felipe and I quarrelled about this: I asked him to have the strength to let me slip away at thirty so I might pass from one dream to another; he refused and turned pale. This distinguished statesman can be surprisingly childlike. Now that we share such confidences, we often surprise each other.

Felipe and I would like to send you a gift—something to bring you joy and to remind you of us. Tell me what you would like. I thought of a luncheon service with baby figures, since lunch is our happiest meal. Let me know soon; we must order it.

Farewell, dear nursing mother. May you enjoy all the delights of motherhood. I eagerly await your first letter describing your experiences. Some parts of your husband’s letter moved me; I will tell my godson how dearly he must love you. Much love, my sweet one.

---

It has been nearly five months since the baby was born, and I have found no moment to write. When you become a mother you will understand. Please write more often! Share your joys; I am happier than you can imagine.

I went to the parish church for the Mass of recovery, supported by both grandfathers. Kneeling before God, overwhelmed with gratitude, one image remains: the Virgin on the altar seeming to bend and point to the infant Christ, who smiled at me. With a heart full of love, I held little Armand for the priest to bless, looking forward to his baptism.

How sweet the word “child” is! In the last two months of pregnancy I wandered through the gardens, scarcely conscious of the burden I carried. Gloomy forebodings haunted me; my heart did not respond to the thought of the little one, though he reminded me with lively kicks. When labor came, I summoned all my strength and bore the agony; I sank into a stupor, feeling like two beings: my body in pain and my soul at peace. Pain formed a halo; at its climax I screamed, then the newborn’s cries silenced my suffering.

They laid me back, and it felt like paradise despite exhaustion. Happy faces pointed to the child, and I cried out in terror, “It looks like a little monkey! Are you sure it’s a child?” My mother, now my nurse, reassured me: “You’ve got the finest baby in the world. Don’t worry; be a mother.” I slept and left things to nature.

Waking from those first days’ pain was heavenly. A ray of light pierced the darkness, and my heart opened like a flower. The moment the little one took my breast was pure joy—this felt like the dawn of motherhood.

Nursing is a miracle of transformation. The cries go straight to your heart; the child’s eyes and lips express everything. What else matters? The father could be forgiven nothing that disturbed the baby. The bond between mother and child is profound; the joy of nurturing is unparalleled.

At five months my little one has become the prettiest darling: he cries little and is content in our love. I strive to envelop him in affection.

My feelings for Louis have changed into a tender fondness, perhaps surpassing romantic love: unselfish gratitude that lights the future and recalls fond memories. The old L’Estorade and his son have redoubled their devotion; each look from them brings me joy. Louis is ecstatic at having a son—he watches him sleep for hours—and the fear of childlessness has given way to renewed purpose; he works harder, driven by fatherhood.

I grow happier by the hour. The bond between mother and child strengthens continually. This love is natural and enduring, unlike romantic passion, which can wane; a mother’s love grows with the child’s needs and combines passion, duty, and joy. Here a woman’s true sphere unfolds, where self-sacrifice flourishes without jealousy.

Society reinforces nature’s instincts: a mother cherishes the child who opens these delights and teaches her the art of motherhood. A mother learns from her child, and her character deepens through these duties and pleasures; a woman without children is incomplete.

Hasten, my sweetest, to fulfill your mission. Your happiness will multiply with mine.

23rd.

I had to leave you because my godson’s cry reached me from the garden. I wanted to say farewell. Rereading this letter, I am surprised at how ordinary my feelings sound; yet every mother must have felt this way. I am as happy now as I once was miserable. This place is my land of promise—the desert is past. A thousand loves, darling. Write to me, for I can now read about your happy love without tears. Farewell.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Do you know, dear, it has been over three months since I last wrote? I admit I failed to answer your last letter; you won’t mind?

Macumer and I took your silence as consent about the baby-wreathed luncheon service; after six months’ work it’s now bound for Marseilles. When Felipe asked me to inspect it before packing, I realized we hadn’t exchanged a word since your letter that revealed the heart of a mother.

I blame dreadful Paris—what is your excuse? Society here is a whirlpool. Didn’t I tell you one must conform? It drives out sentiment, monopolizes time, and can consume the heart. Moliere’s Celimene is the epitome of the society woman—timeless and apt.

Without my breastplate—my love for Felipe—I should be lost. This morning I told him he is my salvation. Evenings are full of parties; nights my heart heals in passionate love.

I dine at home only with friends; I spend one afternoon a week in the house—Wednesday. I have entered the social fray, competing with Mmes. d’Espard and de Maufrigneuse; my house is lively. I became fashionable for Felipe’s pleasure. My mornings are his; from four in the afternoon until two in the morning, I belong to Paris. Macumer is an admirable host—witty, dignified, courteous. No woman could help loving such a husband.

My parents have gone to Madrid. With Louis XVIII gone, the Duchess obtained my father’s appointment as attaché. My brother, the Duc de Rhetore, now acknowledges me. My younger brother, the Comte de Chaulieu, owes me for spending my fortune to secure him an estate and a marriage with Mlle. de Mortsauf, an heiress. The King will confer the de Lenoncourt and de Givry titles on him to preserve their legacy. Mlle. de Mortsauf, sole heiress of the Duc de Lenoncourt-Givry, is expected to inherit over a hundred thousand livres a year. My father insisted the de Chaulieu arms be placed on the de Lenoncourt escutcheon, making my brother Duc de Lenoncourt. The young de Mortsauf is gravely ill, so the marriage waits until next winter, after mourning.

My father’s plan has justified itself, earning me compliments and softening explanations of my marriage to society. The Prince de Talleyrand, kindly toward my grandmother, is now warm to Macumer. Society that once criticized me now admires me.

I now reign where I was once insignificant. Macumer is envied as the husband of “the most charming woman in Paris.” Many women vie for that title; men murmur and gaze. This adulation flatters vanity—I see why women pay dearly for such fleeting privileges. This triumph is intoxicating; I understand how women become selfish and frivolous under admiration. Society’s demands consume your spirit; you give yourself for smiles and jealousy and receive only flattery. You know the cost, yet can’t escape the vortex.

Ah, how I crave a true friend! Felipe’s love and devotion are my treasure, and my heart longs for you. We are joyfully preparing to move to Chantepleurs, where we can escape the farce of Paris. After rereading your letter, I realize no fashionable woman in Paris can be a good mother.

Goodbye for now, dear. We will be at Chantepleurs for only a week and hope to see you around May 10th. We will meet again after more than two years! How we have changed—we are both matrons now, you in motherhood, I in love.

If I haven’t written, it isn’t because I’ve forgotten you. How is my little godson? He must be over nine months now; I long to see his first steps, though Macumer says even precocious infants don’t walk at ten months.

We will have delightful gossip and see if a child truly spoils a pattern.

P.S. If you reply, address it to Chantepleurs. I’m just leaving.

---

My child, if you become a mother you’ll find it impossible to write during the first two months of nursing. Mary, my English nurse, and I are exhausted. I resolved to do everything myself—sewing baby clothes, embroidering caps. I am a slave, day and night.

Master Armand-Louis eats whenever he pleases, and I must constantly change, wash, and dress him. I love watching him sleep, singing to him, and carrying him about, leaving little time for myself. Society has been to you what motherhood is to me!

I cannot express how rich my life has become, and I long for your visit to see for yourself. I fear he will soon be teething, and you may find a fussy baby. So far he hasn’t cried much because I’m always attentive. Babies cry when their needs aren’t met, and I’m vigilant. My heart has expanded while yours shrinks under society’s demands. I eagerly await your arrival, wanting your thoughts on l’Estorade just as you’re curious about Macumer.

Write to me from your last stop. The gentlemen want to meet our distinguished guests. Come, Queen of Paris, to our humble home, where love will greet you!

---

The name on this letter shows my petition has been granted: your father-in-law is now Comte de l’Estorade. I wouldn’t leave Paris until I secured this for you; I’m writing in the Keeper of the Seals’ presence—he has just told me the patent is signed.

Goodbye for now.

---

I am ashamed that my sudden departure will surprise you. But I must be honest: I am horribly jealous.

Felipe’s eyes rested too often on you. Your little talks at the foot of your rock tortured me, and I became irritable. Your beauty reminded him of his homeland and Marie Heredia; I can be jealous of the past too. Your magnificent features stabbed me in the heart. I tried to enhance my appearance, but everything paled beside you.

Felipe doted on the child, whom I began to resent. Yes, I confess—I wanted that exuberance of life for myself. I saw regret in Macumer’s eyes, and I wept for two nights. I was miserable in your home. You are too beautiful and triumphant for me to endure.

You complained of your lot, but L’Estorade is presentable, charming, and likely to become a deputy. His exile has given him a calm, dignified air, useful for a politician. Macumer, too, should achieve high place.

Having satisfied myself of your happiness, I went to Chantepleurs, where Felipe must pursue his aspirations. I won’t receive you there without a fine baby to match yours.

I know I deserve your scorn. I am a fool, a wretch—jealousy is a poor virtue. I am not angry with you, but I was miserable; you will forgive my escape. Two more days and I might have made a scene.

Despite my jealousy, I am glad to have seen you in the pride of motherhood. Even in Marseilles I feel proud of you as a splendid mother.

You are born for motherhood; I am not. A good mother and a passionate wife both require intelligence and grace. I watched you and admired you. Your children will be happy, not spoiled, by your tenderness and reason.

Tell Louis the truth about my departure, but find a decent excuse for your father-in-law and family. Felipe doesn’t know why I left, and he never will. If he asks, I’ll fabricate a pretext—perhaps that you were jealous of me. Forgive my little fib.

Goodbye. I write in haste; the postilion waits.

Kisses to my dear godson. Come to Chantepleurs in October: I’ll be alone while Macumer is in Sardinia planning estate improvements. That seems to calm him; sharing plans with me unsettles him. Goodbye!

---

Dear sister, we were astonished at lunch to learn you had left, especially after your letter. You were happy; there was no reason for such objections. My sentence: you return at my first summons. Your letter didn’t say your next stop, so I address this to Chantepleurs.

Listen, dear sister of my heart. My focus is your happiness. Your husband commands respect—not only a grave exterior but a revealed power and a fire in his eyes. He has been Prime Minister and adores you, which makes him a profound dissembler. Extracting secrets from such a diplomatic soul requires skill.

You know my role is reason while yours is imagination. I am a simple country vicountess, ambitious for family prosperity. Macumer, the Duc de Soria, has a name, and you, a duchess by right, reign in Paris, where it’s hard to maintain power. You have a considerable fortune, which may double if Macumer develops his Sardinian estates. If anyone has the right to be jealous, it’s not you. Thankfully, we both have generous hearts that place our friendship above pettiness.

I know you must feel ashamed of fleeing, but your flight won’t spare you from my words. Read carefully; this concerns you more than Macumer.

Firstly, you do not love him as a wife should. In two years you may tire of adoration. You will never see Felipe as a husband; he will always be a lover to you. You do not revere him as a woman should her god. I have studied love, and this is not love.

You cannot escape the fate of all queens. One day you will long to be treated as a light-o’-love—to be swept off your feet by a strong man who won’t worship you. Macumer loves you too much to resist or criticize. Sooner or later you will scorn this excessive devotion. He spoils you, as I spoiled you at the convent; you are enchanting, and there’s no escaping your charms.

Worse, you are candid; our happiness often relies on social hypocrisies you won’t embrace. Society won’t tolerate a wife’s power over her husband. Convention demands that a man not show himself as lover to his wife, nor may a married woman act as a mistress. You both disregard this.

The unpardonable sin in society is to be happy; if happiness exists, it must remain hidden. More importantly, equality between lovers shouldn't appear in marriage, or it undermines social order. It is painful to see a man become a nonentity, and worse to see a capable man reduced to that. You will reduce Macumer to a shadow; your household will lack balance. You will regret it, but by then it may be too late. Women often lack the qualities essential to family life. Macumer senses this; his Sardinia visit suggests he hopes to regain himself.

You use the power his love gives you. Your position shows in your gestures and tone. You are the wanton your mother called you! Have you ever heard me contradict Louis? I respect his public authority. If I want to advise him, I wait for private moments; I never assume the role of mentor. If I didn’t present myself as submissive publicly, he would lose confidence. The good we do for others is spoiled unless we erase ourselves so they feel no inferiority. There is sweetness in these hidden sacrifices; it is a triumph when you praise Louis.

This is my counsel. Right now, you love Felipe not as a husband but as a reflection of yourself. Your father’s words ring true: beneath your passion lies a selfishness typical of a great lady. How I must love you to speak such bitter truths!

If you promise not to tell the Baron, I’ll share the end of our talk. We praised you, and he soon sensed my sisterly love. I ventured, “Louise has never struggled with life; fortune has spoiled her, and she might pay for this if you weren’t there to be father and lover.”

“Ah! but is it possible?” he broke off—enough for me. If you had stayed, he would have spoken more freely.

Think of the day when your husband’s strength wanes, when pleasure turns to satiety, and he feels diminished before you. The stings of conscience will awaken remorse, painful for you, as you’ll feel responsible and end up despising the man you failed to respect. Remember, scorn can become hatred. You are too noble to forget Felipe’s sacrifices, but what more can he give when he has already given himself fully? Woe to those who leave no desire unsatisfied—women can be insatiable in love.

Oh, Louise, change while there’s still time. If you treated Macumer as I treat l’Estorade, you could awaken the hero within him. Wouldn’t you feel pride in using your power to elevate a gifted man, as I do with one of lesser stature?

Had you stayed I would have told you this in person, but I know you will read and weigh my warnings. You have everything to be happy; don’t spoil your chances. Return to Paris as soon as Macumer does. The demands of society, which I complain about, are necessary; otherwise you’ll be lost in self-absorption. A married woman shouldn't be too generous with herself; a mother should reserve sacred hours for herself—children should not take her presence for granted.

Farewell, my dear jealous soul! Many women would be flattered by your pangs, but I can only mourn, for what is not mother in me is your dear friend. A thousand loves. Make whatever excuse you must for leaving; if you doubt Macumer, I am sure of Louis.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
