################################################
Beloved eager for Italy: Rome, Venice, Paris (Nov–Jan). Honeymoon-like—excuse letters. Chantepleurs. Parliament may dissolve—bad for Crown; Father stand, resign. Godson walks; nursed Armand, wean December. Thousand loves.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Your dreadful letter reached me, forwarded by my steward as I asked. Oh, Renée — I will spare you unnecessary complaint and tell you only what it produced.

We had just returned from an ambassador’s reception where I shone and Macumer was utterly captivated. Then I read your harsh reply and sobbed. My dear Arab flung himself at my feet, calling you mad. He led me to our balcony; speaking Italian, his declarations were like poetry. He swore that even if your predictions were true he would not trade one moment of our love for a lifetime of convention—he would choose our love and death over a dull existence with children.

While he whispered, a bat’s cry startled us and I fainted. I am better this morning, though that sound haunts me. Upon waking I knelt before Felipe, looked into his eyes, and confessed: “My love, I may be naive, and Renée might be right. Perhaps I love only your love, but this is the only feeling in my heart. If anything about me disturbs you, tell me. I want to follow your wishes.” Macumer was too choked with feeling to answer.

I thank you, Renée; your letter revealed the depths of Macumer’s devotion. Rome is the city of love, where passion should flourish alongside art and faith. In Venice we shall meet the Duc and Duchesse de Soria. If you write, address Paris; we leave Rome in three days after the ambassador’s farewell.

P.S. Dear naive child, your letter shows you know love only in theory. Love is a force that yields diverse fruits beyond any theory’s grasp.

My father has been elected to the Chamber, my father-in-law has died, and I am near my second confinement; these mark the year’s end. I mention them first to spare you the fright of the black seal.

Your letter from Rome chilled me. You and Felipe behave like children. Either he is a deceitful diplomat, or his love is like a man’s for a courtesan—lavishing affection while knowing she is unfaithful. Enough. You say I rave, so I will hold my tongue: from our differing fates I draw a harsh lesson—do not love if you wish to be loved.

When Louis joined the provincial Council he received the Legion of Honor cross. Nearly three years on, my father asks the rank of Officer for his son-in-law. I would value your influence with the right person, but do not entangle yourself in my father’s affairs. The Comte de Maucombe seeks a marquisate for himself; reserve efforts for me. When Louis becomes a deputy next winter, we will be in Paris to secure him a government post so we may live on his salary. My father sits between center and right; a title will satisfy him. Our family was noble even under King René, and Charles X will likely grant a Maucombe title. My fear is my father using this to favor my younger brother—if a marquisate is dangled, he will focus on himself.

January 15th.

Ah, Louise, I have been through hell. I tell you because you are like a sister; still, I doubt I shall ever relive those five ghastly days. The word “convulsions” sickens me. Five days felt like five centuries. A mother who has not suffered this cannot comprehend it.

The night before, the weather was close; I thought Armand affected by teething. Usually sweet, he grew peevish, crying and breaking his toys. I put him to bed beside me. Toward morning he cried “Mamma!” in a strange voice and twitched. I rose for a drink; as I handed him the cup he was motionless, repeating “Mamma!” in a voice hardly a voice. His hand was stiff. He gulped water convulsively, clung to me, eyes rolling back, limbs rigid. I screamed for Louis.

“A doctor! Quick!... he is dying,” I cried.

Louis vanished. Armand gasped “Mamma!” then lost consciousness. Veins on his forehead swelled, and convulsions began. For an hour before doctors arrived I held my lifeless baby—my pride and joy. His eyes! I cannot think of them without horror. My pretty Armand was a mere shell—black, shriveled.

Two doctors from Marseilles hovered like birds of ill omen. One blamed brain fever, the other called them ordinary convulsions. Our country doctor offered no opinion. “Teething,” said one; “fever,” said the other. They agreed on leeches to the neck and ice to the head. To see a child like that—purple, silent—was horrible. They even cut his curls for the ice. The convulsions returned every ten minutes; the child writhed, white then violet, limbs clattering like wood. This unconscious flesh had smiled and said “Mamma!”—the thought pierced my soul.

After four days and nights, the doctors recommended a dreadful ointment to create open sores. Sores on my Armand! Five days earlier he had been playing and laughing. I refused, trusting nature. Louis, who believes doctors, scolded me. But in those moments when the disease mimicked death, I felt that remedy might save him. His skin was dry and rough; the ointment would not work. I wept; the doctors were at dinner!

Alone with him, I stripped off the medical appliances, pressed him to my bosom, and laid my forehead against his, begging God to spare him. For minutes I held him, longing to die with him. Then his limbs relaxed; the writhings ceased; the ghastly colors faded. I screamed; the doctors rushed in.

“He is saved!” cried the oldest doctor.

What music those words were! Two hours later Armand returned to life. I was crushed, saved only by the joy of his recovery. What tortures bind a mother to her child! Must the nails be driven to fasten him to my heart? Was I not mother enough? I who wept at his first words and steps—why these horrors?

Now Armand plays and laughs. What causes this dreadful disease in children? I ponder it, especially as I am again with child. Is it teething? A brain disorder? A nervous susceptibility? Our country doctor blames nervous trouble from teething. I would give every tooth in my head to see Armand’s through. One white pearl peeking from swollen gums brings a cold sweat. The heroism with which my angel bore suffering proves he is his mother’s son. A look from him pierces me.

Medical science offers no clear explanation for this tetanus that appears and vanishes swiftly, unpreventable and uncured. One thing is certain: it is hell for a mother to see her child in convulsions. How passionately I hold him now! I could walk forever with him in my arms!

To have endured this six weeks before my confinement made it worse; I feared for the coming child. Farewell, my beloved. Do not wish for a child—this is the essence of my letter!

Poor dear, Macumer and I forgave you upon hearing of your terrible trouble. I felt pain reading of your double agony; there seem compensations in being childless.

I write to tell you Louis has been promoted; he may now wear the ribbon of an Officer of the Legion. You are fortunate, Renée—likely to have a little girl, as you wished!

My brother’s marriage to Mlle. de Mortsauf was celebrated on our return. Our gracious King, who is extraordinarily kind, has granted my brother the reversion of the post of first gentleman of the chamber, presently held by his father-in-law, on condition the Mortsauf scutcheon be placed alongside the Lenoncourt’s.

“The office ought to go with the title,” he told the Duc de Lenoncourt-Givry.

My father is justified a hundredfold. Without my fortune none of this would have happened. My parents came from Madrid for the wedding and will return after the reception I am hosting for the newlyweds.

The carnival will be lively. The Duc and Duchesse de Soria are in Paris, which unnerves me. Marie Heredia is one of Europe’s most beautiful women, and I dislike the way Felipe looks at her. Therefore, I am extra sweet and affectionate. Every look and gesture says what my lips do not: “She could not love like this!” Heaven knows how lovely I am! Yesterday Mme. de Maufrigneuse said, “Dear child, who can compete with you?”

I keep Felipe entertained, making his sister-in-law seem dull by comparison. I am less sorry that a little Abencerrage is not on the way, since the Duchess will likely remain in Paris for her confinement and lose her beauty. If the baby is a boy, he will be named Felipe in honor of the exile. Fate decrees I shall be godmother again.

Goodbye, dear. I plan to go to Chantepleurs early this year: our Italian tour was shockingly expensive. I will leave around the end of March to economize in Nivenais. Besides, I am tired of Paris. Felipe and I long for the tranquility of the park, cool meadows, and the sparkling Loire. Chantepleurs will be delightful after Italy; splendor becomes tiresome, and a lover’s gaze is more beautiful than any opera or painting!

We expect you there. Do not fear my jealousy. You are free to test Macumer’s heart and expose his doubts. I am utterly indifferent. Since that day in Rome Felipe’s love for me has deepened. He just told me (looking over my shoulder) that his sister-in-law, Princess Heredia, his childhood dream, has no brains. Oh, my dear, I am worse than a ballet dancer! If you knew the joy that remark brought me! I pointed out her faulty French—she says “esemple” for “exemple,” “sain” for “cinq,” “cheu” for “je.” She is beautiful but lacks charm and wit. When praised she seems puzzled. Felipe could not have lived two months with Marie after marriage. Don Fernand, the Duc de Soria, suits her perfectly: generous but a spoiled child. I am tempted to be mischievous and make you laugh, but I will refrain. Much love, darling.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
My daughter Jeanne‑Athenais is two months; her godparents are my mother and Louis’s grand‑uncle. Your godson says “Matoumer”—he’s teething. I watch him anxiously but cannot keep him always near; the doctors insist on precautions and his discomfort continues. Don’t tell Felipe—she resembles me and might outshine Armand.

Father saw Felipe thin; you seem unwell. Your last letter was shorter and less affectionate—was it a whim? The nurse is annoyed; Athenais is hungry. Farewell; write.

My dear Renée,

For the first time I am truly alone and in tears. I sat beneath a willow by the Chantepleurs marsh—pretty but desolate without children’s sounds. Nearly three years of marriage and no child; your household full of little ones makes me examine my heart. I would brave any suffering to hold a cherub of my own—an Athenais to call mine! I picture her looks and sweetness, yet you tell me nothing—just like you, Renée. I am sure you share my distress.

Each disappointment plunges me into melancholy, and I compose mournful elegies. When will I embroider caps and sew lace for a tiny head? Will I never hear “Mamma” or have a little despot tug my dress? My gravel may remain untouched by carriage wheelmarks, my courtyard free of broken toys; yet I long to watch a life unfold—another Felipe, only dearer; a son to become my second self. My park and château seem cold and empty. A woman without children feels unnatural; we exist to be mothers. Barrenness is a bleak fate. If I cannot be a mother, I will at least indulge in romantic sorrow.

I told my Moor of this, and he wept; he cannot bear jokes on his love. Sometimes I dream of pilgrimage to carry my longings to some shrine. Next winter I shall seek a physician. I am too angry with myself to write more. Farewell.

---

A whole year has passed with no letter—what does it mean? I am hurt. Is Louis, who visits so often, a sufficient substitute? It is not enough to know you are well; I want your feelings. Your silence and seclusion in the country while your husband gains Parliamentary honors alarm me. His eloquence has secured favor, and he will likely receive a fine post when the session ends. Are you spending your time writing him counsel?

Why did you not come to Paris? I could have enjoyed your company for four months. Louis told me you would fetch him and have your third confinement in Paris—what a monstrous Gigogne of a mother! After pestering Louis, I learned his grand‑uncle, Athenais’ godfather, is very ill; perhaps you, as a careful mother, wish to secure a legacy from your husband’s last relative. Rest easy; we are all working for Louis. Martignac will likely place him in the audit department. But if you will not tell me why you hide in the country, I shall be cross.

Are you afraid that your family’s political wisdom should seem to center in you? Is it the uncle’s legacy? Do you fear you would be less to your children in Paris? Or are you simply too vain to show yourself in the metropolis while expecting? Vain thing! Farewell.

---

My dear friend, have you forgotten my two little brown heads, my subjects and tyrants? I could not have dragged a four‑year‑old and an almost three‑year‑old to Paris while expecting again. I would not trouble you or your husband with such a tumult. I detest hotel life. When I come to Paris for the session it will be to my own house: Louis’s uncle, pleased with his grand‑nephew’s prospects, gave me two hundred thousand francs to buy a house near you, and my mother sent thirty thousand for furnishings. Louis is charged with finding a place; I will do my best not to disgrace you.

I appreciate all you have done for Louis at court; yet I do not want him too conspicuous. I prefer the Audit Office for steadiness rather than a post that would commit him unduly. Our affairs are in good hands; once the steward understands the details, I will support Louis.

As for writing long letters—I cannot. One day is like another here: the children are either well or ill, and that sets the tone. In this solitary grange, minutes stretch and shrink with their health. My delightful hours come only when they sleep and I am no longer needed to rock or soothe them. Every mother is haunted by imagined dangers: razors, fires, ponds, the demon of convulsions. We live on a stage of small dramas, tender and terrible.

At night I dream of their future, comforted by the watch of their guardian angel. Sometimes Armand calls in his sleep; I kiss his forehead and his sister’s feet and watch their beauty. Once an angel roused me to Athenais’ cradle—her head was too low and Armand’s feet purple with cold. How vital it is to have one’s children at night! A nurse cannot soothe as a mother after a nightmare. I have learned to sleep lightly, my ear tuned to every sigh or rustle by their bed.

Morning brings their chatter. Nais strains to reach me while Armand climbs up like a little monkey; they transform my bed into a playground and I am at their mercy. The baby tugs my hair and Armand guards my breast like a jealous sentinel. Their antics replace sleep; their laughter repays all care.

Then the dressing begins: dressing gown, apron, sleeves rolled up, and Mary’s help in baths and scrubbings. Bath time is perilous—so much crying springs from a misjudged temperature or a dropped brush. Amusement is necessary—paper boats and glass ducks—to get them to submit. I contrive diversions: prayers, scoldings, promises; motherhood becomes a constant game of quick invention. Our triumphs are private: Mary and I rejoice when, after a struggle, the children are at last clean and content. Only God, His angels, and perhaps you can grasp the satisfaction of such small victories.

English women excel at arranging comforts; their nurses and systems are admirable. My children will always be bare‑legged in summer and in woolen socks in winter; no swaddling or tight bandages for them. A true mother is never free; the helplessness of many French infants gives nurses liberties their charge’s mothers would not allow. That is why I do not write: besides managing the estate, I am raising two children, with a third on the way.

Motherhood demands silent self‑denial and attention to minute details—a mother blows on the spoon for her child’s soup, minces the chop, endures annoyances. No servant or foreign nurse can replace a mother in those daily contests where gentleness must soothe every little grief. Louise, caring for these darlings, engages my whole soul. A crying child condemns the nurse or mother unless pain is natural. With two to tend (and soon three), they occupy all my thoughts—sometimes even you, whom I adore, recede to a memory.

Often my dressing is not finished until mid‑afternoon. I distrust mothers whose rooms are immaculate and who always look perfect. Yesterday, in a lovely April, I planned an outing with the children—an epic for a mother. I had laid out Armand’s little black velvet jacket, a new collar, a Scotch cap; Nais in white and pink with a baby cap. The little heads were to be brushed; Armand’s curls and Nais’ soft hair arranged; my heart was in my mouth. Once outside, proud of my work, I admired Armand’s princely air as he helped Nais along the path. Then a carriage approached and in our scramble the children tumbled into a puddle, wrecking my preparations. We had to return and change: a crying baby and a soaked child left me with no thought for myself.

At dinner I have oft achieved nothing. Feeding two, securing bibs, turning up sleeves, cajoling food—these recurring cares, joys, and catastrophes make me neglect my own tidiness. To write you I have let them cut pictures from novels and arrange my silks; they are absorbed and content.

Yet I have little to complain of. My children are robust and inventive; they amuse themselves more readily than you might think. A little freedom and a few pebbles, shells, and sand do more for them than many toys. Armand talks to flowers, flies, and chickens; he imitates and admires insects and asks “why” about everything. It is touching to see his curiosity—he even thinks of you, his godmother, as a fairy.

Let me not sadden you with all my joys. A poor man once followed us begging—beggars know a mother with a child cannot resist. Armand, innocent of hunger or money, offered the beggar the trumpet I had bought him: “Here, take this!” That moment of spontaneous charity was incomparable. The poor man, moved, kept it, saying, “I too, madame, have had children.” I wept at the sweetness.

I shudder at the thought of Armand going to school: I have only three and a half years before public institutions tear him away. The bright flowers of his sunny childhood will be cut by the scythe of education; his gracious candor may be lost. What will they do to the thinking being he is, to the curls I brushed and kissed so often? And you—are you still in love with Felipe? I have no fear of the Saracen. Goodbye; Nais has tumbled, and this letter threatens to become a volume.

---

My sweet, tender Renée, you will have learned from the papers of the terrible calamity that has crushed me. I could not write for twenty days; I did not leave his bedside. I received his last breath, closed his eyes, kept vigil with the priests, and said the prayers for the dead. The pangs I suffered felt like a just punishment; yet when I saw the calm smile on his lips, his final farewell to me, how could I think I had caused his death?

He is gone and I am bereft. To you, who knew us both, what more need be said? I would give my share of Heaven to bring him back even for a second. Will you not come soon and soothe me with such impossible promises? You told me beforehand that he would suffer through me. Was it so? Yes; I had no right to all his love. Like a thief I took what belonged to him and in my frenzied grasp crushed the life out of my bliss. The madness is over, but I feel alone and crushed. Merciful God! what torture can surpass the misery in that single word: alone?

When they took him away I lay on the same bed hoping to die. There was but a door between us and I thought I could force it. I was too young for death; after forty days of cruel care and attention, I was slowly brought back to life. Now I sit in the country by a window, surrounded by the flowers he had loved and made flourish, looking at the view he admired. Ah, Renée, how new surroundings wound when the heart is dead. His flowers torment me; to walk in my garden is to walk upon him. When I first ventured out I trembled and could not move.

My parents are in Spain, my brothers far off, and you detained in the country. But two angels of mercy comfort me: the Duc and Duchesse de Soria hurried to their brother in his illness and have been everything I could wish. The last nights found us gathered in mute grief around this great man—rare in any age, distinguished by mind and heart. Felipe’s calm resignation was angelic. His last words to me were simple and frank about his will and his love; he blessed me on his lips and spoke of my memory more fondly than any other. His brother forgave him, knowing what love is.

I owe my life to my brother‑in‑law and his wife; they wish to take me to Spain. To no one but you can I freely pour out my grief. A sense of my own faults sits heavy: my demands, jealousy, and anxious unrest may have worn him down. My love was dangerous because we were so alike in sensitivity; my careless words pierced him to the heart. You cannot imagine how submissive he was: I only had to tell him to leave me alone, and he obeyed without a murmur. His last breath blessed me, saying that a single morning with me was worth more than a lifetime with another. My tears fall as I write.

This is my life now: rising at midday, going to bed early, lingering over meals, standing motionless before a plant, gazing into the trees, finding significance in trifles. I seek shade, silence, and night. I drag through each hour, taking a gloomy pride in surviving them. My park gives me company enough; everything there echoes my vanished joy, visible only to me.

If I still live it is because Heaven tempers grief to the strength of those who bear it. Only a woman can know what it is to lose a love that was genuine—one that satisfied spirit and flesh. How rare a man so gifted that worship does not feel debasing! If such fortune befalls us once, we cannot hope to find it again. Men of true greatness, veiled by poetic grace, charm with a high spiritual power—beware of love: it may ruin you and the woman you love. That is my cry as I walk the paths of my wood.

And he has left me no child! That love, rich in smiles and tenderness, left no fruit. I feel cursed. Is it that intense, single‑hearted passion bears no children? Is only a marriage of reason blessed with progeny? Does Heaven envy our passions? Such wild questions haunt me.

You are the one person whose company I could bear. Come to me; none but you should help Louise in her sorrow. The day I first donned my widow’s bonnet—when I first saw myself in black—I fell back and wept until night. I weep again at the memory. Bring your children; you can nurse your baby here without making me jealous; what is gone is gone, and I will be glad to see my godson. Felipe wished for a child like little Armand. Come, then—come and help me bear this woe.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
My darling—I'll be near when you read this; Louis remains in Provence seeking re-election. Weep if you must—tears will change to joy when we reunite. He is journeying to Heaven; his duties ease their hold. In my arms you'll lift your head to him. While I was preparing, Armand cried, "Godmother, I want to see her," which made me jealous—perhaps he is yours.

Yes, Renée, it is true: I have sold Chantepleurs and the farms in Seine-et-Marne—but I am neither mad nor ruined. After settling everything I have 1,200,000 francs from my late husband's fortune. I invested 1,000,000 in the Three per Cents at fifty; it now yields 60,000 francs instead of the 30,000 the property produced. My past life was tedious—six months in the country with leases and impatient farmers, the rest in Paris plagued by delays and legal troubles. For a twenty-seven-year-old widow it was intolerable. Now my fortune is secured with the State: instead of paying taxes I receive 30,000 francs every six months from the Treasury, handed to me by a pleasant clerk. Even if the nation failed I should still be as well off, and the Comte de l’Estorade, a peer of this semi-republic and a friend at Court, leaves me no cause for anxiety.

What drove me to these calculations? Love.

I must tell you plainly why I acted as I did. I am to be married in a village near Paris. I love and am loved. Forgive me for keeping this from you and de l’Estorade; I wished to spare you both anxiety. You know my jealousy; I thought secrecy kinder.

The man I love had 30,000 francs of debts, which I have paid. You may suspect an adventurer, but I investigated him. He has courted me nearly two years. I am twenty-seven and he twenty-three—a difference that seems wide. He is a poet who has lived on little and spent more time dreaming than making a living. Practical people think artists unreliable; I understand that prejudice.

But my love is deliberate, not a caprice. He waited patiently; his love is noble and tender. My beloved is Gaston, the illegitimate son of the beautiful Lady Brandon, who died tragically. He was brought up by his brother Louis, a sailor whose letters once encouraged him; now Louis has vanished and Gaston is often despairing.

Gaston believed genius would bring fortune, yet his life has been hardship: failure to establish himself in letters, mounting debts, humiliation. I met him at Mme. d’Espard’s and felt an immediate sympathy that has grown into passion. He has talent and pride—qualities that can intimidate a woman—but his love for me seems genuine. He knows nothing of the depth of my feeling.

I am confident of our happiness, though I fear losing him or my charms as he remains young. I shall nourish our love and be loved for myself. If his affection should wane, I would blame myself rather than him. My nature inclines to be a lover rather than a mother; should disaster come, Renée, I beg you to care for my children as my legacy. Your tenderness would sweeten my fate.

I have resolved to marry quietly and to keep control of my property; Gaston must not know everything. I propose to assign him stock yielding 12,000 francs a year, which I shall place where he will find it on the eve of our wedding. If he refuses it I will break the engagement.

This confession has fatigued me; I will continue later. I must spend tomorrow in the country.

October 20th.

I have taken steps for secrecy. I bought a small property near Ville-d’Avray: twenty acres, a little lake, and a charming garden. It is secluded, surrounded by woods. My chalet, inspired by Swiss designs, is simple and solid, with a separate house for servants and a concealed entrance; I have chosen discreet, loyal staff. All my cherished belongings from Paris are here, creating a simple but elegant home. The valley’s tranquility moved me to tears on my first visit; I paid the architect without hesitation, insisting he tell no one I was the owner.

Now you understand my actions and the purpose of my investments. I have cleared financial burdens to focus wholly on him. Gaston knows nothing of my plans; our life at the chalet will be modest, though I shall spare no care in preserving my appearance for him. I want him to love me at the end as at the beginning.

In two days I shall be Mme. Gaston. Is it fitting for a Christian to love so deeply? My man of business says he has lost a client. And you, my dear, may feel you are losing a sister.

Address future letters to Mme. Gaston, Poste Restante, Versailles. We shall keep our names and our retreat secret from the locals and obtain provisions from Paris. Farewell. Writing this I feel grief and joy together—proof that my love for Gaston is as deep as the sorrow of leaving you.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
My Dear Daniel,

I need two witnesses for my marriage; please come tomorrow evening with our friend Joseph Bridau. My future wife insists on a life of complete retirement, which explains our infrequent meetings this past year. After the wedding we will be apart for a long time, but friendship can endure absence; I shall miss you though I shall not see you in my own home. She has given up the closeness of a childhood friend for me, and I must do the same.

This proves our love is deep and genuine, founded on mutual understanding, and it brings me great joy. Yet I carry a painful secret. You know how desperate my finances have been; only through your support have I managed. My wife is wealthy; I am not. I once joked about wanting a rich heiress, and now that it is true I feel a bitter humiliation I cannot shake. This shame, however, confirms my love for her, for I do not recoil from it, though it overwhelms me.

Otherwise my dreams have come true. She is the most beautiful and noble woman, with a charming personality and a keen mind. At twenty-seven she is a widow without children, lively yet sometimes melancholy. Despite her aristocratic background, she loves me despite my humble origins. Our love has grown over time; we share a deep emotional connection and avoid the physical aspect of love out of mutual respect. Once she confessed that her first marriage had been her regret, revealing the depth of her feelings. You, Daniel, are a giant among men, and you love a remarkable woman, just as I love my Armande.

Louise — can it be that you intend a life of wedded solitude after sacrificing your first husband? You seem to court future misery. I see you are resolved, and the man who won your heart must possess a rare charm. But have you forgotten your earlier criticisms of young men, when you claimed they had tarnished purity? You are fortunate to believe in happiness, and while I cannot blame you, I must warn you: nature and society conspire against absolute happiness, and I foresee some disaster arising from your boundless joy. I have no objections to your fiancé, but I hope you will describe him to me one day.

I have not told Louis about your marriage; it would only invite his ridicule. I will simply say you wish to keep it secret. You are brave to face winter first, and I will be your discreet friend. Your journey into this emotional wilderness is like an explorer’s perilous expedition, though fortunately only a short distance from Paris. I wish you a safe journey and a swift return.

What has happened to you, my dear? After two years of silence, Renee has a right to worry about Louise. Is this what love does, scattering friendships like ours? You must admit that, while I am devoted to my children, a mother’s love is expansive and does not diminish other affections. I miss your letters and long to see your face.

Regarding our situation, I will be brief. Your last letter contained sharp comments about our political position. You mocked us for keeping our posts in the Audit Department, which Louis owes to Charles X’s favor. But how could we provide for Athenais and Rene on an income of forty thousand livres, of which thirty thousand is entailed? We must live on our salary and save the estate’s income for their future. In twenty years we will have saved enough to provide for both children.

Louis has been raised to the Peerage and is now a grand officer of the Legion of Honor. He has served in the Chamber and was offered an embassy, which I declined for the sake of our children’s education. I will not leave Paris until Rene has finished his schooling. We could not remain strictly loyal to the elder branch of the dynasty without jeopardizing our children’s prospects; a mother must put her children first. In fifteen years Louis will retire on a good pension, and I hope to secure a post for Armand in the Audit Department.

I am a happy mother; my children give me endless joy. Armand is now a day scholar at Henry IV’s school. I had wanted him in public school but could not bear to part with him, so he attends by day and returns home each evening. He is the same charming boy you loved, and his tutor praises his progress. Home education has drawbacks: children raised at home may be exposed too early to the world’s passions and may lack the social skills gained with peers. A mother must shield her children from society’s distractions, as I have tried to do. Now that I am thirty, I feel a duty fulfilled. Armand, once dull, has lately excelled, winning prizes for translation and essays; I felt immense pride when his name was called.

Rene, inspired by Armand, wants to attend school too. The three children can be noisy, but I cherish every moment. I am the only one allowed to care for them; the joys of motherhood outweigh the challenges. I must study their characters closely, for a mother alone discerns her children’s natural aptitudes. Home-bred children often develop ease and tact, compensating for lack of innate ability. I believe Armand will become an admirable public servant, while Rene will be a bold sailor.

Nais is so dear to me I can hardly see her as separate from myself. I want her to be happy and will only give her to a man who truly loves her. Yet I worry about her future and the risk of a poor marriage. Your silence has reminded me of the anguish marriage can bring a mother.

Farewell for now, dear Louise, and please write back.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
My dear Renée, after two years of silence you wonder why I have written so little. Words fail to express my happiness. We bear everything with ease; we are in perfect harmony. There has been no discord in our feelings, and each day brings its own joy. We do not fear monotony; rather we regret that our time together may not suffice for all the expressions of our love. We take more pleasure in one another than on the first day, and every hour reveals new reasons to love each other.

Every evening we speak of visiting Paris, of the new boulevards and the completed Madeleine, but in the morning we linger over breakfast and take a siesta. Gaston gazes at me as if I were a painting, and we both shed tears of joy over our happiness. I still play the mistress, pretending to give less than I receive, and delight in our small coquetries. There is something sweet about passion tempered by tenderness; Gaston, my master, remains a timid suitor at my command.

You asked me to describe him, yet it is impossible to portray the man we love without emotion. Society alters men so the public man can differ from one of deep feeling. A charming suitor may lack what a woman values, while an unrefined man may possess a profound capacity for love. Our ideal is a man whose outward appearance matches his true nature, a grace that cannot be acquired; this ideal haunts every woman's imagination, and in Gaston I have found it.

I never knew love could be so intertwined with youth, talent, and beauty. Gaston is unpretentious, moving with effortless grace. When we walk together our bodies seem to fit perfectly, a single figure gliding through the woods. Our thoughts, desires, and words harmonize. Sometimes we walk in silence, listening to the rain and admiring the sunset, feeling like in silent prayer for our happiness. A timid kiss exchanged in nature feels like an act of worship.

We return home each time more in love than before. Such passion between older married people would scandalize Paris; only in the woods can we express it freely.

Gaston is of average height, well-proportioned, and agile. He has a fair complexion, dark hair, and a striking resemblance to Louis XIII. His honorable poverty has spared him the vices that ruin many young men. His teeth are magnificent, and he has a robust constitution. His blue eyes, full of tenderness for me, can flash with intensity at inspiring thoughts.

He has a remarkably even temperament. I have heard many tales of women’s troubles with men dissatisfied with themselves, but Gaston is free from such burdens. I have learned that marrying an older man can lead to unequal unions, and I urge you not to let Athénaïs marry one.

His smile is a treasure—always fresh, full of unspoken love and gratitude. Our shared experiences have become part of our joy; even the smallest details of nature recall our time together. We share a deep connection, as if we are two editions of the same book. Our wide view of subjects brings constant pleasure, and this unity of thought feels like a pledge of love.

Despite these pleasures, life here is laborious. I do my own room, refusing a hired hand to intrude on my private space. I arrange it so Gaston finds it pleasing. I rise early, bathe in cold water to refresh myself before he wakes, and emerge like a newly opened flower. He appreciates this freshness without knowing the effort behind it.

My daily grooming is handled by my maid, and I dress several times a day for Gaston’s delight. We take great interest in our flowers and horticulture, surrounding our chalet with beauty. Our garden is a source of pride; we eagerly tend our fruits and vegetables and sell the surplus. This abundance is a comfort hard to find in Paris.

To keep Gaston’s mind engaged I encourage him to finish plays begun during harder times. Literary work demands reflection and inspiration, and I assist him so our winter evenings are never dull. Our servants have an easy time; they need no reprimand. They present us as a traveling couple content with their roles.

I strive to fill the role of society for Gaston, ensuring his solitude does not become tedious. I have learned true jealousy—the kind born of love. The mere thought of him ceasing to love me sends shivers through me; I tremble before him.

Alas, I am still without a child. I long for the day our home will be filled with children running through our garden. It pains me to see your lovely children, for my life feels narrower without them. Motherhood expands a woman's heart, while love can be selfish. I envy your happiness and the joy you find in your children.

As I approach thirty, I feel the limits of my beauty closing in. Though still attractive, I worry about aging while Gaston remains young. I often ask him to promise he will tell me if his love ever fades, and he swears it will not, finding the idea absurd.

Goodbye, dear friend. Shall we let years pass without writing again? Happiness can be monotonous—that may be why Dante seems greater in Paradise than in the Inferno. I am not Dante, only your friend, and I do not wish to bore you. Write to me—your children supply endless stories—while I... No more of this. A thousand loves.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
My dear Louise, I have read your letter many times; its tone is childish. You forget that pure love belongs to nature and is warped by civilization. Society built the family as the basis of organized life at the cost of individual desire. A married man devotes himself to his wife, who benefits from his labor.

You are on the wrong path: the dream of being adored suits the young, not a mature woman. Vanity may savor adoration, but true fulfillment lies in the life of wife and mother. Return to Paris; fear losing ordinary happiness more than hardship.

Life’s simple pleasures—bread, air, silence—sustain us. Passionate love exhausts; the affections of motherhood endure and reward. I chose self‑sacrifice and would choose it again. Your life, masked by romance, is rooted in selfishness; I plead with you as I watch your happiness being tested.

Life seeks balance; excess brings pain or fleeting pleasure. You are ill because you cling to a passion a marriage should steady. The glory of home is calmness and shared life, which you overlook. You are more passionate than tender; you would be both wife and mistress, undermining marriage’s sanctity.

If you could understand a mother’s sweet self‑discipline. My proud nature softened into gentle melancholy, and motherhood’s joys rewarded me. I fear your life may darken. I had hoped time would restore reason, but your appetite for love seems insatiable. Farewell; I despair that my letter, meant to reconcile you with society, only exposed what I see as selfishness.

---

Renee, calamity has struck—no thunderbolt like this for poor Louise. Doubt is my calamity; certainty would be death. The day before yesterday, after dressing, I looked for Gaston to walk and found him gone. In the stable his mare Fedelta was in a lather; it suggested he’d been to Paris without telling me. Suspicion seized me.

When Gaston returned he carried me to the parlor; I would not speak and fled to my room to weep. He waited patiently. I questioned the mud on Fedelta’s legs; he teased, and though I saw no outward disloyalty, I remained unconvinced.

We spent the day together; my heart clung to him. I hated the mare for the distress she caused. I asked if I bored him; he reassured me. I suggested selling Fedelta; his answer made me feel inadequate. Alone, I was consumed by despair.

The next morning I told him to take Fedelta to Paris; my tone betrayed turmoil. He urged trust; I tried to suppress suspicion. He left, and I watched him go with a sense of doom.

When he returned late with a whip as a gift I felt it only distracted from something deeper. I later learned he bought it from Verdier, which confirmed my fears. I resolved to watch him while staying silent.

Days passed; Gaston changed, working harder—seeming to need money. His drawer lay empty; he received letters he quickly tore up. Jealousy consumed me; I followed him to the post office and learned of a letter from Nathan announcing a play’s acceptance.

Despite his success, I couldn’t shake that he hid something. The conviction that he had a rival drove me to find the truth.

After investigation I learned Gaston visited a woman in Paris; I followed and discovered she was his brother’s widow, legally married. The revelation shattered me: he had been providing for her and her children. I returned tormented by thoughts of betrayal and jealousy.

Despite the pain, my love for Gaston remained. I struggled with his past and wanted to confront him about his feelings for the widow, but feared the truth would destroy me. I even pondered my own end, unable to bear the suffering.

---

My dear Louise, I write urgently; your last letter alarmed me. I confided in Louis to save you. Though our methods may seem extreme, the outcome is satisfactory. I even involved the police, kept secret among us.

Gaston is not what you feared. His brother Louis died in Calcutta, leaving a widow and two children. Gaston has secretly supported them, using his earnings for his sister‑in‑law and her sons. The woman you mistook for a rival is a mother in need; Gaston acted from kindness and duty.

You should love him more; he has shown sensitivity and care. Welcome the widow and her children into your life—perhaps adopt the boys. This revelation should bring you peace, not despair.

Ah, my dear friend, what can I say? It is too late for me. My actions have sealed my fate. Tormented by love for Gaston, I cannot bear losing him. I am filled with despair and long for relief. Come, dear friend, and witness my decline.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
My love — Take the children to Provence without me; I must stay with Louise, who has only a few days left. I cannot leave her or her husband, for whom I am anxious.

You know the letter that sent me to Ville d'Avray; I came gathering doctors along the way and have kept watch every night for two weeks beside my dear friend. At first Louise and Gaston seemed charming and happy, but it was a façade. She whispered, "He must not know; I am dying." Her hand burned and her cheeks flushed. I had kept doctors in reserve until needed.

"Leave us for a little," she told Gaston; when we were alone she collapsed into my arms and wept. I urged her to tell me. "I have brought the best surgeon and physician from the hospital, along with Bianchon," I said. "Ah! Have them at once if they can save me! The passion that drove me to death now cries for life!" she answered. When I asked what she had done, she confessed, "I have brought myself to the last stage of consumption in just a few days." She had worked herself into profuse night sweats, then lain by the lake in the dew. Gaston thought it a cold, but she knew she was dying.

I sent Gaston to Paris and fetched the doctors. After consultation none gave hope; her strong constitution had delayed matters, but a few days of folly had made her case desperate. I was stunned; I had lived in Louise as much as my own life. Crushed, I could not escort the doctors out. Then Louise's clear voice startled me: "So there is no hope for me!"

She made me rise and took me to her drawing-room. "Stay with me to the end; I won't have sad faces around me. I must keep the truth from him. I know I have the strength to do it. I am full of youth and spirit, and can die standing! I have no regrets. I am dying as I wished, still young and beautiful, in the perfection of my womanhood."

She admitted that, for Gaston, she might have made his life miserable. "My jealousy has already wounded him. When my suspicions met indifference, that would have been my death. I have had my share of life. There are people who have lived sixty years without two years of real life, while my thirty years are doubled by the intensity of my love." She judged the end happy for them both — she had loved deeply and been loved — but mourned for me: "You lose a loving sister, and that is a loss that cannot be repaired. You alone have the right to mourn my death."

More gravely, she drew a lesson: her fate showed that marriage cannot rest on passion alone. "Your life is noble," she said to me, praising my steady affection for my husband, while confessing her own passionate eagerness had diminished. Twice she had gone astray, and twice Death had reached for her happiness — first taking the noblest of men, now taking her from her poet husband with all his beauty and grace. "I would not complain. I have known two men of nobility—one in mind, the other in form. I die adored—what more could I wish? Perhaps time to draw near to God, whom I may have thought too little of. My spirit will fly towards Him, full of love, praying to be reunited with the two who made my life a heaven."

She warned that her example might tempt others to expect impossible felicities, and dismissed the fuss about women's emancipation: women are weak and should yield their wills to their husbands; the clamour was ridiculous. She spoke gently and gravely, making difficult truths sound pretty.

Soon Gaston returned with his sister-in-law, the children, and the English nurse he had fetched from Paris. "Here are the pretty instruments of my torture," she said when her nephews came, noting their likeness to their uncle. She played hostess to Mme. Gaston and insisted the chalet be hers. I wrote to the Duc and Duchesse de Chaulieu, the Duc de Rhetore, the Duc de Lenoncourt-Givry, and to Madeleine. The next day Louise, exhausted, could not go out; she only rose for dinner. Madeleine de Lenoncourt, her brothers, and her mother arrived. The coolness caused by Louise's second marriage faded; her father and brothers visited daily, and duchesses spent evenings at the chalet. Death unites as well as separates; it silenced petty feelings.

Louise remained charming, graceful, witty, and tender to the last, keeping perfect tact and lavishing her brilliant mind on us—one of Paris's queens to the end. "I want to look well even in my coffin," she said, smiling, as she lay on the bed where she would linger for a fortnight. Her room lacked the usual sick-chamber air; medicines and nursing supplies were hidden. "Is not my deathbed pretty?" she asked the priest who came to confess her.

We watched her like misers. The anxiety and hard truths had prepared Gaston for the worst. He is brave, but the blow struck deep; I would not be surprised if he followed her soon. Walking by the lake, he said simply, "I must be a father to those two children," pointing to his sister-in-law with the boys. "But I want your promise that you will be a second mother to them and persuade your husband to accept the role of guardian." He wore smiles for Louise, but I saw through the façade; his courage matched hers. Louise wished to see her godson, but I preferred he remain in Provence; she might wish to remember him generously, putting me in a difficult position.

August 25th (her birthday). Yesterday evening Louise was briefly delirious, but even her babble was beautiful—proof that the madness of gifted people differs from that of fools. In a faint voice she sang Italian airs from I Puritani, La Sonnambula, Moïse. We stood around her bed in silence; no one, not even the Duc de Rhetore, had dry eyes, as we felt her soul departing. She could no longer see us, yet the charm lingered in the melody, sweet and otherworldly.

During the night the death agony began. At seven in the morning I raised her from bed. A flicker of strength returned; she wished to sit by the window and asked for Gaston's hand. Then, my love, the sweetest spirit we shall ever see on this earth departed, leaving us with an empty shell. The last sacrament had been administered the evening before, without Gaston's knowledge—he had been sleeping during the ceremony. After she was set at the window, she asked me to read the De Profundis in French, facing the lovely scene she had created. She repeated the words after me and pressed her husband's hands.

August 26th. My heart is broken. I have just seen her in her winding-sheet; her face is pale with purple shadows. Oh! I want my children! Bring me my children!

DEDICATION

To Madame la Comtesse Bolognini.

Madame, recalling our Milan conversation, I dedicate this work to you, hoping you will shelter it with your name as you once protected a beloved writer’s tales. You have a daughter, Eugénie, whose beauty and intelligence promise a freer life than the rigid fate of the Eugénie in these pages. Though Frenchmen are deemed fickle, I am Italian in constancy and memory: the name “Eugénie” brings Milan’s warmth, and I regard you as a distant, treasured masterpiece.

Your devoted servant,
De Balzac.

A DAUGHTER OF EVE

CHAPTER I. THE TWO MARIES

In a blue-velvet boudoir on rue Neuve-des-Mathurins two young women weep by the fire. The room belongs to Ferdinand du Tillet, a wealthy banker who bought social standing by marriage but lacks aristocratic ease. Madame du Tillet holds her sister Madame Félix de Vandenesse’s hand as they confide. Raised by a strict, pious mother and married without youthful freedom, they learned only religion and needlework—ignorant of adult passions—so marriage, once imagined as escape, reveals harsh realities.

CHAPTER II. A CONFIDENCE BETWEEN SISTERS

Madame de Vandenesse, distraught by marital troubles, confesses despair over a husband indifferent to her inner life. Madame du Tillet, equally helpless, describes her own cold domesticity. Both admit a craving for intense feeling and seek consolation in each other.

CHAPTER III. THE HISTORY OF A FORTUNATE WOMAN

Félix de Vandenesse, of noble lineage, married hoping to recreate an idealized love. But the duties and restraints of his class cool their relationship. Though kind and attentive, he cannot provide the romantic intensity his wife desires; monotony breeds longing and the temptation to look beyond marriage.

CHAPTER IV. A CELEBRATED MAN

Raoul Nathan, an ambitious literary figure, enters society with talent, audacity, and caustic wit. His unconventional charm unsettles and attracts. At Madame de Montcornet’s salon he catches Madame de Vandenesse’s attention; their exchanges hint at a growing, if unspoken, attraction. Nathan, amid career strain and private troubles, sees her as a possible muse, though social bounds bar any open liaison.

CHAPTER V. FLORINE

Florine, a gifted actress and Nathan’s long-term companion, combines affection with practicality: she sustains him and relies on him for protection and status. As Nathan’s admiration for the countess grows, Florine becomes insecure and jealous.

CHAPTER VI. ROMANTIC LOVE

Nathan and Madame de Vandenesse bond over art and literature; their meetings grow intimate, bringing exhilaration and peril. She is torn—guilty yet enticed—between loyalty to her husband and the lure of a passionate life, aware that yielding risks social ruin.

CHAPTER VII. SUICIDE

Crushed by setbacks, Nathan contemplates suicide. A chance encounter with Madame de Vandenesse revives his will to live; seeing his despair, she vows to protect and encourage him, countering his darkest impulses.

CHAPTER VIII. A LOVER SAVED AND LOST

She becomes more involved, balancing protection with her own feelings. Their secrecy, social risk, and Florine’s intensifying jealousy complicate matters. Nathan is torn between Florine’s practical devotion and the countess’s inspiring influence.

CHAPTER IX. THE HUSBAND’S TRIUMPH

Félix, sensing his wife’s attachment, moves to preserve their marriage. Determined to protect her and their honor, he seeks the truth. Love, ambition, loyalty, and expectation entwine, setting the stage for a dramatic clash between desire and duty.

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

On a Sunday morning in April 1813 a cabriolet stopped before the Tuileries carrying an anxious, aged father and his pretty daughter Julie. Proud of the attention she drew, she persuaded him to stay for the military review commanded by Napoleon—the last before a disastrous campaign. An officer recognized Julie and led them to a good vantage. As the Old Guard prepared and Napoleon appeared to cheers, Julie was seized by admiration but soon noticed a young officer on the Emperor’s staff, Colonel Victor d’Aiglemont. Attracted and jealous, she felt a sudden pull toward him even as her father’s anxiety reminded her of their precarious situation. When the review ended he warned her that youthful dreams could bring sorrow and cautioned against a man like Victor, whom he considered unworthy. Despite this, Julie’s attachment to Victor grew.

Nearly a year later, in March 1814, Julie and Victor were traveling together. The once-vibrant girl had become diminished; her health and spirits suffered as Victor, now a colonel, grew absorbed in military duties and the shifting politics after Napoleon’s fall. Isolated and craving affection, Julie’s need intensified while Victor’s attention waned. During their travels they encountered an Englishman, Arthur, detained in France. Arthur’s quiet admiration and steadiness contrasted with Victor’s indifference, and Julie felt an unexpected kinship with him. Torn between marital duty and desire, she wrestled with feelings that magnified her inner conflict.

With Napoleon’s defeat the political landscape changed and Victor’s ambitions shifted—he embraced the new regime and became more self-interested. When his infidelities became apparent, Julie’s heartbreak deepened; she saw his selfishness and felt betrayed. Desperate for solace, she turned toward Arthur despite the moral and social consequences. Trapped in a loveless marriage and isolated, she resolved not to remain passive and to defy societal expectations to reclaim some happiness. The narrative traces love, sacrifice, duty, and the search for fulfillment amid social upheaval.

Between the Seine and the Loing lies a flat, dreary country edged by the Forest of Fontainebleau, its monotonous horizon breeding melancholy. At its center stands the old château of Saint-Lange, once a sign of wealth now diminished. In late 1820 a woman known in Paris for her beauty and wit arrived at Saint-Lange, startling villagers unused to gentry. The Marquise d’Aiglemont’s frailty and withdrawal prompted gossip: some blamed her husband’s gambling in Spain and feared the estate would be sold, leading locals to eye their own interests.

The Marquise lived in isolation, rarely leaving her room and only briefly dining with her daughter. At twenty-six she carried a profound, unacknowledged grief that had extinguished maternal instinct and made her feel like a ghost. Her anguish sprang from a lost love—a man who had died to preserve her honor—and from social demands that forbade open mourning. Youth and beauty meant little without the true love she had imagined; her respectable marriage provided no emotional fulfillment. She saw her daughter Hélène not as an object of maternal love but as a living reminder of the lost man; duty, not affection, defined their relationship.

One day the village priest, M. le Curé, visited and, sensing her suffering, spoke of the universality of grief and the sustaining power of faith. Initially dismissive, she was moved by his story of a father who had lost all his sons, which placed her sorrow in a broader context and offered unexpected solace. Gradually she confided in the priest. He urged her to find strength in duty, but she continued to wrestle with bitterness toward rigid social constraints. Spring work on the estate brought temporary reprieve; by October she left Saint-Lange with some regained vitality, avoiding the curé as if to move forward without the château’s painful memories. Her quiet struggle—between public expectation and private desire, duty and identity—reveals the costs of rigid social structures and the burdens placed on women.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
At Madame Firmiani’s ball, M. Charles de Vandenesse, a promising young diplomat about to leave Paris for the Congress of Laybach, prepared to depart a city he found largely superficial. Near thirty and philosophically detached, he prized advancement over romance. Madame Firmiani introduced him to the enigmatic Marquise d’Aiglemont, a woman of about thirty whose delicate beauty and melancholy stood out against the surrounding frivolity. Vandenesse saw in her sorrow unfulfilled dreams and the influence she had used to secure a complacent, socially successful husband.

Their conversation shifted from light banter to earnest talk of feeling and love, creating a connection that both excited and unsettled him. He sought another meeting; thereafter she occupied his thoughts. Their encounters blurred friendship and unspoken desire, forcing him to confront affection for a married woman. The Marquise felt drawn to him but was held by loyalty to a deceased lover’s memory and by society’s strictures. As intimacy deepened, obstacles multiplied—her past, public opinion, and his ambitions—producing moments of joy and torment. A tender kiss marked a turning point, abruptly interrupted by her husband’s intrusion. Their bond demonstrated love’s power to cross boundaries yet remained weighed down by duty and unfulfilled longing.

Between the Barrière d’Italie and the Barrière de la Santé, the boulevard toward the Jardin des Plantes yields a stirring view of Paris: a tree-lined avenue descending into a valley where factories and the brown Bièvre meet green fields; the squalor of Faubourg Saint-Marceau set against the domes of the Panthéon and Val‑de‑Grâce; glimpses of the observatory and the Invalides’ lantern. To the right, the canal Saint‑Martin runs beneath lime trees and granaries; beyond, the smoke-streaked slopes of Belleville rise toward the clouds. When sunlight floods the quarter, tiles gleam and foliage brighten, and the city seems momentarily at peace.

One morning, brooding over wealthy travelers who mocked France, the narrator witnessed a sudden family scene: a neatly dressed young mother, a handsome man, a charming little boy, and a dark-haired, solemn girl of seven or eight who stood apart—plainly dressed, scowling, thoughtful. As the couple played, the girl’s indifference turned cruel: when the boy approached with a toy she pushed him; he stumbled into the river and vanished beneath murky water. The narrator rushed to help but was too late. The mother’s frantic grief and the terrible certainty that the daughter’s impulsive act would forever shadow the family left an indelible mark.

Years later, the Marquis de Vandenesse, now mourning his father, hosted a dinner disturbed by a long‑winded notary who monopolized conversation. The husband returned with the children; the little girl’s eyes were red from crying. The notary’s thoughtless levity deepened the tension; the Marquise, overwhelmed, sent her daughter away while the Marquis demanded the notary leave. Later the notary blamed aristocratic whims; his wife warned him that his inability to read social nuance and human pain would cause more misunderstandings.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
A former orderly of Napoleon, known as the General or the Marquis, had spent a spring at Versailles and, after amassing a fortune during the Restoration, taken a country house near Montreuil to find quiet near Paris. His retreat had broad grounds; its entrance still faced the street, a reminder of a once-quiet road.

One winter evening the family was alone: the servants had gone to Versailles for a wedding and, with Christmas revelers still abroad, the General grew uneasy when it struck eleven and they had not returned. Outside lay a deep silence broken only by wind and distant footsteps. Inside the scene was cozy: the rugged soldier by the fire watched his youngest resist being undressed to keep playing with his sister; laughter rose while the Marquise looked on. At a nearby table their thirteen-year-old read the Arabian Nights and Hélène, striking and thoughtful, worked at a tapestry. The children’s play contrasted with the older siblings’ more serious reflections, and the General took simple pleasure in the warmth of home.

Later hurried knocks announced a breathless, bloodied man who begged refuge, secrecy, and water, saying he had been in a duel. Despite misgivings the General felt compelled to hide him in a secret room. The Marquise suspected something and pressed her husband, who insisted on secrecy to protect the family. Hélène, curious and uneasy, overheard and peered at the locked door; there she saw the wounded yet magnetic stranger and their eyes met. When gendarmes later sought the man, the General’s protectiveness surged, forcing him to confront the moral complexity and fragility of his household’s happiness.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
On a June day in 1844 the Marquise d’Aiglemont, about fifty, walked in her daughter’s Paris garden, pausing by the windows to gaze at the dome of the Invalides and her own grounds. The neighborhood was quiet; the aristocracy was only beginning to awake.

Having lost her two sons, Gustave and Abel, she had given her fortune to her daughter, the Comtesse Moïna de Saint-Héreen. Her devotion was profound, leaving only faint memories of her dead children. Society dismissed the gesture as superstition and largely ignored both her sacrifice and her coolness toward her daughter-in-law.

Despite the gift the Marquise was often alone while Moïna plunged into society. Her love was unmistakable but shaded by sorrow; she felt eclipsed by Moïna’s youthful charm. She knew Moïna flirted with Alfred de Vandenesse, whom she regarded with disdain, and feared eventual heartbreak. With the Comte away for months, her warnings were brushed aside.

Years of small hurts signaled Moïna’s growing distance. The Marquise sought conversation and met indifference; the Countess’s flippant replies stung. Confronted, Moïna’s dismissiveness made the Marquise realize her daughter had lost respect.

Overcome by grief and a sense of irrevocable estrangement, she collapsed on a bench. Moïna, not grasping the moment’s gravity, thought a few gestures could repair things. The Marquise was carried inside, lifeless; the physician arrived too late. In her last moments she managed a forgiving smile, while Moïna stood hollow with late regret.

############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
