In spring 1822 Parisian doctors sent M. le Baron Gaston de Nueil to Lower Normandy to recuperate from an inflammatory illness attributed to overwork or excess. Seeking rest, a light diet, and fresh air, he settled in Bayeux with welcoming relatives.

After a few evenings with his cousin, Mme. de Sainte‑Severe, Gaston met the local elite, embodiments of provincial French types. One was an old, impoverished noble family: a patriarch, a resolute sportsman contemptuous of modernity and the new political order; a loud, domineering wife convinced their daughters’ prospects depended on the family name. They clung to obsolete customs—nobility without wealth or influence.

Opposite them stood a wealthier, more recent family attuned to Parisian trends. The wife was a society woman slightly behind the latest fashions; the husband, a Departmental Council member, handled finances shrewdly and trafficked in political gossip. Their Royalism was lukewarm; they engaged with modern life while preserving ancestral ties.

The local bishop, formerly Vicar‑General, mediated between factions—respected for his office though often reminded of plebeian origins. Retired officers and their families preserved a veneer of aristocracy, focused on local affairs. Women dressed as if for court yet lived by routine. A few wealthy townspeople had infiltrated the circle and were viewed skeptically, while ecclesiastics added wit and a bourgeois element to otherwise staid gatherings.

Overall the society was marked by antiquated beliefs and resistance to change: conversation recycled familiar themes, judgments were fixed, intellectual life stagnated, and critics were dismissed.

Before his arrival Gaston had been appraised: at twenty‑three he was promising, with eighteen thousand livres from land in the Auge and a father bargaining for more woodland; omitted was that his elder brother was consumptive and likely dying. Initially Gaston amused himself sketching their quirks, but weeks of monotony left him weary. Ennui and mounting discontent dimmed memories of Parisian intellectual life, threatening to make him one more fossil among the fossils.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
One evening Gaston de Nueil sat between a dowager and a vicar-general in a gray-paneled drawing room amid whist players. After dinner he noted provincial habits—the worn cards, threadbare cloths and indifference to luxury—which deepened his Parisian distance.

A shy young lady with red hands caught his eye, but a remark about Mme. de Beauseant struck him like a fine passage in an opera. An elderly guest said the Vicomtesse had declined an invitation as unwell; conversation revealed her liaison with M. d’Ajuda-Pinto and retreat to Courcelles. Noble, she had been shunned after separating from her husband—an exile that intrigued him.

Fascinated, Gaston imagined Mme. de Beauseant as a hopeful contrast to provincial stagnation. Mindful of rank, he spent the morning near Courcelles and, fearing a direct letter would be refused, asked the Marquis de Champignelles to arrange an introduction. The Marquis’s message met resistance but piqued her curiosity; she agreed to receive him. Nervous, Gaston was announced at Courcelles and entered.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
A great fire burned on the hearth despite mild weather, casting a soft light over a young woman in a high‑backed bergère. The Vicomtesse, with graceful movements, stirred the coals, picked up a fallen glove, and motioned to a chair for M. de Nueil. Her charm and poised, fastidious carelessness contrasted with the automata he had met during two months’ exile in Normandy; in her presence he felt transported back to Paris and its sophistication.

Mme. de Beauseant had fair hair, dark eyes, and a pale complexion that gave her a regal, almost fallen‑angel air; her hair, arranged in a crown of plaits, intensified that bearing. Beneath her beauty lay an underlying sadness—traces of past unhappiness and lost love. Alone in her silent salon she seemed cut off from the world, haunted by memories of a once‑vibrant youth now emptied. M. de Nueil felt humbled and captivated by this blend of beauty, nobility, and misfortune.

When she acknowledged him he felt the weight of his deception. He had come to deliver a message from M. de Champignelles, but the awareness of his falsehood made him flush. Summoning his courage he confessed, “Madame, I do not deserve the happiness of seeing you. I have deceived you basely.” Instead of mercy she rang for a servant to escort him out; her manner shifted from gracious to icy.

As he left he realized that if he went away now he would never return and that the Vicomtesse would think him a fool. He hesitated, then turned back on impulse, saying he had forgotten something; the servant followed unaware. “Jacques lighted me to the door,” he said with a half‑sad smile, which softened Mme. de Beauseant’s demeanor. She invited him to sit; he did so eagerly. She felt a thrill at his pleasure, grateful for what she took as his comprehension of her complex emotions.

He admitted his blunder and claimed much blame. She interrupted playfully then darkened, reflecting on how her life had changed. “I cannot do as I like; I am obliged to think,” she said, accusing him of curiosity, disrespect, and the risk of impropriety, and forbidding his return. M. de Nueil owned his fault, blaming youthful enthusiasm and exile’s weariness for idealizing her and committing a foolish intrusion for which he begged indulgence.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
An impassioned voice broke Mme. de Beauseant’s solitary calm; she was struck by its sincerity and by M. de Nueil’s noble countenance. Gaston, young and ideal—without obvious ambition or vanity—seemed to her a purity resisting society’s corruptions. Both were drawn to one another, each reflecting while concealing much. He perceived in the Vicomtesse not only beauty but depth and goodness; she recognized in him a youth whose troubles revealed an incorruptible heart.

Gaston’s manner was instinctive; Mme. de Beauseant’s deliberate—she tested him with contrary opinions. Her wit and grace soon put him at ease; he exclaimed, “Oh! madame, how could any man have left you?” The remark revived her sense of innocence, condemned the world that had wronged her, and offered an absolution that lifted her above old shame.

She checked the hour and, smiling, acknowledged the fleeting nature of their encounter. Gaston wished to prolong it; she insisted on preserving her solitary life, fearing a new connection would tarnish her reputation. Candidly she spoke of a forsaken love and the lessons solitude had taught her; her eloquence and beauty enthralled Gaston, who longed to declare himself but feared rejection. Torn between admiration and dread of appearing foolish, he finally confessed his love and offered to heal her old wounds. Mme. de Beauseant, moved yet resolute, reminded him they must remain strangers and spoke of the pain of being unable to follow a man through life’s changes.

Gaston left unsettled, unsure whether her charm was genuine or coquetry. Diffident at twenty-three, he wrote faltering letters, then, to avoid being seen, returned to Courcelles at night and penned a heartfelt plea laying bare his feelings. Awaiting her response, doubts gnawed at him; fearing ridicule, he sought distraction and confronted the harshness of unrequited longing.

Mme. de Beauseant’s reply, sealed with the arms of Burgundy, shut him away. She refused his advances, trying to spare him a blunt rebuff by being candid: youthful passion would not move her. Though she cared for him, she did not share his love and would not yield to blind impulse. Nearly thirty while he was just over twenty, she feared vows made lightly would become burdens and that he might one day resent the sacrifices she might require; she would rather die than endure desertion. Her consolation lay in God, not earthly ties; she could offer only the friendship of an older woman, untainted by passion. For both their sakes she urged him to remain in the country and not wreck his life for an illusion.

Gaston answered with fervor: if he could cease to love her and accept the ordinary life she suggested, he would deserve his fate; his oath of fidelity could be absolved only by death, and if she feared his remorse she might take his life. When a messenger reported delivering Mme. la Vicomtesse’s reply as she was about to depart in a carriage, Gaston realized she was leaving and followed her to Geneva, convinced that if she truly cared she would seek the freedom of Switzerland.

Mme. de Beauseant had rented a small house by the lake. One summer evening Gaston surprised her; she dropped her book and embraced him as he explained he had followed her. For three years they lived in the villa on Lake Geneva, alone and apparently in dreamlike happiness: a simple home, beautiful views, quiet days cherished together.

Weighty obligations soon called Gaston back after the deaths of his father and brother. They refrained from selling the villa and instead bought the family house; Mme. de Beauseant sold her property to buy an estate near Manerville adjoining Gaston’s lands. His mother took the family house at Manerville, and they resumed the secluded happiness they had known. For nine years they enjoyed an almost indescribable bliss.

Yet a legal obstacle remained: Mme. de Beauseant’s husband, the Marquis, was still alive, so any lawful union was impossible. The situation was precarious. Gaston’s mother, the dowager comtesse, would never accept Mme. de Beauseant; the Marquise feared the dowager’s influence might one day pull Gaston away. Though tempted to sell her estate and return to Geneva, Mme. de Beauseant could not bring herself to act in a way that might betray distrust.

The nearby arrival of a young heiress, Mlle. de la Rodiere, and Gaston’s meeting with her disturbed the fragile peace. Mme. de Beauseant replied with a letter revealing her inner turmoil and pride. She confessed she had not known love until she met him and that for nine years she had felt no jealousy and had experienced infinite happiness. But now she anguished at the thought that, at forty, she might be a burden to his future: he was thirty and might wish a marriage to bring children and security. She recoiled at the idea his devotion could be motivated by pity rather than desire.

She admitted sensing his hesitation and being unable to hide her tears, yet she had not reproached him. Too proud to bind his future to her own fading life, she urged him to be frank: if he still loved her, he should come after reading the letter and she would love him more than ever; if he sought freedom, she would not display her suffering, because she loved him enough to put his happiness first.

When Mme. de Beauseant learned her letter had reached Gaston, she was overwhelmed and stunned into numbness. Reading her words, M. de Nueil found himself in a painful dilemma—a crisis where passion, duty, pride, and the prospect of a conventional future collided.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Gaston was near yielding to his mother’s urgings to marry Mlle. de la Rodiere, a plain young woman with a 40,000‑livre dowry. Mme. de Nueil praised the respectability of such a match, suggesting future land incomes could raise his take to 80,000 livres, and argued that if Mme. de Beauseant truly loved him she would encourage his marriage. Torn between passion and duty, Gaston felt obliged to choose what he termed “a man’s life,” though he knew it would wound the Marquise.

A letter from Mme. de Beauseant precipitated his decision: he resolved to end their liaison and marry. He planned to spare the Marquise by wedding Mlle. de la Rodiere—reserving later concessions if necessary to honor Mme. de Beauseant’s wishes. He meant to send a composed note, but feared that a perceptive woman might be devastated by mere words.

When Jacques brought that note, Mme. de Beauseant trembled. If he did not come in person she felt all was lost; yet a woman in love endures much. As she read, an inner cry rose—“He lies!”—and she judged the message calculated, not sincere. In despair she tore the letter and cast it into the fire, feeling betrayed and abandoned.

M. de Nueil fetched Jacques, who soon returned with a message beginning, “Madame la Marquise has left the chateau.” M. de Nueil opened it and instead found his own letter from nine years earlier, in which he had sworn eternal fidelity to Mme. de Beauseant; at the bottom Claire de Bourgogne had written, “Monsieur, you are free.” Stunned by the reminder of past promises and by what seemed like her renunciation, he went to his mother and, within three weeks, married Mlle. Stephanie de la Rodiere.

Had the tale ended there it might seem merely domestic, but its notoriety speaks to those who know love’s joys and losses. After their parting Mme. de Beauseant never left Valleroy. She lived in seclusion, rarely seen except by servants, ceased to be the companion she had been, and attended mass daily; her withdrawal silently testified to the rupture.

Soon after his marriage, Gaston sank into desolate indifference that onlookers variously read as contentment or melancholy; his mother insisted he was happy. Mme. Gaston de Nueil was amiable but shallow; society quickly suspected her of being with child. Yet two months later Gaston grew increasingly pensive, and his melancholy deepened.

After seven months a seemingly trivial incident revealed his turmoil. Returning from a hunt, he crossed Mme. de Beauseant’s park and, by habit, asked Jacques whether she still enjoyed game. Jacques confirmed, and Gaston paid him to set aside whatever he shot for her. For days he returned with an empty bag while Jacques discreetly delivered the game to the Marquise—tender and humiliating, showing a man unable to live without the small intimacies of his former passion.

Eventually Gaston dared to write Mme. de Beauseant, but the letter was returned unopened. One evening, while his wife played the piano, he rushed to the Marquise’s room and found her mute, emptied of hope; when she threatened to throw herself from the window rather than be approached, he fled terrified. Back home he wrote another letter, insisting it was a matter of life and death, and sent it with his servant. An hour later the servant returned with the unopened note. In despair, Gaston retired to another room and shot himself.

The story argues that love requires time and care; abandoning such love for a loveless, conventional marriage breeds either despair or a cold, material life. Mme. de Beauseant may not have foreseen that her friend’s anguish would lead to such an extreme act. She may have thought herself the only sufferer, yet she rightly refused humiliation: a wife may compromise for social reasons, but a mistress cannot submit to degradation, for the purity of her passion demands respect.

ANGOULEME, September 1832.

ADDENDUM: Characters appearing elsewhere in the Human Comedy include Beauseant (Marquis and Comte), Marquise de Beauseant, the Vicomte and Vicomtesse de Beauseant, the Champignelles, Jacques the butler, and Nueil, Gaston de.

################################################

La Grenadiere is a quaint house on the right bank of the Loire, a mile downstream from the bridge of Tours. The broad river, spotted with green islands, runs between cliffs topped with white stone country houses, gardens and vineyards; generations have terraced the slopes to grow warm-climate fruit. A church spire marks nearby Saint-Cyr, and further on the Choisille joins the Loire.

Halfway up the hillside, a hundred paces from the church, stands La Grenadiere, an old Touraine house two or three centuries old. It is reached by a fissure in the rock whose steps descend to the “dike,” the embankment serving as the Paris–Nantes causeway. At the top a gate opens onto a narrow path between terraces; trees partly conceal the house until the river view widens.

A crumbling Gothic archway gives into a small garden hewn from the highest terrace, with a wooden summer-house draped in jessamine and honeysuckle. The house perches above vine-covered steps; an arched doorway leads to cellars carved into the rock. Trellised vines and pomegranate trees—the source of its name—surround it. The front shows two large windows flanking a rustic door, three dormers set in a steep slate roof, and yellowwashed walls with green doors and shutters.

Inside, a crooked staircase rises to a lobby. To the right, a large dining room with white tile floors; to the left, a sitting room with saffron-coloured walls and exposed walnut rafters. The first floor holds two whitewashed bedrooms with simple stone chimneypieces. Most openings face south, save one toward the vineyard. A small timber-framed kitchen, a later addition, contains a rustic pump and well, suggesting La Grenadiere began as a vintner’s retreat for townsfolk of Tours who came for picnics or the grape harvest.

The property includes two acres of vineyard with a steep slope at the back. A vinedresser’s cottage leans against the western gable; stone walls and espaliers enclose the land while neglected corners host fig trees, wildflowers and strawberries.

Modest yet dignified, La Grenadiere is rich in fruit and fragrance—grapes, figs, peaches and melons—with Spanish broom and Italian oleanders adding an exotic note. The Loire flows below; evening breezes bring a sea-scent and changing light reveals the south bank, Tours and hills dotted with châteaux. Owned by a prosperous burgess of Tours, it is a peaceful retreat for lovers and dreamers, where the river’s sounds invite quiet contemplation among perennial flowers and fruit.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
If an Englishman rents La Grenadiere for the summer, he pays a thousand francs for six months, excluding vineyard produce; orchard fruit doubles the rent, and the vintage doubles it again. Yet what price its stone staircase, triple terrace, two acres of vineyard, flowering roses, well-head and the view over three valleys of Touraine and cathedral towers? Healthy and beautiful, the house has not been on the market since a 1690 sale for forty thousand francs and has remained a cherished family possession.

In a bright Restoration spring, Mme. Willemsens arrived in Tours with her housekeeper and two children, thirteen and eight, and took La Grenadiere—perhaps for its convenient distance from town. She made the drawing-room her bedroom, gave the children the two rooms above, and had the housekeeper sleep in a closet. The dining-room served as sitting and drawing room, plainly furnished in walnut; the house’s charm lay in neatness and harmony rather than luxury.

Mme. Willemsens resisted easy classification. Her plain dress suggested modesty, her polished manners higher breeding, and her reserve provoked local curiosity. Tall and slender, with delicate features and dark eyes that sometimes betrayed secret anguish and premature wrinkles, she always wore a long black gown with a silk ribbon at the waist and an English-style bonnet—signs of mourning. Her health seemed fragile; she often walked with the children to the bridge of Tours for fresh air and sunsets.

She visited Tours only twice—once to inquire about the best teachers and once to arrange lessons. Her rare appearances on the bridge interested townspeople, but little was known of her rank. The owner of La Grenadiere said she signed the lease as Augusta Willemsens, Countess of Brandon, a hint at her husband’s name; the remark stayed within the business community.

To the local elite she remained a mystery. Her natural manners and sweet voice charmed, but her sadness and beauty made her untouchable. Several young men fell in love yet were kept away by timidity; she burned all letters she received, underscoring her desire for solitude.

The three tutors admired the bond between Mme. Willemsens and her sons. The boys, like their mother, drew envy from local mothers. Louis-Gaston, the elder, had dark hair and a robust demeanour; Marie-Gaston, the younger, had almost golden hair and a delicate appearance. Their contrasting looks matched temperaments—Louis protective of his brother. Well-behaved and attentive, they rose early, prayed and observed a strict morning routine. Their interactions with their mother were affectionate and intuitive; she fostered a nurturing home while hiding small worries. Louis watched her closely, and their life remained largely untouched by outside influence.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Mme. Willemsens dressed at an early breakfast, amused the children, and sat through lessons from ten to three, with a midday lunch in the shared summer-house. Afterwards the children played while she rested on the sofa, looking over the Touraine landscape. They roamed the orchard and garden, chased lizards, examined plants and insects, and returned with eager questions. Mme. Willemsens embroidered during lessons, following them without interrupting, concentrating on her eldest, Louis, and speaking little of Marie. After lessons she walked the tutor to the gate and frankly questioned him about Louis’s progress; the tutors answered honestly about his strengths and faults.

Their days balanced work and play, leaving little room for quarrels. Her steady love smoothed difficulties and taught her sons moderation and submission. She often wept for joy watching them; hours in the garden-house were precious, full of laughter. The children spoke both English and French thanks to their English nurse, and their mother guided their learning so they could understand the world. She gave them engaging books about great historical figures and used those stories to teach ambition and success.

While the children listened, little Marie often fell asleep on her mother’s knee. Afterwards Mme. Willemsens sometimes grew pensive and tearful. One evening Louis noticed her tears; she confessed fears that she might leave them alone and without resources, revealing the deep love behind her sorrow. Louis, puzzled, promised to take care of Marie. She reassured him that love kept her alive and hoped Touraine’s sunny air might help her recover.

As she spoke more of possible death, Louis grew serious and adapted the family’s life to her melancholy. The once vibrant household grew quieter; the old servant Annette watched with concern as Mme. Willemsens’ health declined. By August she remained beautiful but increasingly frail, striving to preserve her appearance; they rarely ventured far and Louis suggested shorter walks.

With autumn’s sunny days and ripe fruits, Mme. Willemsens spent nearly all her time with the children. Meanwhile Louis worked late preparing for the École Polytechnique exam, frequented the bridge of Tours to hear a retired naval officer’s tales, and—encouraged by his mother—took long walks, climbed trees, and learned to swim, slowly maturing into a young man whose sunburned face reflected a new purpose.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
When October came, Mme. Willemsens could only rise at noon. The Loire’s warmth made the air almost like the Bay of Naples, a region recommended by the faculty. At midday she sat in the shade with her two children, Louis and Marie, who never left her side. After lessons they sought closeness free from outside worries: the elder child lying beside her while restless Marie gathered flowers. With tired eyes and languid movements, Mme. Willemsens smiled at her vibrant children—a poignant scene touched by autumnal melancholy.

The doctor soon ordered her to stay in bed, though her room was brightened daily by flowers and her children remained near. One early November day she sat at the piano for the last time, gazing at a Swiss landscape above it while her children played outside; her fingers moved with passionate feeling. When the doctor arrived he insisted she stay in bed; stunned, she asked Louis to take her to the terrace for one last view of her homeland. Outside she looked skyward, captivated by the clouds, then, anguished, clutched her children’s hands and lamented their uncertain future, fearing how they would judge her when older. Seeing Louis and Marie kneel beside her, she returned to bed, never to rise again.

Days blurred with Annette and Louis keeping vigil. On the fifth day the doctor banned flowers—an ominous sign. As her condition worsened Louis refused to leave. One quiet Sunday night she drew the curtain and calmly told him tomorrow would be her last day. She instructed him to fetch important papers—birth certificates and a letter she wished to send to Lord Brandon, her estranged husband. As she dictated the letter she hesitated over how to sign it, finally choosing “Louis-Gaston.” She revealed twelve thousand francs saved—their remaining funds—and urged him to keep it safe, as everything would be sealed after her death. Louis vowed to care for Marie, planning to send her to school while he apprenticed at sea, determined to return successful.

Her eyes brightened at his resolve. Strength fading, she requested a priest; the next morning, surrounded by loved ones, she received the sacrament with her children kneeling beside her. As her breathing slowed the room fell silent; she passed away.

Annette and the vinedresser’s wife prepared the body for burial according to local custom. Only a few mourners attended. Marie, too young to grasp finality, cried as they lowered the coffin; a simple cross marked the grave. Afterwards the children returned to La Grenadiere for a last look before leaving with Annette to her cousin’s house in Tours. Louis entrusted Annette with Marie’s birth certificate and the money; the next day he took Marie to school, gave earnest advice, and departed to apprentice on a warship. A month later, watching the French coast fade, he embraced life at sea with the resolve to honor his mother’s memory, comforted by an old sailor’s words.

############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

I have long wanted to tell a simple true story that would terrify two young lovers and drive them to seek refuge in one another, like children clinging at the sight of a snake. I played a part in this ordinary tragedy; if it fails to move you, blame me and the mundanity of reality.

In 1819, short of money, I took an outside seat on the coach from Paris to Moulins. An Englishman, better off, joined me; we bonded over fresh air and the landscape. Conversation turned to women and love: we agreed a birth certificate meant little—some women at thirty-five or forty were younger in spirit than girls at twenty. We celebrated “the woman of a certain age.” He said his mistress was thirty-eight; I confessed mine was forty. Freed, we traded follies: one had traveled six hundred miles for a brief meeting; the other had risked danger for a secret rendezvous. His countess had lighted a cigar for him; mine made chocolate and wrote daily when we were apart. Both were adored by their husbands, which only heightened the peril. Then the wind took our laughter.

At Pouilly I studied him: dark-haired, blue-eyed, pale, well-proportioned, with shapely hands and a quick, clever air—a Vicomte with means whom many would desire.

About a league out of Pouilly the coach overturned. He jumped to escape, misjudged, and was crushed beneath it. We carried him to a peasant’s cottage where, in agony, he entrusted me: tell his mistress of his death so she would not learn it from a newspaper. He asked me to retrieve a key from around his neck; I found it embedded in his flesh. He gave directions to his home in La Charite-sur-Loire for the love letters, then grew speechless and died. The coachman muttered it was partly his fault; I was consumed by duty.

At La Charite I fulfilled his wishes. His mother was away; an old servant fainted at the news. I took the sealed letters, burdened by the sorrow of a woman who had lost her last love. The countess’s chateau lay eight leagues beyond Moulins. With little money but resolute youth, I walked quickly to bring the news before anyone else. Nearing the Chateau de Montpersan, unease about my role as bearer of such news came over me.

At the chateau the open doors unsettled me. Barking dogs and a servant-maid directed me to the park where Madame was. A playful little girl, calling for her mother, guided me through winding paths.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
I paused behind the last shrub on the avenue, adjusting my shabby clothes so the lady would not mistake me for a collector. I spotted Juliette and her husband among the flowers; at her child's remark her pace quickened. Surprised by my presence, she regarded me coolly, disappointment visible. I fumbled the elegant phrases I had prepared, and my hesitation let her husband approach. I asked if they were indeed M. le Comte and Mme. la Comtesse de Montpersan, using the question to size them up. He was a typical provincial nobleman: large, self-important, badly mannered, more pretension than polish. The Countess was the opposite: petite and graceful in white muslin with rose-colored accents, youthful and lively.

Choosing diplomacy, I took the Count aside with an air of mystery and requested a private word. He obliged, confident his wife could learn his secrets at will, and left her to wander. I told him of my traveling companion’s death and of his sincere attachment to Juliette. Concerned for his wife, the Count urged caution; I explained I could not approach her without first informing him, stressing the sacredness of the message entrusted to me. Flattered, he allowed me to proceed.

As we neared the house, Juliette glanced at us with curiosity and disdain, likely because of my appearance and our unexpected presence. I recalled her beauty in the sunlit alley and sighed. “Alas, madame, I have made a difficult journey solely for you,” I began; her face paled. She asked if he was ill; I answered that he was but urged her to control herself, saying he had entrusted me with secrets concerning her. Pressing for details, she demanded whether he still loved her. I could only nod. Distress overwhelmed her; she cried out and leaned against a tree.

“Your husband is coming!” I warned.

“Do I have a husband?” she fled.

The Count called me to dinner; the Countess was absent. The canon of Saint-Denis inquired after her, but the Count shrugged and hastily served the lavish meal. After a while a maid reported the Countess missing. Alarmed, I rushed out with the canon and the maid to search the garden and park. As we called, I recounted the tragic accident; the maid’s concern for her mistress was evident.

At last we heard a muffled cry from a granary. Inside we found Juliette buried in hay, sobbing. The maid and I helped her to her room; I instructed that she not be disturbed, claiming she had a headache. Returning to the dining room, I was surprised the Count still ate, seemingly unfazed. His indifference was explained: a serious medical condition had left him ravenous, overshadowing his concern for Juliette. The evening was somber; exhausted, we retired early.

Passing the Countess’s room, I asked the servant for news. Juliette called me in, tried to speak but could not. I left, feeling the weight of her agitation.

Later that night I was awakened by the sound of curtain rings. The Countess sat at the foot of my bed, pale and drawn. “Is it true?” she asked, voice trembling. “I don’t know how I can live after this blow, but I want to know everything.” Her eyes were red and swollen; she had lost her youthful glow. I related the accident briefly, avoiding gruesome details. When I described the dying man’s fears and the message he had entrusted to me, her tears dried into a deep pallor. I handed her letters from him; she took them mechanically, lamenting that she had burned all his other letters and now had nothing. “I cut this lock of his hair,” she said, revealing a precious token. I gave it to her; her tears fell on my hands, a moment of profound gratitude and connection.

“Ah! You love! May you always be happy,” she cried, clutching the lock and fleeing with her treasure.

The next morning felt surreal. I searched under my pillow for the letters but found nothing. I spent the day with Juliette, witnessing her noble spirit and selflessness. That evening the Comte de Montpersan accompanied me to Moulins and asked if I could deliver a sum of money to a contact in Paris. I agreed, taking a rouleau of twenty-five louis d’or, which covered my journey back. On arrival I appreciated the delicate way Juliette had arranged the loan, revealing the depth of her love and thoughtfulness. What a joy to share this adventure with a woman who cherished me, saying, “Oh, my dear, not you! You must not die!”

DEDICATION

To M. le Baron Barchou de Penhoen.

Of all our fellow pupils from the Oratorian school at Vendôme, I think we are the only two who have since met in mid-career as men of letters. When we met, you were engaged on German philosophy and I on this study. Neither of us has missed his vocation; and you, seeing your name here, will feel as much pleasure as I in my inscribing this work to you. Your old schoolfellow, 1840 De Balzac.

GOBSECK

It was one o’clock on a winter night in 1829–30 in the Vicomtesse de Grandlieu’s salon; two guests remained. A young man took his leave; as his carriage died away, the Vicomtesse turned to her daughter Camille, who pretended to watch a fire-screen while listening.

“Camille,” she said, “if you continue to treat young Comte de Restaud as you have this evening, I shall have to prevent him from visiting. Trust my love and let me guide you. At seventeen one cannot judge the past or the future or social standings. M. de Restaud has a mother, Mlle. Goriot, who squandered her father’s fortune and is of no birth. Though he behaves admirably toward her and his siblings, any family would hesitate to entrust their daughter’s future to him while she lives.”

Derville, a family friend who had overheard, interposed: “I have won, M. le Comte. I shall assist your niece.” On being challenged, he said he had read the Vicomtesse’s looks and settled by the fire. Camille’s uncle approached and the Vicomtesse sat between them.

“It’s time for a story that may change your view of Ernest de Restaud’s prospects,” Derville said.

The tale was meant for the Vicomtesse. By birth and fortune she led the Faubourg Saint-Germain; that an attorney could be intimate with her seemed odd until the facts were known. After returning from exile with the Royal family, the Vicomtesse had been reduced to a pension from Louis XVIII, intolerable to her pride. Her Hôtel de Grandlieu had been sold during the Revolution. Derville discovered defects in the title, pursued legal action, and reclaimed it. He obtained judgments restoring significant property and indemnities, raising her income to about sixty thousand francs. He did this without exploiting his new popularity: a man of integrity, he refused preferment to keep his practice and her confidence, becoming a close family adviser.

His management of her affairs won him esteem and many aristocratic clients, yet he declined political preferment. Since Ernest de Restaud’s arrival—and especially as Camille showed interest—Derville had visited the Hôtel de Grandlieu more often.

At a recent ball Derville had taunted Camille: “It’s a pity that young man doesn’t have two or three million francs, isn’t it?”

“Is it a pity? I don’t think so,” she replied. “M. de Restaud is capable and well educated. He will have as much money as he wishes when he comes into power.”

“But what if he were already rich?” Derville persisted.

“Rich already?” Camille blushed. “All the girls would be vying for him,” she said.

“Then Mlle. de Grandlieu might not be the one he favors,” he teased. Her blush betrayed her. Camille rose and left. Derville thought, “She loves him.”

Since that evening Camille had been unusually attentive to Derville, who approved of her feelings. Though grateful for his services, she showed respect rather than warmth; gratitude often weighs on the next generation, which may seek to escape it.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“This adventure,” Derville began, “recalls the one romantic episode of my life. It may surprise you that an attorney would speak of romance, yet at twenty-five, after seeing strange things, I met a man I shall describe—a usurer you have not known.

Imagine a sallow, wan face—what I would call lunar. His iron-gray hair was sleek and carefully combed; his features were impassive, his little yellow eyes without lashes peering from under a shabby cap as if afraid of light. Thin lips, a gimlet nose, and a low, suave voice that never rose made him look either prematurely aged or unnaturally preserved. His room matched him—clean but threadbare. In winter the fire smoldered in ashes, never flame. He moved with pendulum regularity, hoarding every sensation; his life flowed soundlessly like sands in an hourglass. Yet toward evening his money seemed to turn to a heart: when he finished his day’s business he would rub his hands; inward glee creased his face into a silent laugh. Even then he spoke in monosyllables with an inscrutable expression.

He was my neighbor in the Rue de Grès while I was a second clerk finishing my legal studies. The house was damp and dark; its gloom smothered even the liveliest spirits before they reached his door. He and his lodging were alike; the oyster resembled its rock. I was the only one he spoke to—borrowing a light or a book, sometimes permitting me to visit in the evenings. These small courtesies had grown out of four years of neighborly contact and my sober conduct. I lived nearly like him through lack of funds. No one knew if he had relatives or friends, whether he was rich or poor; I never saw money in his room—his capital was in the Bank. He collected debts himself, running all over Paris on skinny legs. Once, when gold fell from his pocket, another lodger returned it.

“That isn’t mine!” he cried, surprised. “If I were rich, would I live as I do?”

He made coffee on a cast-iron chafing dish; his dinners came from a cookshop, and the porter’s wife tidied his room faithfully. A whimsical chance named him Gobseck. Later, when I did business for him, I learned he was about seventy-six when we met—born about 1740 near Antwerp, son of a Dutch father and a Jewish mother. His full name was Jean-Esther Van Gobseck. When I mentioned the murder trial of La belle Hollandaise, he said without interest, “She is my grandniece.” That was the only remark he made about the death of his sole surviving relative, Sara Van Gobseck; when I asked why his grandniece bore his name, he smiled, “Women never marry in our family.” He had never cared to trace relatives beyond four generations of the female line. The thought of heirs disgusted him; the idea of his wealth passing to others after his death was intolerable.

Sent away at ten as a cabin boy to the Dutch Straits, he spent twenty years abroad. The inscrutable lines on his brow concealed adventures, panics, sudden luck, and squandered fortunes. He had known Admiral Simeuse, M. de Lally, Tippoo Sahib, notorious pirates in St. Thomas, had hunted treasure in Buenos Ayres, and observed the American War of Independence. Yet when he spoke of the Indies or America, he did so reluctantly, as if indiscreet. If sociability is a religion, Gobseck was an infidel; despite my efforts, his nature remained opaque.

Was he faithful to his mother’s religion? Had he converted? Did he view Gentiles as prey? I never learned his religious opinions; he appeared indifferent rather than unbelieving.

One evening I visited him. He sat motionless in his armchair, staring at the mantel as if reading his accounts. A lamp burned behind him, making his face paler. He gestured to a chair but said nothing. What could such a being think? Did he know God, feeling, women, happiness? I felt pity for him, like pity for a diseased creature. Yet I knew that while millions were at his command, he possessed the world in idea—having explored, ransacked, appraised, and exploited it.

“Good day, Daddy Gobseck,” I said.

He turned, contracting his bushy black eyebrows in an expression as expressive as a Southern smile. “You look as gloomy as when you heard that bookseller you admired had failed, though you were one of his victims.”

“One of his victims?” he repeated.

“Yes. You refused composition at the creditors’ meeting until he promised to pay you in full. He gave you bills accepted by the bankrupt firm. When he resumed business, he paid you the dividend on those bills.”

“He was sharp, but I got it out of him.”

“Do you have bills to protest? Today is the 30th, I believe.”

It was the first time I’d mentioned money to him. He looked at me ironically, then said blandly, “I am amusing myself.”

“So you amuse yourself now and again?”

“Do you think the only poets are those who print their verses?” he replied, pityingly.

I, knowing little of his life, thought, “Poetry in that head!” Then he surprised me.

“What life can be as glorious as mine?” he asked, eyes lighting. “You are young; your visions are colored by blood. You see women’s faces in the fire; I see coals. You have beliefs; I have none. Keep your illusions if you can. I’ll show you life with the discount taken off. You can go wherever you like or stay with your wife, but eventually you settle into a groove. Happiness consists in applying your faculties to realities. Anything more is false. My principles have varied with different men and latitudes. What we admire in Europe is punishable in Asia; a vice in Paris becomes a necessity elsewhere. There are no hard-and-fast rules, only conventions adapted to climate. Throw a man into various social melting pots and convictions become meaningless. The only constant is self-interest. If you lived as long as I have, you would know the only concrete reality worth caring about is GOLD. Gold means every form of human power. I’ve traveled and found hills or plains everywhere; place is irrelevant. Manners are universal; the battle between poor and rich is inevitable. It is better to exploit than be exploited. Pleasures are the same, and when sensations are exhausted, only Vanity remains. Vanity is satisfied only by gold. Our dreams need time and means to be realized; gold contains everything in embryo; it realizes all things for us.

“Only fools find pleasure in shuffling cards for a few pence. Only dupes think they’re useful making political rules amid unpredictable events. Only simpletons delight in discussing actors and repeating their sayings. What is all this but Parisian life? Let us seek a higher outlook: happiness in strong emotions that exhaust us or in methodical occupation that makes existence clockwork. A higher happiness is noble curiosity to learn Nature’s secrets, or to imitate Nature. In two words: Science and Art, or passion and calm. Every human passion, heightened by the struggle for existence, parades before me while I live in calm. As for scientific curiosity, I replace it with insight into the springs of action in man and woman. In short, the world is mine without effort and holds no claim on me. Listen,’ he continued, ‘I will tell you about my morning, and you’ll see my pleasures.”

He rose, bolted the door, drew a tapestry across it, and sat again.

“This morning,” he said, “I had only two amounts to collect; the rest of the bills due I gave away rather than cash yesterday. So much saved—when I discount a bill I always deduct two francs for a hired brougham; it’s absurd for my clients to make me trudge all over Paris for a few francs of discount when no man is my master, and I only pay seven francs in taxes.

“The first bill for a thousand francs was presented by a dandy—spangled waistcoat, eyeglass, tilbury, English horse. The bill bore the signature of one of the prettiest women in Paris, the wife of a Count, a great landowner. How did that Countess come to sign a bill of exchange, legally worthless but practically sound? Such women, poor things, fear the scandal of a protested bill and would rather pay than fail. I wanted to know what that bill truly meant—stupidity, imprudence, love, or charity?

“The second bill, signed ‘Fanny Malvaut,’ came from a linen-draper near bankruptcy. No one with bank credit comes to me. The first step to my door means despair; the news of failure will soon break, and he has been everywhere else first. When I see the stag at bay, a pack of creditors follows. The Countess lived in the Rue du Helder, and my Fanny in the Rue Montmartre. I made conjectures as I set out. If these two women could not pay, they would have more respect than their fathers. What tricks would the Countess try for a thousand francs? She would be so nice to me, speaking in that ingratiating tone of endorsers, pouring out coaxing words, while I...’ (he turned his pale eyes on me)—‘and I would remain unmoved, inexorable! So much for hypotheses. I reached the house.

“‘Madame la Comtesse is asleep,’ said the maid.

“‘When can I see her?’

“‘At twelve o’clock.’

“‘Is the Comtesse ill?’

“‘No, sir, but she returned home at three o’clock this morning from a ball.’

“‘My name is Gobseck; tell her I’ll call again at twelve,” I said, leaving muddy footprints on the carpet. I like leaving mud on a rich man’s carpet—not spitefully, but as a reminder of Necessity. In the Rue Montmartre, I entered a poor house and looked into a dark courtyard where sunlight never reached. The porter’s lodge was grimy, the window like a shabby gown—greasy and full of holes.

“‘Mlle. Fanny Malvaut?’

“‘She’s gone out; but if you’ve come about a bill, the money is waiting with the porter’s wife.’

“‘I’ll come again,’ I said.

“As soon as I knew the porter had the money, I wanted to see the girl; I pictured her as pretty. I spent the rest of the morning looking at prints along the boulevard; just as it struck twelve, I entered the Countess’s antechamber.

“From the soft tone I knew the mistress was not ready to pay. What a woman I saw! She had thrown an Indian shawl over her bare shoulders—covering herself while revealing the outline beneath. A loose gown trimmed with ruffles hinted that her laundress’s bills amounted to some two thousand francs a year. Dark curls escaped a bright handkerchief knotted carelessly on her head. The bed lay disorderly, suggesting broken slumber. A painter would have paid to see the scene: a crushed pillow in an eiderdown quilt, satin slippers on a bearskin rug, a crumpled gown, twisted stockings, ribbon garters, a half-open fan on the chimney-piece. Drawers stood open; flowers, diamonds, gloves, a bouquet, a girdle were scattered about. Beneath luxury and disorder, I saw Misery lurking—she felt those fangs. Her tired face epitomized the room strewn with relics of past revelry.

“What efforts to drink from the Tantalus cup of bliss I read in these traces of love struck by remorse—in this visible account of a life of luxury and riot. There were faint red marks on her young face, signs of fine skin; her features were slightly hardened, and dark circles ringed her eyes. Yet Nature was vigorous; these traces of folly did not mar her beauty. Her eyes glittered. She resembled a da Vinci Herodias: magnificently alive and energetic; nothing in her was starved. She awakened desire; it seemed a passion stronger than love burned in her. I was taken with her. It had been long since my heart had throbbed; I was paid then and there—for I would give a thousand francs for a sensation that brought back youth’s memory.

“‘Monsieur,’ she said, finding a chair for me, ‘will you be so good as to wait?’

“‘Until this time tomorrow, madame,’ I replied, folding the bill. ‘I cannot legally protest this bill any sooner.’ Inwardly I thought, ‘Pay for your luxury, your name, your comfort, your monopoly!’

“While we spoke, someone tapped softly at the door.

“‘I cannot see anyone,’ she cried imperiously.

“‘But, Anastasie, I particularly wish to speak to you.’

“‘Not just now, dear,’ she answered softer.

“‘What nonsense! You are talking to someone,’ said the voice, and in came a man who could be only the Count.

“The Countess glanced at me. I saw how it was; she was utterly in my power. There was a time when I might have been foolish enough not to protest. At Pondicherry in 1763, I once let a woman off and she repaid me. I deserved it; why trust her?

“‘What does this gentleman want?’ asked the Count.

“I could see the Countess trembling; the white satin of her throat stood rough with gooseflesh. I laughed inwardly without moving a muscle.

“‘This gentleman is one of my tradesmen,’ she said.

“The Count turned his back; I drew the bill halfway from my pocket. After that inexorable movement, she came over and placed a diamond in my hand. ‘Take it,’ she said, ‘and be gone.’

“We exchanged values, and I bowed and left. The diamond was worth twelve hundred francs to me. Outside, a swarm of flunkeys brushed their liveries, polishing sumptuous equipages.

“‘This is what brings these people to me!’ I thought. ‘They steal millions with all due formalities to maintain this lifestyle. The great lord and the little man who apes him bathe in mud to save a splash or two when walking through the streets.’

“At that moment the great gates opened and a cabriolet admitted the young fellow who had brought the bill.

“‘Sir,’ I said as he alighted, ‘here are two hundred francs, which I beg you to return to Mme. la Comtesse, and please tell her I hold the pledge she deposited with me this morning for a week.’

“He took the two hundred francs and smiled ironically, as if to say, ‘Aha! So she’s paid, has she? … Faith, so much the better!’ I read the Countess’s future in his face. That fair, handsome young man is a heartless gambler; he will ruin himself, her, her husband, and the children—devouring their portions and wreaking more havoc in Parisian salons than a battery of howitzers.

“I returned to see Mlle. Fanny in the Rue Montmartre, climbed a steep staircase, and reached a neat two-room dwelling on the fifth floor. Everything was as tidy as a new ducat. In the first room Mlle. Fanny sat—neatly dressed, with a delicate fresh face and a winning look. Her neatly brushed chestnut hair formed a double curve on her forehead, lending refinement to her clear blue eyes. Daylight through short curtains softened her girlish face. A pile of shaped linen showed she was a sempstress. She looked like a spirit of solitude. When I offered the bill, she said the porter’s wife had the money. I pretended not to understand.

“‘You go out early, mademoiselle, it seems.’

“‘I very seldom leave my room; but when you work all night, you must bathe sometimes.’

“I looked at her. A glance revealed a life of toil and misfortune—honest farmer folk origins, a freckle or two, an air of goodness. There was an indefinable sincerity about her; it was refreshing. Poor child, she had faith in something; a crucifix and a sprig of green box adorned her little painted bedstead. I felt inclined to charge no more than twelve percent, to help her establish a good business.

“But perhaps she had a cousin who would raise money on her signature and sponge on her,’ I thought.

“So I restrained my generosity; benevolence, when unchecked, often ruins those who take it. I have often thought Fanny Malvaut would make a nice wife; I compared her pure, lonely life with the Countess’s—already sunk to a bill of exchange; she will sink further before she is done!

“Well,’ he said, ‘do you think it nothing to have this power to see into the deepest recesses of the human heart, to embrace many lives, to see the naked truth? There are no two dramas alike: hideous sores, deadly griefs, love scenes, misery soon lying under the Seine, young men’s joys leading to the scaffold, laughter of despair, and sumptuous banquets. Yesterday was a tragedy: a father drowned himself because he could not support his family. Tomorrow will be a comedy: some young man will attempt a theatrical scene. You have heard people praise eloquent preachers; I have wasted time on them; they might change opinions but not conduct. These priests and your Mirabeaus and Vergniauds are mere stammerers compared to my orators.

“Often a girl in love, a gray-headed merchant on the verge of bankruptcy, a mother concealing a son’s wrongdoing, a starving artist, or a great man whose influence wanes comes to me. Their pleading makes me shudder. Sublime actors play for me, for an audience of one, and they cannot deceive me. I read their inmost thoughts as God reads them. Nothing is hidden from me. The holder of the purse-strings can loose and bind. I am rich enough to buy the consciences of those who control ministers, from office boys to mistresses. Is that not power? I can possess the fairest women, receive soft caresses; is that not Pleasure? Your whole social economy is summed up in Power and Pleasure.

“There are ten of us in Paris—silent, unknown kings, arbiters of destinies. What is life but a machine set in motion by money? Methods are confounded with results; you cannot separate soul from senses, spirit from matter. Gold is the spiritual basis of society. We ten meet at the Café Thémis near the Pont Neuf; we reveal finance’s mysteries. No fortune deceives us; we possess family secrets in all directions. We keep a Black Book noting important bills, drafts on public credit, or bank instruments. We are the casuists of the Paris Bourse, an Inquisition weighing every man of fortune’s actions; our forecasts are infallible. One of us watches the judicial world, another the financial, another the administrative, another commerce. I keep an eye on eldest sons, artists, high society, and gamblers—on Paris’s most sensational side. Everyone who comes reveals neighbors’ secrets. Thwarted passion and mortified vanity are great babblers. Vice, disappointment, and vindictiveness are the best detectives. My colleagues and I have had all things, are sated, and love power and money for their own sake.

“Here,” he said, indicating his bare, chilly room, “the most high-mettled gallant who would draw a sword elsewhere will entreat with clasped hands. No city merchant is so proud, no woman so vain, no soldier so bold, but they all entreat me here, with tears of rage or anguish. Here they kneel—the famous artist, the man of letters whose name may go down to posterity. In short, all inheritances and concerns of Paris are weighed in the balance. Do you still think there are no delights behind that blank mask which has so often amazed you?” he asked, stretching out his livid face that reeked of money.

I returned to my room feeling stunned. The little wizened old man had grown great. He had become a strange visionary symbol—the power of gold personified. I shrank from life and my kind.

Is it really so? must everything be resolved into gold? It was long before I slept that night. I saw heaps of gold surrounding me. My thoughts were filled with the lovely Countess; I confess, to my shame, that she eclipsed another quiet, innocent figure—the woman who toiled in obscurity. But through the clouds of slumber the next morning Fanny’s sweet face rose before me in all its beauty, and I thought of nothing else.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“Will you take a glass of eau sucrée?” the Vicomtesse asked, interrupting Derville.

“I should be glad of it.”

“But I see nothing here that concerns us,” Mme. de Grandlieu said, ringing the bell.

“Sardanapalus!” cried Derville. “Mademoiselle Camille’s happiness once depended on Daddy Gobseck; but with his death at ninety, M. de Restaud will soon inherit a fortune. This needs explanation. As for poor Fanny Malvaut, you know her; she is my wife.”

“Poor fellow, he would admit that, with his usual frankness, in front of a crowd!” said the Vicomtesse.

“I would proclaim it to the universe,” said the attorney.

Derville then resumed his recollection. After encountering the miser Gobseck, he completed his legal studies, became a licentiate and then an advocate. Gobseck’s opinion of him improved; the old man consulted him gratis on delicate matters. Derville rose from clerk to head-clerk, moved into his employer’s house, and eventually earned 150 francs a month. Though they drifted apart, Gobseck still sought his counsel.

When Derville’s employer, a pleasure-loving man, fell into financial trouble and sold his practice for 150,000 francs, Derville—the seventh child of a small tradesman from Noyon—had neither money nor connections to buy it. Determined not to beg, he boldly approached Gobseck, who now sat in Derville’s old room and watched callers through a grated window. Gobseck guessed his purpose.

Derville proposed buying the practice: it yielded about 20,000 francs a year and might reach 40,000 under his management; with a ten-year plan he could pay 150,000 if Gobseck lent the sum. Gobseck scrutinized him—age just under twenty-five, no guarantees—then agreed to consider it. The next morning he consented, but on hard conditions: interest first set at twelve-and-a-half then corrected to thirteen percent; payment directly to the seller to secure the lien; fifteen bills of ten thousand francs each payable to an unnamed third party. Derville puzzled over the need for his birth certificate; Gobseck, with a malicious smile, explained that youth’s integrity and brains were commodities one could mortgage—after thirty a man’s value became uncertain.

Further stipulations followed: Derville would give Gobseck legal advice without fees for life so long as it cost no outlay; he would allow Gobseck to visit him, not live ostentatiously, and receive him at dinner on Wednesdays and Saturdays after the “Change” at five. Gobseck promised to recommend him, send him business, and teach him “to know men, and what is more—women!” The miser delighted in investing capital in Derville’s head and watching it prosper.

Derville soon established himself as an attorney, took on and won the suit to recover Mme. de Grandlieu’s estates, and his reputation spread. Despite the usurer’s enormous interest, he repaid the loan in under five years. He married Fanny Malvaut, whom he loved; by hard work, shared fortune, and a timely inheritance—one of her uncles left her seventy thousand francs—they cleared the debt. Derville modestly summarized his happiness and rejoined the company.

About a year after buying the practice, he was drawn into a bachelor breakfast lost as the forfeit of a bet. It began as a display of sumptuousness and dissolved into a chaotic orgy: neatness and refinement gave way to broken glasses, ruined linen, loud jesting, songs, quarrels and smashed bottles—an exhausted Bacchanal. Among the guests was M. de Trailles, the consummate dandy: graceful in clothing and tandem, master of taste, gambler and gourmand of society’s pleasures, spending perhaps a hundred thousand francs a year with no visible capital. Brilliant but frivolous, Trailles charmed or ruined with equal ease; he mostly skimmed life’s surface.

Derville had tried to avoid Trailles, but his friend Goriot insisted. Beneath an affected drunken air Trailles was cool and calculating. By evening he secured Derville’s promise to introduce him to Papa Gobseck, pleading honor, virtue, and urgent misfortune: a young woman’s reputation and marriage were said to be at stake unless fifty thousand francs could be found that morning.

The next day, uneasy but bound by his word, Derville accompanied Trailles to Gobseck. Trailles, outwardly courtly, was inwardly agitated; on the Rue de Grès he grew nervous, glancing about with sweat on his brow. At Gobseck’s, Derville vouched for him; Gobseck replied that he had no free money beyond his clients’ business. Trailles tried to persuade him by boasting of fashionable partners and foreign revenues, quipping that spendthrifts and usurers were interdependent. Gobseck answered coldly: other creditors—Girard, Palma, Werbrust, Gigonnet—were full of Trailles’s paper and offered it at heavy losses; he would not lend a stiver to a man who owed thirty thousand francs and had nothing.

A cab arrived; Trailles left and returned shortly with a woman on his arm—one of Goriot’s daughters, the Countess. Derville, watching unseen at the window, saw her enter Gobseck’s damp room, proud and distressed. She shuddered the instant she recognized Derville; he felt pity for her despite her social brilliance and moral failings. Trailles had become her evil genius, ruling her by vanity and other strings.

The Countess asked whether she could obtain the value of her diamonds while retaining the right of repurchase, and handed over a jewel-case. Derville stepped forward and explained that this would be a sale with faculty of redemption—a formal transfer with a stipulated term allowing the previous owner to recover title by paying a set sum. The Countess breathed freer; the Count (her companion) looked black, fearing Gobseck would undervalue the stones after their fall in price.

Gobseck, impassive, took his magnifying glass and inspected the jewels. Derville never forgot the look that came over the old man—an unnatural glitter in his eyes as he rose and went to the light, holding the diamonds close to his toothless mouth, as if to devour them. He handled bracelets, sprays, necklaces and tiaras like a child or a doting miser, mumbling, letting the light draw out every fire. That flash of greed, the only time Gobseck’s heart seemed to open, shocked Derville and left a lasting impression.

When Trailles had first argued his case, Gobseck had spoken with irony and calculation, mocking the ruin of a man whose bills had been discounted at losses. Yet the arrival of the Countess and her jewels transformed his mood: he rose in triumph, stretching out his arms to Derville and calling the arrival “Life” itself, as if the security had saved him. He admitted he had been guarded, unwilling to trust the elegant scoundrel alone. Now, having the jewels, he felt exultant—his ecstasy frightful in its intensity.

Gobseck rejoiced at the prospect of outwitting rivals Werbrust and Gigonnet, anticipating laughing at them that night. He sat again before his bureau, his face reverting to the pale, impassive mask that made him resemble a statue. Yet the sight of the Countess—beautiful, suffering, controlled by Trailles—registered in him hardly beyond the value of her jewels. Derville reflected that Trailles had become the Countess’s tormentor: an angel-faced youth who, by charm and calculation, governed her life and drove her into degrading necessity.

Mme. de Grandlieu lamented how the Countess’s virtues had been subverted—how Trailles turned devotion into a weapon and made her pay for pleasures. Derville felt no urge to weep for her; he shuddered at the sight of her corrupter, the glittering youth whose angelic look masked moral ruin. In Gobseck’s room they stood before the old man as if before a judge; Gobseck peered at them like an inquisitor, measuring guilt and bargaining worth.

The Countess’s plea and Gobseck’s examination revealed what Derville had learned: Gobseck’s judgments were never sentimental—people were valued for what they could secure and what they promised. The bargain Gobseck struck with Derville—high interest, anonymous bills, visits, unpaid counsel—underscored a system in which personal ties and youthful promise were commodified, and financial life depended as much on shrewd calculation and social maneuver as on law or sentiment.

That evening, Gobseck’s slight intoxication of triumph at possessing the diamonds contrasted with his usual reserve. He had no tenderness for the Countess beyond the stones she offered; his joy at outwitting other lenders, and at the glitter held to his hungry gaze, revealed the fusion of avarice and childish delight in him. For Derville, who had benefited from Gobseck’s money but never from his heart, the scene was emblematic: the usurer’s power came not only from capital but from a cold, almost scientific knowledge of men’s weaknesses—knowledge he wielded to acquire dominion, and to profit emotionally as well as financially from the commodities people brought him, whether practices, promises, or jewels.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“Fine stones! … Genuine Asiatic diamonds from Golconda or Visapur.” Gobseck handled the jewels with alternating rapture and calculation. The Count wanted a loan of one hundred thousand francs for three years and offered the family diamonds as security. The Countess insisted the jewels were hers; I pointed out that a married woman could not legally alienate property without her husband’s consent, so a deed of sale would be void. Gobseck changed tack: possession would stand for title, and he offered eighty thousand francs if they left the diamonds with him.

Pride and panic clashed. The Count bristled, the Countess seemed stupefied, and Maxime, her lover, blustered. Gobseck, unmoved, produced a cheque for fifty thousand francs on the Bank of France, thirty thousand in protested drafts, and even two pistols to show his implacable temper. Cornered, they accepted. Gobseck exulted at having acquired the stones “tolerably cheaply”; his ferocious pleasure in possession chilled me.

The Count soon burst into Gobseck’s room, calling it theft. Gobseck replied coldly: if Mme. la Comtesse could sign bills she could do business; whether the jewels were the Count’s could not be proven without bringing his wife into court and exposing her. He mocked the Count’s authority; our dispute narrowed to law: morally iniquitous perhaps, legally defensible. I advised the Count to settle—pay for the diamonds or secure a redemption clause—since litigation would be ruinous and humiliating and the buyer could claim good faith. With my help the Count signed an acknowledgment of receipt of eighty-five thousand francs, including interest, in consideration of Gobseck’s promise to return the stones.

Days later the Count, agitated and fearing for his family’s future, asked me to draw papers to transfer his estate to Gobseck as a simulated sale; Gobseck would hold it in trust and later restore it to the eldest son. Distrusting his son’s future loyalty to his wife’s line, the Count wanted to secure the fortune. I warned him such a counter-deed risked disinheriting other children; he wept and agreed to provide for them in the instrument. The deed was signed but not delivered to me.

As the Count lay ill for two months—sullen, squalid, refusing nurses—money and secrecy governed the bedside. I suspected the Countess of trying to seize the counter-deed. To prevent this, I sued the Count for a sum ostensibly due to Gobseck so that on his death I could attach the estate and secure the document; I bribed a servant to alert me when the Count was gravely ill. My plan was to intervene swiftly, threaten to affix seals, and secure the deed before the Countess could act.

One lucid morning the Count summoned his youngest, Ernest, and entrusted him with a sealed packet addressed to me, begging him to carry it to the post and to keep the secret even from his mother. Ernest promised. On returning, under his mother’s influence, he refused to repeat what his father had said. The Count confronted her in fury, accusing her of corrupting their son and filling his life with trouble. She begged for mercy and vowed to expiate her sins if the children could be spared. The Count, exhausted of affection, called her a bad wife and mother; she fainted. Priests administered the sacraments. At midnight he died.

Gobseck and I arrived soon after. The house was in chaos. In the death-chamber and adjoining salon the Countess had been ransacking drawers and trunks; her search was violent—mattress overturned, drawers emptied, papers and furniture strewn. At the hearth she had thrust some documents into the grate and partially burned them. I snatched an addressed paper from the embers: it was to me. Confronted, the Countess was near collapse, torn between shame and fear. Gobseck, cold and triumphant, reminded her—without mercy—that he was the legal owner of what her husband had sold. He asked if I would judge him; I could not deny that under the law he had rights. I left her beside her husband’s corpse while Gobseck declared himself trustee of the estate.

From then on Gobseck administered the property with the hard efficiency of a usurer turned landowner—leasing the mansion, spending summers on the estates, repairing mills, planting timber. He behaved in all but name as lord of the manor, showing no softness. When I suggested he help young Ernest, Gobseck scoffed: adversity is the best teacher; let the youth fend for himself. My attempts to move him to honor the Count’s intentions and provide for the children met the old man’s icy thrift.

Years passed. Gobseck grew feebler but retained his iron will. He kept his rooms exactly as always and received clients even from his bed. During the financial reorganizations after France acknowledged the Haitian Republic, his knowledge of claims and fortunes made him influential; he devised agencies, took slices of large transactions, and accepted gifts—plates, snuff-boxes, game—without ever parting cash. He remained as thin and avaricious as ever, his greed concentrated to a point.

Then Gobseck died. Summoned to his bedside by his old porter, I found him half-delirious, kneeling by the grate, naming people and muttering bequests. He died after a final enumeration of riches. At the hearth, amid cinders, I dug and found metal: coins, receipts, and tucked papers—proof of the vast hoard he had amassed. I ordered the justice of the peace to seal everything at once.

The rooms revealed both scale and eccentricity of his thrift: one chamber reeked of putrid pies and moldy fish among chests of tea and coffee; others were packed with furniture, silver-plate, vases, pictures, books, and curios—many likely presents or pledged goods. Jewel-cases bore ciphers and coats of arms; fine linen and weapons lay in confusion. Every item had been argued over, bartered, haggled-for; some bargains remained unsettled and dealers who had expected gifts balked at Gobseck’s stingy terms. The stench and clutter bespoke a miser’s final degradation.

There was talk of Gobseck’s intended bequests: he had given an address for a supposed heiress, a woman called “La Torpille” in the Rue Vivienne; I foresaw the absurdity of pouring an immense fortune into such a character’s hands. But more immediate was the question of the Restaud estate: the Count’s counter-deed, the burned fragments, the tormented Countess, and young Ernest’s uncertain inheritance. Despite Gobseck’s harshness, Ernest would, in time, inherit a fortune sufficient to marry Mlle. Camille and to provide for his mother and siblings—assuming the legal knots could be untangled and the provisions enforced.

Thus closed the arc of transactions, betrayal, and legal scrimmage: Gobseck’s cold mastery of money had altered an aristocratic family’s fate; the Count’s plans were thwarted by rage and sickness; the Countess’s frantic attempts to save her children had degenerated into crime; and a miser’s death revealed treasures amid squalor. The outcome promised both restitution and new trials, as the rightful heirs would have to navigate tangled transfers and the old usurer’s secreted wealth.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Monsieur de Manerville, who married a Bordeaux heiress, became attached to her château de Lanstrac. He fled the Revolution and on returning found Lanstrac managed by Mathias, a notary's clerk. After his wife's death he became miserly and cut off his son Paul.

Paul returned from college to years' stifling paternal control. Unable to oppose his father, Paul lost courage, grew timid and, confined to the mansion, failed to make friends or fit into royalist gatherings. To escape monotony he practiced fencing and riding and read novels.

His father's death freed him: he inherited estates but disliked Bordeaux and Lanstrac as reminders. He invested in consols, traveled and served at embassies, but indulgence and bad management exhausted his fortune. Resolving to be a country gentleman, he sought marriage for companionship; de Marsay warned marriage brings ridicule, responsibility, loss of individuality.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Despite earlier doubts, the Comte de Manerville returned to Bordeaux in the winter of 1821 determined to pursue his plans. Restoring and furnishing the family mansion burnished his reputation for elegance, and his Parisian manners quickly secured him a leading place in Bordeaux’s royalist society. An old marquise called him “the pink of fashion,” a phrase liberals satirized and royalists admired.

Paul de Manerville enjoyed the attention. Charming, witty, and attentive to women, he combined a touch of foppery with a genuine concern for appearance. Of medium height and graceful in movement, he preferred receiving happiness to bestowing it and sought the understanding and encouragement he believed marriage would provide. Young and averse to mundane pursuits, he accepted that provincial life required matrimony and domestic variety. His inclination was sharpened by a secret love for Mademoiselle Evangelista.

Years earlier an affluent Spaniard, Evangelista, had settled in Bordeaux and, by fortune and connections, entered noble salons. His Creole wife, from an illustrious Spanish family, upheld their position with extravagant tastes, indulging whims regardless of cost and supported by her devoted husband. After Evangelista’s death in 1813, Madame Evangelista—now a wealthy widow with a beautiful daughter—continued living lavishly even as her fortune waned.

By Paul’s return Natalie was a striking young woman, yet proposals had not come, largely because her mother’s extravagance discouraged suitors. Gossip exaggerated the situation, claiming only a prince could afford her. Accustomed to luxury and adulation, she remained largely ignorant of life beyond privilege.

Six months after Paul’s arrival they met at a high-society event and found mutual charm. Madame Evangelista perceived his interest and hoped he would become her son-in-law; Paul began to picture Natalie as his future wife. He had known of the Evangelista fortune since childhood, which eased his approach. Dismissing warnings about their extravagance with Parisian disdain, he navigated provincial gossip without deterrence. Madame invited him to formal dinners, and his appetite for marriage deepened.

Spending time with the Evangelistas, he felt proud of their company, and society soon treated him as Natalie’s suitor. Royalist circles called the match prudent given Natalie’s beauty and her mother’s intelligence and connections. Madame, forty and still striking, retained a reputation for virtue that enhanced her allure. Their relationship contained mutual advantage: Madame saw Paul as a way to advance socially in Paris; Paul felt flattered.

A year after his return people concluded he was courting Natalie, though neither mother nor daughter actively pressed for marriage. Sheltered and naive, Natalie was drawn to Paul’s charm but inexperienced in married life; Paul, enamored yet cautious, wondered if he could manage someone who might not fully appreciate him. The narrative stresses that in French society marriage is often governed by negotiations over marriage contracts—frequently overlooked in literature but crucial in shaping couples’ lives—and that those impending discussions would shape Paul and Natalie’s future.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
In winter 1822 Paul de Manerville, through his great-aunt the Baronne de Maulincour, proposed to Mademoiselle Natalie Evangelista. The baroness, after discussing the dowry with Madame Evangelista, reported that Natalie’s dowry would be her patrimony, that her mother would contribute nothing, and advised Paul to consult Maître Mathias to protect his interests.

At Paul’s anxious visit Madame Evangelista proposed a joint meeting of the families’ notaries but privately admitted she owed Natalie a large sum from her late husband’s estate and could not pay. Fearing Paul’s withdrawal, she summoned Maître Solonet, who promised they could conceal the difficulty and draft a favorable contract. Mathias and Solonet met: Mathias was cautious and protective of Paul, Solonet more pragmatic. While Paul and Natalie shared a tender dinner, the notaries debated the risk—Mathias warning the Evangelistas had squandered wealth, Madame Evangelista and Solonet proposing a house sale. They compromised: Madame Evangelista would entail her property to secure Natalie while limiting Paul’s exposure. The marriage could proceed, but tensions hinted at tests of love, ambition, and money.

The next day Elie Magus, then in Bordeaux, answered a summons expecting to value jewels for Comte Paul’s fiancée; instead he appraised her necklace and earrings at 250,000 francs and the “Discreto” at 100,000—warning any sale would bring heavy loss. The appraisal sparked staff rumors and society gossip that the house was being sold and the union endangered. Notaries denied it, but public skepticism—fueled by envy and a conspicuous hunting party—persisted; Madame de Gyas circulated tales to Natalie and her mother, who dismissed them as rival maneuvering.

To counter rumors and display means, Madame Evangelista planned a grand fête of the camellias and to finalize the contract. Near signing she learned that if Paul died first, Natalie would inherit only a fraction of her expected fortune. Struck, Madame Evangelista resolved—at personal cost—to protect Natalie’s future, shifting from careless spender to shrewd strategist determined to reclaim the family fortune and influence Paul, setting the stage for further conflict.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The fête had briefly eased Paul’s anxieties, which returned that night.
