He feared Madame Evangelista might have deceived him without Mathias’s help but reassured himself that in two days Natalie would be his and their finances settled. He failed to see that his future had already shifted: the shrewd woman in his life had become an adversary, considering withdrawing her interests from their shared future. He did not perceive the awakening of his Creole mother‑in‑law—part European intellect, part tropical passion, part Eastern indifference—who, at the thought of losing her daughter, saw she could shape the couple’s life. With a day until the marriage, Madame understood that Natalie trusted her implicitly and that a single conversation might alter their fate.

The next morning, seated on Natalie’s bed, Madame recalled happier times and bewailed the idea of her daughter bound to a man she might have to obey. Natalie, confident that love would govern, brushed aside warnings. Madame countered that conjugal love can fade if not cultivated and urged Natalie to establish authority from the start. She advised leaving occasionally to avoid complicating the new household and recommended feigning obedience while subtly asserting dominance.

Their talk turned practical: keep an active social life to retain Paul’s interest; avoid constant proximity that breeds indifference; preserve allure and independence; and keep him amused so the relationship remains vibrant. Overwhelmed, Natalie sought guidance. Madame promised continued contact and visits but insisted some separation was necessary for Natalie’s happiness and prospects.

When Paul arrived he tried to conceal his unease; Madame noticed and offered to sell her jewelry to support the couple, framing it as a sacrifice for Natalie’s vanity and future. Moved, Paul accepted. The notary Solonet brought news of potential buyers for Madame Evangelista’s property, which could provide substantial funds; Natalie agreed to the sale.

Determined to secure her daughter’s prospects, Madame resolved to step back from their Paris life and support them from afar so Natalie and Paul could form their own dynamic. As the midnight wedding neared, preparations carried a foreboding air and guests noted Madame’s absence. After the ceremony she played the grieving mother, extracted promises from Paul to secure her influence, and sent the newlyweds to Paris. Once alone, Natalie began to assert her authority, setting the stage for complex marital dynamics.

Five years later, in November, Comte Paul de Manerville, cloaked and somber, arrived at the house of his old friend Monsieur Mathias in Bordeaux. The retired notary was absent; his housekeeper led Paul to the late Madame Mathias’s room, where he stayed until evening. When Mathias returned and found Paul there on the anniversary of his wife’s death, he showed concern and embarrassment over public notices of Paul’s financial ruin. Mathias blamed Paul’s marriage to Natalie for the downfall, though he was relieved to have preserved the estate titles even as the usufruct was lost.

Paul appeared altered but calm. Over a meal, Mathias lamented the seizure of Paul’s properties. Paul then revealed he had left Natalie without her knowledge and intended to sail to Calcutta to rebuild his fortune. Mathias, shocked that a count would enter commerce, was told Paul no longer considered himself a count and planned to succeed under another name. Paul recounted enormous debts from lavish living and gifts to Natalie; he admitted selling assets and borrowing from usurers to sustain their lifestyle, producing the present catastrophe.

Despite Mathias’s doubts about de Marsay—who was meant to send funds—Paul insisted India was his only chance and vowed to return wealthy by forty. The next day Mathias received de Marsay’s bill of exchange confirming some funds would arrive, but Paul remained determined to leave. Mathias lamented losing his friend as Paul boarded the ship and waved goodbye.

At sea, Paul was consumed by memories and his love for Natalie, torn between regret and resolve. Struck by seasickness, he spent days in his cabin reflecting. When he recovered, he opened letters from Natalie and de Marsay. Natalie’s letter revealed anguish and a vow to support him: she pledged her jewels to pay his debts and promised to live at Lanstrac with her mother and economize. De Marsay’s letter detailed Paul’s ruin and exposed the mother‑in‑law’s profiteering. Confronted with love and treachery, Paul felt anger, helplessness, and remorse as he sailed away from France uncertain of the future.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Railroads threatened to obsolete many industries and reshape travel around Paris; parts of the old Scene were already relics. The once-common "coucous" of 1830 were dwindling by 1842, surviving mainly at occasional suburban spectacles. In 1820 many districts still lacked regular stage-coach service, so short-route local vehicles remained essential.

The Touchards, father and son, held a near-monopoly on travel within about forty-five miles of their base on rue du Faubourg-Saint-Denis, yet they met brisk competition from local coucous on short routes. Parisians’ appetite for the countryside let small, familiar enterprises—often run by innkeepers who knew the roads, undercut fares, and won local loyalty—thrive. These coucous offered speed and convenience that could challenge the Touchards’ more formal coaches until omnibuses later eclipsed them by carrying more passengers more efficiently.

One lucrative contested line ran from Paris to Beaumont-sur-Oise; in 1822 multiple rivals operated despite Touchard attempts to cut fares and improve equipment, because profitable stops like Saint-Denis and Saint-Brice ensured steady demand. The Touchards even extended to Chambly while rivals reached Beauvais. By contrast, the road to Isle-Adam, prized for quarries and a scenic terminus, had only two coaches; its terminus discouraged speculation, giving Pierrotin and a colleague a virtual monopoly on a route locals often preferred to the crowded Beaumont coaches.

Pierrotin, in his forties, had taken over his father’s coucou after leaving the cavalry in 1815. He married an innkeeper’s daughter, expanded the business, and combined practical intelligence with military rigour. His sturdy boots, bottle-green trousers, and blue embroidered blouse matched his trade and hinted at a soldier’s respect for order; his daily work inclined him to regard passengers as cargo.

Sensing the industry’s future, Pierrotin tried to modernize: he ordered a larger coach from a respected maker even as his fleet still included an inherited yellow coucou built for six but often overloaded, and a noisy four-wheeler capable of seventeen though officially limited to fourteen. He paid taxes only on the coucou, using a permit for the larger coach to skirt regulations.

On a Saturday morning in autumn 1822 Pierrotin stood outside the Lion d’Argent, fretting over scarce passengers and parcels. Optimistic about the new coach but burdened by a thousand-franc debt and eyeing a loan from Monsieur Moreau, steward at Presles, he seized a chance when a footman sought a discreet ride for his master, the Comte de Serizy—whose countermanded carriage and the footman’s secrecy heightened Pierrotin’s sense of responsibility and hope that the fare might ease his debts.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Monsieur Huguet de Serizy descends from the eminent President Huguet, ennobled under François I; the family arms and motto tie modesty to the estate’s name.

The current count’s father was president of parliament before the Revolution. The count became councillor of State at twenty-two, noted for persuasive diplomatic memoranda. He remained at Serizy during the Revolution, protected by his father’s standing; after his father’s death in 1794 he joined the Council of Five Hundred to occupy himself. After 18th Brumaire he won the First Consul’s favor, rejoined the Council of State, and reorganized a chaotic department, shaping much of Napoleon’s administration. In 1806, at forty, he married the widow of a prominent Republican general, improving his fortune. By 1814 ill health forced his resignation; though Napoleon mistrusted him he retained rank. Louis XVIII later trusted him as a cabinet minister. During the Hundred Days he stayed loyal to the Bourbons at Serizy. After Napoleon’s final defeat he returned as privy councillor and vice-president of the Council of State, influencing affairs without ostentation. His regimen—rising early, retiring early—reflected relentless, quiet duty; he accepted honors, including the grand cross of the Legion of Honor, yet his devotion carried an undercurrent of private sorrow.

He had long been passionately attached to his wife; despite strains their bond endured. Work was his refuge from heartache; he treated her with respect and allowed her considerable freedom. Though she acknowledged some of his virtues, her ingratitude often wounded him; only occasionally did she soothe his pain.

A pressing business matter then preoccupied him: a farm called Presles was encroaching on his estate. Leased by tenant Léger from a local burgher, Margueron, the lease was expiring and the property would be sold. The count intended to buy it to end disputes and instructed his notary and lawyer to negotiate quickly.

His steward, Moreau, had served faithfully for seventeen years; the count’s father had saved him during the Revolution, and the count later spared him from a death sentence. Moreau asked to manage Presles and at first was diligent, but after securing the post he grew lax and corrupt, taking bribes and arranging shady deals—already bargaining with Léger. His conduct provoked the retired officer Monsieur de Reybert’s wrath; Madame de Reybert brought the count detailed accusations. Initially skeptical of her motives, the count’s doubts grew and he ordered his notary to accompany Madame de Reybert to Presles. What began as a straightforward purchase thus became an investigation fraught with intrigue and the threat of betrayal by trusted servants.

As Pierrotin left the Café de l’Echiquier he saw Madame Clapart and her son at the Lion d’Argent searching for him. In a faded gown she was anxious yet dignified; poverty was visible but devotion clear, as she entrusted eighteen-year-old Oscar, on his first trip alone, to Pierrotin’s care. Oscar, in patched clothes, remained dominated by his mother and resented her fussing. Once Madame Husson, she had been impoverished by misfortunes and a first husband’s suicide, later marrying the meager clerk Clapart. Moreau aided them and secured Oscar a scholarship, but household strain and Clapart’s coldness worsened Oscar’s faults. Overprotected, Oscar’s vanity and entitlement grew; Moreau proposed testing him by placing him with the steward. As they left, two well-dressed men mocked Oscar, deepening his humiliation.

Boarding heightened his discomfort: a shabby artist and his apprentice, Mistigris, mocked him. The Comte de Serizy, travelling incognito, altered the coach’s tone; Pierrotin rearranged seats for him, drawing complaints. When the coach set off, its mixed passengers—proud mothers, insecure youths, discreet patrons, bohemians—framed Oscar’s simmering struggle with identity, setting the stage for the journey.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Travelers by public conveyance usually begin in silence until the most talkative passenger breaks it. National types are sketched: the English taciturn, Germans somber, Italians cautious, Spaniards rarely using public transport, Russians limited by poor roads, and the French—especially the lower classes and bourgeois—lively.

Georges, a clever young man, quickly sized up his fellow passengers, considered himself superior, and resolved to amuse himself at their expense. As the coach descended from La Chapelle to Saint-Denis he toyed with identities, finally posing as a disguised Russian prince or a soldier returned from the East. When Mistigris complained about dust, Georges joked and steered the talk to travels in the Levant, claiming service under Ali Pacha of Janina and inventing a plague scare that alarmed the count enough to have the driver stop. He also boasted of fighting in the 1813 campaign and at Waterloo, where he’d been promoted to captain.

The count inquired about decorations; Georges said he did not wear them while traveling, but a portfolio found at Saint-Denis identified him as Crottat’s second clerk, quickening doubts. Nevertheless Georges continued to impress—especially Père Léger and Oscar—by paying for treats and cultivating importance. Mistigris, a modest painter, introduced himself; Georges’ tale of Dalmatian romance—Zena, a jealous pirate husband, poisoning, and a narrow escape—captivated the coach with laughter and skepticism.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Pierrotin’s coach stopped at Saint-Brice about half past one to rest the horses. The innkeeper greeted the travelers by name and they stepped out. Georges, flamboyant and boastful, ordered a large breakfast, flourished his cane, produced a straw cigar case, lit a thin cigar, and offered cigars to Oscar and Schinner. Oscar, flattered but awkward, puffed ineptly and became the target of teasing: Schinner smoked easily, Georges fumbled theatrically, and Mistigris mocked Oscar’s discomfort. Oscar watched Georges’s largesse with thinly veiled resentment, mentally noting the bills he suspected Georges was burning through.

Georges proposed a bottle of Bordeaux with Père Léger, and the innkeeper supplied local gossip: François, conductor of Touchard’s coach, had heard that Monsieur Margueron had been invited by the Comte de Serizy to dine at Presles that day. Père Léger laughed and sketched a plan to outmaneuver such patronage: he intended to buy Les Moulineaux cheaply by using new coins and snapping up the farm before the count could act. The innkeeper speculated about lending twenty thousand and calculated returns; Les Moulineaux, he said, yielded six thousand francs—a sound investment. Père Léger explained he’d made Moreau a partner and, leveraging royal favor and the pictures on coins, had secured the estate. The scheme sounded shrewd even if the count tried to intervene.

The count—who had been listening—grew uneasy about discretion. When Pierrotin stepped out to water the horses, the count drew him aside and reminded him of a bribe: ten louis to keep the matter secret. Then he raised the inducement dramatically: if Pierrotin would conceal his name, the count would give him a thousand francs the next day for a new coach. He instructed him not to enter the inn, not to speak of the incident, and to stay with the horses. Pierrotin returned curt reassurance and hurried his customers along, insisting he must deliver fish by three to a big dinner in Stors.

Père Léger suggested harnessing the horse Pierrotin meant to sell into the cabriolet so they could breakfast calmly and overtake Pierrotin. To everyone’s surprise, Pierrotin rebridled and set off; Schinner and Mistigris walked. Georges kept up talk about the hostess and the landscape, joking about leftover cigars. Oscar, stung by being called “the little young man,” defended his dignity: he was not Mina’s aide-de-camp, had not been to the East, and his family intended him for diplomacy. This provoked ridicule: his companions mocked his pretensions, tutors, and “household” language—Oscar proudly noting his mother ran their household—and taunted his social standing and vocabulary.

They goaded him into naming his tutor; Oscar named the Abbé Loraux, vicar of Saint-Sulpice, and hinted at family influence that might aid the abbé’s ecclesiastical rise. Pressed for acquaintance with Abbé Frayssinous, Oscar said the abbé owed favors to his father. Then, unexpectedly, Oscar declared he was bound for the Château de Presles to visit the Comte de Serizy and claimed intimacy with the count’s son. His confident talk about the Serizys—outlining family relations and entanglements—caught the count’s attention.

The count seized the chance to question Oscar, explaining he sought information on Monsieur de Serizy to support a claim he intended to press with the American government. Oscar counseled him to approach the count’s wife rather than the count: the count, he said, was madly in love with her, while she secretly despised him. He described the count’s disfiguring skin disease and humiliating remedies—sulphur and steam baths, even an iron box—portraying a man reduced and ashamed; such afflictions left the count vulnerable at home. Oscar recommended instead seeking the Marquis d’Aiglemont, an old admirer of Madame de Serizy, for influence.

The young man’s ease in discussing intimate matters shocked the company. Schinner scolded him for airing friends’ affairs in public; Mistigris professed indifference. Georges offered his maxim: in a public coach one should know how to talk without saying anything. When the conversation turned to the Comtesse de Serizy, the count interrupted sharply and forbade disparagement. Asserting his friendship with her brother, the Marquis de Ronquerolles, he vowed that speaking ill of the countess would not go unpunished. The painter agreed: no man should blague women. The count hinted at influence—knowing ministers, blocking unworthy men from decorations, and even acquaintance with the architect of Presles—and asked Pierrotin to stop at the next inn so he could step out.

Once they were clear of the others, the count had Pierrotin show him aside in the inn-yard and asked about the talkative young man. Pierrotin explained the boy was the son of a poor woman in the rue de la Cerisaie, Madame Husson, whom he often supplied with fruit and game from Presles. Pierrotin had driven the youngster only once and thought he might be the prince of Maffliers; the boy had told him to leave him near Maffliers, not to go on to Isle-Adam. The count was reassured by this account. When he rejoined the party, Père Léger and the others exchanged awkward glances, feeling exposed; the three young men shared a guilty silence. Mistigris quipped about suffering for loose talk, and Georges reminded Oscar that public conveyances required speaking artfully.

They rode on into the Carreau woods in heavy silence; the count noted their quiet as if they had been led to the scaffold. Mistigris muttered a proverb; Georges remarked on the fine weather. Oscar, striving to assert himself, declared he was Oscar Husson and would be famous within ten years. The companions teased him further, and the count refined his name to Husson de la Cerisaie, giving Oscar’s modest boast a veneer of dignity. The coach descended toward the grand château of Presles.

Before disembarking, the count delivered formal, almost theatrical farewells: the colonel should make peace with the king; Schinner’s fame was assured; Oscar, the budding diplomat, needed no protection, since he possessed “the secrets of statesmen;” Père Léger was urged to pluck the Comte de Serizy and to do so firmly. Asking Pierrotin to set and fetch him at the same spot the next day, the count left the coach to the others’ bewildered speculation about his identity. Père Léger guessed he was the count who’d leased Franconville; Schinner swore never to blague again in a public coach. Mistigris, unrepentant, shrugged with another proverb.

As they neared the château gate, Georges warned Oscar that if the man indeed had been the Comte de Serizy, Oscar should be wary—a chilly reminder of his mother’s warnings. Pierrotin called out: they had arrived at Presles. Some of the young men feigned familiarity, but Georges, not wishing to reveal his destination, pretended he would continue to Les Moulineaux. Père Léger then triumphantly disclosed he was the farmer at Les Moulineaux, forcing Georges to admit it was so.

Oscar asked Pierrotin to leave his belongings at the steward’s; he intended to go straight to the château. He plunged forward along a narrow path, uncertain where to go, and Père Léger corrected him: the little gate, not the forest path, led to the château. Guided thus, Oscar entered the courtyard. While Père Léger watched, Georges slipped away upon realizing Père Léger truly possessed Les Moulineaux. Pierrotin, having obeyed the count’s injunction to keep the matter secret for now, drove in with swagger, deposited the painter’s packages and Oscar’s bundles, and handed a small leather portmanteau to the concierge’s wife. Mistigris and the painter took rooms in the château itself—a discovery that stunned Oscar.

The scene closed on Pierrotin’s satisfaction: he had kept the count’s secret and now looked forward to the promised reward. With a sly peasant’s calculation, he anticipated receiving a thousand francs the next day and buying the splendid new coach he long desired. The coach rolled on, the passengers dispersed through the estate, and the social ambitions, pretensions, and private bargains that had surfaced in their talk settled back into the polite surface of conversation, continuing to move unseen beneath it.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Oscar hid among the trees and watched Monsieur Moreau emerge from the guard-room in a blue overcoat with a riding whip. Moreau greeted the painters Mistigris and his master—sent by architect Monsieur Grindot—told the concierge to show them to their rooms, invited them to dinner at five, and mentioned hunting on the estate. He sent his son Jacques ahead to inform Madame Moreau of their arrival and, stern but kindly, urged Oscar to learn to ride and hunt despite his inexperience. Oscar’s mother had allowed only a two-week stay because Madame Moreau was present, yet Moreau seemed intent on extending it.

Their steward’s house, older than the Revolution and attached to the chateau, sat in a large garden and contained a salon, dining room, and a modern boudoir furnished to Madame Moreau’s taste. The Moreaus lived comfortably on estate provisions. Madame Moreau, once a maid, preserved an air of elegance and strove to be treated as the chateau’s mistress, patronizing villagers and nursing a grudge against the Reyberts dating to an old slight when Madame de Reybert reminded her of her former status. Eager to rise socially, Madame Moreau hoped the visiting artist would enhance her standing.

On the day the painters were due, Madame Moreau dressed carefully, but the Paris coach brought only Mistigris’s packages, leaving her annoyed. When Mistigris and Bridau arrived she welcomed them and professed her love of art; the two men quickly recognized her pretentiousness and decided to humor her with polite talk about painting. Their conversation was cut short when a maid announced the count’s arrival, and Madame Moreau grew flustered. Oscar, feeling out of place, was fetched by his younger brother and scolded for his appearance; Mistigris’s joking remark about Oscar’s future increased the awkwardness. The maid then said the count wanted dinner promptly.

The count had come unexpectedly and was upset to learn Moreau was absent. Having heard remarks that suggested Moreau’s disloyalty, he confronted him, angered that private matters had been discussed—especially in front of Oscar. Moreau’s attempts to explain failed; he returned home distraught over the loss of his position and the damage caused by Oscar’s careless words. Estelle, his wife, was furious and grasped the gravity of their predicament. Moreau demanded an explanation from Oscar, who remained silent from fear and shame. In anger Moreau dragged him back to the count to apologize; the count dismissed Oscar’s behavior and doubted his promise. Humiliated, Moreau took Oscar home to face uncertain consequences for their family.

Moreau wrote to Madame Clapart, distraught that Oscar had revealed Madame de Serizy’s secrets to an incognito count; the count’s decision sent Oscar away, costing Moreau the stewardship at Presles and imperiling the family’s prospects. At the Clapart home Monsieur fretted about Oscar’s character while Madame defended him. When Brochon returned with Oscar and Moreau’s letter, Madame Clapart fainted at the thought of her son’s future.

The next day she found Oscar sullen, reminded him of Moreau’s past support and their precarious position without the stewardship, and urged discretion, hard work, and prudence; he remained largely indifferent. As a last hope she appealed to Uncle Cardot, a self-made, modest businessman who generously supported his children and had been unaware of their plight. Cardot received them kindly, praised Oscar’s promise and—moved by Madame Clapart—offered to fund his legal studies, insisting on integrity, discipline, and effort. Madame Clapart felt renewed hope. Moreau later visited, saying he had begun speculating in land and could place Oscar as a pupil in a law office; he reiterated that disciplined study was essential to repair Oscar’s reputation and secure his future, and Madame Clapart urged Oscar to show gratitude by heeding these admonitions.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Ten days later Monsieur Moreau took Oscar to Maître Desroches, a twenty-six-year-old solicitor from poverty who had just opened a roomy, inexpensive office on the rue de Béthisy. Seeing Oscar’s gaunt look and curt manners, Desroches set his head clerk, Monsieur Godeschal, to train him so he would not lose time before law school and would master the Code. Oscar was lodged in a small attic beside Godeschal’s room; Desroches intended to make him a capable clerk.

For two years Oscar lived at the office under exacting, petty discipline. Under Godeschal’s strict oversight he kept a monk-like routine: rising at five to prepare for the day, running errands, studying the Code by rigorous tests, returning to office tasks after classes, and subsisting on plain meals. His social life was limited to monthly breakfasts with his uncle Cardot, Sundays with his mother, and rare outings arranged by Moreau. Attempts at elegance brought disapproval from his superiors.

Godeschal counseled a modest wardrobe and frugal living, insisting a good clerk should not spend more than seven hundred francs a year — a rule he himself followed. He combined rigid expectations with practical humanity: when a colleague failed, Godeschal discreetly guided him to resign rather than face public dismissal, protecting both the man and the firm’s reputation.

By twenty, Oscar was the third clerk, often performing the second’s duties without pay but receiving room and board. His legal knowledge surpassed many licensed graduates, and he began arguing cases in chambers. Yet he felt constrained by a life at odds with his desires: he envied well-dressed young men and longed for freedom and pleasure, feelings he habitually suppressed. Godeschal, aware of these struggles, tried to shield him and sometimes paid for outings; Moreau supported him but could not connect as Godeschal did. Both feared youthful follies might imperil his future, though they hoped to steer him aright. Godeschal even suggested Oscar’s talents might suit a barrister more than a solicitor’s clerkship.

In November 1825 a new clerk, Frédéric Marest, arrived. Well-to-do and aiming for the magistracy, Marest professed a desire for practical experience. The clerks amused themselves with practical jokes: Godeschal kept a fictitious register of receptions for new clerks filled with absurd entries. When Oscar asked for it, Marest read and laughed, then said he would consult a cousin who was a head clerk — a remark that confirmed his inexperience to Godeschal. Despite office camaraderie, Oscar’s inner conflict persisted.

The next day Georges Marest, now head-clerk to the notary Hannequin, recalled mischief that had led to his transfer and invited Oscar and the clerks to the Rocher de Cancale and an evening with Madame la Marquise de las Florentinas y Cabirolos, promising high society. Desroches interrupted to assign Oscar the crucial task of fetching a legal document from the Palais and warned him to beware of Derville, who might obstruct the errand. Eager to prove himself as second clerk, Oscar accepted.

At the lavish, flirtatious, and intoxicated breakfast that followed, Oscar played vingt-et-un with Florentine (in truth Mademoiselle Agathe-Florentine Cabirolle, a celebrated danseuse supported by Père Cardot) and Fanny Beaupré. He lost the money meant for the legal document, accepted Florentine’s offered thousand francs, and then lost everything. Despairing, he fell into stupor and slept in the boudoir while the party continued into the early morning.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
A loud noise woke clerk Oscar Husson the next morning. His uncle Cardot scolded him for late-night revelry; the danseuse Florentine revealed Oscar as Cardot’s nephew and urged the uncle to help. Oscar admitted he had lost his employer Monsieur Desroches’s gambling money; Cardot reluctantly lent five hundred francs, warning it would be his last aid.

Mariette sent a note and money while Godeschal, another clerk, hurried to fetch documents for Desroches. Enraged, Desroches resolved to dismiss Oscar despite Godeschal’s defense. Godeschal recounted Oscar’s past mistakes and blamed the bad influence of Georges Marest; Desroches half-joked that Oscar might take up law to flatter his vanity.

At home, Clapart berated his wife about their son’s failures; Madame Clapart defended him. Oscar confronted his father asserting independence but was ignored. Determined to take responsibility, he faced conscription—Clapart believed military service would instill discipline. Oscar drew a number and, three days later through the Comte de Serizy’s influence, was drafted into the cavalry.

Overwhelmed, Madame Clapart turned to religion. In the army Oscar applied himself; the rigors of service steadied his character and he gradually rose in rank to lieutenant‑colonel. Years later a decorated Oscar stood with his mother outside the Lion d’Argent; they encountered a diminished Georges Marest. Oscar married Georgette Pierrotin, his earlier errors having become lessons that, with connections and discipline, brought respect and security.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Balzac dedicates the book to a Polish lady of contrasts—childlike in faith, aged by sorrow, inspiring through love—whose inner poetry he feels unworthy to match but seeks to echo modestly.

CHAPTER I. THE CHALET
In October 1829 the notary Monsieur Simon Babylas Latournelle walks from Le Havre to Ingouville with his vain wife, their son Exupère (bound for Paris to study law), and his head clerk Jean Butscha to visit the Mignons. Latournelle coaches Exupère in a ruse: after greeting Mademoiselle Modeste Mignon, Exupère will slip aside to tell Monsieur Dumay “the young man has come,” a signal meant to provoke romantic interest. Butscha, a hunchback who adores Modeste, joins them. Ingouville, a merchant suburb, contains the Chalet, the Mignons’ cottage with a garden and prized hothouse. Jean François Bernard Dumay, the family’s loyal cashier, holds the lease; the new proprietor, Monsieur Vilquin, resents this and offers money to vacate. Dumay refuses—determined above all to protect Modeste from suitors.

CHAPTER II. A PORTRAIT FROM LIFE
The Latournelles find Gobenheim, a pale, serious banker, already visiting; his attentions are worldly. The household is tense: Dumay prepares to confront any man who might compromise Modeste’s honor. Blind Madame Mignon remains the dignified center while Modeste, embroidering, becomes the focus of unspoken anxieties. Dumay’s vigilance promises an evening that will test Modeste’s feelings.

CHAPTER III. PRELIMINARIES
Once a soldier, Dumay became the Mignons’ devoted cashier after their misfortunes. His fidelity to the exiled Colonel Mignon never falters. Dumay’s childless wife treats Modeste as her own; their devotion shapes the household’s protective tone.

CHAPTER IV. A SIMPLE STORY
A business collapse and the Colonel’s exile to Siberia reduced the family to the Chalet. Dumay and Latournelle work to settle debts; blind Madame Mignon, weakened by grief, depends on them. Modeste’s innocence and growing beauty alarm Dumay, who enforces strict guardianship to preserve her reputation amid poverty.

CHAPTER V. THE PROBLEM STILL UNSOLVED
Modeste’s attractiveness fuels speculation; the women watch for signs of love, fearing exploitation. Dumay vows no man will approach without his knowledge.

CHAPTER VI. A MAIDEN’S FIRST ROMANCE
Shaped by books and her sister’s tragedy, Modeste lives in imagination and longs to be a poet’s muse. She writes to the poet Canalis, pouring out her heart and seeking connection.

CHAPTER VII. A POET OF THE ANGELIC SCHOOL
Canalis reads Modeste’s letter with amusement and condescension, recognizing her innocence and wary of being misconstrued. His flattering but cautious reply warns against romanticizing the poet’s life. Disappointed yet determined, Modeste resolves to press the correspondence, setting the stage for conflict between her hopes and social limits.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
To Monsieur de Canalis:

Monsieur, you are a great poet and an honest man. After your frankness to a girl on the verge of despair, answer me truly: would you have replied the same had you known Mademoiselle O. d’Este M. has six million francs and seeks a fool for a husband? Be frank. I am wiser than my years; nothing candid can harm you. If you speak openly, I will answer your first letter.

Ernest de La Brière read this and paced the boulevards with conscience and curiosity at war. To Parisians it might have looked like flirtation; to a man of honor it felt like judgment. Truth accused him of hypocrisy; justice defended a girl’s revolt against mercenary marriage; honor demanded sincerity. He delayed, then wrote:

Mademoiselle, you are not a D’Este. The name is a disguise. Do I owe truth to someone who lies about herself? Are you noble or of the people? Ethics remain, but obligations vary with rank. A daughter of Este with six million may candidly confess a fondness for poetry; a girl of the people would be mocked. What class are you? Answer plainly, and I will reply to your question.

Modeste answered:

To Monsieur de Canalis, you grow sensible, my dear poet. My father is a count; we have a cardinal among us, and I am the last of our name. We are equal to the Canalis. You will excuse my refusing to send our arms. Answer me as truthfully as I have answered you. I await your response, to know if I may sign myself, Your servant, O. d’Este M.

La Brière smiled at her mischief. Paris had taught him intrigue. He booked a seat to Havre, blamed delay on duties, and obtained a note from the post-office director to ensure secrecy. He watched Francoise Cochet collect letters and followed her discreetly to Ingouville, where he glimpsed Modeste at a chalet window. Struck by her beauty, he learned the estate belonged to Monsieur Vilquin, the richest merchant in Havre. The Vilquins were immensely wealthy; one daughter married M. Althor, and there were whispers of fashionable visitors and a possible match with a young duke.

Having seen enough to dream, La Brière resumed the correspondence, regretting the reconnaissance but unable to resist curiosity. He composed a letter shaped by conscience and passion; Modeste read it at her window on a fine summer day.

Mademoiselle, without hypocrisy: had I known your wealth I would have acted differently. Men often confuse happiness with means; money becomes a religion. Faced with such a prize, a young man may court fortune for social gain, flatter, perhaps lose esteem for you while winning yours for the wrong reasons. Honesty compels me to say this.

Your letter forced deep reflection—on you and on myself. You provoked base thoughts yet drew forth something generous. I went to Havre, followed Francoise to Ingouville; you are as beautiful as a poet’s dream. I blush at such spying and stopped. You have altered my opinion: you are not merely a woman but a poetic Ideal. Exceptional souls may transcend the crowd’s laws. If your letter sprang from lofty reveries and the wish for friendship beyond ordinary relations, you are an exception.

Accept my thanks for forcing me to sound my heart. You corrected my false idea that marriage should be for fortune. I swore to make my own fortune and not choose a wife from cupidity. I reproach myself for the curiosity you provoked. You are not worth six million; such a fortune could not be hidden in Havre. I now fix my rule: seek a partner made by God; do not marry a dunce. If I were rich and you poor, I would lay my heart and fortune at your feet; you seem loyal and rich in soul.

Modeste felt delight at touching a noble soul, joy that worth might still meet genius, and triumph at following her instinct. She altered her concealment, giving Francoise envelopes of her own so letters could be slipped back into the box, and resolved to meet the postman herself.

Ernest de La Brière received another letter five days later that revealed more than analysis could infer.

To Monsieur de Canalis:

My friend, I call you that; you have delighted me. I would not change your image from your first letter—oh, may it not be the last! Who but a poet could so delicately excuse and understand a girl on the brink?

I admit with joy I am not the Vilquin nor the withered d’Herouville; my ancestors are respectable and my precautions stand—I will remain veiled. As to my person and belongings, be easy: I am at least as beautiful as the girl whose light you saw, and not poverty-stricken. I have seen the humiliating comedy of heiresses hunted; make no attempt to find me. I am free yet watched—by myself and by resolute people who would not hesitate to stab a man who threatened my retreat. I do not say this to quicken your courage; I merely warn you.

Now to your sermon. When I read your distrust, I thought the Muses may have led you astray. You are safe with me: I love poetry but do not affect hypocritical airs. Should I ever bid you come, I promise no old maid or poor wretch will appear. I regret that you came to Havre; you lowered the charm of the secret meeting I dreamed—God alone knew the treasure I reserved for a man noble enough to come. You spoiled it, but I forgive you; Paris breeds a man in every poet.

Do you think me gullible? When your first letter reached me I did not toss it aside; my net was cast by a fisherman expecting a miraculous catch. You have my approval about family. The man who pleases me will have my heart with my parents’ consent; I will neither grieve nor surprise them. I am guarded by devotion and disdain for calculating motives. I worship the ideal without being romantic; but I know social truths.

For now we must be friends. Your person may be unknown, but your mind and heart please me; an infinity of thoughts in me needs a man of genius to share them. I do not want my poem wasted; I want it known to you as to God. A true comrade’s confidences are precious; accept these leaflets of a young girl’s thoughts. Later, if our souls match, some white-haired servant may guide you to whatever dwelling fate holds for us—but not now. Come to me when you are unhappy; I have balms for your ills. I am twenty but feel fifty; I have known through another the horrors and delights of love and have no illusions but a religion and beliefs. Whoever I marry—if I choose him—will find me faithful and constant. I will not follow my husband blindly; I will be the divinity of his hearth. But I must test and choose the man who will be life’s companion.

Your letter was precious. Yes, it held necessary truths for family life. Act as an honest man, think as a poet, love as women love—that is what I long for. Adieu, my friend. I am poor at present; that is why I hide. I have read your verses in the Revue with delight now that I know your inner loftiness. Will it grieve you that a young girl prays for you, that you are her solitary thought? Send me the counterpart of these pages; your confidences—if full and true—will make me happy.

O. d’Este M.

La Brière, surprised at his absorption, wondered if he was already in love. She thought him the great poet; could he sustain the deception? The charm of the unseen—obscurity that stirs fancy—became an obsession. He answered with a studied letter where passion, pride, and caution mingled.

Mademoiselle,—Is it loyal to enthrone yourself in a poor poet’s heart while reserving the right to abandon him if he disappoints? Your confidence leaves little of yourself but much charm. I offer humble remonstrances.

Do you believe letters—brief, momentary expressions—can replace knowledge gained by daily life? Man is dual: an interior life of heart and a material life. Letters may suffice for spirit, but cannot reconcile both. A solitary, educated, chaste homage consoles literary men, and I thank you as one equal might thank another.

But what next? I am neither Byron nor a colossus. I might have vanity, discordant character, and ambition; I need a career. I have modest means and the external bearing of an ordinary man. I can be neither martyr nor hero. Your project is perilous: if you make yourself the worshiper of a man of genius and keep his memory alive forever, you may live in his glory; if you attach that ideal to me now, my modest gifts may disappoint you. If we continue, I might love you wildly—but if I pleased you, the tale would likely end in common fashion: marriage, house, children. Therefore, adieu.

To Monsieur de Canalis:

My friend, your letter gives me both pain and pleasure; perhaps someday all will be pleasure. I want the soul’s answers you seek from God. Cannot the friendship of Mademoiselle de Gournay and Montaigne be revived in us? Remember Sismondi’s household or the Marquis de Pescaire’s; can two hearts vibrate in unison across distance? Man is harp, musician, and listener. Will you find me jealous like ordinary women? I know you go into the world; I know your beauties and wits. Yet I seek a flower that blooms on Alpine peaks—men of genius—and I wish to cultivate it. I am no common placid creature; I would not belong to a ninny or merchant who could ruin us. My father adores my wishes and will not oppose them. If I please my poet and he pleases me, we will build love high and secure.

I am an eaglet; I want to be imprisoned by love as I am now by my father’s will. A young imagination locked in a tower longs to run in its neighbor’s park—your genius. Parents arrange marriages by appearances; a young girl is often denied choice. I want a long marriage of souls before any legal pledge. My mother followed instinct and beauty and prospered; I follow mine. I will not bind us prematurely to vulgar forms. If our correspondence reveals true sympathy, we may be spared common follies and reach graveside hand in hand. I await your orders, monseigneur, and am with all my heart,

Your handmaiden, O. d’Este M.

To Mademoiselle O. d’Este M.—You are a witch and I love you! Is that what you desire, most original girl? Perhaps you amuse provincial leisure with a folly to make a poet act rashly. If so, you have done wrong. Your letters have mischief. But I am no longer master of myself; my life depends on your answer. If a certainty of unbounded affection that disregards social conventions touches you—if you will let me seek you—say so. If your reply is favorable I change my life; I shall renounce the trivial pleasures we call happiness. For me, happiness is a fusion of feeling, a harmony of souls, an imprint of a noble ideal on daily life—a constancy of heart more precious than mere fidelity. We may call such devotion sacrifice, but is it not the dream of poets and maidens? I cannot speak more of myself—my past nor my filial affection, which you have altered. Say one word and I will love you until death. Either write no more or give me hope. Let me see you, or let me go. Must I bid you adieu? Will you permit me to sign myself,

Your Friend?

To Monsieur de Canalis,—What flattery! How quickly the grave man becomes Leander! To what should I attribute such change—my ink, your fancy, or the memory of the girl at the window? Your letter has given me elating pleasures beyond family affection. What are blood ties compared to those forged by mysterious sympathies? Bless you for the happiness. You explain social injustices: glory dazzles men; God gives love to women to soothe them. I have felt my mission and you confirm it.

Sometimes I wake in inexpressible sweetness; after your feverish letter I felt heavenly freshness, destined to die for one I love. Your poem “The Maiden’s Song” paints these moments; I think you worthy to be me. Your letter lets me read within you: I have guessed your struggles, curiosity, and plans, but do not yet know you well enough. Shall we make a compact? Write your life briefly—tell me what you do in Paris day by day, as to an old friend—and then I will see you. This is a great step.

This involves my life and my parents’—they must find a son in you. To what extent can superb spirits bend to family yokes and endure little miseries? You said Goethe and Byron were egoists; you erred. Great minds are slaves of ideas; their visible egoism often hides abnegation. A man who gives gifts to his epoch is not simply selfish. The poet’s life needs vast organization to bear pleasures and sorrows; consider Molière, whose private suffering darkens his comedy.

I place you among those whose generosity borders on the divine. Had I found self-interest where I now find noble flowers, I would have been anguished. I once met disappointment before sixteen; what would have become of me had I learned at twenty that fame is a lie? I should have surrendered to my father and become resigned to a dull life. But I have you; I believe in you. That belief straightens my thoughts and fancies. Sometimes I wish I were already in the middle of the book we are beginning; I feel persistence, strength to love, constancy tempered by reason, a heroism for duty.

If you could follow me to the exquisite retreat I fancy, if you knew my plans, you might not call me foolish. I wish to be an inexhaustible spring for the twenty years nature allows me to shine; to vary happiness, put intelligence into tenderness, and give piquancy to fidelity. I wish to be courageous for my friend, to abnegate for the nest like no bird can. Tell me, do you now think me to blame for my first letter? The mysterious wind of will drove me to you. In your letter I hold your cry: “God wills it.”

Ah, you will call me a chatterbox; the people around say I am taciturn.

O. d’Este M.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Desplein’s visit moved Modeste; guests praised Mignons as the salon debated his “genius.” Canalis dismissed it as mere application, but Modeste defended a broader view, exposing Canalis’s vanity. Her flirtation with Canalis provoked La Brière; by defying her father she showed independence, deepened his jealousy, rejected his grand gestures, and asserted herself. After the hunt and Duc’s arrival she turned to him. Her beauty and fortune revealed suitors’ motives; she compromises between love and ambition.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Balzac, famed for detailed character studies and everyday life, seldom based characters on real people. In “The Great Man of the Provinces in Paris” some figures recall actual persons; in Beatrix he borrows recognizable traits: Mademoiselle des Touches (Camille Maupin) suggests George Sand, while Beatrix, Conti, and Claude Vignon evoke the Comtesse d’Agoult, Liszt, and critic Gustave Planche. The opening scene records the customs of an old Breton family—a vanished social order—and marks a shift toward modernity, making it distinctive within Balzac’s Comedy of Human Life.

I. A BRETON TOWN AND MANSION

Some Breton towns remained little touched by nineteenth‑century progress because poor links to Paris insulated them, so new civilization was viewed as spectacle and older customs persisted. A traveler seeking bygone types would find traces of different eras across the provinces; many feudal towns declined and escaped historians, yet their memory endures.

Guérande exemplifies preserved feudal life: walled and moated, with battlements and gates. When the mayor planted poplars along the moat there was outcry—the promenade had long been public. Unmodernized houses and quiet streets recall past centuries. The public square still shows traditional Breton costumes, sharply contrasting bourgeois and noble styles; social divisions remain rigid and revolutionary fervor failed to erase them. Fervently Catholic and self‑contained, Guérande resists modern ideas.

Overlooking famed salt‑marshes and linked to France by two slow roads, the town’s sluggish governance reinforces isolation. Near the church stands the du Guaisnic mansion—crested archways, stables, a carved hall with a massive fireplace, a dining room hung with fourteenth‑century tapestries, modest family rooms, and a walled garden—testimony to enduring heritage.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Early in May 1836 the du Guenic household comprised Baron and Baroness du Guenic, the baron’s elder sister Mademoiselle Zéphirine, and their only son Calyste, twenty-one. The baron, Gaudebert-Calyste-Charles, was a veteran of the Vendée and Brittany insurrections who had fought with Charette and La Rochejaquelein; prudently he had sold his property to his sister before the war. After years in the resistance he returned to Guérande in 1813, married Fanny O’Brien, an Irish noblewoman, and Calyste was born the day Louis XVIII landed.

Now seventy-three, the baron still bore the marks of his turbulent life: tall, wiry, deeply wrinkled, with scarred hands. Illiterate except for his prayer book, he dressed plainly, wore the cross of Saint-Louis, and combined serenity with somnolent gravity. Household talk centered on the exiled Bourbons, the Catholic faith, and Brittany’s future; Calyste was their sole hope. The baron trained his son strictly and once took him to join the Duchesse de Berry’s attempted restoration, returning after three months to remark, “The barons did not all do their duty,” a blend of pride and lingering obligation.

That evening the baron dozed in his armchair while his wife read the paper. Fanny O’Brien, forty-two, retained an Irish beauty and warmth—refined, elegant, ringleted—and a tender manner that softened her husband’s austerity. Nearby, blind Mademoiselle Zéphirine knitted; proud and cheerful, she refused cataract surgery to avoid burdening her brother and shared a close bond with the baroness. As the room warmed at sunset, Mariotte lit the lamp and Gasselin tended the horses and dogs—longtime retainers whose steady loyalty, quietly overseen by Zéphirine, embodied the household’s duty-bound cohesion.

When night fell, Gasselin asked if he was needed. The baron, roused, told him he could go out or to bed after prayers unless Madame or his sister required him. The two ladies consented, and Gasselin knelt with the family while Zéphirine led the prayer. A knock followed; Gasselin opened for Monsieur le curé.

Monsieur Grimont, a middle-aged priest in a cassock, arrived with the civility of his office. His plump, calm face and jovial air suggested a clear conscience; though well regarded in Guérande, he acknowledged the du Guenics’ feudal superiority. He inquired about Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel’s tardiness and mentioned the young chevalier’s presence at Les Touches that evening. Mariotte cautioned against speaking of the chevalier’s visits in front of Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, who loved town gossip.

Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel arrived next, thin and elderly, dressing in the fashion of her youth and carrying keys and a silver snuff-box. Of the highest Breton nobility, her family’s influence had waned; she ran her estate with strict frugality, her avarice attracting nieces eager for favor. Jacqueline de Pen-Hoel, a friend of Zéphirine, intended to marry one niece to Calyste to bind the families. As the rector fetched a basket and counters for the evening game, a firm knock announced the Chevalier du Halga: frail, dressed for his health, once a competent naval officer, now gentle and preoccupied with his little dog. The rector’s cough during the prayer for the king quietly hinted at the political tensions that still lingered in the town.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Mouche is a five-card trick-taking game: each player gets five cards and one card is turned up as trump. Players may play or drop out; dropping costs only the stake. Winning a trick shares the pot; failing makes one a mouche, who must pay the full stake and enlarge the pot. Cards may be exchanged from the remaining deck but only in order; the dealer keeps the turned-up trump and may swap it for any card in hand. The highest card is Mistigris, the knave of clubs.

Simple yet absorbing, mouche at the Hôtel du Guénic exposes greed, cunning, and expression. Players start with twenty counters each—five sous apiece—so the stake is five farthings. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoël and Mademoiselle Zéphirine treat the game gravely, likening it to finance or speculation. A convention from September 1825 allows a player who loses ten sous to quit, but the chevalier and the baron undercut this by lending counters to keep play going. The arrival of Demoiselle de Kergarouët and her aunt raises stakes: the young women, besotted with Calyste, pay little heed to small sums.

Evenings are punctuated by local and public talk. Players sometimes forget stakes, provoking accusations of cheating—especially aimed at the rector. Nights can end abruptly, with departures empty-handed and grumbling. Attempts to introduce other games like whist and Boston fail; the circle prefers mouche, which outlasts newer diversions.

One night, while the rector deals, conversation moves from the chevalier’s health to the Duc de Lenoncourt’s death and mortality. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoël announces the Comtesse de Kergarouët’s marriage and sparks gossip about Mademoiselle des Touches, an actress of questionable repute. The baroness and the rector exchange concerned looks—muted when Pen-Hoël holds Mistigris.

After the game the chevalier escorts Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoël home. She confides that Calyste seems besotted with Mademoiselle des Touches; the baroness fears he will be led astray. The rector notes Pen-Hoël intends to leave her fortune to the niece who marries Calyste and warns that the actress is a dangerous, scandal-prone influence. Distressed, the baroness laments her eroding bond with Calyste; the rector promises to investigate. She accompanies him to the gate, anxiously awaits Calyste’s return, and instead hears the rector’s footsteps fade into the night.

Returning home, the baroness finds the town already aware that Calyste frequently visits Mademoiselle des Touches—visits she had assumed private. She had even tried to entice him by taking up needlework, but maternal jealousy failed; Calyste spent more time there and once came home as late as midnight. She wonders what secret charm holds men captive to Mademoiselle des Touches, making them forget family and duty, and longs to meet her and judge her character.

Calyste, of Breton and Irish lineage, was carefully raised; the baroness entrusted his education to the rector of Guérande, believing him sheltered from impure thoughts. Yet his schooling left him ignorant of contemporary literature and science, and he remains a fervent Catholic. The baroness had envisioned a modest, secure life for Calyste—a suitable marriage to a wealthy heiress aided by an expected inheritance. Those plans now seem threatened by Mademoiselle des Touches. When he returns at midnight she notes his fatigue and emotional turmoil, which she attributes to excitement rather than deeper trouble, though bitterness grows as she realizes his affections lie elsewhere.

The next day Calyste sleeps late; at dinner his attire suggests another visit to Les Touches. The baroness and her sister voice concern at his changed behavior, blaming the actress’s influence and morals. Calyste defends her: she rejected his romantic advances and offered friendship, and he insists she is a talented artist deserving respect. The baron dismisses his wife’s fears as youthful alarm. Meanwhile Guérande buzzes with rumors that Mademoiselle des Touches exerts a mysterious hold over Calyste; the rector and townsfolk disapprove.

Mademoiselle Félicité des Touches, known as Camille Maupin, is a celebrated writer whose life provokes curiosity and scandal. Orphaned during the Revolution and raised by an aunt, she cultivated a keen intellect through wide reading. Rejecting traditional marriage to preserve her independence, she made her salon a hub for artists and thinkers. Drawn to Calyste’s youthful innocence, their relationship awakens private longings that conflict with her public ambition and determination to remain autonomous; she must weigh love against the life she has built.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
A few hundred yards from Guérande, Brittany’s soil gives way to salt marshes and dunes. The road drops into a pale landscape of ponds and a narrow arm of sea that separates the mainland from Croisic, a peninsula reached by treacherous quicksand beaches.

At the junction to Guérande stands a country house among wind-sculpted pines. Built of slaty stone and granite, its plain exterior—regular windows and a high roof—faces the road on one side and the desert toward Croisic on the other. A brook runs from the park, crosses the road, and disappears into the sands.

Les Touches, harmonizing in gray with its surroundings, yields over ten thousand francs a year from marshes and farms. It lost feudal rights in the Revolution yet is still called “the château” by local salt workers. Félicité restored the house while preserving its desolate aspect, marking the entrance only with brick gate columns.

The ground floor follows many old country houses’ plans: Félicité turned the entrance hall into a billiard room that opens to a large salon and dining room; the kitchen connects to the dining room through an office. Décor is simple and graceful—gray-painted walls, mahogany furniture draped in green silk. The dining room holds mahogany buffets, horsehair chairs, and engravings by Audran.

Upstairs, Félicité occupies a suite with a salon, a large bedroom, and two cabinets; another suite serves guests. Guest rooms are modest, but her apartment reveals luxurious taste—Gobelin tapestries, heavy silk drapes, and fine Louis XV furniture. Her study, furnished in modern mahogany and filled with intimate objects, contrasts with the bedroom’s opulence and hints at her complex character.

Outside, salt marshes, dunes, and the distant ocean create a melancholic yet elevating atmosphere; Camille prefers Les Touches’ desolation to Guérande’s lushness. Calyste, seeking refuge from Guérande’s confinements, persistently clings to Félicité—rooted in admiration and a longing to love—despite her earlier rejection.

At Les Touches Calyste encounters rich art and nineteenth-century music and literature that awaken dormant sensibilities. Félicité nurtures him like a maternal figure, giving music lessons and filling the house with creativity. On one visit he hears her improvising at the piano; the music stops as he approaches and resumes when he finds her in tears, anxious over Claude Vignon’s absence. He offers to search for Claude, but she asks him to stay, calling her tears a natural overflow and preparing to tell of their fraught relationship—its age difference, the inevitability of separation, and why she once rejected Calyste. He sits at her feet, eager to support her yet unaware of the full depth she will reveal.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“I promised you this tale of the past, and here it is,” begins Camille. The letter’s author is the Marquise de Rochefide. The old marquis, after marrying his eldest daughter to a Portuguese grandee, urged his son to secure a peerage by wedding nobility. The Comtesse de Montcornet proposed Mademoiselle Béatrix-Maximilienne-Rose de Casteran, the marquis’s youngest, of the ancient, dowry-less Casteran line reserved for the male heir. Béatrix, raised at the château de Casteran, was twenty when in 1828 she married. Camille warns that Béatrix’s originality, independence, ardor, and love of art ennoble her but expose her to risks women face even more dangerously than men.

The match pleased the Rochefides: it linked them to an old family and briefly aided a peerage for their son under Charles X, later undone by the July Revolution. Early married life proved perilous. Rochefide mistook Béatrix’s reserve for frigidity and resumed bachelor habits; society praised him for a wife who ornamented his salon without demanding attention. His vanity bred jealousy and a petty tyranny when Béatrix seemed indifferent to his infidelities. Camille uses this to explain Béatrix’s later behavior: admiration curdled into jealousy, and her attempt to found a salon produced only shallow company because she lacked time and experience to form a true circle. Beneath those missteps lay a grand soul—regal pride and an intellect versed in metaphysics and music.

Béatrix was striking: slender, fair, with a long pointed face, noble forehead, pale sea-green eyes and severe lines that, when well dressed, could seem nearly divine. Camille teases young Calyste not to be deceived—by the time he notices her charms it will be too late.

She spent early marriage quietly appraising men and life. Revolutionary disturbances bored her at her husband’s country estate. Returning to Paris after the July Revolution, she explored new doctrines but found the regime left no place for individual superiority; she chose personal happiness over abstract ideals. Then she met Gennaro Conti, a Neapolitan composer whose voice, manner, and appearance suggested genius. Conti proved duplicitous and self-serving beneath the mask of talent. Camille, devoted to art yet clear-eyed about Conti’s character, warned Béatrix. Blinded by passion, Béatrix was undeterred. Conti rejoiced; Camille even lent him money for their journey to Italy. Béatrix left a note for her husband, departed with Conti, spent two years abroad, and wrote enchanting letters to Camille, full of adoration for the only woman she felt understood her.

At this point Claude Vignon appears and the company falls quiet. Imposing despite a haggard look—high forehead, firm sarcastic mouth—Claude is complex: capable of deep original thought yet often paralyzed by it, inclined to criticism that destroys rather than builds; his intellect is sharp and indifferent to worldly life. His dry, brusque remarks unsettle the group. When Mademoiselle des Touches asks how he came, he replies simply, “By the door.” His sardonic asides about sentiment offend Calyste, who leaves angry and pitying Félicité.

Back home Calyste’s mother is delighted by his arrival. He produces Béatrix’s letter, which threatens domestic calm. In it Béatrix describes Italy’s weariness amid constant enjoyment, the strain of life with Conti, a sense of diminished social equality, and the sacrifices made for love. She contrasts her constrained marital position with Camille’s freedom, longs for solitude, admires Italy’s beauty, and laments financial worries likely to force their return to Paris. Camille presents the correspondence as a blend of exaltation and disillusionment.

Folding the letter, Calyste is melancholy. His mother notices and questions him. He confesses that Félicité has opened his eyes to the insufficiency of his education and shown him the possibility of a nobler life. His aunt proposes a safe match with a good Breton girl; Calyste rejects such prudence, yearning instead for the freedom and passion he associates with love. His mother, understanding rather than scolding, embraces him and counsels: pursue happiness but temper your dreams with prudence and a moral aim.

After dinner Calyste returns to Les Touches, where Claude and Félicité remain. Claude and Félicité’s witty exchange—his cynicism against her generosity—captivates Calyste. Claude’s speculation about Béatrix and Conti’s return stirs Camille, and Calyste, imagining Béatrix as Félicité described her, feels deep sympathy. The scene closes on a tender family moment: encouragement blended with grounding wisdom, and a young man’s yearning for passion and purpose that will shape his future choices.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
What young man of restrained feeling would not hurry to see Madame de Rochefide land at Croisic? Calyste slipped out early, ashamed to be seen, and hid on the shore. He watched two boats; one carried a woman and a chest stamped "MME. LA MARQUISE DE ROCHEFIDE," which fixed his attention. A sudden attraction replaced his earlier ardor for Camille.

He overheard servants mention someone from Les Touches and learned the marquise had already been collected. Agitated, he hurried to Guérande, turning the new passion against the old. Returning late, he found a letter from Mademoiselle des Touches inviting him to celebrate the marquise’s arrival and reassuring his mother of the visitor’s noble birth.

Calyste arrived in turmoil. In Camille’s suite he encountered Gennaro Conti, a Byronic presence. The marquise reclined on a divan, surpassing his imagination: her hair braided with bluebells, exquisitely dressed. Hope and fear mingled. At dinner, charged to escort her, he fumbled; she noticed and he confessed he had never seen anyone so beautiful except his mother. Mademoiselle des Touches counseled restraint, warning the marquise could not return such fondness. Absorbed by Conti, the marquise turned away as Conti and Camille’s music moved Calyste to tears; he slipped to a sofa, overwhelmed and conscious of his inadequacy.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Calyste returned to find Claude Vignon waiting. Claude, who had seen more than he admitted, told Calyste the situation was not what it seemed: he believed Camille loved Calyste. Stunned, Calyste asked about Claude’s own feelings. Claude confessed a wounded pride—his melancholy walks had come from imagining Calyste preferred Camille. He planned to leave Les Touches, perhaps for Paris with Conti, warning that his departure would free passions to break. He cautioned Calyste that the choice before him was perilous: Beatrix was relentless, Camille noble and abstruse, and Calyste’s headlong feeling might prevent a prudent use of either. Disgusted with women yet clear-eyed, Claude advised caution: between those reefs Calyste might be wrecked.

Learning that Camille loved him while he loved Beatrix crushed Calyste. He replayed Mademoiselle des Touches’ earlier rejection, Claude’s return, and Beatrix’s salon laughter, silenced by the contradiction. At midnight Claude confronted Camille (Félicité), accusing her of arriving in Paris already in love with Calyste and of using Claude to hide her devotion. He called her scheme both grand and ignoble, saying she had mistaken him for a simple man when he had divined her. Camille asked why love fled her; Claude answered that she was not lovable in the ordinary way—her mind too deep, her charm abstract and overpowering, repelling strong men. She might briefly please youth like Calyste, but such force fatigued others. He suggested she was an anomaly and resolved to leave to spare her the shame of his presence as a screen, insisting they could part without pain because they had had no mutual happiness to mourn.

Calyste then revealed himself. Claude told him plainly that Camille loved him, not in a common way but with an exquisite pain of longing—an affection Claude cynically likened to masculine asceticism. Camille’s tears proved the truth. Claude accused her of driving Calyste toward Madame de Rochefide, saying Camille had dug her own grave and that if she had confided in him the suffering might have been avoided. Camille, moved, took Calyste’s head, kissed his hair, and begged him to forget what he had heard: “I count for nothing in all this,” she urged. Rising, she declared that conceiving love without hope brought one nearest God; she commanded him not to love her, yet vowed, “I will love you as no woman will!” Claude knelt and kissed her hand. She dismissed Calyste to spare his mother concern.

Calyste returned to Guerande distraught, glancing at Beatrix’s window on the way. That night he told his mother, tearful and abrupt, that Camille loved him but that he no longer loved her. The next day he posted a watcher on the road to Saint-Nazaire and rode out. A carriage arrived with two ladies and two gentlemen: Beatrix and Camille, with Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, the Vicomtesse de Kergarouet, and Charlotte. Camille invited the visitors into her carriage; the viscountess, pleased to meet Camille Maupin, accepted despite her elder sister’s misgivings. Charlotte, plain beside the Parisian women, greeted Calyste.

In the crowd Beatrix, elegant in straw and muslin, leaned on a parasol; Camille took Calyste’s arm. The viscountess expressed pity for Madame de Rochefide’s misfortunes; Camille explained her separation from Conti was by mutual consent, prompting sympathy. Calyste kissed Camille’s hand, leaving a tear; Beatrix, wiping anger from her eyes, looked away. Camille’s calm and grace soothed Calyste despite the earlier revelations.

At home the visitors dined amid lively games; Calyste’s sudden gaiety made everyone assume his troubles were over and that a match with Charlotte might prosper. Yet he remained inwardly troubled, comparing the Parisians to his childhood acquaintances. Only his mother Fanny noticed his unease and urged him to offer the viscountess a ride in Camille’s carriage; Calyste ran back to Les Touches to arrange it.

After dinner Camille teasingly accused him of abandoning the viscountess for them. When Beatrix suggested her niece might suit Calyste, Camille demurred: “The marriage will never take place.” Mortified by the marquise’s indifference to the Breton girl, Calyste stayed silent. Camille, seeing his discomfort and feeling herself stirred, yielded him her hand briefly and then sat at the piano to play melancholy variations.

Later, in private, Camille took Calyste into her chamber and gave candid counsel. She told him that if he wanted to win Beatrix he must appear to love Camille still; otherwise Beatrix would be lost. Loving and making oneself beloved were different arts, she said, and candor would not serve him. She proposed deliberate deception: promise blind obedience, spend every day with her until four, avoid private encounters with Beatrix, and let her use her art to direct the marquise’s feelings. Camille trusted her ability to fashion the marquise’s heart; in a week she boldly claimed Beatrix would love him. Desperate and naive, Calyste agreed and embraced Camille, who wept but did not stop him.

He left gaily, convinced of success, and returned to the card table where Charlotte believed victory assured. After he departed, Camille, alone and distraught, confessed to Beatrix the depth of her feeling: she was forty and in love; she had mourned youth and feared being loved only from pity. She admitted she might fling herself into the sea if Calyste asked it—an image of despair. Foreseeing how Beatrix’s coldness could secure Calyste for her, she pleaded with the marquise’s generosity and warned she too feared losing him. Yet she possessed a secret means to keep him and begged Beatrix’s help. Beatrix, piqued and curious, left with mixed feelings; Camille sank into tears and smoked opium-laced tobacco, brooding on the young lover’s head and her own pain.

Next morning Calyste returned before midday; Camille, pacing and watching the sea, told him to stay to dinner but to show Beatrix a look of regret when she passed—a signal to conceal his devotion. For six days Camille employed every conversational art and subtle deception—the feints and false magnanimities of a duel of hearts—aiming to conceal her love while revealing enough to inflame Beatrix’s pride and curiosity. Her remarks sometimes wounded Beatrix, who took offense at perceived distrust, yet Beatrix was secretly amused to find such a great writer caught in pettiness and resolved to show where Camille’s greatness ended.

Camille’s tactics included keeping Calyste constantly in her company, assigning him to study, and hinting at the marquise’s supposed indifference. She reported that Beatrix had written only once to Conti that week, implying a thaw. Calyste lived for the daily moments before Beatrix and read late into the night, bringing home armfuls of books. His mother, alarmed, implored him to leave Les Touches, but he refused. He nearly broke his promise and resolved to approach Beatrix privately in the garden one morning to read her eyes.

After a marsh outing, Calyste met Beatrix on a garden path. He trembled and asked if he had displeased her. She replied faintly that he had neither pleased nor displeased her. When he implored whether he was indifferent to her, she hinted indifference was proper given a “binding attachment” elsewhere. Calyste, stunned, declared that he had once loved Camille but loved Beatrix now. Beatrix, hurt yet composed, noted their constant mornings together and suggested Camille did not prefer a cigar to his company. Camille then appeared and rebuked Calyste: was this what he had promised? She took his arm and drew him away. The scene exposed Camille’s sacrifice: she forced Calyste into a reciprocity of appearances—an abnegation that could be read as noble or ruinous.

At breakfast Beatrix resolved to stifle any rising love and behaved with gentle indifference, following Camille’s counsel. Félicité proposed excursions, but Beatrix refused, saying her position was delicate and she would not jeopardize her happiness because Conti was coming to fetch her. Calyste took the news with pallor, left Les Touches depressed and near madness at the thought of losing Beatrix, and spent his time pondering a decisive explanation to win her.

Throughout these scenes strategy, pride, and genuine feeling intertwine. Claude, preparing to leave, remains the cynical observer who recognizes motives and predicts calamities. Camille, inwardly torn and capable of self-effacement, chooses to guide events through subtle deception, convinced she can shape Beatrix’s heart and preserve her claim to Calyste. Beatrix, proud and resolute, answers with polite aloofness that may hide deeper sentiment but insists on autonomy. Calyste, immature and ardent, is trapped between youthful passion and artful manipulation, a pawn in the hands of stronger wills whose consequences he cannot yet foresee.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
When Calyste returned home he shut himself away and wrote; his mother found him among crumpled pages composing a letter to Béatrix, driven by renewed distrust of Camille after the marquise’s conduct.

His first letter poured out an intense, almost feverish love: meeting Béatrix had awakened a passion beyond any dream. He called himself young and unworthy, likened himself to an insect drawn to her light, contrasted this passion with his former attachment to Félicité, invoked Camille’s teaching that true love must grow and endure, and begged for kindness, recognition, or even the mere right to accompany her. The confession moved his mother to tears and alarm.

Unsure how to deliver it, Calyste consulted the Chevalier du Halga—who suggested using Béatrix’s maid—but he chose to present it himself. At Les Touches he found Béatrix and Camille together and impulsively handed her the letter; she accepted it without hesitation.

After a sleepless night he awaited her reply in the garden. Béatrix acknowledged his noble feelings but called him a child and reaffirmed her commitment to Camille. She doubted she could be his partner, warned that accepting his love would insult Camille, and candidly admitted her jealous, capricious nature would likely make him unhappy, urging him to seek a younger, more fitting bride.

The baroness, moved, questioned Calyste and read Béatrix’s reply aloud—admiring Béatrix’s nobility but anxious for her son’s future. She urged him to marry Charlotte de Kergarouet to secure the estate; Calyste dismissed her counsel and even fantasized about elopement. Troubled, the baroness consulted the amused Chevalier and prayed; Calyste reassured her and they planned a boat trip to Croisic. The correspondence showed surprising maturity yet exposed the intensity and likely consequences of his infatuation.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
One of Beatrix’s chief pleasures was outwitting those she felt superior to, though she admired Camille Maupin’s talent and passion. While Calyste rushed to Les Touches intoxicated by first love, Beatrix enjoyed being adored without intending to return it. She prided herself on repressing feeling, imagining a noble sacrifice like Camille’s; French vanities flattered her and she felt morally upright in resisting Calyste.

The two women reclined in the salon, the calm garden outside at odds with their inner unrest. Camille, aware of the disturbance she’d caused, guarded her secret love and regarded Beatrix as a shrewd rival, seeking refuge in nature and reflection. Calyste seemed almost a guardian angel to her—yet Beatrix’s apparent triumph unnerved Camille.
