When Calyste arrived with a letter, Camille watched and perceived an unspoken understanding: Beatrix now treated Calyste as hers while he looked guilty and transparent. He announced plans for an excursion; Camille, suspecting a conspiracy, grew anxious. After he left to arrange the boat and later returned, neither woman met his eye; he departed for home heavy with despair, a stark contrast to his earlier joy—his mother noticed but he dismissed it. Beatrix found the letter stirring unexpected feelings; doubts about a plot with Camille provoked jealousy and anger.

Dinner was tense. Veiled insults and hostile looks culminated in confrontation: Beatrix accused Camille of trickery and cruelty; Camille exposed Beatrix’s motives. Beatrix attacked Camille’s lack of conventional femininity; Camille declared her deep love for Calyste. Beatrix retorted she would not be his mistress—only marriage would do. The remark wounded Camille, who broke off the friendship and threatened to write to Conti. Beatrix refused to yield. The night left both women reflective, their conflict unresolved and balanced precariously between desire and social constraint.

Late August brought clear skies and a shimmering sea. Paludiers harvested salt while the baron and baroness admired the marshes from the jetty. A party from Les Touches arrived: the marquise, followed by Calyste and Camille. The baroness felt a strong aversion to Beatrix, who, though tastefully dressed, seemed heartless. The marquise remained cool; Camille chastised Calyste for disobedience, and he was torn between anger and love for Beatrix.

On the wind-sculpted Croisic peninsula, Calyste led Beatrix to a viewpoint while Camille sought the rocks to reflect. Beatrix confessed an inner desolation and insisted she could never be with Calyste despite loving him: she feared abandoning her life and the pain of unfulfilled love. Calyste, desperate, offered to sacrifice everything, but she rejected sacrifice and reaffirmed her commitment to her situation.

At a rock crowned by a lone box bush, passion overwhelmed Calyste; in a moment of intimacy Beatrix slipped and fell. He rushed to save her; amid the confusion their feelings intensified. Help arrived, and Beatrix was carried to a farmhouse for care. Calyste stayed by her; moved by his devotion, Beatrix softened and accepted his vow of exclusive love—yet on returning to Les Touches they encountered Conti, shattering the moment and revealing the tangled complexities of love and loyalty.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The marquise’s distress was evident when Conti arrived, seized Béatrix’s arm and pulled her from Calyste, who felt humiliated and stunned. Convinced of his love, he was shaken by her visible anguish and fell silent.

Camille explained the tangled power dynamics: Béatrix could not refuse Conti because of lingering obligations, and Conti, opportunistic after an opera failure, used insinuations to regain control. He feigned indifference to Béatrix’s attachment while subtly undermining her confidence. At dinner his jovial manner masked intent; veiled remarks about women’s loyalty wounded both Béatrix and Camille. Béatrix later told Camille she felt helpless and even blamed Camille for Conti’s return, exposing deep rivalry. Béatrix insisted she would always occupy Calyste’s heart; Camille countered that such reigns were fleeting.

Conti and Calyste exchanged tense words. Conti posed as the injured party, professing disinterest and urging Calyste to pursue Béatrix so her pride would retaliate. Unaware of Conti’s design, Calyste confessed details of his relationship with Béatrix, inflaming Conti’s jealousy.

That evening Conti staged a salon performance to provoke Béatrix and assert dominance. Entranced by Conti’s singing, Calyste approached Béatrix with renewed confidence—walking into Conti’s trap. The next morning Béatrix left with Conti; Calyste was devastated. Camille informed him and tried to expose Béatrix’s true nature, but he clung to hope.

Weeks passed and his sorrow deepened. He wandered aimlessly; his family assumed melancholy was temporary and worried as his health and social life declined. Pressed to marry, he refused Charlotte, confessed his love for Béatrix, and begged that she be released. After the baron’s death Calyste sank further until Camille proposed helping him search for Béatrix in Paris; renewed by action, he agreed.

Félicité’s devotion had already shaped Calyste’s future. She praised him to the Duchesse de Grandlieu, sold a house for 2,500,000 francs, bought another for 700,000, and planned to use one million to recover the du Guenic estates, placing the remainder on Sabine de Grandlieu. Aware the duke and duchess sought suitable matches for their daughters and that only the youngest, Sabine, was marriageable, Félicité aimed to redirect Calyste’s affections toward her.

On the trip to Paris, Mademoiselle des Touches confided with the baroness, who prepared a new house for Sabine and Calyste. Welcomed at the hôtel de Grandlieu, they received proper respect. Mademoiselle des Touches urged Calyste to enjoy Paris while she searched for Béatrix abroad. The city’s bustle eased his obsession; noticing Sabine’s likeness to Béatrix, he grew drawn to her. By winter 1837 he seemed refreshed and listened to his mother’s proposal for Sabine, though he concealed any reluctance.

On the day of the marriage contract, Calyste surprised everyone by accepting Mademoiselle des Touches’s benefits but then reading a letter she had sent through Sabine. In it Camille confessed love for him, declared her intention to enter a convent, urged him to accept her gifts for his future welfare, and asked him to remember her kindly while placing duty above passion.

After reading the letter Calyste rejoined the others and the contract was signed. The wedding at Saint-Thomas-d’Aquin followed the next week; Calyste and Sabine departed for Brittany amid tears and congratulations. The duchess and Clotilde worried for Sabine, knowing Calyste had not married wholly by his own will and that a woman’s happiness in marriage was precarious. The Baronne du Guenic, with only one son, accepted responsibility for Sabine’s care.

On the journey the newlyweds felt awkward, each conscious of their first moments together. Calyste suggested they be like friends or siblings, unsettling Sabine. He reluctantly recounted his former love for Béatrix; Sabine was hurt that their union had arisen from his rejection of another, though she found some comfort in his promise to forget Béatrix. Gradually their relationship warmed.

In letters to her mother Sabine described Brittany, her longing for home, and the difficulty of loving Calyste fully. As châtelaine she felt fulfilled—adored by family and tenants—and embraced Breton duties. A convent visit to Mademoiselle des Touches yielded blunt advice: be capricious when necessary and never let Calyste see Béatrix again. Sabine felt conflicted—unable to feign caprice or fully control her feelings—trying to balance devotion, insecurity, social expectation, and identity to make the marriage endure.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Guerande, July 1838.

To Madame la Duchesse de Grandlieu:

Ah, my dear mother! After three months I know what jealousy is. My heart is torn between love and hatred; I feel betrayed and unloved. How fortunate to have you to confide in!

A few words from others were enough to kindle my curiosity and poison my peace: “Here’s the key to your palace; go everywhere, but avoid Les Touches!” Mademoiselle des Touches—Madame de Rochefide—has sown bitterness in me. Why forbid Les Touches? What happiness hinges on avoiding one house in Brittany? My mother-in-law said, “Les Touches belongs to you.” Aunt Zéphirine cried, “If Calyste had never set foot in Les Touches!” Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoël called it “a place of perdition.” The Chevalier du Halga said they had “saved her soul and enriched a convent.” My curiosity was tormented.

Calyste never proposed visiting the hermitage—now his property—but when I asked he said, “Let’s go,” and chance caught me. Les Touches is enchanting and deeply artistic—poisonous flowers can charm. I delighted in the place of horror, watching Calyste for signs that his old passion for Mademoiselle des Touches survived. I mentioned Madame de Rochefide; he begged me to stop reviving that passion. The plea inflamed me. He blushed when I spoke of forgiving past misrecognitions; I embraced him. Still, I left Les Touches and vowed never to return.

My hatred of Madame de Rochefide proves my love for Calyste. She haunts my dreams. The Visitation postulant was right—Les Touches is fatal. Calyste has revived past emotions there stronger than our joy. If Madame de Rochefide is in Paris, I shall stay in Brittany. Poor Mademoiselle des Touches might regret the role she played when she knew how our odious rival haunted me. I am ashamed—sometimes I wish to flee Guerande.

August 25: I am determined to live in the ruins of the old château. Calyste consents, either suspecting nothing or loving me not. I tremble at finding out. Oh, mother! I am not loved as I love. Calyste is charming—what man wouldn’t be amiable when adored by a pure, loving girl? Yet suspicion intrudes. By September I oscillate between despair and clinging joy, fretting whether his past has returned to claim him. Sister Clotilde’s melancholy wisdom haunts me: a woman faithful to the dead fears no rival. Poor me: I fear I shall never win Calyste back from himself.

A thousand tendernesses, dear mother. If my terrors are not delusions, Camille Maupin has sold me her fortune dearly. My affectionate respects to papa.

These letters reveal the private breach between husband and wife: Sabine imagined a marriage of mutual passion; Calyste accepted a conventional match. Their honeymoon joys misaligned with expectations.

In Brittany the hôtel du Guénic was restored and furnished under architect Grindot, supervised by Clotilde and the Duc and Duchesse de Grandlieu. The young couple planned to return to Paris in December 1838. Sabine took pleasure in settling rue de Bourbon—presiding over a great household and reading her family’s judgment of her marriage.

Calyste, indifferent, let Clotilde and the duchess guide his social life. He owed his standing to name, fortune, and alliance. Sabine, celebrated in Paris as one of its most charming women, found the season’s diversions and duties enlivening. Her pregnancy and the birth of a son in October 1839 confirmed a neutral, socially respectable union; Sabine mistakenly nursed the child herself, believing a mother should be wholly devoted.

By August 1840 Sabine was nearly finished nursing. In two years in Paris Calyste had shed the innocence of his early experience of passion. He associated with Duc Georges de Maufrigneuse, Vicomte Savinien de Portenduère, and others, keen to learn Parisian life. Wealth breeds idle hours; Paris provides amusements. Contact with husbands who abandoned their wives for cigars, whist, and clubs eroded domestic virtues. A proud wife may tolerate such dissipation as proof of her success.

One October evening, to escape the cries of their newly weaned child, Calyste went to the Variétés, urged by Sabine. He took a stall near the avant-scène. During the interlude he saw Madame de Rochefide in a proscenium box—Béatrix in Paris! The sight struck him like an arrow. He had sometimes even substituted Béatrix for his wife in thought; Béatrix embodied light, life, the Unknown; Sabine embodied duty and the expected.

Calyste’s first impulse was to leave. Yet the proscenium door was ajar and he approached. Béatrix sat between Canalis and Raoul Nathan. In three years her appearance had changed—gaunt, faded, eyes hollow—but Parisian artifice made her more poetically attractive to him. She greeted him with affected grief and coquettish nostalgia—complaining at his marriage and teasing him about “such a little fool.” She revived old intimacy, pressing his hand and turning tears into performance. She spoke of living modestly in a rue de Chartres house “suitable to my means,” filling her head with literature.

Calyste left with Béatrix’s perfume in his nostrils; for the rest of the evening she cast sidelong looks that tore at his soul. He returned home haunted: his apartments’ splendor reminded him of the things she had mocked, and he resented his wealth because it could not belong to her. He delighted in possessing a night to relive emotions and feared Sabine’s easy read of him—she loved so purely she could detect every quiver.

The next morning Calyste learned Sabine had watched the child through the night and was joyous to hear the child better. Yet he avoided breakfast, preferring a walk to his bachelor café. A man raised with sacred morals may still be drawn to a fallen, dangerous woman while his virtuous wife waits at home; vanity, the thrill of triumph, and the desire to revive a conquering youth can all play a part. Calyste was drawn to Béatrix for motives neither philosophy nor morals fully explain.

Consumed by the thought of seeing her again, he went to rue de Chartres. The house was familiar though he had never been there; the old footman asked his name. Calyste waited, gazing into a garden marked by rain-streaked stucco.

Béatrix, who had left fortune to her husband when she fled and now relied on small sums from her mother and friends, lived modestly yet tastefully in a house formerly owned by a lord, with servants and frugal comfort. Calyste entered rooms whose elegant relics—garnet velvet, celadon vases, a silver cup ascribed to Benvenuto Cellini—seemed like tokens of a past life. A little table held jeweled trinkets and a work-in-progress: a dagger handle used as a paper-cutter; watercolors showed bedrooms of her wandering life.

She appeared in studied attire, a gown revealing her bosom, hair in a lace cap, smiling as though expecting a lover. The perfume she wore recalled Les Touches; the scent, her dress, the glint in her eyes made Calyste dizzy. She bade him sit; she said she treated him as a friend and would be no one’s honest mistress, yet she spoke of the impossibility of belonging to any man because of what she had once expected of him. Three hours passed with her urging him to resolve his conjugal faith; she affected to see Sabine as a stiff, dry, brown woman—good, dutiful, untempting—hardly the ideal for a man like Calyste.

Pressed, Calyste rose to leave; she teased him to dine with her. He had the impulse to write Sabine a note, and when he began “My dear Sabine—” Béatrix snapped, “‘My dear’? Can you say your wife is still dear?” She accused him of cowardice and deceit and forced him to write that he dined out; she would send the note by her messenger. He returned home at two in the morning. Sabine, who had waited until half-past twelve for him, had gone to bed exhausted. She slept, disturbed by the terse note, yet made the charitable assumption that love explains everything.

The next morning the child was better. Sabine, smiling, came with the baby before breakfast; Calyste overplayed affection. In the salon she caught his face in a mirror and saw weariness. She asked, “What did you do yesterday?” He lied that Savinien had kept him to dinner and they went to the club to play whist. Sabine gently reproached the coldness of his first note; he blamed dandified club directors. Small suspicions accumulated: she remembered a paper she had thrown into her letter-box—perhaps it bore a coronet.

Calyste, planning to conceal his doings, rushed to Savinien and then back to rue de Chartres to see how Béatrix had passed the night. He found her after her bath, fresh and lively, breakfasting on eggs with wit and candor. He stayed until late; his horse returned sweating so that Sabine, watching from a window, noticed.

Meeting him on the staircase she said, “Abd-el-Kader is nearly foundered. You said you’d be gone a moment. I waited three hours.” Calyste, trying to recover, presented as a surprise a dressing-table being finished for her, with contributions from his mother and aunt. Sabine clasped him, faint with joy. Then, like a viper’s bite, she recoiled—she had smelled a perfume on his cravat: the scent of another woman’s note-paper. She had kissed where the rival’s kisses had been. Panic seized her: “Fetch the doctor and my nurse!” Calyste fled to call them.

Sabine, seized by a chill, lay shivering, crying, “I am dying!” Her maid fetched Madame de Portenduère. In feverish haste she wrote to her mother—a letter of thanks for the dressing-table, an attempt at ordinary gratitude—but her mind raced: “My God! my God! my God!” She feared betrayal after three years and twenty-two years of age; her teeth chattered, her face turned greenish. “I don’t know yet [the rival], but Calyste has told me two lies. Do not pity me. Oh! that letter of yesterday!” She seized the thrown-away paper from her letter-box: a coronet—could it belong to a marquise? She begged Ursula to find out if Madame de Rochefide had returned to Paris.

Ursula, stunned, went to the duchess, pledging secrecy. The duchess, who had seen such scenes, advised deceit for Sabine’s sake: “Friendship gives you an experience of a woman my age. I know how Sabine loves her husband; she might become insane.” “Or lose her beauty,” Ursula added. “Let us go to her.”

Fortuitously, they arrived before the accoucheur Dommanget and Calyste. The duchess told Sabine that Béatrix was not in Paris; as for Calyste’s night, she declared he had lost a great deal of money at cards and didn’t even know how to pay for the dressing-table. She scoffed at the coronet—“Everybody writes on coroneted paper now”—and burned the fatal sheet.

When Calyste and Dommanget arrived, the duchess informed them: “Sabine’s life is at stake; you have betrayed her for Madame de Rochefide.” Calyste blushed like a schoolboy. The duchess, with the physician, invented a lie to steer the diagnosis: Calyste had lost thirty thousand francs at cards. Dommanget, quick and self-interested, used the lie to explain Sabine’s fainting as hysterical symptoms due to overexertion from nursing and anxiety. The physician prescribed measures; Calyste sat at the foot of the bed, trying to look tender. Sabine, hearing the explanation, retorted that her husband could lose any sum without being lectured. Calyste embraced her and called her an angel.

Two days later, thought safe, Sabine improved. Calyste visited Madame de Rochefide, making a merit of his infamy.

“Béatrix,” he said, “you owe me happiness. I have sacrificed my poor little wife to you; she discovered everything. The fatal paper bore your name and coronet; I didn’t notice—the ‘B’ was erased. Your perfume betrayed me. Sabine nearly died; her milk went to her head; erysipelas set in; she may bear marks for life.”

Béatrix’s face froze. “So much the better,” she said; “perhaps it will whiten her for you.” Her icy cruelty continued in a series of sarcasms. There is no greater blunder than to speak of one’s virtuous wife to one’s mistress—unless it is to tell one’s wife of the mistress. Calyste had neither the Parisian training to lie to his wife nor the tact to handle his mistress. He begged Béatrix for pardon; she forced him to beg for two hours. She answered from an elevated bitterness: “To speak to me of your wife the day after my fall! Why did you not tell me she is a pearl of virtue?” She declared her love for his soul and then scorned Calyste’s looks.

This speech was part of Béatrix’s system: with each passion she became another being, advancing into profligacy. She had judged herself by the mirror, counting wrinkles and crow’s-feet; she knew her resources and used them. Reduced by Conti’s desertion and grateful for scraps from friends, she aimed at a terrible notoriety—vengeance through fame. To prevail over a splendid young woman, she used the arts of courtesans: contempt mixed with rare affection, comparisons meant to mortify, and a cultivated disdain to stimulate conquest. She resolved to make Calyste think himself ugly and unwelcome—thus rekindling his desire to conquer. She contrasted her poetic, comfortable home with the hôtel du Guénic’s Faubourg Saint-Germain luxury, painting the latter stupid and empty.

Thus the scene of reconciliation—where she made him swear hatred for his wife and denounce her as playing comedy—took shape in a bower of flowers and rare plants, amid trifles and sumptuous yet carefully economical luxury. Fallen into a mortifying position by Conti’s desertion, Béatrix was determined to gain, at least, the triumphs of notoriety. The misfortune of a rich and beautiful wife like Sabine was to be her pedestal.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
When Sabine returns to ordinary life after nursing her first child she seems refreshed but finds her husband Calyste changed. A letter from her mother hints at his discontent and names another woman, Beatrix, confirming Sabine’s fears. The vague note offers no explanation; in despair she scrawls a remark on it and leaves it where Calyste will see it. He finds the comment, ignores it, and burns the paper, humiliating her and deepening her distress.

Beatrix’s influence soon becomes tangible: perfume on a later letter betrays her presence. Recognizing it, Sabine feels the rival’s hold. In a febrile moment she dances with her child, clinging to motherhood’s innocence, then weeps as Calyste departs to read Beatrix’s message. Her attempts to win him back—decorating the house with flowers, improving her cooking and appearance—meet only indifference, intensifying jealousy and self-doubt.

Despite her suffering, Sabine’s love persists; she remains a devoted wife, but these virtues prove ineffective. After another failed effort to move Calyste she collapses, tears off a beautiful dress, and her health falters. The duchess, her mother, notices and tries to distract her. Sabine, fearing interference, at first stays silent but eventually confides in her sister Athenais, describes her anguish, and asks that her child be cared for if she dies.

Warned by experience, Sabine counsels Athenais to be cautious in love, lamenting that her straightforwardness and passion now obstruct happiness and may force her toward duplicity. Alarmed, the duchess consults Abbé Brossette and reveals a plan to break Calyste’s attachment and restore Sabine. The abbé cautions against mixing spiritual authority with worldly intrigue, but the duchess, determined to spare her daughter further suffering, resolves to act even if it means morally questionable measures.

Meanwhile a storm brews for Monsieur de Rochefide, who continued to enjoy Parisian life after Beatrix left, confident such existence was nearly impossible to disrupt. The narrative contrasts how society treats men and women in similar situations, implying women must guard their dignity at home as Sabine did.

After Beatrix’s departure, Arthur de Rochefide, now an only child after his sister’s death, became sole owner of the hôtel de Rochefide and inherited a large fortune. His income reached about a thousand francs a day, ample for his tastes. While Beatrix had fulfilled maternal duties, Rochefide enjoyed his wealth without great extravagance. His vanity, aided by good looks and a jaunty military air, let him dismiss women and borrow others’ wit; acquaintances found him amusing if shallow. As a husband he was pitied; society blamed Beatrix for leaving such a “good” man. Seeking distinction, he joined clubs and championed fashionable causes; his chief indulgence was racehorses, kept by an English groom at considerable cost.

Rochefide’s indifference to domestic life was clear: he rarely dined or slept at home. After several affairs he married Madame Schontz, known as La Petite-Aurélie, two years after Beatrix left. Their relationship moved through courtship, domestic improvement, motherhood and prudent financial management—Aurélie modestly preserving their reputation and quietly amassing a small fortune, turning the match into mutual benefit.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Madame Schontz had a plan born of two years’ jealousy of Madame du Bruel: she resolved to win a respectable, church-and-civil marriage. Her secret rival was unclear—Bixiou guessed Léon de Lora, others named Victor de Vernisset or Stidmann—but none realized Madame Schontz’s sole aim was an honest match.

The dubious regular in her salon was Couture, a fallen stock-gambler loyal to her. When he introduced Fabien du Ronceret, a flashy provincial, Madame Schontz took notice. Fabien, son of a chief justice, had left a judgeship in Alençon for Paris with a modest income and a knack for theatrical self-promotion: a showy apartment and a greenhouse arranged as spectacle to create talk. Seeking entry into Madame Schontz’s circle, he first tried known acquaintances, then relied on Couture’s recommendation. She flattered him and let him imagine he was the object of her interest. Bewitched, he dressed in his finest clothes. A month before a key meeting she disclosed her identity and aims; Fabien misread her intentions. When she corrected him during a carriage ride—declaring she wanted a husband with ambition who would value her character—Fabien hesitated. Couture, learning of her fortune, promptly proposed.

Meanwhile the Duchesse de Grandlieu engaged Comte Maxime de Trailles to resolve a delicate family matter. Maxime agreed but imposed conditions—chiefly that the duchess accept his future wife into her circle—and planned to eliminate a rival, demanding her obedience. He noted Calyste’s infatuation with Béatrix and the ethical murkiness of manipulating it. Rochefide informed Madame Schontz of Maxime’s impending visit; she prepared, aware his reputation could be both asset and threat.

Maxime summoned Finot to organize a breakfast at the Café Anglais with Couture and Lousteau to discuss Madame Schontz. Posing as having business with du Tillet, he waited for the Comte de la Palferine, questioned him about debts and, admiring his spirit, offered to bankroll a courtship with a loan of twenty thousand francs. Maxime targeted a well-bred marquise, urged La Palferine to woo her sincerely, and arranged a meeting at Madame Schontz. He visited the duchess—who fretted over Sabine’s distress at Calyste’s absence—promising funds and secrecy.

At Madame Schontz’s dinner, which included Claude Vignon and La Palferine, she insisted she was an artist seeking respectable marriage, not a courtesan. Maxime lauded her success with Rochefide, privately gave La Palferine the promised funds and sharp advice, and schemed with Madame Schontz to manipulate Rochefide’s affections, vowing a post for Couture in return.

Calyste, feeling neglected, confronted Béatrix, who was torn between genuine feeling for him and attraction to La Palferine. Maxime arranged encounters that deepened Béatrix’s entanglement; Calyste’s attempts to reclaim her failed, culminating in her public humiliation at the opera. As she drifts into La Palferine’s orbit, loyalties shift. The episode examines love, betrayal, and ambition—how manipulation and desire breed disillusionment and force characters to re-evaluate choices and futures.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

If the French are less keen on travel than the English, it’s because foreign lands cannot easily replace France’s particular charms—wit, conversation, and an implicit social understanding—so many French take pleasure in recognizing home even when away.

In 1836, while the Sardinian Court stayed in Genoa, two Parisians lodged in an old palazzo. Genoa’s evenings, especially after rain, favored intimate company and Italian fare. At a dinner given by the French Consul-General were Mademoiselle des Touches (Camille Maupin), the painter Léon de Lora, and the critic Claude Vignon.

Camille, come from Florence on business, had brought Léon to see Italy; their Genoese stop honored the Consul-General’s wish to pay court to her. Despite her fame she avoided ceremony, preferring an easy evening. Conversation flowed—lively but light on literature—until the Consul-General told a personal story.

He was thirty-four, with a Byron-like air, and had married Onorina Pedrotti, the wealthy banker’s only child, after initial reluctance; the match had been encouraged by the ambassador and by the Consul’s own private struggles. Onorina’s grace brought harmony, yet Camille perceived a persistent melancholy in him.

A table debate on women’s morality in cases of infidelity split the company—women judged harshly, the men urged nuance. To change the mood, the Consul recalled being twenty-two, when his uncle secured him as secretary to Comte Octave. The Count, outwardly devout and composed, secretly harbored a tender sorrow—an affection for flowers and a fragile echo of beauty—revealing an unrest behind seeming perfection.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“On a certain Monday,” he continued, “M. le Président de Grandville and M. de Serizy (then Vice‑President of the Council of State) met at Comte Octave’s house. They formed a committee of three with me as secretary. The Count had secured my appointment as Auditor to the Council of State; all the papers for their inquiry lay in the library, and MM. de Grandville and de Serizy relied on the Count for the preliminary examination. To avoid carrying the documents to M. de Serizy, they agreed to meet first in the Rue Payenne. The Tuileries valued this work, and it led to my appointment as Master of Appeals that year.

“Though the Comtes de Grandville and de Serizy, who rarely dined out, were still discussing late, a servant interrupted: ‘MM. the Cures of Saint‑Paul and of the White Friars have been waiting in the drawing‑room for two hours.’

“It was nine o’clock.

“‘Well, gentlemen, you find yourselves compelled to dine with priests,’ said Comte Octave. ‘I do not know whether Grandville can overcome his horror of a priest’s gown—’

“‘It depends on the priest.’

“‘One of them is my uncle, and the other is the Abbe Gaudron,’ I said. ‘Do not be alarmed; the Abbe Fontanon is no longer second priest at Saint‑Paul.’

“‘Well, let us dine,’ replied the President de Grandville. ‘A bigot frightens me, but there is no one so cheerful as a truly pious man.’

“We entered the drawing‑room. The dinner was delightful. My uncle’s wit was refined and reserved, the manner one expects of a man in black. The Abbe Gaudron, as M. de Grandville observed, was simple and devout; his ignorance enlivened the talk. They began discussing adultery. My uncle pointed out the contradiction the Revolution left between civil and religious law.

“‘In the eyes of the Church,’ he said, ‘adultery is a crime; in your tribunals, it is a misdemeanor. Adultery drives to the police court in a carriage instead of standing at the bar. Napoleon’s Council of State, touched with tenderness towards erring women, was inefficient. Should they not have harmonized civil and religious law and sent the guilty wife to a convent, as of old?’

“‘To a convent!’ said M. de Serizy. ‘They must first have created convents; monasteries were being turned into barracks. Besides, think—give to God what society would have none of?’

“‘Oh!’ said Comte de Grandville, ‘you do not know France. They had to leave the husband free to take proceedings; well, there are not ten cases of adultery brought up in a year.’

“‘M. l’Abbe preaches for his own saint,’ said Comte Octave. ‘In the East, woman was merely a luxury, a chattel; no virtues were demanded but obedience and beauty. By exalting the soul above the body, modern Europe—daughter of Christ—invented indissoluble marriage and made it a sacrament.’

“‘Ah! the Church saw the difficulties,’ exclaimed M. de Grandville.

“‘This institution has given rise to a new world,’ the Count continued with a smile. ‘But its practices will never suit a climate where women marry at seven and are old at twenty‑five. The Catholic Church overlooked half the globe. Let us discuss Europe only.

“‘Is woman our superior or inferior? That is the real question. If woman is inferior, placing her on such a high level as the Church does required severe punishments for adultery. Formerly, cloister or death summed up legislation. But practice has modified the law. The throne served as a hotbed for adultery, and the growth of this inviting crime marks the decline of Catholic dogmas. Nowadays, where the Church demands only sincere repentance from the erring wife, society is satisfied with a brand instead of execution. The law still condemns the guilty, but it no longer terrifies. In short, there are two moral standards: that of the world and that of the Code. Where the Code is weak, the world is audacious and satirical. Few judges would not gladly have committed the faults they condemn. The world punishes a blunder after encouraging hypocrisy. The entire marriage law seems to require reconstruction. The French law would be perfect if it excluded daughters from inheriting.’

“‘We three know the question thoroughly,’ said Comte de Grandville with a laugh. ‘I have a wife I cannot live with. Serizy has a wife who will not live with him. As for you, Octave, yours ran away from you. So we represent every case of the conjugal conscience, and if divorce is ever brought in again, we shall form the committee.’

“Octave’s fork dropped, breaking his glass and plate. He turned pale and shot a thunderous glare at M. de Grandville, hinting at my presence.

“‘Forgive me, my dear fellow. I did not see Maurice,’ the President continued. ‘Serizy and I, after witnessing your marriage, became your accomplices; I did not think I was committing an indiscretion in front of these venerable priests.’

“M. de Serizy changed the subject, recounting his efforts to please his wife without success. The old man concluded that human sympathies and antipathies cannot be regulated; social law is most perfect when closest to natural law. Nature does not consider affinities of souls; her aim is the propagation of the species. Thus, the Code, in its current form, wisely leaves latitude to chance. The incapacity of daughters to inherit while male heirs exist is an excellent provision, whether to prevent degeneration or to make households happier by abolishing scandalous unions.

“‘But then,’ he exclaimed, lifting his hand in disgust, ‘how are we to perfect legislation in a country that insists on bringing together seven or eight hundred legislators! After all, if I am sacrificed,’ he added, ‘I have a child to succeed me.’

“‘Setting aside the religious question,’ my uncle remarked, ‘Nature only owes us life; society owes us happiness. Are you a father?’ he asked.

“‘And I—do I have any children?’ said Comte Octave in a hollow voice, making such an impression that the topic of wives and marriage was dropped.

“When coffee was served, the two Counts and the two priests quietly left, noticing that poor Octave had fallen into a melancholy fit, oblivious to their departure. My patron sat in an armchair by the fire, crushed.

“‘You now know the secret of my life,’ he said to me when we were alone. ‘After three years of marriage, I came home one evening to find a letter from the Countess announcing her flight. The letter was dignified; women often retain some virtues even when committing such a sin. The story now is that my wife went abroad on a ship that was wrecked; she is presumed dead. I have lived alone for seven years! Enough for tonight, Maurice. We will discuss my situation when I am ready to speak of it. When suffering from a chronic disease, it takes time to adjust to the idea of improvement. That improvement often seems merely another aspect of the complaint.’

“I went to bed greatly agitated; the mystery seemed more obscure than ever. I anticipated a strange drama, realizing that the events leading the Countess to leave such a noble and loving man must have been extraordinary. M. de Grandville’s remark had illuminated the caverns I had been navigating, and though the light was dim, I could perceive their vastness. I could imagine the Count’s suffering without grasping its depths. His sallow face, parched temples, overwhelming studies, and moments of absentmindedness stood out vividly during the twilight of mental questioning before sleep.

“Oh! how I loved my poor master! He seemed sublime. I read a poem of melancholy, seeing perpetual activity in the heart I had accused of being torpid. Must not supreme grief eventually lead to stagnation? Had this judge, with so much power, ever sought revenge? Was he feeding on her long agony? Is it not remarkable to harbor anger for ten years in Paris? What had Octave done since this great misfortune—when the separation of husband and wife has become a social question?

“We allowed a few days to pass, for great sorrows have their own diffidence; but eventually, one evening, the Count said gravely:

“‘Stay.’
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
This, as nearly as may be, is his story.

My father’s ward, Honorine, was rich and lovely; she was sixteen when I returned from college to our old house. Raised by my mother, she awakened to life with an innocent, poetic view of religion and happiness, seeing the future as a perpetual fête. We lived like siblings until, by the garden basin, I asked her to marry me. At my mother’s insistence we waited eighteen months; when I was twenty-six and Honorine nineteen, we married. Out of respect for my parents we kept the old house unchanged; I introduced her to society more from duty than delight.

I later learned how treacherous such marriages can be: a husband becomes teacher and moralist, and love may wither under authority. I trusted her innocence too much and reproached myself like an anatomist seeking the cause of a disease. After my parents’ deaths, eighteen months later, Honorine left me—she wrote a farewell letter. The shock stunned me; for a month I was numb, then I resolved to live on memory. I loved Honorine and never ceased to worship her; since her departure I have subsisted on recollections of pleasures she perhaps never appreciated.

Do not make a hero of me; I sought no diversion. I was too young or too in love and met no one like her. After struggling to forget, I almost fell into infidelity, but the thought of Honorine restrained me. My life became a relentless agony of fears, joy, and despair; I felt no anger toward her, only compassionate, paternal sorrow. I realized I had idealized her and perhaps exhausted her with my uncritical passion. Too young to understand the unwritten laws of a woman’s heart, I blamed myself and absolved her. Love held me captive.

Society mocks a husband who cannot keep his wife; I had to remain silent. Serizy is happy—able to see and protect his wife—while I have nothing but a love that starves. I loathe prostitution and remain faithful under a kind of spell. Without religion I might have killed myself; instead I threw myself into work, feverish and sleepless.

The interior drama of my life interests no one; one can only vaguely perceive another’s sorrow. I calm despair by contemplating a miniature of Honorine, kissing her brow, breathing her perfume. I suffer fits of collapse and wild hilarity, and terrors like a criminal meeting the police. The public believes I am absorbed in official duties—Council of State, Chamber, Courts, Politics—but seven hours of nightly work suffice for those. Honorine is my true occupation. To recover my wife, to guard her without her knowing, to satisfy small pleasures she allows, to be always near her like a sylph unseen—that is my life. For seven years I have never gone to bed without checking her night-lamp and the shadow on her curtains.

Honorine left my house with only the dress she wore. Eighteen months later she had been deserted by her lover, who recoiled at poverty. She had a private income of sixty thousand francs a year. In November 1820, I secured the services of a skilled accoucheur disguised as a humble suburban apothecary and induced a parish priest to act as charity when she needed help. To hide her, save her from discovery, and find a devoted housekeeper who would be my confidante—this was a task worthy of Figaro. Locating where my wife had taken refuge was not difficult.

After three months of despair, I devoted myself to Honorine with God as my witness. Love needs daily nourishment; should I not protect this child whose guilt sprang from my imprudence against further ruin? At seven months her infant died—mercifully for both of us. For nine months she lay between life and death, deserted when she most needed a man’s support; but my arm was extended over her head. Honorine was cared for as she would have been at home. When she recovered and asked who had assisted her, she was told it was the Sisters of Charity, the Maternity Society, and the parish priest.

Honorine’s pride, almost a vice, has at times become obstinacy in misfortune. She determined to earn her living. For five years I lodged her in the Rue Saint-Maur, in a charming little house where she made artificial flowers. She believes she sells her creations to a shop, earning twenty francs a day and living as if on fifteen thousand francs a year. She adores flowers, pays a gardener handsomely—an expense I secretly meet. I promised the gardener a market-garden with a house near the porter’s lodge in the Rue Saint-Maur, held in the name of a law-clerk; the smallest indiscretion would ruin him. Honorine has her little house, a garden, and a splendid hothouse for a rent of five hundred francs a year. She lives under the name of Madame Gobain, her housekeeper, an old woman of impeccable discretion who has won Honorine’s affection. For three years Honorine has been happy, believing she owes her comfort solely to her own toil.

Yes, I have tried to reconcile with her. Once, believing there was a chance, I wrote her a letter—rewriting it twenty times. I will not describe my agonies. It was dark and foggy; I went to meet Madame Gobain. Honorine, recognizing my handwriting, threw the letter into the fire without reading it, declaring she would leave the next day. It felt like a dagger in my heart. I had just succeeded in deceiving my wife once—sending an Indian shawl presented as a gift from an actress, which I had wrapped myself for a night. My life may be summed up: I love, and I wait.

Madame Gobain is my faithful spy. I visit her every evening for reports of Honorine’s day; a single exclamation might reveal the secrets of a soul that remains willfully deaf and mute where I am concerned. Honorine is pious—she attends church and prays—but she has never confessed or taken Communion, anticipating what a priest would say. She will not return to me; the horror I inspire overwhelms her, though I have never harmed her and have always been kind. Even if I was hasty in teaching her, is that a reason for her implacable determination? Her silence is absolute: she has never told Madame Gobain who she is; the housekeeper cannot advocate for me. The others know nothing; they live in awe of the Prefect of Police and respect the power of a Minister. Thus it is impossible for me to penetrate her heart. I have no means of action; any violence would ruin me forever.

How can I argue against reasons I do not know? Should I write and have my letter copied and delivered, risking a third removal, which cost me fifty thousand francs previously? The last purchase of a house had been made in the name of my second secretary. The poor man, unaware of my vigilance, was caught opening a private box; I intimidated him into selling the house and dismissed him. If I did not feel my noblest faculties satisfied by this divine fatherhood—if I did not drink delight in every pore—I might think myself a monomaniac. Sometimes at night I hear the jingling bells of madness and dread the violent transitions from feeble hope to complete despair. Recently I thought of the tragic ends of Lovelace and Clarissa Harlowe; if Honorine were the mother of my child, she must return under her husband’s roof.

I have such faith in a happy future that ten months ago I bought one of the finest houses in the Faubourg Saint-Honoré. If I win her back I will not let her see the room from which she fled; I will place my idol in a new temple where life will seem entirely new. That house is being transformed into a marvel of elegance and taste. I was inspired by an anecdote of a poet’s extravagant purchase for an actress; great passions share such impulses. I love like a poet—like an emperor!

From the vehemence of my speech you might imagine a burning physical passion, but it is nothing compared to my worship for her soul, mind, and heart. By retrospection I now see the graces of Honorine’s mind and heart I overlooked in happiness. Day by day I appreciate my loss, discovering gifts in that capricious and proud young creature who has grown strong under poverty and the cowardly shock of desertion. That heavenly blossom is fading in solitude and hiding. The law we discussed cannot compel love—seizing my wife by force would be triumph over a corpse. Religion has little hold on her; she craves its poetry but does not heed its commands. I have exhausted mercy, kindness, and love; I am at my wit’s end. Only one chance remains: the cunning and patience of bird-catchers who finally trap the wariest birds.

When M. de Grandville’s indiscretion revealed my secret life to you, I regarded it as fate’s decree. Have you affection for me to show romantic devotion? I replied with a question about danger: my first secretary had tried to open my deed box; my second might fall in love with my wife. Can anyone put his hand in a brazier without burning it? The Count answered that he would send me well-gloved. It would not be his secretary who lodged in the Rue Saint-Maur but his distant cousin, Baron de l’Hostal, a lawyer high in office—an appearance of respectability.

Shortly after, Madame de Courteville and her daughter called; the Count had family ties on his mother’s side. A young woman of twenty might suit the Count, but he did not propose that match. Instead, three weeks later, I moved into the gardener’s cottage, repaired and furnished by Paris workmen and money. I was as much in love as the Count could desire for security. Would a young man of twenty-five play this part faithfully for his friend’s cause? I confess I relied on my uncle’s advice; the Count had authorized me to confide in him if needed. I engaged the garden, devoted myself to horticulture, and worked frantically—obsessing over varieties of flowers as part of my disguise. The Count dictated the tragi-comedy enacted in the Rue Saint-Maur. Each night, after Honorine went to bed, Octave, Madame Gobain, and I held council; Octave lived only for that hour.

For two months I worked in the garden without ever seeing the little house where my fair neighbor lived; I had not even inquired if I had a neighbor. Her garden front was divided from mine by a paling, her cypress trees already four feet high. One morning Gobain warned me that a neighbor intended to build a wall between the gardens. My curiosity burned to see the Countess—my wish nearly extinguished the budding affection I had briefly felt for Amélie de Courteville. Building a wall would close off fresh air from Honorine’s garden and turn her plot into a narrow alley. The cottage, once a summer villa, imitated a trellis with flowers up to the second floor—a charming, fragile place. My wall would cut away three-quarters of it.

“My good Gobain,” the Countess asked, “what sort of man is this florist?”
Gobain replied that he disliked women, was the nephew of a Paris cure, and perhaps a little cracked. A harmless lunatic is the only man no woman ever distrusts in matters of sentiment—so the Count had chosen a wise disguise for me.

When Gobain informed the Countess that the florist wished to be left in peace, she replied she would speak to him herself. The next day as I walked the path I saw the curtain parted and a face peering out. Gobain announced me as the eccentric neighbor. I had dressed as a countryman—gray flannel trousers, wooden shoes, shabby coat, peaked cap, hands soiled with mold—to strengthen the part. The Countess did not shrink.

It was early May and the air was pure; seeing Honorine, I understood Octave’s passion. Her pallor was of a particular white, with pink flushes at emotion. Sunlight through the acacia gave her a pale gold halo. Her brown eyes were tender and lively, framed by long lashes; a glance could enchant. Light-brown hair framed a high brow; her mouth was voluptuous; her head bore a stamp of nobility. Slight but not thin, she embodied mignonne—pliant, ready to be petted, yet proud and imposing. Her walk had a proud carriage, and even humble people made way. Gay and tender, haughty and commanding, she combined contradictions and remained childlike. She could be as implacable as an angel if hurt.

The violet perfume she wore made sense of the Count’s adoration: she inspired devotion that demanded chivalrous sacrifice. Many women study their expressions; in her it flowed from a nature that went straight to the heart. If I sketch her it is because her soul and exquisite heart are central to this tale, and you would blame me had I not done so.

I was about to forget my ridiculous role as half-crazy rustic when I asked her, with assumed gravity, if she loved flowers. She replied she made artificial flowers—after growing them she imitates them, as a mother paints her children—an occupation that proved she was poor. I pretended to be a gardener with an interest in blue dahlias and blue roses; I argued that blue is the color of superior souls. I proposed open railings to unite our gardens. She, who loved solitude and independence, allowed me the right to enter her garden anytime and urged I be treated as a neighbor with whom she would keep good terms. With a leap I cleared the paling.

For a fortnight I seemed indifferent; by late May on a lovely evening we walked on opposite sides of the paling and exchanged civil words. I leaped the fence and joined her. She led me into the house—a charming sanctuary of eighteenth-century taste: frescoed dining-room, tasteful staircase, faded but elegant drawing-room, a bedroom, dressing-room, and workroom used as a library. The kitchen lay below. Everything harmonized with the woman I had come to love.

Exquisite simplicity reigned. Her little house was like a box of 18th-century art; but occasionally a bitter smile hinted at deeper sorrow. In days we reached intimacy born of proximity and her conviction of my indifference to women. A look would have spoiled everything; I kept my feelings masked. Honorine saw me as an old friend and treated me with pity rather than coquetry. She soon allowed me into her pretty workshop, full of books and curiosities—a boudoir where elegance softened the vulgarity of the tools of her trade. She had poetized manufacture.

Perhaps making artificial flowers allows a woman to remain herself: she can chat, laugh, sing and still work. Honorine had an artist’s sense in arranging petals and shades; white china saucers held colors; a cabinet with drawers held molds; a Japanese bowl held the paste; a Venetian glass held the living model she strove to imitate. She pursued difficult tasks—tiny corollas, wildflowers—moving like an accomplished pianist. Her fingers seemed fairy-like in their deft economy of action.

I could not tire of admiring her; she displayed the genius of a painter in bold attempts, copying faded flowers and yellowed leaves to create poetic crowns suitable to women’s moods. She explained a fanciful botany of feelings—flowers for bacchantes, for bigots, pensive flowers for bored women. She employed me to stamp leaves, cut material, and prepare wires. My affected desire for occupation made me useful. We talked as we worked; when idle I read to her to keep my role. My gloomy, misanthropic persona amused and matched her antipathy to love; she was content with a companion on her desert island.

I spared you the shame I felt as I feigned wounds in my heart to excite her compassion. Her renunciation of love, her dread of happiness, she proclaimed with vehemence. These happy days convinced me that a woman’s friendship can be superior to her love. She resisted any trap of sentiment; mulish obstinacy is common among women, and she was stubborn.

One evening, playing the cynic, I argued that women, once they have inspired passion, hold men sacred forever, while women forget. She rebuked my generalization and denied being like those who give themselves to successive loves. She declared herself a nun, seventy-two years old in spirit, not a woman. Her disgust for life made her invulnerable. She often spoke of the angel Gabriel singing in her heart; if rich she would work still to prevent herself from soaring on “many-tinted wings.” She found some solace in her flowers but sometimes they failed to occupy her. She felt some great event impending; her life seemed about to change.

After three months of this diplomatic struggle between my assumed melancholy and her proud indifference, I told the Count I could not coax her out of herself; the shell must be broken. The Count left me free to act.

One Saturday in July, she gleefully showed me five gold pieces she had earned for her flowers and caps. It was late, a moonlit evening. The room smelled of perfumes; the Countess jingled the coins that Octave’s accomplices had provided as part of the ruse. She said she earned her living by amusing herself and felt transports of pride every Saturday. I suggested the image of a pretty child running among the flowers calling her “mother,” and her reaction was immediate and extreme: she suffered an attack—what she called “a cramp of the heart.” Madame Gobain tended her; I walked the path weeping and doubting whether I should abandon the bird-catcher’s role I had assumed. Gobain returned saying I cried like a child, and the Countess, roused by the danger of misinterpretation, wore a wrapper and came down to me.

“You are not the cause of this attack,” she said, and when I asked about her troubles she declared her heart a poem laid before God. I threatened, lightly, that I could know all her secrets by tomorrow. She defiantly challenged me to be curious and told me to reveal what I discovered and the means by which I discovered it, for the preservation of the small happiness she so dearly bought depended on my discretion.

“I defy you,” she said; she wanted me to find out who she really was. I warned that if I learned anything carelessly, others would be informed, and then—she would fly “on wings” to the New World. I interrupted that Paris is a place where those who must work can hide. She persisted: find out who I am. I promised to tell her what I might discover the next day and pleaded she not hate me. She replied coldly, almost imperiously, that only two words would suffice to destroy her peaceful fraud and she must be allowed to keep her carefully arranged retreat. After a few more words, she dismissed me abruptly.

Thus the Count’s scheme had reached a crucial point: I was to be the Count’s instrument, the gardener-spy and companion, someone who could win Honorine’s confidence without revealing his identity. The house’s delicate balance—Honorine’s love of flowers, her pride, her vow of solitude, and the secret charity that sustained her—would be the stage for whether pity, cunning, or patience could redeem what force and authority could not.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“The battle is to-morrow, then,” I said, forcing a smile; the phrase named the coming confrontation of hearts. Octave and I walked until two in the morning like generals before an engagement—each aware that victory might hinge on an instant. We lay awake—one hopeful, the other dreading reunion. For Octave and Honorine the true drama was interior, a conflict of lofty souls.

The next evening I arrived at ten and entered Honorine’s white-and-blue bedroom, the sanctuary of this wounded dove. The Comtesse seemed about to speak but held back before my respectful manner. I told her plainly she was not living in her own house but in Count Octave’s: M. Lenormand, the usher, was a front; the seclusion, money, and protection all came from him. Her disappearance had been plausibly explained; her husband even hoped she had been saved from a shipwreck. He had hidden her as she hid herself, obeying her through seven years of magnanimity.

The Countess trembled—defiant, eyes dry and fierce, later yielding helpless tears. She had believed herself free; marriage weighed on her like a prison. “I will go!” she cried. “He forces me to it; I will go where no one will come after me.” When I asked if she meant suicide she insisted she had powerful reasons to refuse returning to Comte Octave.

I urged her to tell me and my uncle—both devoted advisers. My uncle, a priest but not a pedant, would listen and seek a solution. If she were the victim of a misunderstanding we could clear it; if guilty, expiation could be sought. I promised friendship and refuge from Octave’s tyranny if she proved she could not and ought not return. She spoke of the convent as last hope, but I warned that as a Minister of State the Count could block many convents; nonetheless I pledged to save her if she showed she had no other life to lead. I offered peace, solitude, and independence, and vowed not to see her without her consent.

She answered that no man could understand her conscience, “like circles of Dante’s hell.” Another man, though unworthy, had had all her love; for her there could never be two loves. The cup of happiness once broken could not be refilled. She would not be a prostitute to Octave—she esteemed his generosity and respected him but could never love him. Expecting tenderness from him would be impossible; every time her eyes met his she would see her sin reflected. To return would be a snare: she feared killing him with coldness or provoking suspicion, and could not bear to kneel while he stood. She clung to the memory of a child conceived in rapture and nursed for seven months; other children would draw tears in their milk. For her there was no situation in the life he offered that did not hide a precipice. Religion offered solace, but she lacked the strength to face that suffering. She had chosen between this hell, where God did not forbid blessing Him, and the hell awaiting her in Count Octave’s house.

I asked where she would go. Could a woman at thirty, beautiful and with undiscovered powers, live unprotected in the wilderness where I could hide her? I reassured her that the Count, who had not shown himself at her house for nine years, would not go anywhere without her permission; she had his devotion, a guarantee for her tranquillity. I urged her to discuss the future in confidence with my uncle, a man whose moral influence equaled a minister’s. He would be tender before her flowers.

I left at midnight; Honorine seemed calm but depressed, harboring a secret purpose I could not divine. I found Octave a few paces away in the Rue Saint-Maur—drawn by irresistible attraction, he had left our agreed spot. On hearing of my visit he fretted: “What a night my poor child will go through! Suppose I were to go to her! What if she saw me suddenly?” I warned that at that moment she might throw herself out the window; she was one of those Lucretias who could not survive such violence. He countered that passions change like a tempest on a lake; tonight she might either rush into his arms or hurl herself out. He joked of opium to sleep away the torment.

Next day Gobain brought a letter informing me the Countess had gone to bed at six, taken a chemist’s draught, and fallen asleep. Gobain read Honorine’s anguished letter aloud. She acknowledged conscience condemned her: conscience is God’s interpreter to man. If she did not reconcile with Octave she would be damned; civil law condemned her to obey; if her husband did not reject her the world would deem her pure. Legally, religiously, and socially she ought to return; it would be cruel to deprive him of happiness, to deprive him of children, to blot his name. Her sufferings and repugnance she admitted were selfish and ought to be sacrificed to family. She should be a mother; a child’s caresses might wipe many tears. God, law, and society were in accord.

Yet she declared she could not live false to herself. She would become “white and innocent again” only in death. It was not obstinacy, she said, but a nature that could not renew passion once consummated. “A young bride is like a plucked flower; a guilty wife is a flower trodden underfoot,” she wrote. She could not love Octave. Every time their eyes met she would see her sin reflected; his generosity would measure her crime. Neither esteem nor sacrifice could survive. She feared she would by coldness kill him or provoke suspicion. She could not bear to be treated as chattel, to kneel while he stood. She clung to the memory of a child conceived in rapture; other children would draw tears from her in their milk. Religion offered solace but she lacked energy for that particular suffering. She had chosen between a hell in which God did not forbid blessing Him and a hell awaiting her under Count Octave’s roof. She closed asking me to tell her where to find the solitude, peace, and silence I had promised.

After copying the letter I went to the Rue Payenne. Opium’s power had been overcome by anxiety; Octave paced his garden like a madman. I gave him Honorine’s letter and urged him to answer; he must reassure modesty by experience, for it is harder to restore conviction than to seduce ignorance. He cried with joy on reading her words: “She is mine!” Ecstasy and torment possessed him; he would act. I visited other friends and, comparing Mademoiselle de Courteville with Honorine, reflected that certain women inspire perfect, inevitable fidelity while others, like Honorine, demand extraordinary generosity. Such comparisons, fatal at marriage’s threshold, are wrong. Life teaches that marriage excludes passion and family cannot be founded on tempests of love. Having dreamed impossible passion and tasted its torment, I saw modest reality and took a manful resolution.

When I returned to the Count to announce his relations’ arrival I saw him grow young with hope. He asked what ailed me; I replied with a crucial condition: if he succeeded in bringing the Countess back, she must never see me again, must never know Maurice was his secretary. “Never mention my name to her, or all will be undone.” I told Octave to get me a diplomatic post abroad instead of the position he had obtained for me at home; I asked not to marry Amélie and to be sent away. He pressed my hand, understanding. We agreed on evening arrangements at Honorine’s.

That August evening the Countess sat under an arbor in a loose white muslin dress with blue bows. She did not rise but signaled me to sit. “Is not life deadlocked for me?” she asked. I told her it need not be if she chose differently. She asked how. I told her her letter was in the Count’s hands; she started like a frightened deer and accused me of being a traitor, a spy sent by her husband. I explained I had forwarded her letter through my uncle and would bring the Count’s reply. There is a law, divine and human, that even hatred obeys: do not condemn without hearing the defense. She must read the Count’s answer in the presence of my uncle and me, or she would be mere wilful child. Make this sacrifice to the world, to the law, and to God.

She consented. Months of effort had led to this instant. The Count had pinned all hopes on it and had reached it. My uncle’s entrance into the little Pompadour drawing-room at ten was formidable: his fine head, silver hair against black, and divinely calm face soothed the Countess. My uncle, the Cure of the White Friars, handed Honorine Octave’s letter.

In that letter Octave said that if she had trusted him and read a letter he had written five years earlier she would have spared five years of privations; he admitted faults and ignorance at the marriage’s start; an angel had been in the house and he felt punished for his audacity. He begged mercy, saying he had so appreciated her susceptibilities he would not bring her back to Rue Payenne where he could not bear to see her. He was decorating another house in the Faubourg Saint-Honoré and hoped to conduct not a wife secured by ignorance and law but a sister who would allow him to press a fatherly kiss on her brow. He promised not to force passion on her until sure of his strength to leave her free. He argued she could live as a sister or daughter presiding: unapproachable, alone, occupied as she pleased, under legitimate protection and with fortune for good works. If she longed for absolution it would wait on her pride; he would not use ecclesiastical threats or legal force; he wanted her happiness and would not take peace except from her consent. If she persisted in condemning him to loneliness he would yield; she would be troubled no more.

Honorine folded the letter, pressed it to her bosom, and, soothed, said she would avail herself of the Count’s permission to remain here. My involuntary cry betrayed my feeling and revealed to her that I was more than a spy; she gave me a mischievous glance and took my hand at the door, pressing it affectionately. I replied that I would leave tomorrow—“You love your husband,” I said after a squeezing farewell. I rushed away, leaving my uncle to explain my agitation as a touch of madness.

Six days later I left with an appointment as vice-consul in Spain. After I established myself, Count Octave wrote to me: if I were happy he would not write; he had entered a new life of suffering, grown young again in desire but governed by a diplomat’s prudence. When I left, Octave had not yet been admitted to the pavilion in Rue Saint-Maur, though a letter promised permission. He waited more than a month and finally called. Gobain stalled, saying “Madame had to dress,” flattering Octave’s hopes. Once admitted, he spoke with trembling: he told her he had no crime but being in love with his wife and proposed she no longer work, offering her certificates worth twelve thousand francs each—an offer which was freedom more than money. She took them, languidly, and understood the gift. She conceded and said she would be seen as often as he liked.

Two months of habit brought out her true character; it was a delicious springtime of love that gave Octave ineffable bliss. Yet when he proposed she go to England to resume rank and life in their new residence she feared to leave their experiment. “Why not live always as we are?” she asked, and he submitted, wondering if she were making an experiment. He painted his case in a letter and begged her answer; she made none and seemed sad. When she finally received him in her blue-and-white room she was bewitching in dress, but her face was grave, fires beneath ice. She told him she would return as his wife when he wished, but warned that submission had dangers: she could be resigned but could not give the love he wanted. She loved his soul enough to die in his service—an expiation—and knelt, sobbing she might not die.

For two months he struggled. His heart was too full; he sought a friend and cried, “What shall I do?” I did not answer that letter. Two months later newspapers announced the Comtesse Octave had returned on an English vessel, restored to her family after contrived adventures. When I moved to Genoa I received formal notice of the birth of a son to the Count and Countess; I held the letter for two hours on the terrace bench. Two months later, urged by Octave and friends and broken by my uncle’s death, I agreed to marry.

Six months after the Revolution of July I received a final letter concluding the story. The Countess wrote that she was dying though already a mother—perhaps because she was a mother. She had played her part as a wife well; she had deceived her husband; she had had happiness as genuine as an actress’s tears. She was dying for society, family, marriage, as early Christians died for God. She did not know of what she was dying; she tried to find out but could not. She was not perverse; she had been bent on explaining her malady to me—the man who had brought the heavenly physician, my uncle, at whose word she surrendered. He was her director; she had nursed him in his last illness; he had shown her the way to heaven, bidding her persevere in duty.

She had done her duty. She did not blame those who forget; she admired them as strong natures. But she had the malady of memory. She could not twice feel that love which identifies a woman with a man. To the last moment she cried in the confessional and to her husband, “Have mercy!” but there was none. She died with stupendous courage, playing the actress’s cheerful mask while the invisible rival—her dead child—came every day to claim her. She was rent and smiled; two children existed, but the elder, the dead one, would triumph. She told me she had said this before: the dead child calls her. The intimacy of marriage without love degraded her soul every hour. She could only weep or dream when alone. Social duties and care of her child left no moment to renew herself as she could in solitude. The constant need for watchfulness starved her heart. She lacked tender lips to cool her burning eyes; her soul acted a part and so she died. The doctors, who discovered her secret, allowed a plausible complaint as cause. Octave believed in her apparent adoration; she feared he might follow her. She begged me to be her little Count’s guardian; she had placed a codicil expressing her wish and told me not to produce it unless needed. She asked me not to mourn; she had been dead these many days if Saint Bernard was right that where there is no love there is no life.

The Consul closed the reading by putting away the letters and locking his pocket-book. The guests asked about the Count. The Consul-General recalled seeing him a white-haired man, an old man of forty-five, passing through Genoa in search of health and amusement; the man had placed his will in the Consul’s hands and appointed him guardian of his son. He suspected the truth of Honorine’s death and that suspicion was killing him. At the steamboat Octave, poor friend and patron, had entrusted his will and looked down at the Mediterranean, pondering the mystery of why human nature sacrifices exquisite beings to fugitive pleasures. He confessed he was consumed by remorse. In the Rue Payenne he had been dying of joys not had; now in Italy he would die of joys he had had. He wondered where the discord lay between two equally noble natures.

A profound silence followed. The Consul then asked the two women present, “Was she virtuous?” Mademoiselle des Touches took his arm and answered by turning the question to men: are they not equally wrong when they take a young girl to wife while cherishing an angelic image and then compare us to unknown rivals? She argued that if marriage were based on passion her point would be valid—that kind of marriage would be Paradise. Others remembered such passions devastating men and making women like nuns or suicides. Camille Maupin and others reflected that such exceptional souls find peace only in solitude or in religious life. As the guests dispersed along the seashore at eleven, the conversation lingered on the rarity of Honorine: perhaps the most extraordinary intellect, a pearl whose tragedy was that life alternates pain and pleasure and that to ask Dante’s Paradise of life is to demand a luxury nature resists. Those great souls still existed, they agreed, though to live by such intensity often meant sacrifice beyond the world’s comprehension.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

“HULLO! There is that old Box-coat again!” cried the lawyer’s clerk Simonnin, leaning from a window and tossing a breadcrumb at a shabby stranger crossing Maître Derville’s courtyard.

“Come, Simonnin, don’t play tricks… However poor a client is, he is still a man!” Boucard, the head clerk, scolded. Simonnin, about thirteen or fourteen, and others including Godeschal mocked the visitor and joked about Derville seeing clients only at odd hours. The office, cluttered and musty, buzzed with laughter and routine irreverence.

When the weary man entered and politely asked for M. Derville, Boucard brusquely told him to return at one. The clerks jeered—nobleman or porter?—and laughed when the visitor insisted he was Colonel Chabert, reported dead at Eylau. The porter noted Derville’s habit of arriving late; Boucard said the Colonel was only the third late caller.

When Derville finally arrived he was struck by the stranger’s thin, pale appearance and the scars across his face and hands—clear signs of severe trauma. Though Derville and Boucard suspected madness, Chabert insisted on telling his story.

Pressed for brevity, Chabert recounted Eylau: gravely wounded and buried among corpses, he had miraculously survived because his horse’s body had shielded him. He described waking in a grave, struggling free, and being rescued by a woman who took him to a hospital. After months of recovery he discovered he had lost everything—especially proof of identity. Without documents, attempts to reclaim his life met disbelief and ridicule; he wandered as a beggar, was often treated as mad, and spent two years confined in a mental institution. Upon release he learned his wife, the Countess Ferraud, had remarried and borne children.

Derville, accustomed to varied clients, was moved despite skepticism and agreed to help. He promised to obtain papers from Germany, gave a small sum for subsistence, and provided a letter to a notary. Overwhelmed with gratitude, Chabert felt hope for the first time in years and voiced a longing for vengeance rather than obscurity. Derville acknowledged the case’s complexity but vowed to treat it seriously, restoring Chabert’s purpose.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
About three months after the interview, in Derville’s room, the notary Crottat—who had advanced half-pay to Derville’s eccentric client—demanded the six hundred francs given to the old soldier be refunded.

“Are you amusing yourself with pensioning the old army?” Crottat laughed; he had taken the office after his chief fled in bankruptcy.

“Thank you for reminding me of that affair,” Derville replied wryly. “My philanthropy will not extend beyond twenty-five louis; I fear I have already been duped by my patriotism.”

Noticing German-stamped papers on Derville’s desk, Crottat ordered his head clerk Boucard to translate. A Berlin notary reported that the requested documents would arrive and be legally stamped; witnesses to the recorded facts still lived in Eylau, and the woman who had saved Comte Chabert was alive in Heilsberg. “This looks like business,” Derville observed.

Questioning Crottat about Chabert’s estate and the rogue Roguin, Derville learned the essentials. Roguin—mockingly called “the unfortunate Roguin”—had absconded with eight hundred thousand francs of clients’ money. Crottat, who had copied the papers as third clerk, explained that Rose Chapotel, wife of Hyacinthe (called Chabert, Count of the Empire), had married without settlement so they held property in common. The personalty was about six hundred thousand francs. Before marriage Colonel Chabert had made a will leaving one-quarter to hospitals and assigning another quarter to the State; the will was contested, forcing a sale and leaving the widow with the portion bequeathed to the treasury. Thus Chabert’s personal fortune had been reduced to roughly three hundred thousand francs.

Derville then sought out the Colonel, who lodged in the Faubourg Saint-Marceau with an old quartermaster named Vergniaud; Derville walked because his coachman refused the unpaved street. The house was dismal—mismatched windows, a trodden-earth floor, a single chair and a straw bed, damp walls—testimony to poverty. Yet when Chabert emerged smoking a pipe he preserved the military calm of a man unshaken by hardship.

“Why did you not write to me?” Chabert asked, giving practical directions to avoid manure.

“Here, we are not distinguished for luxury,” he added when asked about his lodgings. “But I have done no one wrong, and I sleep in peace.” Derville hesitated about the money he had advanced and instead asked why Chabert had not moved to Paris to live better.

“The good folks here have taken me in and fed me for a year,” Chabert replied. “I cannot leave them now that I have a little money.” On hearing the Heilsberg news, Chabert, frustrated at the thought of money he did not possess, threw down his pipe.

Derville then outlined the legal complexities: proving Chabert’s identity would require lengthy, costly proceedings. The case could be prolonged by the bigamy issue—Comtesse Ferraud had remarried—and opponents would demand inquiries, translations, and foreign legal formalities that might take years and drain resources. If the death certificate were annulled and the second marriage attacked, French courts would require precise proof of identity and foreign authorities’ cooperation; witnesses would have to be found, depositions translated, documents legalized—procedures expensive and slow.

“You will face many challenges,” Derville cautioned. Overwhelmed by the prospect of a drawn-out lawsuit, Chabert expressed despair. Derville reassured him the outcome could be favorable and proposed a friendly suit to annul both the death certificate and the subsequent marriage. He offered to send a power of attorney for Chabert to sign and to provide financial help. Chabert, feeling hopeless, agreed to trust Derville completely.

As Derville left he met Louis Vergniaud, who asked for a loan to improve the Colonel’s living conditions. Derville agreed but insisted Chabert should be the one to help his friends. He then planned to visit Comtesse Ferraud to assess her position and potential leverage.

Monsieur le Comte Ferraud, the son of a former Councillor, had returned to France after the Revolution and married Madame Chabert, who had turned her share of her husband’s fortune into a substantial income.
