Their marriage was socially accepted; Ferraud was politically ambitious but had begun to regret his union. The Countess had become comfortable and influential, a sharp contrast to Chabert’s squalor.

Derville arrived at the Hotel Ferraud and found her surrounded by luxury. “Good-morning, Monsieur Derville,” she greeted him, but he quickly shifted to serious matters. “I have come to speak with you on a very serious matter,” he stated, revealing that Colonel Chabert was alive. The Countess laughed dismissively until Derville’s grave tone silenced her. He warned her of the danger she faced if Chabert’s identity were proven, hinting at legal and social ramifications. She insisted her second marriage was secure because of her children, but Derville countered that the law might not favor her and that Ferraud might not support her if his name and fortune were at risk.

Derville’s words struck a nerve; the Countess began to weigh the implications. He proposed a compromise, sensing her vulnerability. “Does he still love me?” she asked, contemplating how to use Chabert’s affection to her advantage. Derville left her with the suggestion to consider a settlement, confident he had gained the upper hand.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
A week after Derville’s visits, Colonel Chabert and his wife, Madame la Comtesse Ferraud, set out from opposite ends of Paris to meet at their lawyer’s office. With Derville’s help Chabert arrived transformed—blue suit, Legion of Honor ribbon, his soldierly bearing renewed. The Countess followed, made up to seem youthful. Clerks noticed his change; sensing tension, Derville separated them and presented a memorandum: Chabert would formally acknowledge his identity in exchange for financial support from his wife. The Countess, aghast at the prospect of public exposure, resisted.

Reunited, Chabert confronted her about their past, revealing the pain of their separation. She feigned surprise and tried to leave; Derville urged her to stay. A heated exchange followed; despite cautions, Chabert pressed for justice. The Countess then took him to her carriage and brought him to her country house in Groslay. During the ride she confessed turmoil: she loved another man, Comte Ferraud, and wished to preserve her dignity and avoid scandal. Hurt, Chabert answered with measured understanding, aware of the social consequences she feared.

At the country house she treated him kindly and they reminisced; Chabert found temporary solace though resentment lingered. Meanwhile the Countess acted to secure her position, writing to Delbecq to neutralize Chabert’s claim. When he discovered this betrayal he resolved to confront her but ultimately sacrificed his identity to protect her and their children, renouncing his name and withdrawing from society.

Months later Derville found him reduced to a vagabond in a courtroom. In June 1840 Godeschal, an attorney, accompanied Derville to Ris. Near Bicêtre they found an old pauper under an elm, drying his handkerchief. In a shabby wrapper-coat with a carved, grotesque look—Godeschal likened him to German woodcuts—Derville recognized him as Comte Chabert, husband of the pious Comtesse Ferraud, who had likely consigned him to the workhouse because he reminded her of their past.

Curious, Godeschal urged Derville to ask about Chabert’s story. Two days later they returned and found the old man drawing in the sand. When spoken to he insisted he was no longer Chabert but “Hyacinthe,” merely a number in the institution; he even envied a condemned man for not being married. Godeschal gave him money for snuff; Chabert accepted eagerly, saluted them as “brave troopers,” waved his stick and cried “Vive Napoleon!” Derville reflected on Chabert’s tragic arc—from foundling to Napoleonic soldier to workhouse inmate—and on the disillusionment facing priest, doctor, and lawyer, all exposed to human baseness: betrayal, greed, moral decay. An addendum lists characters from Balzac’s Human Comedy and their other appearances, including Napoleon and Comtesse Ferraud.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Trained by Desplein, he became physiologist; Desplein’s dissection‑hardened skill and atheism seemed paradoxical, prompting critics’ scorn. Bianchon said Desplein aided water‑carrier, later learning Desplein attended Mass, complicating the portrait.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Some time passed before Dr. Bianchon, despite his friendship with Desplein, found a chance to ask about a pivotal episode in the surgeon’s life. Their meetings rarely allowed private talk, but seven years later, after the Revolution of 1830, Bianchon saw Desplein entering Saint‑Sulpice; they attended mass together.

“Can you explain your sudden piety?” Bianchon asked as they left. Desplein at first deflected, noting that many display outward religion while doubting inwardly and criticizing politicians; but pressed about why he had founded this mass, he answered plainly: he was nearing the end of his life and could trace its beginnings. Standing in the Rue des Quatre‑Vents, he pointed to the house where he had spent two desperate years and recalled hunger, isolation, and an irritable temper that kept friends away. With no money for books or training, he had struggled alone from destitution.

He spoke of meager meals, tireless labor, and a lack of sympathy; those born to wealth could not imagine his plight and treated debt as a moral failing. Early hardships shaped his view of humanity: he had seen selfishness and envy, and expected mediocrity to try to pull down those who strove for excellence.

A turning point came at the depth of his poverty, when he contemplated enlisting. He had hoped for a box of linen from a relative, but when the porter demanded too much to release it, despair set in. Then his neighbor Bourgeat—a humble water‑carrier—appeared and changed his fate.

Though poor himself, Bourgeat paid Desplein’s debts and secured new lodgings. He devoted his savings to Desplein’s future, understood his mission, and supported him selflessly. Desplein described Bourgeat as nurturing, almost maternal, proud of his protégé yet seeking no recognition. When Desplein became a house surgeon, leaving Bourgeat pained him; Bourgeat remained proud nevertheless.

Later Desplein repaid him by buying him a horse and a barrel—fulfilling a long‑held dream. Bourgeat was at once angry that Desplein had spent his money on him and delighted; he never claimed credit for Desplein’s rise, though Desplein felt he owed him everything.

When Bourgeat fell ill, Desplein tended him devotedly; Bourgeat died in his arms, leaving him all he had. A man of strong faith, Bourgeat’s wishes were honored with masses after his death. Desplein explained that the mass he founded was a tribute to Bourgeat’s faith; he hoped, if there is a paradise, that Bourgeat had found it. He admitted he would give anything for Bourgeat’s conviction.

Bianchon, who had witnessed Desplein’s last days, could not say whether the great surgeon died an atheist. He wondered whether Bourgeat might have opened Heaven’s gates for Desplein just as he had opened the doors to the earthly temple they had shared.

PARIS, January 1836.

ADDENDUM: Bianchon and Desplein appear across Balzac’s Human Comedy—Bianchon in Father Goriot, Lost Illusions, Cousin Betty, and others; Desplein in Cousin Pons, Lost Illusions, The Thirteen, Pierrette, A Bachelor’s Establishment, Modeste Mignon, and related pieces.

############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Middle story: central section where key events unfold. "MIDDLE OF THE STORY" repeated with decorative hash-mark separators.

############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

At one in the morning Dr. Horace Bianchon and Baron de Rastignac discussed the Marquise d’Espard. Bianchon distrusted the fashionable marquise—likening her to a “one-eyed horse” whose cosmetics could not hide age or temper—and scorned society women as insincere. Rastignac, dazzled by rank and beauty, hoped she might advance him and settle his debts. Bianchon dismissed that view but agreed to introduce Rastignac to his uncle, Judge Jean-Jules Popinot, though he doubted the rigid magistrate would be useful.

The next morning Bianchon visited Popinot’s house in a decayed quarter; the building still hinted at former grandeur. Popinot himself was unkempt and unambitious, reduced to a supernumerary judge, yet combined firm integrity with an intuitive ability to elicit confessions without force and to detect hidden virtues in the poor. Frustrated by the legal system’s limits, he devoted himself privately to justice and charity.

His conversion began twelve years earlier during the 1816 famine, when he was appointed President of a Commission Extraordinary to distribute food locally. A prominent lawyer and criminal judge who had observed outcomes at a distance, direct contact with destitution awakened deep compassion. One act of charity led to another until he became a local protector of the poor, likened to a modern Saint Vincent de Paul.

He converted a storeroom into a modest parlor for the needy, furnished with benches and plain chairs, and kept meticulous records of donations and requests to allocate resources fairly. His aide Lavienne handled pawn tickets and made house visits in the hardest-hit districts. From dawn until late morning the parlor filled with women, children, and the indigent; their number made heating unnecessary, and even thieves respected the household. Popinot spent mornings attending the poor, afternoons on his legal practice, and evenings on judicial duties. This close observation sharpened his judicial instinct—he learned to read subtle signs of wrongdoing and virtue alike.

His modest inherited fortune, his late wife’s savings, and a restrained judge’s salary were stretched by charitable outlays. Despite public service, he remained one of the few judges of the Court of the Seine without the Legion of Honor.

Popinot’s routine was interrupted when the President of the Second Chamber, at the Marquise d’Espard’s request, commissioned him to examine the Marquis d’Espard for a Lunacy Commission. Bianchon went to the Rue du Fouarre expecting a clash between Popinot’s rough domesticity and the Marquise’s courtly world. The parlor was a tableau of hardship: worn faces and quiet resilience. Popinot, in a threadbare nightcap and dressing gown, paused amid his ledger to help a grocer’s wife whose husband was jailed for debt, recording the transaction. By midmorning he had attended to over two hundred people; when Bianchon returned after an errand, the last of the needy had been helped.

Popinot’s home reflected his divided life—dust, scattered papers, his late wife’s things, and tokens of gratitude. As they prepared for breakfast Bianchon urged him to dress for the Marquise’s meeting. Magistrates should not accept hospitality from parties in cases they oversee; Popinot hesitated but, persuaded the visit would be revealing and kinder, agreed and began to consider the Marquis d’Espard’s mental state and its implications for the family.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The next evening at nine Bianchon climbed his uncle’s dusty staircase and found him bent over a complicated judgment. Because the tailor had not sent the coat Lavienne ordered, Popinot still wore his old stained coat; Bianchon straightened his cravat and buttoned it, but the judge’s habitual movements revealed a gap between waistcoat and trousers. Bianchon noticed the absurdity just as his uncle entered the Marquise d’Espard’s room.

A brief account of Madame d’Espard frames the interview. For seven years she had been a Parisian fashion-figure. After separating from her husband in 1815, the Marquise—about thirty-three and mother of two boys, fifteen and thirteen—kept her vogue with elaborate hair, cold baths, and strict diet; emotionally detached, she admitted neither love nor hatred and parted from M. d’Espard with satisfaction when he took the children, whom she regarded as obstacles to her ambitions.

With an income of twenty-six thousand francs a year she lived quietly at first but by 1820 reappeared socially, hosting gatherings and replacing other prominent women. She cultivated ties with duchesses and countesses, turning her drawing-room into a discreet hub for political discussion. Skilled at social maneuvering, she attracted ambitious young men while remaining vain and instrumental—using intimacy as influence without personal compromise.

Rastignac, seeking an alliance, viewed her as a useful, cunning power. Popinot’s visit confirmed the household’s opulence; she received him with feigned weakness while the Chevalier d’Espard observed silently. Perceptive and probing, Popinot questioned the husband’s supposed mental change and their separation. The Marquise offered a crafted story, but Popinot’s practical inquiries—about the children’s welfare and the household’s arrangements—exposed contradictions: claims of hardship sat uneasily with lavish living, suggesting she might be maneuvering to control her husband’s affairs for self-serving reasons.

When she tried to deflect, Popinot warned that the court would scrutinize motives and transparency and that her façade might collapse. Afterwards Bianchon and Rastignac discussed the case’s scandal potential and Popinot’s character; Bianchon feared the Marquise’s influence. Popinot resolved to investigate further and summon Madame Jeanrenaud.

The next day Madame Jeanrenaud arrived: stout, unrefined, a sharp contrast to the Marquise. She denied wrongdoing and spoke simply of concern for M. d’Espard’s well-being. Her straightforward honesty intrigued Popinot. As he began tracing M. d’Espard’s financial dealings, he realized the situation was more complex than it first appeared; untangling the truth would require careful examination of all involved.

Those who have matured know how trivial incidents can shape larger events: Popinot’s minor illness delayed a crucial visit. Feeling unwell, the judge stayed home instead of seeing the Marquis d’Espard, losing a day whose absence echoed longer historic delays.

Before following the lawyer and his clerk to the Marquis’s residence, consider the father’s home life, which his wife’s petition branded as mad. In old Paris some buildings still display a taste for permanence. The house on Rue de la Montagne-Sainte-Genevieve where M. d’Espard lived was one such stone structure: once grand, now weathered by time and revolution, formerly housing dignitaries, later partitioned and partly occupied by a printing establishment. M. d’Espard took the ground floor, likely for his children’s sake, because the garden was unusually large for the area; he paid a modest rent given the building’s decay.

The Marquis furnished his rooms in rich browns, creating a quiet, harmonious atmosphere with carefully chosen pieces. Few visitors were admitted; family life remained secluded. The household comprised the Marquis, his two sons, and a small, disciplined staff—a long-serving cook and a man-servant. To shield his children from outside influence, M. d’Espard avoided socializing with tenants, and that isolation bred gossip: neighbors branded him mad and his servants untrustworthy. Noble by birth, the family embodied increasingly rare ideals, their dignity clashing with egalitarian neighbors. The Marquis, about fifty, had a distinguished, somewhat cold appearance and wore the same coat for years. His sons, Comte Clément de Négrepelisse, nearly sixteen and fashionable, and Vicomte Camille d’Espard, were handsome. The Marquis concentrated on a broad education for them—mathematics, Eastern languages, and history—eschewing what he considered unproductive studies.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The day Popinot planned to question M. d’Espard was a Thursday holiday. Before their father rose, his two boys were in the garden; the younger begged to visit the shooting-gallery and urged Clément to go. The Viscount delighted in wrestling with Clément; their quarrel roused their father, who watched amused from the window. Neighbors joked that the Marquis seemed mad to make his children fight. Noticing them, the Marquis called the boys; they leapt into his room and, after a moment’s hesitation, Clément obtained permission from Camille.

Through the house people whispered about the Marquis’s supposed new madness. When Popinot arrived near noon with his clerk, the portress led him to the third floor and remarked that M. d’Espard had made his children fight that morning and had laughed when the younger bit the elder until he bled; she added that the Marquis rarely showed himself. The landing’s muddy floor and grimy banisters lent credence to her gossip and made Popinot, though impartial, receptive to the tale.

They entered an outer office where an old man sat amid shelves of published numbers; a grating separated a private room, and a cash-desk showed the enterprise’s commercial aspect. A shopman opened the inner room to reveal the Marquis, a venerable, plainly dressed old man wearing the Cross of Saint-Louis, seated at a desk. Popinot explained they came on private business. After formalities the Marquis asked their purpose. Popinot identified himself as a judge on the Inferior Court of Appeal for the Department of the Seine, charged with examining M. d’Espard regarding a petition for a Commission in Lunacy filed by the Marquise d’Espard. The old man reddened and trembled, then steadied himself.

“How is it,” he asked, “that I have had no notice of such a petition?” Popinot explained that notice is unnecessary because those deemed subject to such a commission are presumed to lack reason; the Court must verify the petitioner’s claims. The Marquis accepted this and asked what he must do. Popinot urged him to answer fully and confidentially, especially concerning delicate accusations Madame d’Espard might make. The Marquis feared his explanations could blame his wife. Popinot promised discretion and the customary courtesy should the inquiry reveal a misunderstanding.

Popinot then confronted the major allegation: that the Marquis had dissipated his fortune for the benefit of Madame Jeanrenaud, widow of a bargemaster, or her son, Colonel Jeanrenaud, for whom he allegedly procured an appointment and exhausted influence with the King. The Marquis flushed; tears came, but pride returned. He said he was in a strange dilemma: revealing his motives would mean disclosing secret wounds and trusting the family’s honor to strangers. Popinot assured him of confidentiality. Freed from reserve, the Marquis spoke with clarity and passion.

After his marriage his wife had incurred heavy expenses that forced him to borrow. He could not afford a steward; most of his title-deeds had been taken to Paris by his father, who feared scrutiny. Their family name was Negrepelisse; the title d’Espard had been acquired through marriage in the time of Henri IV. An ancestor had lost the town of Negrepelisse during the religious wars and been ruined by property destruction. Though a later ancestor secured favor with Louis XIV and fortune, a stain of blood and disgrace remained, and the Marquis was determined to erase it.

He discovered that his grandfather had obtained the lands of Negrepelisse by means tied to the Revocation of the Edict of Nantes: a Protestant merchant was arrested and hanged, and the land passed to his ancestor by iniquitous means. The merchant’s family had the rightful claim; the victim’s name was Jeanrenaud. The Marquis could not bear the disgrace and resolved to find the heirs. Eventually he located M. Jeanrenaud, reduced to poverty and serving as a lieutenant under Napoleon. Believing the heir’s rights incontestable and their lack of means to sue, he offered to pay them the lands’ value at the time they were taken—eleven hundred thousand francs—payable at his convenience and without interest.

To effect restitution he lived on little for a long time. When he proposed to Madame d’Espard that they move to the country to live on half her income, she treated him as mad; her coldness convinced him she would have condoned his ancestors’ crimes. Terrified by her attitude, he resolved to leave her in control of her fortune after settling their debts, and to take charge of educating their children himself, determined to raise them as gentlemen. He invested prudently, repaid the debt sooner than expected, and had recently completed the payment of the eleven hundred thousand francs, making restitution without harming his children's prospects. These were the reasons for payments to Madame Jeanrenaud and her son.

Popinot asked whether Madame d’Espard knew his motives for retiring from public life. “Yes, monsieur,” the Marquis replied. Popinot, visibly moved, excused his clerk. He then inquired about other allegations, such as that the Marquis was engaged in a business unbecoming his rank. The Marquis declined to discuss it there and summoned Comte de Nouvion, an old family friend, explaining that he used the rooms as offices for the publication of the Picturesque History of China by a reputable firm, not as his private residence.

He showed Popinot his ground-floor apartment—a place of simple, touching harmony that struck the judge. The pleasant weather and garden convinced Popinot that a madman would hardly produce such beauty. The Marquis said he intended to remain until his younger son finished his studies, then travel with the children to European capitals to further their education and languages. He explained his China project: Comte de Nouvion, returned to France without fortune, was his partner; the Abbé Grozier, Keeper of the Books at the Arsenal, had been his tutor and taught him about China. Though enamored of China’s history, he remained a French gentleman. Financially, they had two thousand five hundred subscribers—twelve hundred in France—the book would cost about three hundred francs, and the Comte would earn six to seven thousand francs a year from it; the hundred thousand francs he hoped to make would cover his children’s education and pleasures. Twelve years of retirement to educate his children had cost him his memory at Court and political prospects, but he believed he had purified his family’s past and secured a glorious future for his children—a noble, if secret, task.

At that moment horses clattered in the courtyard. The two boys entered, robust and sunburnt, dressed for riding. They greeted their father and the lawyer. Popinot needed no further evidence of the Marquis’ bond with his sons. The boys reported shooting success and that they had ridden in the Bois, where they had seen their mother. “Why didn’t you speak to her?” the Marquis asked. “I think I noticed, father, that she doesn’t want us to speak to her in public,” Clément whispered. A cloud passed over the Marquis’ brow; Popinot felt deep sympathy at the scene of fatherly devotion. “Justice may look in here at any time,” the Marquis said. “If anyone is mad, it can only be the children, who are a little crazy about their father, and the father who is very crazy about his children—but that kind of madness rings true.”

Madame Jeanrenaud then entered, disregarding protests from the man-servant. “I take no roundabout ways!” she exclaimed. Bowing to those present, she announced she had come too late because the criminal judge was there. She declared that she and her son were of one mind to return everything, for their honor was at stake; they preferred to give back everything rather than cause trouble. “They must be as stupid as pans without handles to call you a lunatic,” she added. The boys clung to their father, alarmed.

“Silence, madame,” Popinot interposed. The Marquis sent his children to the garden. Popinot addressed Madame Jeanrenaud: the payments made by the Marquis were legally due, though prompted by an expansive sense of honesty. If every possessor of confiscated goods were compelled to make restitution every century and a half, few legitimate owners would remain in France. The law allowed M. d’Espard to dispose of his income without accounting for it. A Commission in Lunacy is appropriate only when actions lack reason; here, the remittances were driven by the highest motives. She could keep the money without remorse and let the world misinterpret a noble action. Popinot declared he would rather see the Marquis crowned than subjected to a Commission. Madame Jeanrenaud applauded his defense, calling it judge-like and warmly praising his words.

The Marquis extended his hand to Popinot, who pressed it gently; a private understanding passed between the humane judge and the noble, sublime man. “Monsieur le Marquis,” Popinot added, “from the first words of this inquiry, I regarded my clerk as unnecessary.” Leading M. d’Espard to the window, he said it was time to return home and that Madame la Marquise had likely acted under an influence the Marquis should counteract. Popinot left, the scene lingering with him; he even thought the rooms would suit him if the Marquis left them.

The next morning, having written his report the night before, Popinot went to the Palais de Justice to pursue prompt justice. As he prepared to put on his gown, the usher said the President wished to see him privately. “Good morning, my dear Popinot,” the President greeted. “I’ve been waiting.” Popinot asked if something was wrong. The President called it a silly trifle: the Keeper of the Seals, with whom he had dined, had suggested it would be best if Popinot did not sit on the case. Despite Popinot’s protest that he had left Madame d’Espard’s house before tea, the President cited propriety—“Caesar’s wife must not be suspected”—and the need to avoid any appearance of conflict that might expose the Bench to journalistic malice.

It was to be a matter of propriety, not discipline. Popinot was to signal his retirement from the case as a personal favor; in recompense, the President would secure the Cross of the Legion of Honor for him, long deserved. M. Camusot, a newly appointed judge from a provincial court, had been named in Popinot’s place. Popinot noted with ironic amusement Camusot’s pale ambition. He bowed and withdrew, choosing not to contest the implication.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Dedicated to Lieutenant-Colonel Periollas, as a testimony of the author's esteem.

Have you felt unease and sadness in the crowded galleries since the revolution of 1830? Balzac argues the Salon has been swamped by quantity. Where careful selection once showcased the best and provoked debate—helping form reputations like Delacroix and Ingres—exhibitions now drown in numbers. Before 1817 only a few paintings were shown; today galleries overflow, individual works lose impact, shows close quickly, and the Salon’s role as arbiter of taste has become a tumultuous bazaar. Great artists are obscured by mediocrity, and talk of unrecognized genius grows when the Academy’s discerning judgment is absent.

Amid this swelling catalogue stands Pierre Grassou, nicknamed “Fougeres.” In 1832 he lives in a cramped rue de Navarin apartment with a modest studio overlooking Montmartre. Tidy and careful, Grassou is a genre painter who avoids grand historical subjects, producing easel-sized works suited to his means.

One December morning the dealer Elie Magus visits. Habitually buying Grassou’s pictures cheaply, Magus proposes a commission: portrait the bourgeois Vervelle family, who wish to enter the art world. Their daughter's substantial dowry and Magus’s hint that marriage might secure Grassou’s future startle him; he sees the commission as a chance to become a portraitist and improve his status.

Balzac traces Grassou’s past: he studied under Servin and Schinner but never mastered their techniques; his training brought little recognition and frequent ridicule. After a rejected 1819 Salon attempt Schinner advised him to quit; Grassou refined the piece and sold it to Magus for a meager sum, launching a long career of perseverance. His story embodies many contemporaries’ plight: ambition checked by limited talent, and the reality that circumstance and market forces often outweigh true merit.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
For days Pierre Fougeres (later called Grassou) waited near Elie Magus’s shop hoping his painting would attract notice, but it was ignored. A week later it disappeared; when he asked Elie, the dealer said he had sold it and would buy more if Fougeres produced Flemish interiors, anatomy lessons, and landscapes. Overjoyed, Fougeres felt validated and missed the ironic distance a more cynical artist might have kept.

He met his friend Joseph Bridau but, absorbed in painting, could not enjoy the evening. Back home he sketched, bought supplies, hired models, and devoted himself to his craft. After two months he produced four canvases. Schinner and Bridau called them derivative and urged literature, but Fougeres retouched the works and sold them to Elie for twenty-five francs apiece, barely breaking even. He noticed a strange mist over his paintings in the shop but dismissed it.

Over seven years he developed competent, if unremarkable, work. Elie sold the pieces and Fougeres earned a modest living. At the 1829 Exhibition, with friends’ help, he entered a striking piece—a young man in prison inspired by Gerard Dou—which drew attention even from Charles X. Madame bought it and commissioned more.

Grassou’s success rested more on imitation than innovation; yet his modesty, perseverance, punctuality, and steadiness won supporters. He furnished a studio, completed commissions reliably, and by thirty-seven had produced around two hundred paintings in a style that pleased the bourgeoisie. Friends often preferred his character to his art and regretted his career choice, though he offered sound advice and kept a keen appreciation for beauty.

After the July Revolution he continued submitting to the Salon, lived frugally, and managed finances with his notary Cardot. He dreamed of a comfortable artistic life and aspired to join the Institute. One day the wealthy Vervelle family came for portraits; the father admired Grassou’s Legion of Honor cross, the family liked his work, and Pierre was charmed by their daughter Virginie. They began to regard him as a suitable match, blending personal hopes with professional ambitions.

While painting Virginie, Grassou became part of the Vervelle household. Near the end of a sitting Bridau burst in, disheveled, begged five hundred francs to meet creditors, and criticized Grassou’s style, urging acceptance of nature’s imperfections. Bridau left; Madame Vervelle feared he had spoiled the portrait, but Grassou assured her he had improved it.

Flattered, Grassou accepted an invitation to the Vervelles’ country house at Ville-d’Avray. They showed off their gallery, boasting its value at three hundred thousand francs. Grassou was shocked to find many canvases were his own works, long sold to Elie. Vervelle promised to double his daughter’s dowry if Grassou could prove the claim. The revelation raised Grassou’s standing; when he later completed their portraits he gifted them, securing their favor.

Today Pierre Grassou is a competent portrait painter, earning about twenty thousand francs a year, living on his wife’s six-thousand-franc dowry, painting bourgeois portraits for five hundred francs apiece, and wearing the Legion of Honor. Yet he remains insecure—sure other artists mock him and critics ignore him—secretly swapping weaker works in the Ville-d’Avray gallery for those by celebrated painters. His mediocrity, tempered by kindness and generosity, makes him oddly sympathetic.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

In some provincial towns a cloistered melancholy hangs over certain houses; silence seems to freeze life, broken only by a solitary figure at a window. That mood brooded over a Saumur house on a steep, seldom-used pebble street lined with centuries-old wooden buildings. These houses, prized by antiquarians, display carved oaken beams, slate façades, worn sills with neglected flower-pots, and heavy studded doors marked with Protestant signs or bourgeois insignia—so the history of France appears written in wood and stone.

Beside a dilapidated dwelling stood a country gentleman’s mansion; its stone arch bore armorials eroded by the revolutions since 1789. Ground floors were plain, dark rooms where goods lay exposed and a girl might call to her parents to serve a customer. Local life and commerce bowed to the weather: merchants, wine‑growers, and coopers watched frost and rain anxiously, for a bad season could ruin them. On Saturdays vineyards drew their owners away; streets fell quiet and neighbors merely watched one another.

The most melancholy mansion belonged to Monsieur Grandet. To understand the house one must know the man. Grandet began as a master cooper in 1789 and married a rich wood‑merchant’s daughter. With his savings and his wife’s dowry he bought prime vineyards during the sale of church property, earning a reputation as protector of local nobility and supplier to Republican armies. Through shrewd investments and service as mayor in 1806, he steadily increased his fortune; that year he also inherited three fortunes, adding vast vineyards, farms, and an abbey, making him one of the region’s wealthiest men.

Townspeople speculated about his riches; his timing of sales secured maximum profit, making him both admired and feared. By 1816 his fortune was estimated at nearly four million francs, and many believed he kept as much again in cash. Frugality governed his life; he spent only on necessities. Plain and unpretentious, he spoke little and stuttered in long conversation, an awkwardness that concealed calculation; he never acted without consulting his wife, whom he kept dependent.

Only a handful entered his house, among them the young magistrate C. de Bonfons, who hoped to win Grandet’s daughter Eugénie. The town’s social life revolved around rivalry for her—chiefly between the Cruchots and the des Grassins—until early 1811, when the Cruchots secured the advantage by arranging Grandet’s ostentatious payment in gold for the Froidfond estate, confirming his wealth and influence throughout Saumur.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“The house of Monsieur Grandet” perches cold and silent above the town, sheltered by ruined ramparts. Its entrance, set between two pillars beneath a tufa arch—soft white stone that rarely survives two centuries—bears weathered holes giving the façade a jail‑like look. Above the arch, a crumbling bas‑relief of the four seasons is topped by a plinth where plants, even a small cherry tree, have taken root.

A solid oak door, split and iron‑bolted, has a small rusted grating through which curious visitors peer into a dark vault that opens onto a garden enclosed by thick, damp walls—the remnants of ramparts dividing neighboring plots.

The principal ground‑floor room serves as antechamber, salon, office, boudoir and dining room. Wood‑paneled and gray with exposed beams, it has two street‑facing windows, a white stone chimney topped by an old brass clock and a greenish mirror, and antique chairs in faded tapestry. In each corner stand closets or buffets with dirty shelves. Between the windows sits an old card table with a barometer and two pastel portraits of Madame Grandet’s ancestors. Red gros de Tours curtains—a rare luxury—contradict Monsieur Grandet’s stinginess. A straw chair by the window lets Madame Grandet watch passersby; a work table and Eugenie’s armchair complete the room.

For fifteen years Madame and Mademoiselle Grandet’s lives have been governed by strict economy: fires lit only on specified dates, the same foot‑warmer used through cold months. La Grande Nanon, the family servant for thirty‑five years, earned only sixty francs a year yet managed to invest in an annuity. Unattractive but indispensable, she cooked, washed and ran domestic affairs with tireless devotion; Grandet’s rare favors—an old watch, a pair of stockings—were treated as monumental largesse.

During the difficult 1811 grape harvest, Grandet’s relationship with Nanon combined dependence and domination: he relied on her labor while maintaining firm authority, and she, grateful for employment and small kindnesses, remained devoted. Their bond was one of power and dependence rather than affection.

Nanon’s domain contrasted with the rest of the house. Her kitchen was a model of cleanliness and order; she worked late and slept in a small closet‑like space, while the upper floors remained bare compared with the hall’s modest comforts.

On a November evening in 1819, Nanon lit the fire for the first time that season, coinciding with Eugenie’s birthday. The household prepared for a gathering as local notables arrived. Grandet preserved the ritual of giving Eugenie a gold coin; Madame Grandet presented a new dress. Grandet urged Eugenie to save for her marriage, underscoring the family’s thrift and fixed habits.

Guests, including the des Grassins family—Madame des Grassins lively and pushy, her son Adolphe bearing gifts—offered compliments that masked rivalry and calculation. Attendees subtly vied for a share of Grandet’s wealth; he watched with pride and possessiveness, manipulating relationships for his benefit. The evening blended familial warmth with cold calculation—until a loud knock at the door signaled an unexpected interruption that would change the night.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Nanon lit a candle and opened the door; Monsieur Grandet followed. His wife called, the players exchanged looks, and Monsieur des Grassins suggested leaving. A young man arrived with trunks. Grandet sent his wife back and went to speak with the newcomer.

The game resumed in silence. Madame des Grassins asked if he came from Saumur; her husband said Paris. The notary checked the hour; Abbé Cruchot asked his age. Eugénie noticed Nanon’s absence; others guessed the newcomer a relative. Madame Grandet urged they continue, aware of her husband’s irritation.

Grandet returned alone; the traveler entered and received polite bows and curtsies. Madame Grandet offered warmth, but Grandet, absorbed in a letter, dismissed concern. The stranger, Charles Grandet, thanked Eugénie but said he was not tired. At twenty-two, handsome and self-assured, he displayed Parisian polish: a hunting costume, carefully arranged waistcoats, a small gold toilet set—and despite instructions to travel modestly, he had used a private coupé to show his means.

His elegance contrasted with provincial decorum. The shabby Cruchots were taken aback; locals met his nonchalance with mingled admiration and disdain. Eugénie was captivated, longing for the world he represented; enchanted by his appearance and delicate possessions, she yearned to connect.

The loto ended when Nanon returned with sheets for Charles’s bed; Madame Grandet followed. Guests began leaving. Eugénie impulsively excused herself to help prepare his room, suggesting refinements—a warming pan, a wax candle—using her birthday as pretext.

While Eugénie fussed, Madame des Grassins engaged Charles; Abbé Cruchot made sly remarks. Grandet, having read a letter from his brother about impending bankruptcy asking him to protect his son, hid his emotions and offered Charles a modest room, downplaying his means. After guests departed, talk rose that Charles might unsettle Saumur; the Cruchots and des Grassins resolved to unite against any attachment between Eugénie and her cousin and to use the situation to their advantage.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
When the four relatives were left alone, Monsieur Grandet told his nephew Charles it was too late for business and set household hours: breakfast at eight, a light lunch at noon, and dinner at five. He professed poverty despite appearances and insisted his fortune came from hard work. He told Nanon to bring candles; Charles at first refused but accepted a single shabby one.

Grandet led him through the house, avoiding the cold stairwell. As they climbed Charles’s expectations fell—the staircase and decor were shabby—and he wondered why his father had sent him. On the landing Charles noticed the doors, especially one built into the wall that led to Grandet’s office, the secret room where he did business and hoarded wealth. Across from it, Eugenie sat in her room; after a brief goodnight she and her mother retired. Grandet showed Charles to his attic room, warning him to call Nanon if needed because the dog could be dangerous. Nanon returned with a warming pan, which Grandet dismissed with a grumble as he left.

Alone, Charles surveyed the shabby surroundings and questioned his father’s intentions; he felt out of place and uncertain of his purpose. Meanwhile Eugenie rose early with a new sense of beauty and longing. Dressing, she admired herself but felt inadequate beside her cousin. The garden and courtyard, once dull, now seemed charming; her heart swelled with unfamiliar emotions toward Charles and she longed to impress him.

Eugenie hurried to Nanon, asking her to prepare cream for Charles’s breakfast. Nanon reminded her they needed Grandet’s permission for provisions. When Grandet entered, he and Nanon quarreled over supplies, exposing his miserly nature; reluctantly he agreed to give some flour and butter for a galette.

After breakfast Grandet took Eugenie to the fields, where they met Cruchot, the notary. Grandet spoke of his land and the recent sale of poplars, revealing his calculating side. Cruchot mentioned gossip that Charles might be a suitor for Eugenie. Grandet reacted vehemently, declaring he would rather throw his daughter into the Loire than marry her to her cousin—words that shattered Eugenie’s hopes.

Back at the house, news arrived of Grandet’s brother’s suicide—a development with dire consequences for Charles. The subsequent meal was heavy with unspoken grief. Eugenie, moved and tearful, felt deep sympathy; Grandet remained indifferent. Despite the risk of provoking him, Eugenie and Nanon conspired to prepare Charles a better breakfast, and her affection for him deepened as she worked.

After two hours’ effort Eugenie prepared a simple meal that broke the family’s custom of a standing midday offering of bread, fruit, and wine. She set the table and placed an armchair for her cousin, anxious about her father’s reaction; Madame Grandet promised to take responsibility if he returned.

Charles arrived in Parisian fashion, cheerful despite a difficult journey. He said he seldom ate before midday but welcomed the meal and joked about wanting a partridge. His charm captivated Eugenie; she admired his elegance and felt a flutter of affection. Their fingers briefly touched when he showed her his ring, deepening the spark. The mood shifted when Nanon brought coffee: Charles called it outdated, and the women exchanged anxious glances, fearing Monsieur Grandet’s return.

When Grandet entered he surveyed the scene with suspicion, noting the breakfast and especially the sugar, and demanded its source. Charles asked for sugar for his coffee, but Grandet rebuffed him, suggesting more milk instead. Eugenie, quietly defiant, returned the sugar to the table.

Grandet’s demeanor stiffened as he spoke to Charles, then revealed devastating news: Charles’s father had died by suicide, leaving him bankrupt and penniless. The revelation shattered Charles; he broke down in tears. Eugenie and her mother felt deep compassion, while Grandet remained detached and immediately focused on the financial consequences, lecturing Eugenie about the dishonor of bankruptcy. He declared Charles must go to the West Indies to seek fortune. Eugenie offered to help; Grandet reluctantly agreed to fund the journey. The house remained heavy with grief as Charles mourned; Eugenie urged him to preserve his honor and resolved to share his sorrow—her life irrevocably changed.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
About four o’clock a sharp knock startled Madame Grandet.

Grandet burst in, rubbing his hands, exulting that he had “trapped them all”: he’d sold the wine to the Dutch and Belgians at two hundred francs a puncheon, half paid down. He displayed six louis to his wife and predicted prices would fall in three months. News spread through Saumur. Eugenie, excited, asked if he had a thousand puncheons. He confirmed it; she pictured the sum in small coins and asked, “Then, father, you can easily help Charles.” The mention of the neglected nephew stung Grandet. He scolded her for presuming to advise him, insisted any duty to Charles would be on his terms, and threatened to send her to the Abbaye des Noyers with Nanon if she meddled.

When Madame Grandet said Charles had not come down, Eugenie answered that he was weeping for his father. For a moment bewildered, Grandet withdrew to his room to consider investing the proceeds in public funds. His thinning forest had already yielded six hundred thousand francs; with this sale his total reached nine hundred thousand. Though tempted by twenty percent returns, he rationalized a more modest eight percent, imagining that in two years he might withdraw fifteen hundred thousand in gold. Nanon’s call to dinner interrupted his calculations; he accepted the eight percent plan, saying hunger would bring his nephew from grief.

At table he learned Charles refused to eat; he commented curtly, and Nanon approved—“So much saved.” When Madame Grandet suggested mourning, Grandet mocked the expense and told her to buy it out of her six louis: a hat-band would suffice. Eugenie sewed in silence while he ran through arithmetic. The town buzzed about the sale; Nanon kept spinning, and Grandet repeated his maxim, “Nothing should be wasted,” as he pursued more schemes.

That night Grandet, consumed by present gain and dismissive of any future beyond material increase, revealed how money governed his laws, politics, and morals. He bargained with conscience and religion, ordered Madame Grandet to pray, and finally growled, “The devil take your good God!” He went to Charles’s dark room and reproached him in his miserly way: he permitted weeping as natural, insisted he was a “good uncle,” offered a little glass of wine, and scoffed at the theft of a small wax candle. Madame Grandet and Eugenie, overhearing, fled in fear.

Alone, Madame Grandet prayed for Grandet and fretted over the punishment he had threatened for Eugenie. Eugenie lay restless; her concern for Charles swelled into a personal disturbance. In the early hours she rose and found him asleep in an armchair, exhausted by grief; his smoothed, pale face touched her in a way she barely understood. She watched him with a feeling that made her both ashamed and secretly happy. He woke, apologized; she urged him to bed and fled, fearful of what he might think. This innocent boldness marked a turning point: she feared his judgment yet hoped he might see love in her visit.

Next morning Grandet returned distracted but outwardly affectionate, kissed Eugenie, and acted as if forgetting his threats. “What has become of my nephew? The lad gives no trouble,” he said; Nanon replied he was asleep. His unexpected softness surprised Madame Grandet but was only surface; he had spent the night calculating schemes to enlarge his fortune and dominate others, equating imposition with power.

That day he devised a way to restore his brother’s honor without spending a franc: to save Charles by manipulating public opinion in Paris. In his absence Eugenie found tender pleasure in caring for her cousin. She prepared his breakfast and carried it to his room to spare him the shame of eating before her. Her innocent devotion moved him; their unspoken understanding suggested a closeness money could not buy. Life went on around this quiet intimacy—Grandet’s calculations, Nanon’s spinning, the Cruchots’ dinner—but beneath routine Eugenie’s tenderness and Charles’s gratitude hinted at obligations and changes that would shape their lives.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Saumur was more scandalized by the condolence dinner Grandet gave the Cruchots than by his earlier sale of the vintage, which the wine-growers had called a betrayal. Grandet, who fancied himself superior to those he duped, paid little heed to their censure.

When the des Grassins learned of Guillaume Grandet’s financial failure and death, they came that evening to offer sympathy and to find out why he had invited the Cruchots. By five, Monsieur C. de Bonfons and his uncle the notary arrived in their Sunday best. The dinner was sombre: Grandet grave, Charles silent, Eugenie mute, Madame Grandet speaking little—a fitting meal of condolence. Afterwards Charles excused himself to write a letter.

Once the women left, Grandet dropped the mask and displayed the craftiness that earned him the nickname “the old dog.” Affecting a practiced stutter, he controlled and provoked his guests while steering conversation toward bankruptcy. The president of the commercial court explained that a respected merchant facing insolvency could have a receiver appointed, but liquidation need not mean total ruin. It could be managed privately: if the merchant didn’t file a schedule and no creditor appeared, family or heirs might liquidate the business; debts could be bought at a discount so that, by acquiring all claims, heirs might owe nothing.

The notary urged this course to save Grandet’s reputation and his nephew’s prospects. Grandet, mindful of family standing, professed ignorance of Parisian tricks, saying he tended only vineyards. The president offered to go to Paris to deal with creditors and suggested Grandet provide some assets to facilitate negotiations.

Before committing, the des Grassins declared they would liquidate the brother’s debts honorably; the banker applauded, and Madame des Grassins even offered to go to Paris. Favoring the banker, Grandet pulled him aside to discuss investing in government bonds and asked him to say nothing to the Cruchots. They agreed to meet the next day. As word of his supposed generosity spread, the town quickly forgave the vintage sale. Once the house closed, he ordered Nanon to keep silent and prepared a secret trip to sell gold in Angers. In the early hours, Eugenie, worried about Charles, thought she heard a cry but was distracted by her father and Nanon carrying a heavy keg. Grandet told Nanon to tell his wife he had gone to the country and hurried off, determined to reach Angers before dawn.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“My father has gone,” Eugenie thought as she listened from the top of the stairs. The house was hushed; the carriage’s rumble faded and a cry from her cousin’s room cut the silence. A strip of light from the doorway lit the staircase.

“He suffers!” she cried, running up. Charles sat in an armchair, head bowed, a pen fallen from his hand; his breathing was labored. On the table were sealed letters to tradesmen—signs he was settling affairs to leave France—and two open letters. The address “My dear Annette” struck her to the core. Rooted, she felt shame and curiosity; curiosity won. She adjusted his head, kissed his hair, and read.

Charles’s letter to Annette explained that misfortune had ruined him: his father had killed himself, their fortune was gone, and he was penniless. He could not remain in Paris where the scorn for a ruined man would be intolerable. He would not ask Annette to sacrifice comforts to keep him; better to part. He planned to try his luck in the Indies or America—perhaps to go to sea—and might return aged and altered; he urged Annette to treasure their happiness but accepted that he could not demand fidelity, for marriage or new ties might become necessities. The letter ended abruptly, as if his hand had left the page.

Eugenie excused the coldness in the words, convincing herself a girl in love might not see its selfishness. She read passion into his phrases; “My dear Annette” became music to her. She mourned his misfortune and thought, “I have gold; I will give it to him.”

The second letter, to his friend Alphonse, asked him to settle affairs: sell possessions, collect whatever could be recovered, keep his horse Briton if possible, press builders to delay delivery of an unpaid carriage, and pay off debts, including six louis lost at cards. It revealed a practical, calculating mind intent on avoiding dishonor and salvaging what remained.

Overwhelmed with compassion and admiration, Eugenie ran to her room and opened the old oak cabinet where she had hidden a red velvet purse—a grandmother’s relic. Inside were coins: various foreign gold pieces, rare rupees—about five thousand eight hundred francs in all. Joy and resolve filled her. Secret love for her cousin and pity for his plight dissolved propriety. She returned upstairs at once, candle and purse in hand.

Charles was awake, stunned at seeing her. Eugenie, trembling, confessed she had read the letters and then, without hesitation, offered him her savings. She called herself a poor girl who wanted nothing, a sister needing to help a cousin. Kneeling, she implored him to accept her gold and, by taking it, to show that he respected her and was generous. Tears fell from Charles onto her hands; moved, she poured the coins onto the table. “You will be rich,” she cried; “this gold will bring you happiness; someday you shall return it to me—are we not partners?”

Charles could not refuse. He rose, kissed her, and at last revealed he would reciprocate not with money but with an object he held dear: a leather dressing-case containing, in a hidden drawer, two portraits by Madame Mirbel, set with pearls—his mother and his father, who were Eugenie’s aunt and uncle. He declared the case more precious to him than life, and begged Eugenie to be its guardian. If she guarded it and, in the worst event his fortune vanished, the pearls and gold in the case would repay her; yet he demanded that it ultimately be destroyed to prevent its passing into other hands. This exchange of trust and sacred confidence marked a turning point; Eugenie looked at him with her first deep, womanly affection. He kissed her hand, calling her an “angel of purity,” and vowed that feeling and sentiment must be everything between them henceforth.

They exchanged gentle words and good-nights. Eugenie, practical and blushing, suggested Charles keep the treasures in her old cabinet. He sighed in his mourning but kissed her hand and, as she closed the door, he murmured the ruin of his fortunes. “What matter? My father is rich; I think so,” she answered naively. He scoffed; she retorted about Froidfond and Noyers, and he dismissed them as poor farms. The exchange was sweet and foolish: from that moment, beneath his black, Charles began to wear roses.

Next morning, before breakfast, Madame Grandet found Eugenie and Charles together in the garden. He remained sorrowful; the three women of the house, respectful of his grief, allowed him the morning in the hall while they tended chores and awaited tradesmen for neglected business. Around five, Monsieur Grandet returned from Angers, unexpectedly jubilant, having made fourteen thousand francs on an exchange of gold. He had left his groom at Angers to look after the exhausted horses.

“I am hungry,” he proclaimed upon entering. Nanon scolded him for not eating. Shortly Des Grassins arrived to take Grandet’s orders. Grandet barely registered his nephew’s presence, absorbed in his financial success and amused to baffle others. Des Grassins, the banker, informed him that gold fetched high prices in Angers and that people had come from Nantes; he talked of sending theirs. Grandet curtly refused, hinting at giving his old friend respite from wasted exertion. A startled consultation between the two men followed; they glanced often at Charles. Des Grassins learned that Grandet intended to invest a sum that might yield—so it was suggested—an enormous return; talk even floated of producing a hundred thousand francs a year, though the deputies’ figures and Grandet’s actual plans remained shrouded in his dry, cautious talk.

Des Grassins took the moment to offer to go to Paris and asked Charles if he had any commissions. Charles declined stiffly. Des Grassins rebuked the nephew for the curt reply; he reminded him of family ties and honor. Charles, pale, kissed his uncle and withdrew. Eugenie watched her father with secretly swelling admiration for his mastery over affairs.

Grandet dismissed Des Grassins with a jocular “decoy those people; lead them by the nose,” and the two men shook hands. Grandet resumed his armchair and asked Nanon for black-current ratafia. His excitement made him fidget at first and then sing snatches of a ditty, pacing in what Nanon called “his dancing steps.” The women exchanged uneasy glances at his towering contentment; his gaiety frightened them when it rose to a peak. He chose to go to bed early, expecting others to follow his lead. Madame Grandet, caught in his current, lived according to his will.

During the two hours before retiring, Grandet grew more talkative and philosophical about money, offering peculiar maxims and showing the mindset that governed his life: “You have no sooner put your lips to a glass than it is empty! Such is life. You can’t have and hold. Gold won’t circulate and stay in your purse. If it were not for that, life would be too fine.” His cheerfulness came with small generosity—offering Nanon a share of ratafia—and an undertone of the calculation that made him formidable. The evening passed under a mood blending domestic routine, latent anxieties about affairs, and the quiet bloom of a new intimacy between Eugenie and Charles, sealed by her sacrifice and his trust.

Thus the house resumed its ordinary hum: tradesmen, household tasks, and the unspoken commerce of feelings and fortunes. Eugenie’s secret gift and Charles’s confided treasures had altered their relation; Grandet’s profit from the Angers exchange had reawakened his habit of managing and tightening the household’s finances. Between these threads—Eugenie’s selfless devotion and Grandet’s thrifty triumph—Balzac sketches the beginnings of passions, calculations, and the mingling of sentiment with the sober arithmetic of money that will shape the family’s future.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The next morning the family breakfasted together in a subdued, private way. Grief drew Madame Grandet, Eugénie, and Charles closer; even Nanon felt sympathy. The old man, absorbed in thrift and intent on sending Charles away to avoid expense, stayed aloof, busy with farm work and leaving Madame Grandet to watch over Eugénie and Charles as their relationship quietly deepened.

For Eugénie, love grew from their shared secret. Their bond took on the simplicity and tenderness of childhood: soft glances, gentle words, and small consolations. Eugénie delighted in comforting Charles, soothing his pain with the sweetness of new love; Charles, hardened by worldly passions, found in her a pure, sincere attachment and prized the modest, unpretentious life they shared. While Grandet worked in the fields after breakfast, Charles savored the company of Eugénie and her mother, charmed by virtues he had once thought limited to Germany. Eugénie became his ideal; she surrendered to their love without foreseeing the looming separation.

Three days after the des Grassins left, Grandet took Charles to the civil court to renounce his claim to his father’s estate—a legal act that felt like a painful betrayal. Charles signed powers of attorney, obtained a passport, and sold his belongings to prepare for departure. Grandet admired Charles’s new appearance in mourning and regarded him as ready to seek his fortune. When Charles gathered valuables to sell, Grandet offered to buy them, valuing them at nearly a thousand francs; Charles accepted with gratitude and gave his mother’s thimble to Madame Grandet. Grandet then insisted on paying for Charles’s passage to the Indies, a generosity that paradoxically cost him little.

As Charles prepared to leave, Eugénie confessed her sorrow; he acknowledged his obligations and his coming-of-age. The family wore mourning for a Requiem Mass for Guillaume Grandet, and Charles received letters from Paris about his affairs, which he shared with Eugénie. In the garden he assured her his affairs were in order, that his effects would be sent to Nantes, and urged her not to tie her future to his uncertain fate. Still, their love held firm and they kissed passionately.

On the day of departure Grandet unexpectedly packed Charles’s things for Nantes. The family saw him off at the diligence; after he left the house felt empty. Eugénie, determined to preserve his memory, kept his room unchanged and conspired with Nanon. Her beauty grew more radiant and dignified as she followed his voyage on a map and confided her constant love only to Nanon, who, though resigned, deeply empathized.

Two months later the once-monotonous life of Eugénie and her mother was consumed by the secret that bound them: Charles filled their thoughts, and Eugénie even looked for him in her aunt’s portrait. One Sunday Madame Grandet discovered the truth about the gold Eugénie had given Charles, and panic followed. They feared Grandet’s reaction and missed Mass; Madame suggested consulting Monsieur des Grassins in Paris, but Eugénie said it was too late.

On the cold morning of January 1, 1820, Madame asked for a fire to avoid the confrontation. Grandet, unexpectedly cheerful, agreed and revealed he had received a large sum from investments, boasting over breakfast and deepening their unease. Afterward he demanded to see Eugénie’s gold and ordered its return. Cornered, she said she no longer had it. His anger flared; he accused her of deceit and threatened confinement on bread and water. Heartbroken, she fled to her mother, who pleaded for her, but Grandet remained obstinate. Nanon, fearing for Eugénie, vowed support and that evening secretly brought a hare pâté, strengthening their bond as they faced Grandet’s harshness.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
For months the old wine-grower tended his ailing wife while confining their daughter Eugénie to her room by his orders. Townspeople learned she was given only bread and water; Nanon secretly brought extra food. The community condemned Grandet, and Eugénie’s gentle attendance at Mass drew sympathy and curiosity.

Eugénie’s chief sorrow was her mother’s decline, which she often blamed on herself. Madame Grandet urged reconciliation, begging her husband to restore their daughter, but he remained cold as she weakened. In spring Madame confided in the Cruchots, who were appalled and discussed possible legal consequences of Eugénie’s confinement. Eugénie overheard, feared scandal, begged them to be silent, and resolved to obey her father for the family’s honor.

When notary Maitre Cruchot warned Grandet that if Madame died Eugénie’s inheritance might force the sale of property and threaten his control, Grandet began to relent. Returning home to reconcile, he found Eugénie and her mother admiring a gold dressing-case sent by Charles. Possessive at the sight, he confronted her; Eugénie demanded it be returned and, in despair, threatened self-harm. Shocked, Grandet relented, allowed her to keep the case, scattered coins on the bed, and promised kindness. Despite this brief gentleness, Madame Grandet died peacefully in October 1822, urging Eugénie to seek happiness in heaven and warning of a selfish world.

After her mother’s death Eugénie clung to the childhood home; familiar objects moved her to tears. Her father’s sudden tenderness — taking her to breakfast and watching her with an almost kind gaze — made the household suspect his mind was failing. But at the mourning dinner Maitre Cruchot revealed Grandet’s true plan: to avoid dividing property or paying taxes on supposed funds he needed Eugénie to renounce her rights so he could retain control. Confused, Eugénie asked for the deed to sign. Grandet, visibly moved, proposed instead that she trust him and accept a monthly allowance of one hundred francs. She agreed; the next morning she signed away her inheritance. A year later he had not paid the promised money; when she reminded him he awkwardly offered jewels.

Over the next two years Grandet trained Eugénie in household management and his frugality; by the third year she became mistress of the house, and their monotonous life continued for five years. Eugénie’s sadness was well known though she never spoke of it. Her only company was Nanon and the Cruchots’ circle, who played whist with her father.

In 1827 Grandet, now eighty-two, suffered a stroke. As he declined Eugénie devoted herself to his care; he died still fixated on hidden wealth, urging her to take care of his riches. Left alone with only Nanon, Eugénie then learned from Maitre Cruchot that she had inherited seventeen million francs. Despite this fortune, her thoughts centered on finding her cousin Charles. She secured an annuity for Nanon, who married Antoine Cornoiller and remained housekeeper while he managed the estate. At thirty Eugénie had never known joy; mourning and an unfulfilled love for Charles, who had not contacted her in seven years, left her indifferent to society’s matchmaking.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
While events unfolded in Saumur, Charles prospered in the Indies. His early ventures earned six thousand dollars, and the slave trade proved most profitable. He traveled to Africa, bought enslaved people and other goods, and returned to Bordeaux by 1827 aboard the Marie Caroline with nearly two million francs in gold-dust, planning further profit at the Paris mint. Hardened and amoral from his experiences, he engaged in exploitative trades, customs fraud, and reselling pirate merchandise, adopting the alias “Shepherd.”

On the ship he met Monsieur d’Aubrion and his family. Once prominent, the d’Aubrions were now strained; Madame d’Aubrion, anxious for her family’s future, sought a wealthy husband for her plain daughter Annette and saw Charles as suitable, promising to help him obtain a title and income through marriage. Ambitious, Charles entertained the match. Des Grassins, aware of Charles’s return and pending marriage, asked him to settle his father’s debts; Charles refused, saying his father’s failures were no longer his problem.

In August, Eugénie—sitting where they had once pledged their love—received a trembling letter from Charles recounting his success, announcing the engagement as a pragmatic step to status, and declaring romantic love in marriage a delusion. Devastated, she recalled her mother’s warning and resolved to a life of prayer and acceptance. The parish priest, connected to the Cruchots and allied with Monsieur de Bonfons, had been urged to speak to her about marriage; consumed by unhappiness, Eugénie told him she wished to withdraw from the world and live only for God. He warned against such a drastic retreat, reminding her of duties to society and the poor.

Madame des Grassins arrived, driven by vengeance, to reveal Charles’s return and engagement. Eugénie masked her shock as Madame des Grassins produced a letter detailing Charles’s situation and the marriage’s consequences. Tormented, Eugénie asked the priest whether remaining a virgin after marriage would be a sin, exposing her inner conflict. She secluded herself in her father’s secret room, skipped dinner, and avoided guests. Later she asked Monsieur de Bonfons—the president—to stay, an act taken as a pledge of marriage.

Privately she told the president she could give only friendship and asked him to settle her uncle’s debts in exchange for her hand. He eagerly accepted, hurried to Paris, met creditors, and arranged payment with interest. Carrying a letter from Eugénie to Charles explaining her decision and conditions, he informed Charles, who was at first excited but then shocked when he learned of Eugénie’s fortune; Madame d’Aubrion remained unaware.

The president returned to Saumur and announced his marriage to Eugénie. Though wealthy, she lived simply and devoted herself to charity. Her husband entered politics but died soon after being elected deputy. Widowed, Eugénie remained pious, dedicating her fortune to the community and rejecting worldly desires. Rumors linked her to the Marquis de Froidfond, but she stayed independent, supported by loyal servants Nanon and Cornoiller. Her story reveals the tension between wealth and personal fulfillment, showing her struggle to meet social expectations while staying true to her values.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
MIDDLE OF THE STORY — the central narrative section; the core middle prominently emphasized. MIDDLE OF THE STORY: central, repeated and emphasized again.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

To Mademoiselle Anna Hanska:

Dear Child,—You, the joy of the household, whose pelerine flutters in summer among the groves of Wierzschovnia, how can I dedicate such a melancholy story to you? Yet sorrows should be spoken of to a young girl like you, since the day may come when your sweet hands will be called to minister to them. It is difficult to find a subject worthy of your eyes, so I have no choice; perhaps you will feel fortunate when you read this story sent to you by your old friend, De Balzac.

PIERRETTE

I. THE LORRAINS

On an October morning in 1827 a sixteen-year-old boy in modest clothes stood in a small square of Lower Provins, watching mills and simple bourgeois houses under linden trees. He noticed a recently restored white-stone dwelling with gray blinds and yellow iron balconies—a neatness that spoke of a retired petty shopkeeper.

As the sun lit a calico curtain in a garret window, the boy began a Breton ditty for newlyweds. A girl, Pierrette, signaled from the window; he offered a yellow broom-flower. He was Brigaut, her childhood companion, and told her he planned to settle nearby. Alarmed, Pierrette urged him to flee when a grotesque old maid below—sharp-eyed and prone to imagining scandal—appeared.

Overcome by Brigaut’s visit, Pierrette reflected on her past. Seated in a shabby armchair, frail and thoughtful, she recalled childhood pleasures shaded by illness and uncertainty. Her mother, a Demoiselle Auffray from a tangled family—the father had married twice and left a complicated inheritance—later married Major Lorrain, a Breton officer who died in battle, leaving them impoverished; the Lorrains could not provide for Pierrette.

She was eventually sent to live with her uncle and aunt, the Rogrons, in Provins. Unaware of all the intricacies of her lineage and her parents’ fortunes and losses, Pierrette’s fate becomes increasingly shaped by these relatives—the Rogrons’ control setting the stage for the drama to follow.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Père Rogron, an indulgent, ruddy-faced innkeeper in Provins, and his unattractive, selfish wife scraped by on their land and inn until inheriting a dilapidated house from old Auffray, Pierrette’s grandmother. Their children, Sylvie and Jérôme-Denis, were neglected and ill-bred; Rogron sent them to Paris to work in shops with little guidance.

In Paris Sylvie apprenticed on rue Saint-Denis and became a forewoman; Jérôme rose to head-clerk. They rarely saw each other but pooled savings to buy the retail “Family Sister,” achieving modest success. Severe and competitive, Sylvie ran the business shrewdly though plainly; Jérôme proved ill-suited to broader trade. Obsessed with the shop, stingy and harsh to clerks, they were pleasant to customers but sour at home.

While mourning their father they learned of cousin Pierrette’s inheritance. To protect their wealth they refused to take Pierrette in, delayed replying, and returned to Provins four years before Brigaut arrived.

Back in Provins their Parisian airs quickly faded as provincial norms reasserted themselves. The city’s vibrancy yielded to local stillness. With new means they took up travel, politics, hunting, and remodeled their house to mimic wealthier families, trying to join the town’s circle led by the Julliards, Guepins, and Guenées. Despite lavish dinners and ostentation—Madame Tiphaine promoted her husband—the Rogrons lacked polish. Invitations dwindled and ridicule spread. Sylvie’s belief that money ensured status collapsed; the siblings sank into ennui, nostalgic for business life. Bringing Pierrette back exposed their neglect; bitter and sidelined, they befriended lower bourgeois figures like Colonel Gouraud and lawyer Vinet, who exploited their wealth. The story shows provincial hierarchies and social codes ultimately trump money.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
After her grandparents were hospitalized, Pierrette—ashamed to live on charity—was relieved when wealthier relatives agreed to take her in. Brigaut, a childhood friend and her mother’s friend’s son, gave her sixty francs for the trip to Paris; she accepted without thanks. Penniless, a conductor briefly paid for a hotel but treated her like baggage; four days later a kinder conductor took her to Mademoiselle Rogron’s, where her cousins received her with astonishment.

Dressed in Breton clothes and carrying a small bundle, Pierrette was shy and spoke little while Sylvie and Rogron questioned her. Shown a modest room that seemed luxurious to her, she quickly became the focus of Sylvie’s anxiety over her lack of proper clothes; Sylvie resolved to buy her a wardrobe. The welcome revealed a chilling preoccupation with appearances. Where Pierrette had known affection and freedom in Brittany, the Rogrons prized possessions and outward respectability. Reproached the next morning for dirty shoes, she found their constant fault-finding and concern for material matters sharply at odds with the love she expected. Her innocence made their disdain especially painful; she began to stifle her joy and grow passive to avoid scorn.

The Rogrons’ obsession with appearances treated her as a burden and suffocated her spirit. When she tore a dress playing, she braced for stern rebuke. Life in Provins worsened as Sylvie forbade visits to friends and pursued social ambitions with Vinet and the colonel. As they plotted for status—dining, alliances, and local influence—Pierrette was increasingly confined and instructed; she was transformed from a lively child into a pale, silent shadow, enduring self-interested care that was not love.

One night, when Pierrette went to say goodnight, Sylvie turned away coldly and made her cry. Pierrette protested that her rich cousin was not as kind as her poor grandmother; Sylvie retorted that the grandmother had taken money while the cousin would leave her some—Pierrette wanted affection, not wealth. Accused of mischief, disobedience, and breaking a watch, she left ashamed. Rogron demanded punishment; Madame Vinet suggested school; the colonel hoped instruction might keep her quiet. Vinet called her a rough diamond to be polished and hired a writing master. The lessons produced ink stains and scattered papers blamed on Pierrette, and she was sharply told she must earn her keep. Rogron’s severity and Sylvie’s indifference deepened her isolation; complaints of pains were dismissed.

Only Adèle, the servant, showed kindness, secretly warming Pierrette’s bed. Meanwhile the colonel and Vinet blamed her Breton nature and pursued local prestige—investing in a newspaper—while Pierrette’s situation worsened. She considered escaping to her grandparents, but a death and Rogron’s appointment as guardian prevented it; Vinet hinted at an inheritance and Rogron secured a promise of eight thousand francs from her grandmother.

Preparations for Pierrette’s first communion altered the household. Sylvie insisted on the zealous priest Monsieur Habert, whose influence gave Pierrette solace; she abandoned escape plans and embraced the Church’s teachings, a change even Sylvie imitated. Habert’s sister offered to take her to school, but the Rogrons refused, fearing a convent education. Later, with the arrival of the Chargeboeufs and the alluring Bathilde, Sylvie forced Pierrette into domestic tasks. Without Adèle’s protection she was treated worse than the servants—scolded for trifles, denied praise—and over the years her health and spirit faded into a silent, overlooked shadow.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Before recounting the domestic drama Jacques Brigaut’s arrival would provoke in the Rogron family, it is necessary to explain how he came to Provins, since he plays a significant but passive role.
