Gravier suggested a country trick: affix hairs in wax on the doors of Madame de la Baudraye and the Public Prosecutor to detect nocturnal comings and goings. That night the doctor, Lousteau, the receiver, and Gatien crept barefoot to press hairs into wax seals on the doors and agreed to inspect them at five.

At dawn they found the seals unbroken—same wax, same hairs. Lousteau declared they had been chasing a will-o’-the-wisp and admitted the matter was more complex than they’d supposed. The discovery puzzled Gravier and Gatien. Lousteau privately told Bianchon that if Dinah were truly virtuous she deserved the effort it required and compared the situation to storming a fortress. His tone softened from mockery to admiration, and he sought Dinah in the garden.

Their conversation was partly contrived: she had asked him to study provincial life at La Baudraye; he accused Gatien’s gossip of misrepresenting him as adoring her and said her silence at dinner had been an unguarded indiscretion. He confessed he had plotted the evening’s stories to rouse emotion and test both her and Clagny. He reassured her that her innocence had been proved—if she had yielded to Clagny, he would lose respect for her—and professed a taste for perfection and disdain for cold, taciturn men. Dinah, embodying an ideal of superior womanhood, replied she believed Lousteau sought pleasure but not love, and that true love often comes late; she showed sympathy for his vulnerability, and for an instant the Parisian’s bravado softened.

Lousteau’s frankness and their exchange delighted Dinah. He and Bianchon spent the day entertaining her with anecdotes and wit—an intellectual feast she savored—while Clagny, Gravier, and Gatien watched, noting a warmer bearing from her. Word spread; by sundown sixteen people had assembled at Anzy—coaches and horses bringing curious neighbors. Dinah prepared the drawing-room with hostessly pride. Lousteau found a shabby romance among a footman’s packet—“Olympia, or Roman Revenge”—and, with Bianchon, turned its brigands, a Duchess’s glove, and Cardinal Borborigano into an improvised comic critique. The audience—old grandees and curious young women—drank in the show. Thus the evening, meant as both entertainment and a test of character, became a stage where Parisian cleverness dazzled provincial complacency, while the mystery of the sealed doors remained unresolved for the men who had come to spy on hearts and reputations.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
For three days Dinah’s thoughts were fixed on her two Parisian visitors. Lousteau tried to charm her while Bianchon, more serious and professorial, mocked the local men and probed her unhappy situation, putting her on the defensive. When Bianchon bluntly told her she had shut herself against love but now needed it, Dinah, startled, asked if he prescribed love. He warned that her habits made her unattractive; Lousteau half‑jokingly defended him as offering sound counsel.

At Cosne, after a crowd gathered by an old carriage, Dinah insisted they disembark and walked rapidly with Bianchon along the Loire. There Bianchon suggested Lousteau loved her but was frivolous and hard to keep; he advised making a friend of him, since Lousteau could bring beauty and happiness. Under Bianchon’s scrutiny Dinah felt vulnerable and unsure how she might measure against Lousteau’s past lovers.

Their coachman then announced Gatien’s return. Bianchon blessed them as they entered town. Lousteau immediately took Dinah’s hand and declared his love; she resisted, asking whether she should give herself to a man who treated her as a passing pleasure. Gatien’s arrival complicated matters. Lousteau whispered that she should feign resistance to preserve his honor and, in a reckless instant, crushed her muslin dress, leaving her disordered and humiliated. Gatien, oblivious, rode up while Lousteau tried to distract him; Dinah sent Gatien to fetch Bianchon’s papers.

Back at La Baudraye, Madame Piedefer appeared anxious; Dinah fled to her room. Outside, Lousteau and Gatien confronted one another—Gatien accused trickery, Lousteau shrugged it off as Dinah’s mockery. Dinah changed into a velvet gown and, weighed down by recent events, rejoined them in silence.

Intent on winning her, Lousteau declared he would stay in Sancerre and swore he had renounced Parisian vices. On the road back to Anzy his charm began to dissolve Dinah’s reserve; she laughed, a sign her feelings shifted. After a stolen intimacy in the gardens and an exchanged pledge before his return to Paris, Dinah accompanied him to Cosne, openly flouting propriety. At La Baudraye she praised him for showing what true love felt like. Her three provincial admirers, including Monsieur de Clagny, were left to reckon with their feelings; Dinah took Clagny as a confidant, lavishing affection while he remained devoted despite her new happiness.

Back in Paris, Lousteau soon forgot the days at the Chateau d’Anzy. He lived by his pen in an age when writing—after the rise of the bourgeoisie—had become near servitude: constant production or the appearance of it. He wrote daily for a newspaper and several literary magazines without artistic pretensions, ranking among Paris’s literary hacks. Deprived of advancement, he had become a journalist. Still his life was not unpleasant: he moved among blue‑stockings, aspiring actresses, publishers, and authors, enjoying a lifestyle that required little beyond paying rent. He kept theatre boxes, and the sales of books he reviewed covered minor expenses. Ten years on he remained financially constrained, imagining a modest increased income would make him rich.

Lousteau lived in well‑furnished ground‑floor rooms in the Rue des Martyrs rented for twelve hundred francs. His income varied—about seven to eight hundred francs a month—but he spent heavily on cabs, meals, and gifts for lovers, so certain months were tight and he remained perpetually broke despite receipts. Occasionally weary of this literary life, he joked with friends about marriage and romance. He had a mistress, Madame Schontz, who took pride in having a writer as her lover; she lived nearby and liked teasing friends about their entanglements. This bohemian existence would later affect Dinah: he could laugh about past affairs, including her, whom he had almost forgotten.

When asked about Sancerre, Lousteau recounted the three timid provincial men who’d failed to pursue a local beauty. He received a long, affectionate letter from Dinah, which he read and burned, dismissing her feelings; a week later another arrived and he discarded it unread. Madame Schontz noticed the scented paper, opened the hampers Dinah had sent—game and flowers—and suggested they host a dinner. Lousteau sent a clever but insincere letter he believed would charm Dinah. By December her letters were piling unread in a drawer.

Then Madame Schontz told him of a marriage opportunity. A young woman’s previous lover had died, and her father, a notary, wanted to marry her quickly, offering a substantial dowry. He sought a respectable man to restore the daughter’s position. Madame Schontz urged Lousteau to consider it, stressing the financial benefits and a comfortable life while warning about a strict mother‑in‑law who would scrutinize his past.

Hesitant at first, Lousteau agreed to meet the notary and family. He flattered the notary, recalled his own father, and presented himself as ambitious. The notary liked him, and at his first dinner Lousteau noticed Félicie, the shy grateful daughter; Madame Cardot regarded him as a man of promise. Buoyed by this reception, Lousteau felt a new purpose—stability, respectability, and the financial security the marriage promised.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Madame Cardot, who had borrowed the illustrated Gil Blas to inspect prints, impulsively took her daughter Félicie to Lousteau’s bachelor rooms to judge a prospective son‑in‑law. Madame Camusot, uneasy after unflattering reports from her husband, a Supreme Court Councillor, had shared concerns. One January day in 1837 they took a cab; the porter’s daughter handed over the key after Madame Cardot slipped a gold coin.

Around noon Lousteau, back from breakfast, was startled when Dinah, his former lover, arrived by coach and revealed she was a mother. Overwhelmed, he tried to dismiss her, but she insisted on staying. The child, Pamela, sensing the awkwardness, announced that his mother‑in‑law was inside; Lousteau found Madame Cardot and Félicie hiding. Dinah declared her love; Lousteau, fearing the arranged marriage he faced, began to see her as a means of escape.

Madame Cardot openly disapproved and left with Félicie, while Dinah, trusting Lousteau’s reassurances and his high‑minded airs, stayed. She admired the elegance of his rooms more than the poverty behind them. Pamela had borrowed dinnerware from Madame Schontz, who, learning of Lousteau’s predicament, warned him Cardot would not pursue the match. Lousteau, however, focused on his intimacy with Dinah and on avoiding a wealthy marriage.

When his friend Bixiou challenged him—arguing provincial women often fail to adapt to Paris and that marriage should secure legitimacy—Lousteau dismissed him. Later Madame Schontz told him Cardot had already arranged Félicie’s marriage to another, extinguishing his respectable prospects. Having lost a stable future, Lousteau became more entangled with Dinah; their domestic life, sustained by his entertainments, slid into the provincial romance he had once mocked, leaving both trapped in dependency and illusion.

That winter in Paris transformed Dinah as October had transformed Sancerre. Étienne, eager to introduce her to Parisian life, took her to the theater, where she at first felt self‑conscious. Lousteau, vain and ambitious, stripped away her provincial modesty by taking her to fashionable dressmakers and society. Dinah adapted quickly, immersing herself in the city’s culture and entertainments.

A newspaper item about Monsieur le Baron de Clagny’s promotion reminded Dinah of Sancerre, and she teased Lousteau about lingering attachments. Their passion remained intense: they exchanged extravagant vows even as Lousteau privately toyed with deceiving her when his enthusiasm waned, treating her as a useful asset. Dinah, however, retained dignity and ambition—intelligent, well read, and able to hold her own among sophisticated women; Lousteau came to regard her as both muse and partner.

When Dinah became pregnant she prepared to withdraw from public life but first attended a premiere where Lousteau abandoned her, exposing her to scrutiny beside Anna Grossetete. Humiliated, she later received a visit from Clagny, who warned that her liaison with Lousteau threatened her reputation. After the child’s birth Dinah devoted herself to motherhood and the household, pawning jewelry to help finances and believing her sacrifices would secure a better life.

Lousteau’s irresponsibility soon emerged: he failed to write steadily, accumulated debts, and grew indifferent. A second child increased Dinah’s burdens while his detachment deepened. Her estranged husband, Monsieur de la Baudraye, reappeared seeking a power of attorney to manage an inheritance. Clagny—now Attorney‑General—intervened, negotiating an allowance in exchange for her cooperation and protecting her interests. Confronted with betrayal and loss, Dinah resolved to reclaim her identity and seek a future defined by mutual respect rather than mere passion.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
From that day Etienne lived in luxury and Dinah kept pace with Paris’s best-dressed women. Lousteau, however, pretended ruin before friends, blamed Madame de la Baudraye, and affected helplessness to exploit Dinah’s supposed desperate love—all while remaining unfaithful. Monsieur de Clagny, disturbed by Dinah’s disgrace, warned her of betrayal; she only answered, “I know it.” When he asked if she still loved Lousteau, she cried, “I would lose my soul for you!”—a confession that left him speechless and tearful.

Gradually Dinah recognized Lousteau’s true nature. She described him to her mother as a poet: idle, pleasure-seeking, someone she felt responsible for and could not hate because she believed she was his only support. Her mother deplored the hell she endured; Dinah replied, “I will be a mother to him!” She accepted humiliations to keep him, compromising her dignity in hope of winning affection by emulating Madame de Pompadour. His theatrical devotion eroded her self-respect, yet she clung to the warped consolation of being his plaything.

Dinah rehearsed Le Dernier Jour d’un condamné, imagined their separation, and even contemplated suicide while walking in her garden, but her devotion persisted. Influenced by the novel Adolphe, she vowed not to become like Éléonore—refusing to display her pain in tears or reproaches.

Meanwhile Monsieur de la Baudraye planned to restore his fortune by manipulating his wife’s inheritance: underreporting its value, pocketing the difference, and investing in land. He told himself he could defer obligations to Dinah, promising to restore her fortune later. Ambitious for status, he envisioned a noble life in Sancerre, sought a title, and aimed for greater social prominence.

By early 1842 Dinah felt reduced to Lousteau’s convenience, sacrificing herself for his comfort. She dressed in mourning as a sign of remorse; her mother worried and sought to intervene. In reality the situation was less dramatic than tragedy—largely fatigue and disillusionment. With Clagny’s help, Madame Piedefer urged Dinah to consider life apart from Lousteau, suggesting her husband might allow her to keep the children and live in Paris. Dinah let Clagny negotiate; he assured her her husband would support a respectable return to society.

Unknown to Dinah, Monsieur de la Baudraye had been investing in a grand Paris residence to polish his image, aiming for a peerage and a well-furnished home. He invited her to live with him in Paris and promised financial support for their children’s education.

Now a Countess, Dinah felt the weight of title and expectation, preparing for social prominence that contrasted with earlier struggles. In May 1842 she paid off her debts and invited Lousteau to dinner—an event that marked a decisive change. When he arrived he was struck by her transformation. Dinah told him of her new status and declared their separation: she still cared but insisted on a calm parting. Lousteau, feeling betrayed, accused her of ruining his life; she stood firm, refusing blame. Their last exchange mixed sorrow and defiance. Dinah left both liberated and heartbroken.

In her new life she made her home a model of comfort, often spending more than her husband expected, and kept a composed, fashionable exterior. But she missed passion, struggled to connect with other women, and—after Clagny revealed Lousteau was living with another woman—sought public success to fill the void. In March 1843 she was appointed to collect donations for charity, a turning point that restored her image. Summers in Sancerre found her a doting mother in a household proudly presented to relatives. Dinah maintained composure through these shifts, balancing roles as mother, Countess, and a woman shaped by sacrifice, yet still torn between love and ambition—an unresolved tension that defines her journey.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

Across France there were many Chevaliers de Valois; this tale concerns the one in Alençon. Though sharing the name, these chevaliers mostly did not know one another and had accepted the Bourbon monarchy after the Valois line ended. The Alençon chevalier was an elderly, tall, thin gentleman whose youth in Paris and romantic pursuits had been interrupted by the Revolution.

Well regarded among the local aristocracy, he was noted for his manners, wit, and anecdotes from Louis XV’s reign. A regular at social gatherings, his charm and discretion made him a favorite. At fifty-eight he insisted he was fifty, keeping a youthful air despite age. He had a prominent nose and a pale complexion that reddened on one side — his excuse for the flush, and for a voracious appetite, was a “hot liver.”

Seemingly frail but vigorous, he bore the marks of a lived life. He groomed himself carefully and kept worn clothes clean, maintaining aristocratic standards. Living modestly in a rented room above Madame Lardot’s laundry, he dined out daily on a small annuity. Beloved in Alençon for card-playing and amiability, he mingled with all ranks and was sought for conversation. Yet curiosity shadowed his private life, including persistent rumors of a secret marriage to a woman named Césarine. Financially precarious, he managed provincial life with dignity.

One spring morning Suzanne, a young laundry girl, interrupted him with news that might bring pleasure or pain; she feigned indifference about his rumored link with Césarine. Ambitious and clever, Suzanne aspired to leave Alençon for Paris, convinced she could better herself. The chevalier, struck by her charm and drive, encouraged her in a paternal tone and advised courting Monsieur du Bousquier, a wealthy bachelor who had shown interest.

Du Bousquier, by contrast, bore a dubious reputation: once wealthy and influential, his role as a purveyor during the Revolution had tarnished him. Returned to Alençon, he hoped to marry Mademoiselle Cormon, a rich old maid, to restore his fortunes. His brusque opportunism contrasted with the chevalier’s elegance; their rivalry over Mademoiselle Cormon, and Suzanne’s ambitions, set in motion an intricate dance of desire, ambition, and social expectation in a changing world.

Madame Granson, a widow on a modest pension with a small property, struggled to support her son Athanase, who earned only a meager salary at the mayor’s office. Athanase, a pale, introspective twenty-three-year-old, secretly loved his cousin Mademoiselle Cormon, whom he believed to be wealthy. What began as a practical hope to secure a better life for himself and his mother grew into genuine affection. Constrained by circumstance, he admired her from afar while his mother, aware of his feelings, saw the match’s advantages despite his lack of fortune.

Suzanne’s arrival altered the dynamics. She felt an immediate bond with Athanase and understood his struggles, yet her ambition pushed her toward deception as a way out of poverty. After a brief meeting with Madame Granson, Suzanne left town with money and new clothes; the community lamented her departure. As Athanase prepared for a dinner with Mademoiselle Cormon—encouraged by his mother—their fates diverged: Suzanne headed for Paris to remake her life, while Athanase remained in Alençon, weighed down by unfulfilled dreams and provincial pressures.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
In many second‑class French prefectures a salon gathered influential—but not elite—locals. Hosted by prominent residents, it included lesser nobility, clergy, and magistrates who kept a formal distance from the highest echelons. The salon shaped local life: members prized practicality over luxury, tended land and provincial concerns, resisted Parisian fashions, and used gossip as communal judgment. Young women rarely appeared except to seek validation.

In Alençon the upper classes met at the house of Mademoiselle Rose‑Marie‑Victoire Cormon, an unmarried heiress courted by relatives and two aged bachelors. Living with her uncle, a former grand‑vicar, she descended from a family long in service to the nobility and—through advantageous marriages—had acquired an aristocratic air despite middle‑class origins. Her house, dating from Henri IV, provoked envy with its prime location and careful upkeep: a neat courtyard, flowerbed, solid architecture, and period decor signaled meticulousness and status. Rooms were arranged for reception—a portrait‑hung salon for cards and local talk, and a dining room of provincial charm. Her circle numbered about 150 people, mostly mature, rooted in provincial life and rarely traveling.

Wednesdays were notable: dinners drew larger assemblies where modestly dressed women spoke familiarly about gossip, land disputes, and judgments of aristocratic behavior—topics reflecting the community’s concerns and values.

Despite wealth and standing, Mademoiselle Cormon remained unmarried. She had hoped to wed a nobleman, but the upheavals of 1788–1815 left few suitable matches; many were too old, too young, or otherwise unsuitable. Fearing suitors sought her fortune, she set subtle tests. As she aged her longing for love intensified—especially for Athanase Granson—exposing her innocence and naivety. Pious and virtuous, she stiffened under social expectation and became a local byword; occasional returning soldiers offered hope, but none met her standards. Her yearning for companionship and motherhood turned to despair, revealing the limits placed on women by rigid class conventions.

Completing the household were the Abbé de Sponde and loyal servants Jacquelin, Josette, and Mariette, who ensured comfort. Jacquelin, short and stout, had served twenty‑two years, doing everything from waiting at table to grooming the family mare Pénélope. He loved Josette, a thirty‑year‑old maid whom Mademoiselle would dismiss if she married him; the couple quietly saved, hoping Mademoiselle’s marriage would free them to wed. Childless, Mademoiselle directed maternal affection toward Pénélope. Each season she visited her estate at Prébaudet to inspect apple trees and mark the harvest with a farewell dinner—an Alençon event—where gossip about suitors, notably the rejected M. du Bousquier, swirled. At one such dinner she awkwardly defended du Bousquier against rumors involving Suzanne; an unexpected remark won laughter and admiration, leaving her to ponder her place amid love, ambition, and social expectation in Alençon.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The next day Mademoiselle Cormon, packed into her old carriole with Josette, set off for Prébaudet, unaware an event there would hasten her marriage. The morning after their arrival, Jacquelin brought a letter from her uncle, the Abbé de Sponde: Monsieur de Troisville, a grandson of the abbé’s friend, wished to stay in Alençon and required hospitality. Alarmed by the urgency and hopeful for marriage, Mademoiselle Cormon returned to town at once despite the rain.

Their sudden arrival provoked gossip; Josette and Jacquelin managed the household while Mademoiselle Cormon prepared for the viscount, fretting over her old-fashioned home and appearance and even sacrificing prized liqueurs to make a better impression.

When Monsieur de Troisville arrived he was greeted by the abbé and Mademoiselle Cormon. Distinguished and courteous, he praised her house and the region in a way that made her feel noticed and desirable.

The evening changed when the Chevalier de Valois and other guests arrived. During introductions the viscount casually revealed he was married, devastating Mademoiselle Cormon; she fainted. Du Bousquier carried her to her room amid watching townspeople, and news of the fainting and the viscount’s marriage swept Alençon.

To restore her standing—and with Du Bousquier and the Chevalier now seeking her favour—she accepted Du Bousquier’s proposal on the condition he consent to a public falsehood about their engagement, which he agreed to. The Chevalier remained unaware; the evening closed with Mademoiselle Cormon feeling mingled triumph and uncertainty as the town buzzed about her supposed engagement.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The news of Mademoiselle Cormon’s marriage devastated Athanase Granson, though he hid his turmoil. He first heard it at Chief Justice du Ronceret’s while his mother played cards. Having long pinned his hopes on Mademoiselle Cormon and schooled in Imperial fatalism, he retreated into indifference; his mother mistook his pallor for overwork.

Athanase wandered the Sarthe countryside, finding bitter consolation in its neglected beauty, and withdrew from politics and society. Townspeople, unable to grasp his suffering, lost interest; his mother fretted over imagined enterprises. Stirred by romantic tales, Suzanne returned to Alençon determined to help him financially, though they never met.

Gossip swirled about Mademoiselle Cormon’s match—motives and legitimacy debated. When the contract was signed and the wedding held, ominous signs—most notably a priest accidentally reading from the De Profundis—set an uneasy tone. The union with du Bousquier lacked celebration; the couple soon retired to Prébaudet. Du Bousquier began modernizing the estate, alarming locals who feared losing provincial charm.

Consumed by grief, Athanase killed himself, leaving a letter his mother misread as evidence of independence rather than despair. His suicide shocked Alençon. Madame Granson mourned privately, bearing a grief others barely comprehended; the Chevalier de Valois then revealed Athanase’s unrequited love, deepening the town’s sense of tragedy.

Madame du Bousquier returned and met Madame Granson in a tense, wordless encounter. Madame Granson arranged a discreet burial to avoid scandal; the quiet ceremony reflected the community’s wish to contain the episode. Over time the marriage became gossip and a cautionary tale: Madame du Bousquier, outwardly advantaged, proved powerless and unfulfilled—childless and subject to her husband’s domestic tyranny—while political upheavals and the Chevalier’s death closed an era. Suzanne honored the past with a small memento, leaving du Bousquier to endure the consequences of constrained choices.

DEDICATION

To Baron Von Hammer-Purgstall, Member of the Aulic Council, Author of the History of the Ottoman Empire.

Baron, your encouragement of my “History of French Manners in the Nineteenth Century” led me to associate your name with my work. As a representative of studious Germany, your approval gave weight to my efforts; your devotion to research sustained me while writing on modern civilization. Receive this token of respect from your sincere admirer and friend.

DE BALZAC.

THE COLLECTION OF ANTIQUITIES

In a small French town stands the Hotel d’Esgrignon, seat of the old Marquis Charles Marie Victor Ange Carol. Proud of Northman lineage, the family treated its name as a badge of stubborn provincial nobility, refusing dowries, titles, or concessions to court and state, and keeping local reverence despite neglect.

The Revolution threatened them: the Marquis hid instead of emigrating and family lands were sold. Mlle. d’Esgrignon, through the steward Chesnel, rescued some property. After the Terror the Marquis returned to a ruined castle; the old hotel was demolished and Chesnel used the family’s last funds to buy a new dwelling, wryly named the Hotel d’Esgrignon.

Around 1800 émigrés returned, including the Baron de Nouastre and his daughter, whom the Marquis married; she died in childbirth, leaving a son. Grief and reduced circumstances deepened the family’s decline. When a local contractor proposed marrying Mlle. d’Esgrignon—an offer she and her brother found insulting—a rift grew and Chesnel, who had negotiated with the contractor, was blamed.

Chesnel proved more than a servant: confidant, manager, protector as the house decayed. Politically the nobles split between Bonapartists and monarchists; the d’Esgrignons remained staunch royalists. By 1804 many émigrés had returned but fortunes did not revive. The Marquis became a local emblem of old nobility, presiding over a salon mockingly called the “Collection of Antiquities.” As the bourgeoisie rose and rivals like du Croisier undermined them, the 1822 Restoration brought little relief: finances strained, life modest, yet they clung to their ideals and lingering legacy.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The Marquis neglected estate management, and most believed the d’Esgrignons could not regain their former glory. People hoped the handsome heir Victurnien might marry into nobility to restore the family’s prestige, but after forty years the family lacked connections, finances, and continuity. Only Chesnel, the notary, urged the Marquis to adapt to post-Revolution realities; the Marquis instead relied on fate or divine intervention.

Victurnien, adored and indulged by his father and aunt, had never been opposed, which made him selfish and reckless. The Chevalier, a relic of the old order, fed his extravagance with romantic tales of past grandeur, reinforcing a sense of superiority. Surrounded by flattery and exempt from ordinary restraints, Victurnien felt above common people; his behavior alienated townspeople and produced legal troubles Chesnel discreetly managed. Encouraged by companions who enjoyed his company, his arrogance hardened into excess, assuming lineage would shield him from consequences. Du Croisier, a rival, sought to exploit these weaknesses to hasten the d’Esgrignons’ ruin.

Sent to Paris to revive the family name, Victurnien found court life self-interested and indifferent but adopted the elite’s extravagant ways. His charm and looks opened doors, and he became infatuated with the Duchesse de Maufrigneuse, blind to her debts and convinced their passion was pure. As pleasures mounted so did obligations: friends egged his spending while Chesnel kept propping him up. The tension between noble pretension and dire finances foreshadowed collapse, illustrating the dangers of vanity, excess, and the illusion of inherited status.

By the end of 1823 the Kellers had advanced Victurnien two hundred thousand francs without Chesnel or Mlle. Armande knowing. Victurnien had also taken two thousand crowns from Chesnel to conceal his sources and wrote deceitful, flattering letters to his father and aunt, who remained unaware of the looming disaster. Only Du Croisier knew; he delighted not only in ruining the d’Esgrignons but in dishonoring them as Victurnien’s debts mounted.

Du Croisier first targeted Chesnel, who lived modestly on the Rue du Bercail. After dinner he presented a bill for two hundred and twenty-seven thousand francs, demanding payment or threatening to expose Victurnien’s liabilities. Overwhelmed, Chesnel realized he could raise the money only by mortgaging his lands. Du Croisier seized the chance: he proposed to buy Chesnel’s property and completed the purchase within two days. Stripped of his lands, Chesnel was left with only his practice and his house, consumed by worry for Victurnien’s future.

Chesnel dreaded telling Mlle. Armande, who had just received a romantic letter from Victurnien describing travels in Italy with the Duchesse and remained enchanted. Chesnel’s revelation shattered her reverie: all was lost, and Victurnien risked dishonoring their house. Devastated, she resolved to rescue him—going to Paris under the pretense of a medical consultation and taking Chesnel’s last bag of louis to confront the Duchesse.

Meanwhile Du Croisier planned to cut Victurnien off from further support, informing the Kellers they would advance no more without security. Facing ruin, Victurnien briefly contemplated suicide but instead plotted to flee with the Duchess, forging a bill of exchange; when she agreed and he presented a bill for three hundred thousand francs, the Kellers advanced the sum unknowingly. Du Croisier, learning of the transaction, saw his scheme succeeding.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Victurnien entrusted the money to Mme. de Maufrigneuse, who hid it and persuaded him to return to the Opera. He nearly confessed to Chesnel but, captivated by the Duchess, stayed at her request. Planning to flee the next day, he returned to find her resolved to face the consequences. Torn between admiration and despair, Victurnien recognized the depth of his love; after a quarrel Diane’s coldness left him distraught and impaired his judgment.

Matters worsened when a warrant was issued for his arrest. Josephin urged him to go to the Hotel du Bon la Fontaine, where Mlle. Armande waited. Reeling, Victurnien and his aunt plotted to hide and slip into Chesnel’s house — a scheme that became a trap.

Chesnel, preparing to sell his practice to M. Lepressoir (who hoped to profit from Victurnien’s misfortune), was devastated by the young man’s plight. He considered taking responsibility for the forgery and resolved to confront Victurnien’s creditor, du Croisier, to negotiate a settlement. Du Croisier, relishing his ascendancy over the d’Esgrignon family, treated the affair as an opportunity to assert dominance and settle aristocratic scores. At a gathering in his house, news of Victurnien’s arrest spread and guests debated the implications for the impending trial.

Recognizing the stakes, Chesnel tried to influence the examining magistrate, M. Camusot, by appealing to his ambitions and connections, and pressed Mme. de Maufrigneuse—who, once she grasped Victurnien’s love and the consequences, felt compelled to help. Negotiations with du Croisier proved thorny: he demanded money, political favors, and social recognition—terms Chesnel could scarcely meet. Desperate, Chesnel offered a large sum, but du Croisier’s pride resisted compromise. As the trial approached both sides hardened; Chesnel even contemplated lying under oath. The legal battle came to symbolize a broader clash between aristocracy and bourgeoisie, where loyalty, love, honor, and ambition would decide more than one man’s fate.

Blondet, a competent judge, was distracted by gardening and domestic troubles: a much younger wife who had an affair with the local prefect. Though he had a legitimate son, Joseph, Blondet favored his illegitimate son, Émile, and after his wife’s death sent Émile to Paris with little support. Émile prospered in Paris with help from his mother’s family and Mlle de Troisville, gaining literary recognition unknown to his father. Joseph was groomed for the bench, while Michu, a young lawyer, moved through circles and often outshone older judges.

A shrewd public prosecutor was drawn into a political scheme against the d’Esgrignons that produced the forgery charge. Pressured by the Tribunal’s president and du Croisier’s ambitions, the prosecution unfolded amid maneuvering. The Duchesse de Maufrigneuse and Chesnel intervened, funding efforts to free Victurnien; the trial ultimately exonerated him and exposed du Croisier’s plots. Though socially scrutinized, the d’Esgrignons kept their honor; Victurnien’s future remained uncertain, illustrating shifting nobility, loyalty, and ambition in a changing France.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

To Monsieur J. B. Nacquart, Member of the Royal Academy of Medicine.

Dear Doctor—Here is one of the most carefully hewn stones in the second course of the foundation of a literary edifice which I have slowly and laboriously constructed. I wish to inscribe your name upon it, as much to thank the man whose science once saved me as to honor the friend of my daily life.

De Balzac.

Felix de Vandenesse to Madame la Comtesse Natalie de Manerville:

I yield to your wishes. It is the privilege of the women whom we love more than they love us to make the men who love them ignore the ordinary rules of common-sense. To smooth the frown upon their brow, to soften the pout upon their lips, what obstacles we miraculously overcome! We shed our blood, we risk our future!

You exact the history of my past life; here it is. But remember this, Natalie; in obeying you I crush under foot a reluctance hitherto unconquerable. Why are you jealous of the sudden reveries which overtake me in the midst of our happiness? Why show the pretty anger of a petted woman when silence grasps me? Could you not play upon the contradictions of my character without inquiring into the causes of them? Are there secrets in your heart which seek absolution through a knowledge of mine? Ah! Natalie, you have guessed mine; and it is better you should know the whole truth. Yes, my life is shadowed by a phantom; a word evokes it; it hovers vaguely above me and about me; within my soul are solemn memories, buried in its depths like those marine productions seen in calmest weather and which the storms of ocean cast in fragments on the shore.

The mental labor which the expression of ideas necessitates has revived the old, old feelings which give me so much pain when they come suddenly; and if in this confession of my past they break forth in a way that wounds you, remember that you threatened to punish me if I did not obey your wishes, and do not, therefore, punish my obedience. I would that this, my confidence, might increase your love.

Until we meet,

Felix.

To what genius nourished by tears shall we one day owe the most touching elegy—the tale of torments silently borne by souls whose tender roots find stony ground at home? The true history of a childhood stunted by neglect and coldness would move pity more surely than public tragedies. Such was my childhood.

From infancy I knew a mixture of neglect and occasional scrutiny. Sent at three to a nurse in the country, I returned to a household where indifference prevailed; even the servants seemed kinder than my blood relations. My elder brother and sisters, encouraged by our mother's unequal temper and favoritism for the eldest, persecuted me. Protective ties among children were annulled in my case. Often punished for my brother's faults and forbidden to plead my innocence, I learned mistrust early. These wrongs did not make me servile; they hardened me. Constant expectation of blows bred sullen resignation and a premature pride that tempered the ill effects of my upbringing.

Sometimes my mother spoke of educating me herself; the thought chilled me, and I blessed the neglect that let me wander through the garden, watch insects, and fix my attention on a star. One evening, crouched under a fig-tree and absorbed by a star, I was falsely accused by our governess, Mademoiselle Caroline, of mischief with a garden faucet and severely punished. The star, however, remained mine; forbidden pleasures grew more desirable, and I kept that glowing point in my imagination through boyhood.

Charles, five years older, was the household favorite: handsome, robust, indulged. I, puny and sickly, at five was sent as a day pupil to a town school with only a scanty lunch. The contrast between my hunger and my companions’ plenty wounded me deeply. Boys taunted me over rillons and delicacies I did not possess; one cruel trick—the withdrawal of a promised slice—left me ashamed and steeled my resolve. Once I was stoned; my mother heard of it and only lamented my supposed dangerous temperament.

Rejected by family and classmates, I distrusted myself and others. At twelve I was sent to Pont-le-Voy, an Oratorian school where dull boys languished. For eight years I lived a pariah’s life; my three francs a month barely covered study needs; I could not afford games and was excluded from playground life except by humiliating flattery of the wealthy. I took refuge beneath trees with borrowed books and thought much. When I won prizes for composition and translation and my parents did not appear at the theatre, I burst into tears, burned my crowns, and felt the deeper ache of being forsaken.

The sense of injustice drove me toward religion. At first communion I sought consolation in prayer and martyrology; youthful zeal fed sublime visions that strengthened imagination and deepened my inner life—qualities that would later temper any talent I might have. My father, uneasy about the Oratorians, sent me to Paris to the Lepitre Academy at fifteen. There, as before, neglect continued: meager pocket-money, strict discipline, and social restrictions. Monsieur Lepitre—old, ceremonious, given to Bourbon sympathies—managed a small subvention for me but never enough.

In Lepitre’s house the porter Doisy, who smuggled comforts for pupils, set up breakfasts and small luxuries for the well-to-do; resisting his blandishments was heroic for a hungry boy. Once my parents’ arrival in Paris promised escape: my brother planned to take us to dinner in the Palais-Royal and to a theatre. But I owed a hundred francs to Doisy; my mother, infuriated, refused indulgence and reproached me with future ruin. Sent back to Lepitre’s charge, I lost the dinner and the play—the first meeting with my mother after twelve years apart ended in reproach.

At nineteen, still under Lepitre’s guardianship and constrained by lack of money and supervision, I grew desperate for the city. My aunt, the Marquise de Listomère, on the Île Saint-Louis, was ceremonious and cold; she would not offer money and made me feel ashamed of my youth. Several attempts to slip away to the Palais-Royal were thwarted by unlucky coincidences: once Monsieur Lepitre’s coach appeared, another time my mother unexpectedly arrived, and a third time a post-chaise caught me. Political anxieties and punctilious morals kept me under restraint.

Study became both refuge and peril; absorbed in books, I delayed the normal stirrings of youth. In Tours, my mother’s coldness crushed me; on a journey home I threw myself at her feet, begged for affection, and was mocked as playing at “comedy.” Once at Blois I stood on the bridge intending to throw myself into the Loire but could not commit the act. Back at home I found only the slight mercies of my sisters and poor clothing. My mother’s ironical speeches and rigid sense of duty made affection a painful, unreliable thing.

Yet life at Tours had public excitement. The Duc d’Angoulême passed in triumph; Touraine celebrated, and I longed to attend a ball given in his honor. Despite my mother’s mockery, secret preparations were made: a bottle-blue coat, silk stockings, and a frilled shirt—my first finery. The ball overwhelmed me: heat, music, a crush of people, military bands, and cries for the duke. At first the spectacle disgusted me, but then a strange ambition stirred—an appetite for glory and influence over the masses.

Amid the crowd I saw a woman who changed me. Mistaking her for my mother’s shoulders, I was rebuked by a single word, “Monsieur!” Tears sprang to my eyes, and the look that followed—noble, angry, forgiving—awoke a passion I had never known. She moved with queenly grace and a purity that transformed my life: my star had become flesh. From then on the sky seemed bluer and I sought her everywhere in Touraine.

My mother, seeing my melancholy, sent me for a few weeks to Frapesle, a château on the Indre, hoping country air would restore me. For a young man newly in love but ignorant of his beloved’s name or station, this was an opportunity. Walking the country, I examined every house and old tower as if she might be there. The landscape—moors, woods, the valley of the Indre—enchanted me; there beauty is intrinsic to the air and my soul felt in tune with it.

From a walnut-tree where I later loved to pause, I first saw the castle that housed the woman I sought. A white spot among the vines, cambric fluttering in an arbor, convinced me she was there. The castle, Clochegourde, belonged to the Comte de Mortsauf and his wife, a Mademoiselle de Lenoncourt—an ancient house, proud and secluded, their fortune limited, habits fixed. My host, Monsieur de Chessel, who knew the count and countess, proposed we cross the river and call; his teasing reading of my enthusiasm revealed how transparent my feelings had become.

Clochegourde was modest but tasteful: five windows on the façade, terraced gardens, acacias, vines, and neat outbuildings. From the road the ground-floor seemed an upper story; a little tourelle and carved crest over the portico testified to lineage. The house sat in the valley like a jewel in green; approaching it quickened my heart with foreboding and delight.

We were announced shortly after the bell for breakfast; the count was at Azay but the countess—whose voice I had heard at the ball—welcomed us. My agitation nearly betrayed me; she returned to her embroidery and spoke with a calm dignity that both concealed and revealed feeling. Monsieur de Chessel explained my fatigue from walking and my being a Touraine native newly returned. Madame de Mortsauf offered hospitality with gentle courtesy; her glance, severe at first, softened when she saw my distress. She gave restoratives, words of comfort, and after consultation with Monsieur de Chessel invited us to dine. That invitation—accepted—seemed a promise: if I pleased her, my introducer might remain in the count’s good graces.

Madame de Mortsauf, though reserved, possessed a voice and manner that entranced me. At dinner she spoke of harvests and local affairs; at first she seemed to shut me out, but I discovered the charm of listening to her. The cadence of her speech, the brevity of her glances, the rare but luminous intonations moved me more than any artifice. I would prolong conversations and accept reproach merely to hear the melody of her voice and drink the subtle air of her presence.

Her appearance—white shoulders, gauze veiling her bosom, braids above a soft throat—had impressed me at the ball; up close, in everyday dress, she retained that luminous appeal. Her face defied easy description: outwardly simple, inwardly composed of restrained feeling; her hair was fair and soft; her brow broad and thoughtful; her eyes green tinged with brown—usually wan but capable of sudden gleams touched by sorrow or joy. A Grecian nose and graceful lips gave spiritual beauty; her figure combined strength with unusual softness. She seemed both mother and maiden—grave from suffering, yet childlike in feeling. Her movements, rare glances, and simplicity of dress formed a charm that made men dream: she revealed, without ambition, a virginal purity and moral elevation that made others thoughtful.

Beyond physical features, it was the constant emanation of her soul—tenderness to family, cheerfulness in serenity, resignation amid storms—that impressed me most. Clochegourde’s salon, panelled and modestly ornamented, matched her spirit: gray paint, porcelain vases with Cape heather, cambric curtains, and an embroidery frame. From its windows the valley unrolled—Pont-de-Ruan, château d’Azay, Sache—images that nourished my ideas and transformed my mind. Many of my thoughts in science and politics seemed first to live there, in that composed, reflective atmosphere. The salon’s quiet regularity deepened the impression of a domestic epic lived in a small and noble sphere.

When Monsieur de Mortsauf returned, my curiosity about him vied with fear that he might obstruct my hopes. A little girl, Madeleine, entered—fragile and pale—and the mother’s brow clouded. Madeleine, though thin and wan, preserved a spirit; Jacques, her brother, similarly weak, followed the count. The children’s frailty suggested family sorrow; Madame de Mortsauf’s health and spirit bore the cost. Monsieur de Mortsauf, about forty-five but aged by care, offered a polite, uneasy courtesy. His manner concealed more than it revealed, but the domestic picture—motherly care, frail children, the reserve of the house—revealed a life of tenderness restrained by trial.

As the visit continued, I grew more attentive to the count’s attitudes. Though outwardly civil, he was awkward and uneasy, like a man whose distrust springs from inability to understand rather than malice; yet his demeanor warmed when he learned my name and family connections. There was a respectful recognition of lineage: my father had served the Bourbons, and such devotion could not be disregarded by a man of old family like Mortsauf. The count’s complexity hinted at tragedies and virtues entangled by history—a man whose public reserve sheltered private tenderness.

Thus began a sequence of days at Clochegourde that would shape my life. The valley, the house, the maternal presence, and that first vivid encounter set in motion a passion that combined gratitude, reverence, and the consuming hunger of a first love. My lonely childhood had prepared me to adore and worship with an intensity only such neglect can generate. The domestic world of the Mortsaufs—humble and dignified—provided the soil into which my youthful devotion sank its roots.

I learned quickly the subtleties of Madame de Mortsauf’s manner: the economy of her speech, the weight of her silences, the glances scarce as jewels. Her conversation, even on trivial matters, carried an undertone betraying a depth of soul. Her benevolence and austere tenderness drew me into a state where admiration and self-sacrifice mingled. I noted the simplicity of the household, the English tidiness of the salon, the embroidery, the backgammon board, the garden, the interlaced paths. Each small thing seemed to speak of moral discipline and inner warmth.

The more I saw of Clochegourde’s family life, the clearer it became that respect would come as easily as passion; here was a woman whose humanity compelled love. The contrast between their modest means and noble air made an impression: pride without display, ancestral dignity humbled by circumstance. This image of noble poverty added to my reverence.

In the strangeness of my past—neglected by my mother, punished unjustly, driven inward by study and prayer—the spectacle of such domestic devotion opened me like a flower. It is not only beauty that wins the heart, but constancy, sacrifice, and the gentle influence of a soul that turns small duties into holy acts. Thus Clochegourde, its valley, and above all its countess, became the scene and cause of a passion that would shape my destiny.

Our visit ended with impressions words could not fully render. Madame de Mortsauf’s presence, the music of her voice, her discreet tenderness for the children, and the quiet dignity of the home remained with me. I left with a mingled feeling of hope and helplessness—hope because I had shared an attentive hour in her presence; helplessness because I was too young, too dependent, and too ignorant of the world to know how to win her friendship beyond the simple worship of a distant admirer.

Thus closes the account of my early years to the point where my life after twenty begins under influences both tender and decisive. From the neglect of my family to the sanctuary of the valley, my character was formed—partly by solitude and hard study, partly by the sudden appearance of a woman whose moral and physical beauty made of her my guiding star. The meeting at the ball and at Clochegourde introduced an affection equal parts devotion and pain, opening the way to all that followed.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Though ten years of emigration and farm life had altered him, the count still bore traces of nobility. Even a liberal could admire his chivalric loyalty and stubborn convictions: devoted to a cause, honest in politics, yet ignorant of France’s practical affairs. Upright but impractical, he was the kind of man whose zeal could harm his party—ready to sacrifice his life rather than his money.

At dinner his hollow cheeks and furtive glances at his children betrayed a burdened mind and frail constitution he had passed on to them. He blamed himself yet refused judgment; his harsh self-awareness magnified his family’s misery. When his wife returned with the children, an air of unhappiness settled over the house.

As conversation shifted, the count introduced me to his wife and, with curious intimacy, revealed details about my family I had not known. He asked my age; the countess, surprised at her daughter’s age, showed maternal curiosity and guarded hope for her children, otherwise remaining mostly silent.

“If study has made you ill,” she said, “the air of our valley will restore you.”

“Modern education harms children,” the count replied. “We burden them with mathematics and sciences, aging them prematurely. You must stay and rest here. You are overwhelmed by ideas. What future will universal education have unless we return it to religious hands?” He later explained his refusal to support a talented candidate: “I shall always distrust men of talent.”

He proposed a tour of the gardens.

“Monsieur—” his wife interjected.

“What is it, my dear?” he replied, revealing his desire to control his household.

“Monsieur de Vandenesse walked from Tours this morning, and Monsieur de Chessel has already taken him on foot over Frapesle.”

“Very imprudent of you,” the count said, shaking his head.

I quickly realized that handling his royalism required tact. When the manservant announced dinner, Monsieur de Chessel offered his arm to Madame de Mortsauf and the count took mine.

The dining room was modest but pleasant: white-tiled floor, varnished floral paper, family silver and mismatched china. Dinner was plentiful yet unpretentious. I sat beside her, served her wine, and felt a profound connection. A kiss we exchanged bound us in a secret that filled me with joy and shame. I strove to please the count, charm the dogs, and engage the children, hoping to be accepted as one of their own—any sign of gloom would jeopardize my place in their lives.

Dinner passed in a haze of happiness: I reveled in being under her roof, ignoring her apparent coldness and the count’s polite indifference. Love had its adolescence, and I was content. I made several foolish remarks driven by passion, but no one noticed their cause; even she seemed unaware of my feeling. The rest of my visit felt dreamlike as I returned across the Indre, enchanted by moonlight and nature’s sounds. That evening marked a turning point; the weight of past struggles clashed with the joy I had just experienced.

Approaching Frapesle I glanced at Clochegourde and saw a small boat tied to a tree—Monsieur de Mortsauf’s fishing boat. Once out of earshot, Monsieur de Chessel remarked, “I needn’t ask if you found those shoulders; I must congratulate you on the reception Monsieur de Mortsauf gave you. You stepped into his heart at once.” His words lifted me, though I sensed underlying bitterness.

Monsieur de Chessel—whose real name was Durand—had abandoned his father’s merchant name, a successful Revolutionary businessman, to adopt an old parliamentary title. Ambition led him to claim a count’s title under Louis XVIII, a distinction his children enjoyed without understanding the sacrifices it cost him. Pettiness and envy marked his character; his ambition, rekindled by royal favor, made him ostentatious—fine horses, lavish entertaining—while he showed kindness to me, offering the paternal interest I had longed for. His hospitality brought me peace.

The owners of Frapesle were intertwined with the dawn of my happiness, and I cherished those memories. Monsieur de Chessel’s ostentation annoyed neighbors, while the Comte de Mortsauf, with limited means, struggled to maintain his estate. Their relations were civil but lacked intimacy.

Jealousy did not alone account for the count’s isolation. His education had been superficial; he emigrated just as his learning should have deepened. Long idleness followed, and hopes for a restored monarchy kept him from seeking work; the trials of life eroded his spirit. A natural gaiety faded into moroseness; he grew hypochondriacal. Hidden affairs and other misfortunes further undermined his health and prospects.

After twelve years of hardship he returned to France under a decree allowing emigrants back. Crossing the Rhine he cried “France! France!” like a child calling for its mother. Once wealthy, he came back impoverished, stripped of authority and influence, burdened by his name. His unyielding opinions and past made him vulnerable to ridicule in a society that often mocked weakness. He eventually settled in Maine on a neglected farm that offered some stability.

The Lenoncourt family, having lost their fortune, welcomed him; he stayed with them while preparing a home. Mademoiselle de Lenoncourt accepted his proposal and lived with her aunt, the Duchesse de Verneuil, whose devout, disciplined guidance profoundly influenced the young woman. After the duchess’s sudden death, Madame de Mortsauf received visits from Saint-Martin, who corrected his last works at Clochegourde. The duchess had endured trials and left Clochegourde to her niece, but her death cast a shadow over the early years of the marriage.

Monsieur de Mortsauf found some solace in the valley, but the birth of his first child, Jacques, brought despair: the doctor pronounced the child unviable, a truth the count concealed from his wife. Subsequent births confirmed his fears—children who were frail or defective—deepening his sense of failure. The countess, sensing her husband’s turmoil, devoted herself to his care, striving to reconcile him with himself. She accepted his miserliness and mistrust, guiding him toward what was right, often at the expense of her own happiness. She resolved to remain at Clochegourde, fearing that leaving would harm their children. Her efforts masked the count’s incapacity and allowed him to find peace in her presence.

This brief history also illuminated Monsieur de Chessel, who, despite worldly knowledge, could not grasp Clochegourde’s complexities. My own understanding deepened as I spent time there and felt the weight of unspoken truths. One evening I found Madame de Mortsauf watching a sunset, and I sensed a shared sorrow.

“Some days are hard to bear,” I remarked.

“You read my soul,” she replied.

“We touch at many points,” I said, recognizing our shared experiences of joy and sorrow.

She shuddered, and I felt compelled to express my understanding of our mutual suffering.

“Ah! My childhood was like a long illness—”

“I hear Madeleine coughing,” she interrupted, and left abruptly.

The countess welcomed my visits, seeing in me an amusement for her husband. I often played backgammon with the count, enduring his harshness and criticism. Despite his tyranny, her presence softened my hardships.

As I grew better at the game, the count’s temper flared when I began to win. I learned to balance our matches—letting him win early, then evening the score. The countess understood my strategy and thanked me with a grateful look that filled me with joy.

One evening, after an intense game, the count’s mood exploded into rage. I left the room and waited in the garden. When she joined me, she revealed her husband’s condition and asked me to forget the incident.

“Promise me to bury the recollection of that scene,” she implored. “I don’t ask you to swear it; just give me your word of honor.”

“I will do all you wish,” I replied, “but I cannot change my opinion of him.”

She acknowledged my feelings and urged me to understand his struggles.

“Perhaps for my sake, you will forget what he said to you today,” she suggested. “He will wish to undo the wrong he did.”

“Five days without seeing you?” I lamented.

“Do not put such warmth into your manner of speaking to me,” she cautioned.

We walked in silence; she eventually said, “It is late; we will part.” I wished to kiss her hand; she hesitated before allowing it.

“Never take it unless I give it to you,” she warned. “Leave me my freedom.”

As I departed I felt a profound connection, pledging my love in silence.

The next day I reflected on our bond, feeling purpose and commitment to her. I recognized her sacrifices and strength. Our shared sufferings had forged a connection beyond friendship, binding us in a deep understanding of each other’s pain and resilience.

In the days that followed, small moments deepened my affection. I was determined to support her, to be a source of strength, and to honor the bond we had formed. Navigating my feelings, I understood our connection was rare and precious—something I would cherish and protect. Beneath the stars I vowed to honor her spirit and nurture the love that had blossomed between us—an affection meant to endure through life’s trials.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
This scene happened on a Tuesday; I had delayed crossing the river until Sunday. Much had changed at Clochegourde in those five days: the count’s promotion to general was confirmed; he received the cross of Saint-Louis and a pension of four thousand francs. The Duc de Lenoncourt-Givry was restored as a peer of France, reclaimed two forests and his place at court; his wife recovered unsold imperial property. As a consequence the Comtesse de Mortsauf became an heiress: her mother arrived at Clochegourde with a hundred thousand francs—the dowry the count had never asked for in their former poverty. With savings the count bought two neighboring estates yielding about nine thousand francs a year. Their son would inherit his grandfather’s peerage; the count thought to entail the estate on him without harming Madeleine, and the Duc de Lenoncourt might help secure a good marriage.

These changes calmed the count. The duchesse’s presence at Clochegourde altered the neighborhood’s tone and revealed her caste spirit, which my love for Henriette had hidden. Who was I—poor and insignificant, with only courage and faculties? In the chapel on Sunday, with Monsieur and Madame de Chessel and the Abbé de Quelus, I looked to the duchess’s pew and saw Henriette praying with composed indifference that bound me more tightly to her than all our past. She prayed with such abandonment she seemed a religious statue; it moved me deeply.

After mass vespers, the company proposed going to Frapesle rather than crossing the Indre twice in the heat. Madame de Chessel took the count’s arm; I offered mine to the countess and felt her arm for the first time. Walking through the woods of Sache, with light filtering through the foliage, sensations and proud thoughts overwhelmed me; my heart beat violently.

When she asked what was the matter I spoke of her good fortune and asked if their new dignities would change her and lessen our friendship. “Change me!” she cried. She insisted they had neither sought nor profited by these new laws; neither she nor Monsieur de Mortsauf were place-hunters. The forced modesty had benefited their son Jacques: the king—whose household her father joined—would favor his education. She had no ambition except for him; he must represent both Lenoncourt and Mortsauf. “Who will keep him safe in Paris? My friend,” she added, “be some day his guardian and sponsor. Go to Paris; if your family will not support you, ours—especially my mother—will help you. Put your desires into a noble ambition—”

“I understand,” I said. “Ambition will be my mistress. But I will not be rewarded for obedience here by favors there. I will go and make my own way. From you, I would accept everything; from others, nothing.”

“Child!” she murmured, pleased. When I declared I had “taken my vows” and meant to bind myself to her in a way that could never be broken, she trembled. I proposed to enter a seminary, become a priest, and educate Jacques—living near her so my love, enclosed in religion, could never be suspected. She turned pale and forbade such binding. “I should die of grief,” she said. Despairing love was not a vocation; she demanded I remain free. “You are twenty-one—My God! can I have mistaken him? I thought two months sufficed to know some souls.”

“What hope have you?” I cried.

“Accept our help, rise in life, make your fortune, and you shall know my hope. And,” she whispered, “never release the hand you are holding at this moment.” She bent to my ear. “Madeleine!” I exclaimed. “Never!”

Her purity provoked tears of admiration mingled with the bitterness of passion. I thought she did not love me enough even to wish me liberty; love that recoils from crime seemed limited. To hide my pain, I kissed her hair and joked that I feared her mother.

“So am I,” she answered playfully. “Don’t forget to call her Madame la duchesse and speak of her in the third person. Young people have lost polite manners; you must learn them for my sake. It is good taste to respect women—and social distinctions. The respect shown to established superiority guarantees what is due to you. You have drunk the milk of the Revolution in your academy; but as you advance, you will find crude principles of liberty powerless to create happiness. Before considering what an aristocracy ought to be, my common sense tells me societies can exist only through a hierarchy. Be on our side—especially now,” she added, laughing, “when it triumphs.”

I admired the combination of political feeling and affection. In defending her husband she revealed the temperament that made the count so humiliated before the duchess. Monsieur de Mortsauf, king in his castle, had dignity; yet he was humbled by the duchess. I treated the duchess with respect because of my youth; where others saw a duchess, I saw Henriette’s mother, and that sanctified my homage.

At Frapesle the duchess examined me coldly. Madame de Lenoncourt was fifty-six, erect and imposing, with a hard face and courtly manner. I behaved well; the countess murmured, “You are perfect.” The count, softened by his changing fortunes, invited me to dinner and warned me to cultivate the duchess’s acquaintance: her salon would set the tone for the faubourg St. Germain. The duchess learned my family connections and grew almost affectionate, telling me my great-aunt was a Grandlieu and that by the Charter my father was once more Marquis de Vandenesse.

To me, the revolution meant nothing; my sole ambition was to love Henriette better than Petrarch loved Laura. The duchess took me for a child. A large company assembled at Frapesle, and to a young man unused to the world, seeing the woman he loves admired by all is intoxicating. The count seemed almost young; the duchess hoped for change; I amused myself with Madeleine, whose cleverness made others laugh. It was a happy day; a word, a hope awakened in the morning and illuminated nature. Seeing me joyous, Henriette was joyful too. “That happiness smiling on my gray and cloudy life seems good,” she told me afterward.

I spent the following day at Clochegourde; the count left early for Tours. A serious disagreement arose between mother and daughter. The duchess wanted the countess to move to Paris, where she promised a place at court; Henriette opposed leaving—the Clochegourde air calmed her husband and soothed the children’s health. She had persuaded him to go to Tours to hide his condition from the duchess. I alone knew Clochegourde’s dark secret.

Henriette discovered the duchess cared little for Jacques and Madeleine. The duchess brooked no opposition; she used sweetness, coldness, and sarcasm to compel compliance. In those ten days Henriette endured the struggles a young woman must face to assert independence. She was tortured and wounded, and she found in me someone who would build a shield around her. When she rested upon my heart she told me of her troubles; day by day I learned what she meant by “Love me as my aunt loved me.”

“Have you no ambition?” the duchess asked me at dinner.

“Madame,” I replied, “I have enough in me to conquer the world; but I am only twenty-one, and I am all alone.”

She looked at her daughter in astonishment. The duchess’s visit meant constraint; the countess begged me to be cautious. The Thursday of ceremony was wearying, but the duchess returned to court and Clochegourde resumed its ordinary life.

My quarrel with the count had made me more at home; I could go there at all times. I clung like a climbing plant to the beautiful soul that had opened the world of shared emotions. Our fraternal marriage, based on trust, grew firmer; Henriette enfolded me with maternal care, while my love for her burned when I was away. I loved her with a double love—seraphic in her presence, searing when absent. At my age I was not yet man enough to torment her; she feared harm to her children and sat beside them when her husband allowed her rest. The mere sound of ardor shook her; she needed veiled love—support mingled with tenderness—the devotion she gave others. This brought languor and secret immolation. Her conscience and self-devotion awed me; the piety that bound her virtues filled the air with spiritual incense. She gave me only the back of her hand to kiss, never the palm, as though limiting sensuality. No two souls ever clasped each other with such ardor while remaining unbound.

I became part of the household and knew infinite sweetness—the privilege of being the mistress’s friend in a strange family. Servants treated me kindly; the children divined my affection and were discreet. True passions bloom more charmingly in barren soil.

But I also saw Monsieur de Mortsauf’s failings: perpetual scolding about nothing, complaints of imaginary ills, an inward dissatisfaction that demanded victims. On walks he chose the way yet complained whichever way we went; at home he blamed others. He took pleasure in touching his wife’s sensitive chords. Sometimes he fell into gloomy silence that alarmed her and brought tender attentions. Like a spoiled child, he let her serve him as she did the children. I discovered his petty tyranny in household details and realized how it stifled the family, hindered management, and impeded improvement of the estate. The countess had been noble in describing these daily struggles. My admiration for her rose above my love; I yielded to be a voluntary victim to come nearer her. She allowed me a place beside her and permission to share her sorrows; eager to die in her arena, I became a repentant apostate seeking heaven with my brethren.

“Were it not for you, I must have succumbed under this life,” Henriette said when the count had been more venomous than usual.

He had gone to bed. Henriette and I stayed under the acacias with the children playing in the setting sun. Our few words revealed mutual thoughts; when language failed our silence served, and our souls seemed to enter one another. We luxuriated in pensive languor and asked by a look, “Among all our days on earth, will there be one for us?”

Despite the evening’s poetry, desire crept over me. After three months of repression I could not accept my assigned fate. I took Henriette’s hand and caressed it. She withdrew at once; tears fell from my eyes and she offered her hand to my lips.

“You must know,” she said, “that this will cause me grief. A friendship that asks such a favor is dangerous.”

Then I lost control; I reproached her, spoke of my suffering and of a slight alleviation I dared request. I told her that at my age the soul had a sex and that I could meet death but not silence my cries. She forced me to silence with a proud glance. Ever since the gate of Frapesle, when I had taken her hope to be born from a grave, I had shunned the thought of staining her soul. She said she could never be wholly mine; if I could not be to her somewhat as her old confessor—less than a lover yet more than a brother—she must never see me again. She could die and offer her sufferings to God.

“I gave you,” she said, “more than I ought, so that nothing might be left to take, and I am punished.”

I calmed her, promised never to cause pain, and to love her as an old man loves his youngest child.

The next day I came early; there were no flowers in her gray salon. I rushed into the fields and vineyards to make two bouquets; as I gathered them it occurred to me that color and foliage have meanings, and by melody awaken memory. I enlisted Jacques and Madeleine and arranged two bouquets on the portico steps: white roses and lilies with blue flowers intermingled, symbolizing two purities—the child and the martyr. The countess discerned the sentiment and gave me a poignant glance, reward enough. I pressed Père Castel’s theories into service and recovered that lost science: the language of flowers. What charm in expressing our sensations through these daughters of the sun! I communed with the flora, seeking varieties that would complete my messages.

Twice a week I pursued this poetic enterprise, walking long distances to find flowers, taking pleasure in the world of feeling rather than form. Nature offered scenes rising to high moral conceptions: heather with dew like diamonds, mossy rocks like savage cries, anemone pulsatilla like my pure idol alone in her valley, sheets of water with floating life, a cottage garden as humble existence’s image, a forest path like a cathedral nave. I gathered these images and poured them into bouquets.

In September and October no bouquet cost less than three hours’ search. These floral symphonies expressed what no declaration could; my baffled desires found expression as Beethoven finds it in notes. In their presence Madame de Mortsauf was my Henriette. She looked at them often; they nourished her spirit. These neutral pleasures soothed me: abstinence exhausts, and a few crumbs of heavenly comfort keep one from disaster. Sometimes I found her standing before the bouquets, lost in agitation; she would lift her head and murmur, “Ah, how beautiful!”

Throughout the month I often saw her at the window when I arrived. If I was late, she wandered on the terrace and said she had come to meet me and tend her youngest child.

Backgammon had ended; the count’s purchases occupied him in inspecting his new properties. He was neither arrogant nor offensive, and our walks together were mutual joys. Once, the count, wishing to know what we talked about, asked; Henriette answered with words having a double meaning that satisfied him. Madeleine laughed. Our spiritual union continued.

The days passed with new joys. The grape harvest—the vintage—began in late September. The vintage brings abundance and hilarity: coopers and carts, girls and panniers. Classes are equal for the day; everyone shares the harvest. Jacques and Madeleine, who had never seen a vintage, were full of delight. We went to Villaines for baskets; none of us were to eat too many grapes. Jacques made me swear to stay at Clochegourde. The day in the vineyard was glorious: the children’s chatter, their racing about, the mother laughing with the joy of youth; she passed a hand round my neck and tapped my cheek—the only caress she ever gave me. I filled my basket and emptied it into the vat, moving slowly in regulated motion that kept my spirit free. I found the pleasure of external labor that regulates passion; I learned monastic discipline.

For the first time in weeks the count was neither surly nor cruel. His son’s rosy face rejoiced him. He had promised a dance in front of Clochegourde to celebrate the Bourbons’ return. As we returned, the countess leaned on my arm and whispered, “You bring us happiness.” To me, burdened with knowledge of her sleepless nights and cares, those words were more than any other woman could give.

“The terrible monotony of my life is broken; all things are radiant with hope,” she said. “Oh, never leave me! Be the elder son, the protector of the younger.”

We had another fête. The countess, wishing to accustom her children to practical life, gave Jacques the produce of the walnut trees and Madeleine that of the chestnuts. Gathering chestnuts and walnuts, seeing the children take pride in their small granaries, made charming scenes of innocent happiness amid autumn’s fading colors. Jacques’ walnuts suffered from rain; I advised him to hold back and sell later. His father urged prudence; the mother rejoiced to see Jacques listen. These homely scenes attached me ever more closely to Henriette.

I learned how, over ten years, the countess had changed cultivation—rotating crops and dividing the estate into four farms on share leases. She induced the count to cultivate a model farm and rebuild two farmhouses to relet for money rents afterward. Fearing to die before her husband, she sought to secure his income and her children’s property. Fruit trees planted in the last ten years were bearing; hedges and elms grew well. With the new purchases and farming system, Clochegourde—divided into four farms—could bring in forty thousand francs a year, not counting vineyards and woodlands. The new road to Chinon would attract tenants. The countess wished to invest fifteen thousand francs in each new estate to improve houses for better rents; this required thirty thousand francs, which she and her husband argued about. The fear “If I die tomorrow, what will become of them?” drove her. The count objected, but common sense won. That day we went to the Cassine and the Rhetorière to decide on buildings; she spoke gently, confident of success. A carrier wanted a large farm on the new route; another honest man was ready to take la Baude; the soil would attract tenants. In two years Clochegourde might yield twenty-four thousand francs. Gravelotte in Maine was rented for seven thousand a year; his pension was four thousand. Later additions might let her go to Paris and arrange Jacques’ education.

With what feeling she said “Paris!” I knew she wished not to be separated from her friend. I told her I would finish my education and work to be Jacques’ tutor. She looked grave.

“No, Félix,” she said. “That cannot be, any more than your priesthood. I thank you as a mother, but as a woman who loves you, I can never allow you to be the victim of your attachment. Such a position would be a social discredit to you. My father would never forgive your taking such a humble place; he would refuse you his protection. You must accept society as it is; never fly in its face.”

“Love,” I whispered.

“No, charity,” she said, controlling tears. “This wild idea enlightens me as to your character; your heart will be your bane. I shall claim the right to teach you sometimes. Let my woman’s eye see for you. From the solitudes of Clochegourde I will share in your successes. As to a tutor, do not fear; we shall find some good old abbé, my father will devote sums to the boy’s education. Jacques is my pride; he is eleven years old.”

At the Cassine Jacques, Madeleine, and I followed her like children. I left to speak with Martineau and saw how beloved she was. A laborer told me she was good and knew our hardships and treated the people well. I gave that man all the money I had.

A pony arrived for Jacques; the father, an excellent horseman, would accustom him gradually. The morning of the first lesson, with Madeleine shouting and all the household clustered round, was a maternal festival. Jacques wore a blue collar embroidered by his mother and rode fearlessly. The count smiled for the first time in months; the mother burst into tears and leaned on my arm, “I think I have never suffered.
