Do not leave us today.” The child jumped into her arms; I and Madeleine arranged bouquets for dinner. She said, “The fifteenth of October is certainly a great day for me. Jacques has taken his first riding lesson, and I have just set the last stitch in my furniture cover.”

“Then, Blanche,” the count laughed, “I must pay you for it.”

He offered her his arm; an open carriage her father had sent awaited with two English horses. As we returned, she whispered anxiously, “I am too happy; happiness is like illness—it overwhelms me; I fear it may vanish.” I loved her so passionately I was jealous. She asked me what my eyes said; I told her. She was more touched than by any gift, then poured comfort into my heart. “Love me as my aunt loved me,” she said. “If I take your life, must I not be grateful?”

“It was time I finished my tapestry,” she added. “Men find occupations a resource against troubles; we women need physical exercise to control inward sufferings. Each stitch rocked my thoughts; to every stitch I confided my secrets. While doing my last chair I thought much of you. What you put into your bouquets I have said in my embroidery.”

Dinner was lovely. Jacques offered his garland to his mother with grace. In the afternoon we played backgammon; the count was charming. They accompanied me to Frapesle in the tranquil twilight. It was a day of days for the poor woman.

Soon the riding lessons became a source of contention. The countess feared the father’s harsh reprimands; Jacques grew thin and wore dark circles. I advised him to tell his father when tired, but the measure failed, and the old huntsman took over. Angry reproaches resumed. Then the cost of the Cassine and Rhetorière buildings exceeded estimates by half. The news, given first to Monsieur de Mortsauf instead of to his wife, sparked a quarrel that grew bitter until the count’s malady displayed itself.

That day I had gone from Frapesle to search for flowers with Madeleine. Returning, I could not find her and heard cries in the house. Madeleine ran out: “The general,” she cried, “is scolding mamma; go and defend her.”

I entered the salon unseen. Monsieur de Mortsauf was in a violent tirade.

“Never marry, Félix,” he said. “A woman is led by the devil; the most virtuous would invent evil if it did not exist; they are all vile.”

He raged through old grievances, cursed the new farming system, and accused his wife of ruining him. He sprang about, displacing furniture; then stopped, moaned of pain, and accused her of hypocrisy. “She is smiling! Do you know why she smiles, Félix?”

I kept silence.

“That woman denies me all happiness; she is no more to me than to you, and yet she pretends to be my wife! She fulfills none of the duties of laws, human and divine; she lies to God and man. She has made me mad through privations; she is killing me by degrees, and she thinks herself a saint and takes the sacrament every month!”

Henriette wept bitterly, answering only, “Monsieur! monsieur! monsieur!”

His words burned me with shame for him and anger because they struck at chastity and delicacy—the warp and woof of first love.

“She is virgin at my expense,” cried the count.

At this the countess cried, “Monsieur!”

“And what mean you by your imperious ‘Monsieur!’?” he shouted. “Am I not your master? Must I teach you that I am?”

He advanced, face white, eyes savage. Henriette slid from her chair to avoid a blow, fell full-length, and lost consciousness. The count stopped bewildered. I took her in my arms; the count opened her bedroom door. We laid her, still dressed, on the bed. When she revived she motioned to unfasten her belt; the count found scissors; I gave her salts and she opened her eyes. The count left the room, ashamed. Two hours passed in silence. Henriette’s hand lay in mine; she pressed it but could not speak. Once she rose on her elbow and whispered, “Unhappy man!—ah! if you did but know—” then sank back. The memory of past sufferings and the present shock threw her into convulsions I calmed by instinctive magnetism. I smoothed her hair—the first time I touched it—and sat looking at the simple room: chintz curtains, a crucifix, her aunt’s portrait, the children’s drawings with locks of hair. What renunciations of luxury for such a woman—the chamber of a married nun, filled with holy resignation. Painful as the sight was, it exposed hidden, irreparable woe.

When the children and Manette came in I left. The count waited for me; he seemed to seek me as mediator. He caught my hands, exclaiming, “Stay, stay with us, Félix!”

“Unfortunately,” I said, “Monsieur de Chessel has a party, and my absence would cause remark. But after dinner I will return.”

He left the house with me and took me to the lower gate.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
As I read the letter I felt a maternal warmth beneath my fingers that contrasted with my own mother’s coldness; no wonder the countess had forbidden me to open it in Touraine, fearing I might break down. Soon after I met my brother Charles again—haughty and distant. His condescension and social lectures embarrassed me; only my deep love kept me from feeling foolish. Childhood sorrows and solitude had sharpened my perception of the affections around me, and since returning from Clochegourde I could better discern what genuine attachment meant.

I frequented the Duchesse de Lenoncourt’s salon, where Henriette’s name was rarely spoken; only the old duke, whose welcome eased my shyness, seemed aware of our bond. Just as I began enjoying society again, the events of March 20 forced a change. At Henriette’s advice I followed my brother to Ghent, accompanying the Duc de Lenoncourt. When he learned of my loyalty to the Bourbons his amiability became protection; he introduced me to the king, who appreciated my steadfastness in trying times. Then a letter from Madame de Mortsauf told of Jacques’ illness; her distress was plain and I felt compelled to help. Setting aside rivalry with Charles, I resolved to leave court and serve my sovereign as a reliable messenger — a mission I embraced, eager to return to Clochegourde.

After carrying out the king’s orders I evaded Bonapartist authorities, traveling through the Vendée until I reached Clochegourde. Jacques was better; the count welcomed me, and in the salon I met Henriette. Her surprise and joy were immediate: she prepared my room, the count saw to my food, and at dinner we learned of the disasters at Waterloo and Napoleon’s flight. My concern, however, centered on Henriette. She had even had an ice-house built for me; that thoughtful effort revealed a tenderness that moved me. During the following week she regained health and spirit, and absence had only intensified my love — her beauty and devotion impressed me anew.

Henriette spoke plainly of long nights at Jacques’ bedside, prayers and exhausted vigilance, and of the count’s harshness that pierced her reflections. She wondered why her words never pleased him and bore the emotional toll, yet held fast to duty, urging her children to respect their father despite the strain. She described their agricultural success with practical wisdom and a knack for foreseeing matters concerning her children, which she sometimes attributed to divine intervention. I admired her counsel and felt gratitude for her guidance.

She encouraged me to seek opportunities in Paris, stressing the importance of remaining available to the king. Our conversations deepened our bond: we shared hopes, fears, and a growing sense of belonging. But the count’s presence and marital tension were always present. One day she confided that the count’s behaviour threatened the children’s well-being; torn between duty and personal happiness, she hesitated to assert herself. I urged firmness, yet she feared the consequences.

When the count’s health declined, Henriette and I alternated caring for him; that shared duty drew us closer. His slow recovery, however, brought back erratic behaviour that tested her patience. Still she put her children first, often at the cost of her own happiness. Eventually I returned to Paris, absorbed in responsibilities but faithful to her. Our correspondence sustained us; news of Jacques’ relapse later reached me and the pain in her words was palpable. Throughout, Henriette’s resilience and maternal devotion shone: she navigated obligations with courage, frequently sacrificing personal happiness for family. My loyalty grew with her suffering; our love remained a rare, guiding light I cherished and hoped would endure.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
During a deeply emotional period I met a remarkable woman at the Élysée-Bourbon salons: immensely wealthy, of noble lineage and married to a distinguished British peer. Her beauty, grace, and wit made her the idol of Parisian society; she ruled with a firm yet charming manner.

The English, distant and reserved, live by strict laws and rigid virtues, producing an Englishwoman who, though delicate and charming, is trapped in hypocrisy and compelled to maintain appearances; her passion is intense but constrained. You may envy these women, but they are complex and convinced that love alone suffices.

My liaison with the Marchioness of Dudley became notorious. At a time when my better feelings were subdued, I was drawn to her; my fidelity to Madame de Mortsauf only increased her desire. Lady Dudley craved the chase, and my resistance inflamed her passion. Her mocking remark about my relationship with Madame de Mortsauf—“I am wearied to death of these turtle-dove sighings”—should have warned me.

Men have fewer means to resist women than women have to escape men. Social codes keep men from appearing harsh; women are expected to be coy. Though I loved Madame de Mortsauf, I was not immune to Lady Dudley’s beauty and rank. Her attention in society stirred me; she often hinted she would be my mistress. After one beguiling evening she made her intentions clear, provoking scandal in the British aristocracy.

Seemingly fragile, Lady Dudley possessed a strong will. Her physical courage was remarkable—she could outpace any horse—and she pursued her desires relentlessly. By contrast, Madame de Mortsauf was nurturing and expansive, drawing others into her warmth; Lady Dudley was intense and consuming. English customs reflect a materialist bent, valuing comfort and order often at the expense of spiritual depth. Lady Dudley embodied that materialism: compelling physically but leaving an emptiness I did not feel with Madame de Mortsauf, whose love was spiritual and boundless.

Lady Dudley’s jealousy was masked by charm; she sought to erase Madame de Mortsauf from my heart. I continued to write to Madame de Mortsauf, but my letters went unanswered, filling me with dread. When I finally returned to Clochegourde, a coldness revealed the harm my infidelity had caused.

Madame de Mortsauf’s demeanor had altered; she was distant and sorrowful, her beauty dimmed by grief. The count, unaware of what had provoked her change, welcomed me warmly, while she remained aloof. Realizing the depth of my wrongdoing, I learned the count’s health and spirits had suffered; Madame de Mortsauf, devoted to her children, sought solace in them even as despair crept in.

In desperation I sought her forgiveness, but she rebuffed me, insisting I had caused her too much pain. Left to grapple with guilt, I was forced to confront the true nature of love and sacrifice: my desires carried duties, and the harm I caused those I cared for weighed heavily on me.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
I have rarely seen anything more destructive than the wit of an Englishwoman; it wears the pompous certainty with which the British mask prejudice. French wit is lace, adorning joys and quarrels; English wit is corrosive, stripping flesh to the bone. Arabella wielded that power like a sultan, lopping rivals with her words.

She came to me with a speech that mingled moral argument and flattery. Calling me “my angel,” she insisted loving me could not be a crime; God created beauty to inspire adoration, and to refuse love would be impiety. Henriette, she said, insulted me by comparing me to ordinary men; I was above moral law. Arabella framed her passion as higher, almost divine, and contrasted it with Henriette’s supposed reserve. “Don’t frown; I do not wish her ill. I adore the morality that has freed you for me—because you are mine forever, aren’t you?” When I answered yes, “forever and ever,” she rejoiced: she alone understood my worth; she would cultivate my heart while Henriette minded estates.

Her intoxication revealed what followed. Arabella’s impulsive affection sought to erase Henriette’s impressions; her fierce attack betrayed fear and admiration. After a night of jealous imaginings she was tearful, lamenting a sleepless night. “I fear my love will ruin me,” she sobbed. “I have given all. She has something you still desire. If you prefer her, forget me; I will not trouble you. I will go far and die, like a plant deprived of sunlight.” She extorted promises, then, reassured, dried her eyes. “Go back to her; I do not want her to owe you to my force, but to your will. If you return, I shall know you love me as I love you, which I have always doubted.”

Under Arabella’s pressure I returned to Clochegourde. I did not then grasp how false my position was; had I refused, Lady Dudley might have found in Henriette a triumph and taken me to Paris. My coming back, however, insulted Madame de Mortsauf; would any loving woman forgive such a crime? Unless she were an angel, a loving woman would sooner see her lover dead than happy with another. By leaving Clochegourde for the Grenadiere I fatally wounded my true love and favored the transient passion that had ensnared me. Lady Dudley had played her cards with skill.

When I met Henriette that morning she was pale and depressed, as if sleep had fled her. Perception revealed acts the world ignores but lofty spirits call criminal. Like a child at a precipice I felt separated from her by a universe. Henriette’s illusions were shattered; her heart had endured. She refused to look at me, denying me the light that had shone on my life for six years. I felt the wrong of bringing into this calm roof a face that had known joy. Had I let Lady Dudley go and returned repentant, perhaps Henriette would not have resolved to treat me as a brother. But now she paid me ostentatious attentions—humiliating, like caring for a sick man.

“You were out walking early,” the count remarked, to make Henriette smile. The lightness completed my sense of ridicule. Day and night I could not be at Clochegourde and Saint-Cyr. By day, the transition from lover to brother seemed illness. I was ashamed; I cursed the pleasure Lady Dudley gave me; I wished Henriette would demand my blood. I could not tear Lady Dudley apart before Henriette; Henriette avoided mention of Arabella; had I spoken, Henriette would have despised me. After five years of intimacy we had nothing left to say; our words had no connection to our thoughts; we hid intolerable pain.

Henriette feigned cheerfulness but suffered. Calling herself my sister, she made no common ground, and we sat in constrained silence. Once, when I said I suffered more than she did, she asked haughtily, “How so?” “Because I am the one to blame.” Her manner grew cold enough that I resolved to leave Clochegourde. That evening on the terrace I took leave. As I mounted my horse she said, “Let us walk down the avenue together, alone.” At the grove that marked the boundary she stopped.

“Farewell, my friend,” she said, resting her head on my chest and wrapping her arms around me. “Farewell, we shall never meet again. God has given me the sad power to see the future. Do you remember the terror when you first returned, young and handsome? I saw you turn your back on me as you do now. Once again I have seen into the future. Friend, we speak for the last time. I can hardly say a few words; part of me speaks. Death has seized something in me. You have taken the mother from her children; I ask you to take her place. You can; Jacques and Madeleine love you, as if you had always made them suffer.”

“Die!” I cried, terrified by the fire in her eyes. “Henriette, I command you to live. Swear to me you will send for Origet and obey him in everything.”

“Would you oppose God’s mercy?” she cried, misreading me.

“You do not love me enough to obey blindly, as that miserable Lady Dudley does?”

“Yes, yes, I will do all you ask,” she cried, goaded by jealousy.

“Then I stay,” I said, kissing her eyelids.

Frightened, she escaped my arms and walked home without looking back. I followed; she was weeping and praying. We reached the lawn; I respectfully kissed her hand. This submission touched her.

“I am yours—forever, and as you will,” I said; “for I love you as your aunt loved you.”

She trembled and wrung my hand.

“One look,” I implored, “one last of our old looks! The woman who gives herself wholly gives less of life and soul than I have now received. Henriette, thou art my best-beloved—my only love.”

“I shall live!” she said; “but cure yourself as well.”

That look erased Arabella’s memory. Thus I was the plaything of two irreconcilable passions: I loved an angel and a demon; two women equally beautiful—one adorned with virtues we decry because they reveal our imperfections, the other with vices we idolize through selfishness. Returning down the avenue, seeing Madame de Mortsauf surrounded by her children, I felt a brief, guilty pride in being arbiter of two destinies; proud of inspiring such passion that death must come to whichever I abandoned. That conceit punished me doubly.

I do not know why I remained with Arabella and awaited the moment the count’s death might give me Henriette—she would ever love me. Her harshness, tears, remorse, and Christian resignation testified to a feeling that could not be erased. Walking the avenue I felt no longer twenty-five but fifty; a man passes quickly from youth to middle age. Perhaps evil thoughts came from the Tuileries, the king’s cabinet. Who could resist the polluting spirit of Louis XVIII?

Back in Paris Arabella and I grew intimate. We soon abandoned the conventional restrictions I had tried to keep—the comedies and concealments that renew desire. Young passions raze forests rather than cut timber. Arabella adopted none of the bourgeois strategies; she wished to exhibit me to all Paris as her sposo. She used her powers of seduction to keep me under her roof, not content with whispered scandal. Seeing her happy in an imprudence that frankly admitted her position, how could I not believe her love?

Once plunged into our illegal union, despair seized me; I saw my life bound to a course defying Henriette’s ideas and advice. I lived in a rage like consumptives who, knowing death near, cannot bear scrutiny. There was no corner of my heart where I could escape suffering; an avenging spirit filled me with forbidden thoughts. My letters to Henriette depicted this moral malady and did her infinite harm. “At the cost of so many treasures lost, I wished you to be at least happy,” she wrote in the only answer I received. But I was not happy. Happiness is absolute; it admits no comparisons. Once the first ardor fades, comparisons arise. All great passions first smooth character’s asperities; later, when lovers are accustomed, moral physiognomies reappear and natural antipathies resurface. This period began with me. Less blinded, I undertook a critical examination of Lady Dudley that harmed her.

First, she lacked the qualities of mind that distinguish Frenchwomen. When a Frenchwoman loves she metamorphoses: coquetry adorns love; she abandons dangerous vanity and claims no merit but loving well. She espouses her lover’s interests and, heedless and prodigal by nature, acquires the experience to manage suddenly. She becomes mother, adviser, doctor, blending other nations’ traits with a vivifying wit that relieves monotony. A Frenchwoman can love always, in public or solitude; in public she can speak by silence or look. The Englishwoman, by contrast, makes her love bend to the world. Educated to icy manners, she opens and shuts her heart with a British mechanism. Passionate with no eye upon her, she is cold before the world. A lover doubts his empire seeing her immobility, her aloof countenance, the calm voice with which she leaves her boudoir. Hypocrisy becomes indifference; she seems to have forgotten all.

A woman who can lay aside her love like a garment may be thought capable of changing it. What tempests in a man’s heart when he sees a woman take and drop attachment like embroidery! Such women, too much mistress of themselves, never wholly belong to you; society's influence prevents you reigning supreme. Where a Frenchwoman comforts with a look, an Englishwoman’s silence irritates and frets. Fashion often reigns over pleasures. An Englishwoman forces everything into form; Protestantism’s reasoning kills belief; it is the death of art and love. Passionate hearts flee worldliness.

Imagine my shock to find Lady Dudley could not live without the world; the English system of two lives was natural to her. When she loved, she loved madly—no woman could compare—but when the curtain fell, she banished memory of it. In public she never answered a look or smile; she was ambassadress-like, folding phrases and elbows; her composure outraged me. She degraded love to a need rather than an ideal. She expressed neither fear nor regret nor desire; then, at a given hour, tenderness reappeared like sudden fire.

By a thousand pinpricks I felt the infinite differences between Henriette and Arabella. Madame de Mortsauf’s smallest movements—the sound of her gown, her lowered eyelids, her musical voice—were a continual caress, a consistent thought. She did not halve her soul for two atmospheres. Arabella’s mind existed for the world and sarcasm; she loved to rend and bite to satisfy craving. Henriette would have hidden her happiness; Lady Dudley chose to exhibit hers to all Paris yet kept an impenetrable English mask in public. This mixture of ostentation and dignity, love and coldness, wounded me. When I complained, she replied with triple sting, mingling boasts of love with cutting sarcasm. She caricatured my ideas and dared to jest about kissing me in the opera before all Paris. Despite passion, she was never meditative or reverent like Henriette; she hungered for excitement, noise, and celebrity; everything concerned herself, not me.

Henriette’s letter—proof of how this virtuous woman obeyed the genius of a Frenchwoman—shows how she studied my prospects, political relations, moral conquests, and with what ardor she took hold of my life within permissible bounds. Lady Dudley affected the reticence of an acquaintance; she never inquired into my affairs as a man. Prodigal for herself but not generous, she separated self-interest and love too decidedly. I knew, without proving it, that to save me a pang, Henriette would have sought what she would never seek for herself. In any great misfortune I would have turned to Henriette for counsel; I would sooner have been dragged to prison than say a word to Lady Dudley.

The contrast extended to outward things. In France luxury expresses the man; it reproduces his ideas and gives attention value between lovers. English luxury, at first alluring, proved mechanical. The thousand attentions at Clochegourde Arabella considered servants’ business; each had a duty as if horses. She never attached to servants; money could replace them. Her selfishness was candid and created laughter. Crimson draperies covered an iron nature. The siren of amorous folly could make a young man forget the hard Englishwoman; only gradually I discovered the rock where my seeds were wasted. Madame de Mortsauf had seen this at a glance. She was right; Arabella’s love became intolerable to me. I have since remarked that many women who ride well show little tenderness; like Amazons, they lack a breast; their hearts harden in some direction.

When weariness seized me and I comprehended the sanctity true feeling imparts to love, when memories of Clochegourde brought the fragrance of roses, the warmth of the terrace, and the warble of nightingales, I received a blow whose echo wakes still in my heart.

I was working in the king’s cabinet when the Duc de Lenoncourt entered. The king asked news of the countess. I raised my head hastily; the king, offended, gave me the look that precedes harsh words. “Sire, my poor daughter is dying,” replied the duke.

“Will the king deign to grant me leave of absence?” I cried, tears in my eyes.

“Go, my lord,” he answered, withholding reprimand in satire.

More courtier than father, the duke asked no leave but got into the carriage with the king. I left without bidding Lady Dudley goodbye, leaving a note that I was sent on a mission. At the Croix de Berny I met his Majesty returning; he threw me an ironic look, as if to say, “If you mean to be anything in politics, come back; don’t parley with the dead.” The pompous equipages rolled away to cries of “Vive le Roi!” It seemed the court had driven over the dead body of Madame de Mortsauf with utter insensibility. Though the duke was an excellent man, he would no doubt play whist with Monsieur after the king retired. The duchess had long ago given her daughter the first stab by writing of Lady Dudley.

My hurried journey felt like a ruined gambler’s dream; I despaired at receiving no news. Had the confessor excluded me? I accused everyone. Beyond Tours, on the poplar-bordered road to Poncher, I met Monsieur Origet. He guessed I was going to Clochegourde and told me: “I doubt if you find her living. She is dying a frightful death—of inanition. When she called me last June, no medical power could control the disease; she had the symptoms Monsieur de Mortsauf knows, for he thinks he has them. Madame la comtesse was not in a transient condition; her disease had reached a point where science is useless; it is the incurable result of grief. Her physical condition is produced by inertia of an organ as necessary as the heart. Grief has done the work of a dagger. Don’t deceive yourself; she is dying of hidden grief.”

“Hidden?” I exclaimed.

“No,” he said, “and since she was so seriously attacked, Monsieur de Mortsauf ceased to torment her. I am no longer needed; Monsieur Deslandes is all-sufficient; nothing can be done; her sufferings are dreadful. Young, beautiful, and rich, to die emaciated—for she dies of hunger! During the last forty days the stomach has rejected all nourishment.”

Origet pressed my hand with respect. “Courage, monsieur,” he said, lifting his eyes.

These words were poisoned arrows. I sprang into the carriage and promised a reward if I arrived in time.

Despite impatience the distance seemed short as bitter reflections crowded my soul. Dying of grief, yet her children were well? Then she died through me! My conscience arraigned me. What weakness in human justice, which avenges open deeds but forgives murderers of the soul! How many victims—Madame de Beauseant, the Duchesse de Langeais, Lady Brandon—our period teems with such events. Society and science are accomplices in crimes without assizes. The world declares no one dies of grief; our scientific labels serve as passports to the coffin. Is there some law we do not know? Must the century pitilessly strew earth with corpses? My God! do I belong to the race of tigers?

Remorse gripped me as I entered Clochegourde’s avenue on a damp October morning. Jacques, Madeleine, and the Abbé Dominis knelt at the foot of a wooden cross. I sprang from the carriage toward them, my heart aching. The old huntsman stood apart.

I kissed Jacques and Madeleine; they gave a cold look. The abbé rose; I took him by the arm. “Is she still alive?” I implored. He bowed sadly and said it might be better to wait; she was greatly changed. “It is necessary to prepare her for this interview, or it might increase her suffering—death would be a blessed release.”

He told me how for six months the countess had suffered more despite Origet’s care. The doctor had come every evening for two months. “To heal the body, the heart must first be healed,” the doctor had said. As the illness increased, the countess’s words grew bitter; she alternated calls to live with repentant cries. “She falls, but each time she stumbles she rises higher toward heaven,” he said.

I cried, “Surely this pure lily will flower in heaven?”

“You left her still a flower,” he answered. “You will find her consumed, purified, pure as a diamond buried in ashes.”

The count, white-headed, hurried out, declaring, “She was right! He is here! ‘Felix, Felix, Felix has come!’ she kept crying. My dear friend, death is here. Why did it not take a poor madman like me?”

We reached the house. The abbe warned me: “Madame la comtesse begs you will not enter yet.” Inside, servants moved, all dumb with grief. Manette searched for Madeleine; we paced the lawn. I saw the dear “mignonne” gathering autumn flowers. Overcome, I sat, broken.

“Poor Felix,” said the count, “she forbade me to write to you. She knew how much you loved her.”

All was gloom. The servants wept; the place seemed uninhabited. The count, speaking in bursts, blamed and then wept for his wife, mixing despair with accusations. Madeleine told me her mother was ready; the Abbé Birotteau accompanied me. He warned, “I have done all to prevent this meeting. The salvation of this saint required it. I shall be present to protect you against yourself and perhaps against her. Respect her weakness. She is dying of hunger and thirst. Since morning she suffers feverish irritation. Pray God she die not in a moment of doubt.”

We reached the chamber. Henriette sat in white, before the fireplace, flowers about her. The abbe’s amazement at the room’s restored tenderness showed she had prepared to receive me. Her shrunken face had the greenish pallor of a magnolia; hollow eyes shone; her forehead kept proportion; inward fires issued despite waxy features. The smile on her whitened lips resembled death’s grin. Her robe showed emaciation. She knew she was changed; thought filled her with bitterness. No longer the sublime Madame de Mortsauf, she was a nameless thing struggling against annihilation. I took her dry, burning hand to kiss. Her discolored lips tried a smile—the forced smile of one seeking to hide irony, expectation, or fury.

“Ah, my poor Felix, this is death,” she said, “and you do not like death; odious death. This is the end of love; I knew it would be so. Lady Dudley will never see you thus surprised. Ah! why have I so longed for you, Felix? You have come at last, and I reward your devotion by the same horrible sight that made the Comte de Rance a Trappist. I, who hoped to remain beautiful and noble in your memory, destroy your illusions! True love cannot calculate. But stay; do not go, stay. Monsieur Origet said I was better this morning; I shall recover. Your looks will bring me back to life. When I regain strength and nourishment, I shall be beautiful again. I am scarcely thirty-five; there are many years of happiness before me—we will leave Clochegourde and go to Italy.”

Tears filled my eyes; I turned to the window. She pressed her burning lips to my ear: “My anguish has been in not seeing thee! Did you not bid me live? I will live; I will ride on horseback; I will know life, Paris, fêtes, pleasures!”

Those tones pictured a lifetime’s battles—the anguish of true love lost. The countess rose with the motion of a child. The confessor fell on his knees and prayed. She flung an arm around my neck, kissed me violently, and cried, “You shall not escape me now!” She fainted; I laid her on the bed. “You carried me thus before,” she murmured.

Deslandes entered; surprised at the decorations, he declared the flowers gave her delirium. The doctor felt a pulse and murmured that it was the forty-second day since she had eaten or drunk. The Abbé Birotteau took me to the garden. “Let us leave her to the doctor,” he said; “they will wrap her in opium. Well, you have heard her—if indeed it is she.”

“No,” I said, “it is not she.”

Stupefied, I hid in a punt to be alone. I tried to detach myself from my life—torture like that afflicts adulterers. My life was a failure. Despair suggested dying beside her or burying myself among Trappists. I looked at the windows where Henriette lay, fancying I saw the light burning the night I betrothed my soul to hers. Ought I not to have followed the simple life she created, keeping faithful while I worked in the world? Had she not bidden me become great to be saved from shameful passions? Chastity! Love as Arabella understood it disgusted me.

Madeleine walked slowly and sat on a bench. When I approached she rose and returned to the house, hating me; she fled from her mother’s murderer. At the portico Jacques sat, expressionless. Young persons carrying death are often unmoved at funerals. I longed to question Jacques.

“You know, Jacques,” I said, “that in me you have a most devoted brother.”

“Your friendship is useless; I shall follow my mother,” he replied sullenly.

They each had a fatal secret. The brother and sister avoided each other. Henriette lay low; all was ruins at Clochegourde.

“Madame is asleep,” Manette said, happy that the countess was out of pain. “Monsieur Deslandes ordered the flowers taken away; they excited Madame’s nerves,” she added.

I returned as the sunset gilded Azay’s roofs. All was calm and pure. A soft light lit the bed where my Henriette lay wrapped in opium. The body seemed annihilated; the soul reigned. Blanche and Henriette, two sublime faces of one woman, reappeared. The abbes sat at the bed. The count stood stupefied. I took a seat on the sofa. We exchanged looks of admiration mingled with tears. The Abbé Dominis and I communicated in signs: yes, angels were watching. Her face cleared; the green of suffering gave way to pure white pallor; she seemed to embrace the divine.

Madeleine knelt, adoring, crying “My mother!” Jacques smiled; he would follow her. “She is entering the haven,” said the Abbé Birotteau. The Angelus rang; Nature’s poetry joined the sacred hour. We burst into tears. Even servants knelt and prayed. The doctor signaled that this sleep was the hour without pain; it was time for the last sacraments. At nine o’clock she awoke quietly with gentle eyes, and we beheld her in former beauty.

“Mother! you are too beautiful to die—life and health are coming back to you!” cried Madeleine.

“Dear daughter, I shall live—in thee,” she answered, smiling.

They embraced. The count kissed his wife. She colored when she saw me.

“Dear Felix,” she said, “this is, I think, the only grief I have ever caused you. Forget all I may have said—I, a poor creature beside myself.” She held out her hand; I took and kissed it. “As in the old days, Felix?” she asked.

We left the room during the last confession. I approached Madeleine. In the presence of others she could not escape civility; but, like her mother, she looked at no one.

“Dear Madeleine,” I said softly, “what have you against me? Why such coldness before death, which should reconcile us?”

“I hear in my heart what my mother says,” she replied, with a look like a rigid religious painting.

“And you condemn me when your mother absolves me—if indeed I am guilty.”

“You, you—always yourself!” Her voice revealed determined hatred, implacable as judgment.

An hour passed in silence. The Abbé Birotteau returned after receiving the countess’s confession. We found Henriette sitting up, dressed in the long white garment to be her shroud. She had had her letters burned—sacrifice she had delayed until this hour. She smiled with eyes moist and inward lucidity; she saw the promised land.

“Dear Felix,” she said, pressing my hand, “stay with us. You must be present at my last scene; be not absent from the least painful among many things in which you are concerned.”

She made a sign and the door closed. She knelt before the astonished count, then laid her head on his knees.

“Though I have been a faithful wife to you,” she said faintly, “I have sometimes failed in my duty. I have given a friendship outside my family more affectionate care than to you. Perhaps I have irritated you by comparisons. I have had a deep friendship which no one—not even he who has been its object—has fully known. Though virtuous by human laws and an irreproachable wife, other thoughts, voluntary or involuntary, have crossed my mind, and in this hour I fear I welcomed them too warmly. But having loved you and continued submissive, I now pray for your blessing with a clean heart. I shall die without a bitter thought if I can hear a tender word for your Blanche, for the mother of your children—if you forgive her those things she did not forgive herself till reassured by the great tribunal which pardons all.”

“Blanche, Blanche!” cried the broken man, shedding tears on her head. “Would you kill me?” He raised and kissed her solemnly. “Have I no forgiveness to ask? Have I never been harsh?”

“Perhaps,” she said. “But dear friend, indulge a dying woman’s weakness; tranquillize me. Will you grant that I leave to our friend here that pledge of my affection?” She showed a letter on the mantel. “He is now my adopted son, and that is all. The heart makes bequests; my last wishes impose a duty on that dear Felix. If you do not wish to marry Madeleine, at least seek my soul’s repose by making Monsieur de Mortsauf as happy as he can be. Farewell, long farewell like that I gave last night to our valley, where I shall rest and where you will often—will you not?—return.”

Henriette’s last words lighted the depths of her life. My clouds of egotism rolled away. She had suffered as much as I—more, for she was dead in spirit. She believed others would be kind to her friend; she was blind to her daughter’s enmity. That last proof of tenderness pained me. Poor Henriette wished to give me Clochegourde and her daughter.

From that dread day when I first followed my noble Henriette to the grave, the sun has been less warm, nights more gloomy, movement less agile, thought more dull. Some departed we bury in earth; others are buried within us, their memory mingling with our heartbeats; they live in us by a metempsychosis of love. A soul is within my soul. When some good thing is done by me, that soul acts and speaks. All that is good within me issues from that grave; sarcasm and bitterness are mine. Natalie, when my eyes are darkened or my lips silent, do not ask, “Of what are you thinking?”

I ceased writing for days; these memories were too bitter. Henriette’s letter set a star of hope before me. To live at Clochegourde with Madeleine, consecrating my life to her, seemed a rescue. But it was necessary to know her true feelings. As I bade the count farewell, we walked the terrace. He spoke of his loss but soon of the future; he feared his daughter, who lacked her mother’s gentleness. Madeleine’s firm character alarmed him; he foresaw a struggle between father and daughter ending in bitterness. His son was to leave; his daughter marry; what son-in-law might she have? Though talking of dying, he feared being left alone without sympathy.

Madeleine could see me from the terrace but would not come down. I seized a moment to beg the count to call her, saying I had a last wish of her mother to convey. The count fetched her and left us alone.

“Dear Madeleine,” I said, “if I speak to you it should be here where your mother listened to me. I know your thoughts; but are you not condemning me without knowledge of facts? My life and happiness are bound up in this place; you know that, yet you seek to banish me by coldness instead of brotherly affection that death should have strengthened. If my mother’s name still has power, in her name I beg you never to return me to Clochegourde so long as you are in it. The sight of me causes a repugnance I cannot express, which I shall never overcome.”

She stopped me.

“Monsieur,” she said, voice trembling, “I know all your thoughts; but I shall not change my feelings. I would rather fling myself into the Indre than ally myself to you. If my mother’s name has power, in her name I beg you never to return while I am here. The sight of you causes repugnance I cannot overcome.”

She bowed with dignity and returned to the house without looking back. The searching eye of that young girl had discovered her mother’s secrets; her hatred of the man she fancied fatal to her mother may have been intensified by a sense of complicit shame.

All before me was chaos. Madeleine hated me without considering cause or victim. The edifice of my happiness fell in ruins. I alone knew the life of that unknown woman; none had fathomed her heart. Strange truth: how many families have their Henriette! How many noble feelings leave earth with no historian to measure their depths! Mothers know their children little; so with husbands, lovers, brothers. When Madeleine disappeared, I left with a broken heart. Bidding farewell to my host at Sache, I started for Paris, following the right bank of the Indre.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

At the story’s opening the modern Stanhope press and ink‑distributing roller were unknown in provincial shops. In Angoulême, tied to Paris by its paper mills, wooden presses still groaned: leather ink‑balls applied ink, and the pressman inked by hand. This old mechanism, cherished by Jérôme‑Nicolas Séchard, anchors the tale.

Séchard, once a journeyman pressman, survived the Requisition of 1793, obtained a master‑printer’s license from a Representative of the People despite being illiterate, and bought his late master’s business cheaply. He partnered with the noble Comte de Maucombe, printed decrees discreetly during the Terror, and by 1795—after hiring a compositor who later became a bishop—had learned to run the shop. Frugal to avarice, he bought paper when prices fell, became his own landlord, and acquired the old printing house outright.

He had one son, David, schooled in grammar and later sent to Paris to be a suitable successor. David excelled academically and studied economically so as not to burden his father. Séchard’s shop enjoyed a regional monopoly on official printing until the Cointet Brothers pushed for a second license, threatening that position. Confident in David, Séchard looked forward to retirement.

Now in his seventies and drinking since his wife’s death, Séchard’s bloated face, shrewd gaze, three‑cornered hat and old‑fashioned clothes matched his habits. When David returned, Séchard feigned warmth but drove business, seeing him as both heir and rival. The long‑established shop—a large room with a glass door to the workshop—drew customers who watched the processes. The vine‑grown yard, the kitchen where inky runoff mixed with household waste, and the cramped, unchanged living quarters above reflected Jérôme‑Nicolas’s stinginess and single‑minded pursuit of profit.

The sitting‑room, partly modernized by the late Mme. Séchard with powder‑blue wainscoting and Oriental paper, remained sparsely furnished: lyre‑backed chairs, bare arched windows, and incomplete plans—no clock, sconces, or mirror, because Mme. Séchard died before finishing them. Practical and profit‑minded, Jérôme‑Nicolas left it as it was.

He brought David in, showed him a valuation of the printing plant, and had him read the inventory aloud. David scoffed at the old presses and flung aside the paper; affronted, Séchard led him downstairs and briskly demonstrated the presses’ efficiency, challenging David to name an English press that could match his. He boasted they could earn enough without a foreman and dismissed newer machinery as ruinous to type. David doubted the high price placed on the type, which Séchard defended—insisting it had been cast at M. Vaflard’s foundry and, after twelve years, was nearly new. The inventory totaled thirty thousand francs, including license and goodwill. David, remembering his father had once bought the business for ten thousand francs, found the asking price exorbitant and hesitated about whether he could afford it.

Séchard, who preferred argument to silence, pressed the point: the license alone brought income from advertisements and official work, and attached to the valuation was a partnership deed—Séchard would rent the premises to David for twelve hundred francs a year and reserve a room for himself. Cornered, David calculated the license and goodwill’s worth and, thinking he might manage the debt, accepted the deal to his father’s surprise.

The next day Séchard had the apprentice move David’s belongings to the attic, leaving him three empty rooms and no reserve of funds to pay wages. When David asked for help, Séchard refused—he had already paid his share—and warned that any legal claim to Mme. Séchard’s fortune would be long and costly. Humiliated and alone, David resolved to work hard.

Séchard spoke then of a “treasure” he left behind—Marion, a capable country girl who ran many tasks in the shop—and departed for his Marsac vineyard, fretting over the thirty thousand francs and making periodic visits, suspecting David’s mismanagement as the Cointet Brothers gained ground.

David, indifferent to politics, struggled for work. The Cointets, aligned with the clergy, monopolized the market and smeared him as a Liberal and unreliable because of his father. Though Séchard at first urged David to hold out and even framed negotiations as protective—speaking of a sixty thousand franc price—David was ultimately forced to sell, marking the establishment’s decline. Séchard grew more detached, visiting less though still attached to the old shop.

David’s fortunes worsened when Lucien Chardon, an old school friend reduced to poverty after his father’s death, sought work; David made him a printer’s reader. Their friendship deepened—Lucien dreaming of poetry, David of natural science. Lucien’s accounts of his father’s schemes for refining sugar and making paper suggested other ventures. But those ambitions distracted them from immediate needs; while they indulged plans and conversation, the Cointet Brothers consolidated their advantage, Séchard & Son scraped by, and the building and workshop fell into disrepair.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
One day in May 1821 David and Lucien slipped out while the men dined and sat under the vines, where sunlight emphasized their differences. David was robust, fighter-built, swarthy and weathered—shaped by humble origins and melancholy reserve. Lucien was graceful and beautiful, with a fine profile and striking blue eyes; delicate in appearance but ambitious and shrewd, willing to treat society pragmatically and even use dubious means to rise.

Both were low in social standing but nourished loftier ideals, which made them bitter when reality intruded. Lucien, impetuous and adventurous, usually led; David, stronger but more introspective and cautious, admired him and often yielded to his charm, content to be the more ordinary companion. For three years their friendship thrived on literature and art: they read voraciously, worked hard, and dreamed of fame as an escape from hardship.

David brought a new volume of André de Chénier whose verses moved them, offering solace and kinship with the poet’s struggles. Their reverie was broken by Cérizet, an apprentice who brought a stranger seeking to print a monograph. Lucien eagerly engaged the author, who mentioned Mme. de Bargeton—a woman Lucien admired. He blushed, thanked the author, and confessed to David his passionate love. David cautioned him about social barriers, but Lucien hoped love could bridge them and vowed not to visit Mme. de Bargeton without David’s welcome—a pledge that revealed loyalty and pride.

Walking through Angoulême, Lucien reflected on the gulf between his origin and Mme. de Bargeton’s world. The city, perched on a crag, was split between an upper town of nobility and a lower town of commerce. The upper class clung to old values, resisted outsiders and reform, and looked down on merchants; the bourgeoisie resented this disdain. Despite some modernization, the hierarchy remained rigid, breeding tension and the prospect of social upheaval as the middle class pressed for recognition.

In that climate, Lucien’s ambition to enter Mme. de Bargeton’s circle was daunting. His background as a druggist’s son marked him an outsider, yet his literary talent, charm, and Mme. de Bargeton’s artistic tastes gave him hope. David’s mixture of admiration and concern captured their friendship: he understood Lucien’s depth but saw the obstacles. Lucien’s determination and his pledge to include David in any future visits encapsulate their bond as love and ambition unfold against entrenched divisions.

M. de Bargeton descended from a Bordeaux alderman ennobled under Louis XIII, but the family declined after his grandfather, “the Waster,” squandered its wealth. By 1789 he held only entailed lands and a house in Angoulême with modest rents. In 1805 he married Marie-Louise-Anaïs (Naïs), taught languages and music by the Abbé Niollant during the Revolution. Her father’s thrift and provincial seclusion produced a haughty independence; she married at thirty-six to a fifty-eight-year-old M. de Bargeton hoping for Paris and an inheritance that never came. Modest means and petty provincial life stifled her until M. du Châtelet arrived—charming and ambitious, scandalizing the local elite and invigorating her salon.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Political opinion was loud and exaggerated: the Quotidienne showed tepid loyalty, Louis XVIII was mocked, society women were awkward, and in the countryside lingering nobility masked many defects while Bourbon loyalty remained a provincial badge.

Châtelet sought solace in society because of his interest in Mme. de Bargeton. He flattered women, embodied what their husbands lacked, and courted the clergy and the queen of Angoulême’s petty tastes to keep his place. Mme. de Bargeton—Naïs—was enchanted by the Bourbon revival but longed for Parisian genius; through Châtelet she learned of a promising young poet in Angoulême.

That poet, Lucien, was unaware of the stir. Praised by his headmaster, he expected a warm reception at the Hôtel de Bargeton. His sister Éve secretly supported him—saving for clothes and coaching him in aristocratic manners—leaving him proud and anxious about his prospects.

At the Hôtel de Bargeton Lucien found the house less grand than imagined, but Naïs embodied the poetry he loved. She admired his beauty and diffidence; their meeting was mutually attractive, and Lucien overlooked her faded airs, sensing intimacy.

Châtelet, sensing a rival, asserted influence over Lucien with unsolicited advice about society and claimed Naïs. Though intimidated, Lucien continued to visit—initially flattered by noble condescension, later resentful.

Naïs cultivated Lucien as a project: patroness, confidante, and encourager of his literary ambitions. She introduced him to music and books, revealed vulnerabilities, and addressed him intimately. A portrait of a dead lover provoked jealousy, yet Lucien declared his feelings and their relationship deepened.

When their liaison became public the town buzzed. Châtelet tried to undermine Lucien; Naïs defended him, arguing genius can rise from humble origins. To launch him she hosted salons and urged him to adopt the noble name Rubempré, promising courtly patronage. Exhilarated, Lucien’s aspirations widened; his earlier egalitarian ideals gave way to social ambition. In a passionate letter he asked Naïs’s help for a friend, David, exposing his conflict between love, artistic sincerity, and pursuit of recognition.

Descending from Angoulême, Lucien worried that Mme. de Bargeton might be angry and whether she would receive David. He sensed the social gulf: a queen and her favorite occupied separate worlds, and Naïs could be ruined by associating with commoners. Lucien’s aristocratic instinct noted that noble bearing did not equal noble feeling; many notable men lacked polish. David lacked graces and would be ridiculed, Lucien feared. After a poetic reverie he returned to worldly wisdom, regretting his impulsive letter and anticipating the sacrifices success demanded. He preferred to win by his own efforts, inspired by Napoleon’s rise, though ashamed of his father’s shop.

At home M. Postel praised Lucien’s grandfather, pricking Lucien’s resentment. Searching the shop, Lucien found Mme. de Bargeton’s reply: she offered to help David but revealed prejudice toward lower social standing. Lucien read this as a triumph, convinced David would shine in her circle.

Éve noticed his elation and hoped for his happiness; Lucien reassured her, though Éve sensed his priorities shifting. David, feeling unworthy, decided to decline the invitation and remain a printer—his humility moving Lucien even as ambition began to eclipse gratitude.

Preparing for his first public reading, Lucien and David chose different poems. David and Éve shared a quiet moment hinting at mutual affection. At the reading Lucien met the awkward, ineffectual M. de Bargeton, whose limited intellect and shallow pleasantries underscored the social dynamics Lucien must navigate. The evening, marked by Lucien’s ambition and strained relationships, set the stage for future conflicts and aspirations.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
There was talk of nominating the mute gentleman for deputy, but Lucien, who had only briefly visited, had not yet fathomed him. M. de Bargeton, reclining and silent, watched with a cool reserve that awed Lucien; imaginative, Lucien fancied him a sphinx and behaved deferentially. “I am the first comer,” Lucien said with an exaggerated bow. “That is natural enough,” Bargeton replied; Lucien took it as a barb and grew self-conscious. When Lucien asked why those from farther away arrived earlier, Bargeton shrugged and relapsed into silence. Lucien tried to converse—imagining Mme. de Bargeton fussing over the room and his attire and lingering by a Japanese jar for a topic—but Bargeton’s curt answers and piercing scrutiny unnerved him.

The old servant announced “M. du Châtelet.” Baron Sixte entered with easy confidence, inspecting Lucien’s clothes and deepening his discomfort; he dismissed Lucien’s poetry as vaudeville. Mme. de Bargeton soon appeared, elaborately dressed and theatrically charming; Châtelet’s compliments delighted her while she scarcely acknowledged Lucien.

Guests arrived in a parade of social types: the dignified Bishop with his Vicar‑General; Mme. de Chandour and her eccentric husband with Amélie posing like a rival queen; the solemn M. de Saintot (Astolphe) and his austere, scientifically affected wife Élisa; M. de Bartas craving praise for his singing; the artist Alexandre de Brébian and his wife; fashion‑obsessed Lolotte and Fifine with their dowdy husbands. The Comte de Senonches came with Zéphirine, tall and striking despite ill health; Francis, a devoted family friend who ran their household, showed unusual attention to Zéphirine, provoking speculation. The wealthy Marquis de Pimentel and his wife arrived with the Baron and Baroness de Rastignac, drawing envy as Mme. de Bargeton lavished them with attention.

The Prefect and the General came last, followed by a nervous country gentleman who awkwardly offered a treatise on silkworms. Mme. and Mlle. du Brossard, noble but impoverished, hovered about, pressing social connections with thinly veiled desperation; M. de Séverac, a childless widower, spoke of nurseries while Mme. du Brossard pushed her daughter’s prospects.

Throughout the evening Lucien felt increasingly scrutinized and diminished. Most called him M. Chardon rather than M. de Rubempré, a stinging slight. The assembly seemed absorbed in flattery and rivalry rather than his art, leaving him isolated. When he read, announcing poems by André de Chénier provoked misunderstandings of affectation. Le Malade met mild applause; L’Aveugle fell flat amid impatience and inattention. Sweating under pressure, Lucien drew courage from Louise’s supportive glance and read Chénier’s Iambes, which finally captured some attention though many missed the meaning. During the break Zéphirine sent Francis to inquire after the poems; women whispered about Lucien’s origins. Amélie’s remarks about his noble name and printing background sparked gossip, complicating his precarious standing—yet absorbed in the poet’s beauty, Lucien remained largely unaware of the mounting tension.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Lucien tried to immortalize his love for Mme. de Bargeton with an ode he fancied rivaled Chénier’s. Confident under her protective gaze, he misread the room: the Baron dismissed the poem as ornate but empty, the women feigned admiration while whispering sarcastically, and Francis spread rumors that Lucien’s mother had written the verse. The Bishop’s sincere praise only intensified the aristocrats’ scorn, leaving Lucien overwhelmed and isolated.

After the mockery, Mme. de Bargeton withdrew with him to her boudoir, consoling him and romanticizing suffering as the road to greatness—urging him to turn pain into art. Meanwhile Lucien’s sister Eve and her lover David argued over his ambitions. David feared Lucien was unsuited to high society and might be ruined; Eve hoped they could sustain him until success. When David proposed, Eve hesitated at their unequal standings but accepted out of love and a desire to help Lucien. David vowed to provide so Lucien wouldn’t be alone. Frustrated and determined, Lucien resolved to win Mme. de Bargeton’s love and rise above his detractors.

In Angoulême the Bishop’s epigram and Mme. de Bargeton’s rejoinder made Lucien a local celebrity. Walking through Beaulieu he was envied and flattered. He told Louise of Eve’s impending marriage; she greeted him passionately, praised his reading, and promised a poetic refuge despite the envy around them. Buoyed by family and friends, Lucien’s self-regard swelled. He teasingly asked Louise to be his Beatrice; she promised future love and urged him toward greatness. With M. de Bargeton’s patronage and Louise’s intimacies he felt established—calling himself M. de Rubempré—and expected a literary breakthrough. Eve and David postponed their wedding to support him, though David feared Lucien might change with status.

Their closeness drew Châtelet’s attention; in the small town innocent interactions sparked gossip and constrained behavior. An outburst, misread by Stanislas, seeded rumor that Châtelet eagerly exploited while Stanislas reveled in the drama. Alarmed, Mme. de Bargeton defended her honor and urged her husband to act. Insecure but resolute, M. de Bargeton confronted Stanislas with modesty and won respect at a tense gathering—setting the stage for a confrontation that would entangle lives and expose the town’s mix of love, honor, and social expectation.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Châtelet, alarmed by a late visit to the bedridden M. de Bargeton and suspecting Naïs’s involvement, intervened as household authority. He asked whether Bargeton wished to see Stanislas; relieved to have a mediator, Bargeton was brought to Amélie’s room for a confrontation that could make Mme. de Bargeton a widow and implicate Lucien.

Châtelet warned Stanislas that Bargeton would press him about remarks involving Naïs and urged decorum. In the room, Bargeton demanded a retraction about Mme. de Bargeton and M. de Rubempré. Cornered, Stanislas accepted a duel and chose pistols.

News of the duel depressed the guests and divided opinion. Stanislas was shaken; Amélie, sensing the strain, fell ill and was removed. The men debated consequences, some recalling Bargeton’s former marksmanship.

At breakfast the next morning Mme. Chardon arrived with alarming news: Bargeton had nearly killed Stanislas after accusing him of compromising Mme. de Bargeton. Stanislas had been shot in the neck and was injured. Lucien, hearing this, publicly defended Mme. de Bargeton’s innocence.

Lucien then received a note from Louise warning him to avoid her home because of the duel’s fallout and urging him to spend the day with her. David, preparing for his own marriage, advised Lucien to limit visits to Mme. de Bargeton. Visiting her, Lucien found her at breakfast with her husband and M. de Nègrepelisse, who watched him closely.

Louise revealed plans to go to Paris to secure a political post for Bargeton and contain the scandal, and she pressed Lucien to accompany her, praising Paris over provincial stagnation. Torn by Eve’s imminent wedding and family duties, he hesitated; after a heated exchange Louise fainted and he, desperate, promised discretion and to be ready to leave the next night.

At home Eve and their mother fretted over wedding costs. David offered help and agreed to endorse a loan; they arranged to send Lucien’s belongings to Paris covertly. Mistrustful, Châtelet sent someone to follow Mme. de Bargeton. Early the next morning Lucien left with David as Mme. de Bargeton’s carriage approached.
